[SOT]Wilson was able to enjoy lovely things that passed him on the wing as[SEP]completely and deliberately as if they had been dug-up marvels, long[SEP]anticipated, and definitely fixed at the end of a railway journey.[SEP]For[SEP]a few pleasurable seconds he quite forgot where he was going, and only[SEP]after the door had closed behind her did he realize that the young[SEP]woman had entered the house to which he had directed his trunk from the[SEP]South Station that morning.[SEP]He hesitated a moment before mounting the[SEP]steps.[SEP]“Can that,” he murmured in amazement,—“can that possibly have[SEP]been Mrs.[SEP]Alexander?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]When the servant admitted him, Mrs.[SEP]Alexander was still standing in the[SEP]hallway.[SEP]She heard him give his name, and came forward holding out her[SEP]hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is it you, indeed, Professor Wilson?[SEP]I was afraid that you might get[SEP]here before I did.[SEP]I was detained at a concert, and Bartley telephoned[SEP]that he would be late.[SEP]Thomas will show you your room.[SEP]Had you rather[SEP]have your tea brought to you there, or will you have it down here with[SEP]me, while we wait for Bartley?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson was pleased to find that he had been the cause of her rapid[SEP]walk, and with her he was even more vastly pleased than before.[SEP]He[SEP]followed her through the drawing-room into the library, where the wide[SEP]back windows looked out upon the garden and the sunset and a fine[SEP]stretch of silver-colored river.[SEP]A harp-shaped elm stood stripped[SEP]against the pale-colored evening sky, with ragged last year’s birds’[SEP]nests in its forks, and through the bare branches the evening star[SEP]quivered in the misty air.[SEP]The long brown room breathed the peace of a[SEP]rich and amply guarded quiet.[SEP]Tea was brought in immediately and placed[SEP]in front of the wood fire.[SEP]Mrs.[SEP]Alexander sat down in a high-backed[SEP]chair and began to pour it, while Wilson sank into a low seat opposite[SEP]her and took his cup with a great sense of ease and harmony and[SEP]comfort.[EOT]
[SOT]“You have had a long journey, haven’t you?[SEP]” Mrs.[SEP]Alexander asked, after[SEP]showing gracious concern about his tea.[SEP]“And I am so sorry Bartley is[SEP]late.[SEP]He’s often tired when he’s late.[SEP]He flatters himself that it is a[SEP]little on his account that you have come to this Congress of[SEP]Psychologists.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It is,” Wilson assented, selecting his muffin carefully; “and I hope[SEP]he won’t be tired tonight.[SEP]But, on my own account, I’m glad to have a[SEP]few moments alone with you, before Bartley comes.[SEP]I was somehow afraid[SEP]that my knowing him so well would not put me in the way of getting to[SEP]know you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s very nice of you.[SEP]” She nodded at him above her cup and smiled,[SEP]but there was a little formal tightness in her tone which had not been[SEP]there when she greeted him in the hall.[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson leaned forward.[SEP]“Have I said something awkward?[SEP]I live very far[SEP]out of the world, you know.[SEP]But I didn’t mean that you would exactly[SEP]fade dim, even if Bartley were here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Mrs.[SEP]Alexander laughed relentingly.[SEP]“Oh, I’m not so vain![SEP]How terribly[SEP]discerning you are.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She looked straight at Wilson, and he felt that this quick, frank[SEP]glance brought about an understanding between them.[EOT]
[SOT]He liked everything about her, he told himself, but he particularly[SEP]liked her eyes; when she looked at one directly for a moment they were[SEP]like a glimpse of fine windy sky that may bring all sorts of weather.[EOT]
[SOT]“Since you noticed something,” Mrs.[SEP]Alexander went on, “it must have[SEP]been a flash of the distrust I have come to feel whenever I meet any of[SEP]the people who knew Bartley when he was a boy.[SEP]It is always as if they[SEP]were talking of someone I had never met.[SEP]Really, Professor Wilson, it[SEP]would seem that he grew up among the strangest people.[SEP]They usually say[SEP]that he has turned out very well, or remark that he always was a fine[SEP]fellow.[SEP]I never know what reply to make.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson chuckled and leaned back in his chair, shaking his left foot[SEP]gently.[SEP]“I expect the fact is that we none of us knew him very well,[SEP]Mrs.[SEP]Alexander.[SEP]Though I will say for myself that I was always[SEP]confident he’d do something extraordinary.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Mrs.[SEP]Alexander’s shoulders gave a slight movement, suggestive of[SEP]impatience.[SEP]“Oh, I should think that might have been a safe prediction.[SEP]Another cup, please?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, thank you.[SEP]But predicting, in the case of boys, is not so easy as[SEP]you might imagine, Mrs.[SEP]Alexander.[SEP]Some get a bad hurt early and lose[SEP]their courage; and some never get a fair wind.[SEP]Bartley”—he dropped his[SEP]chin on the back of his long hand and looked at her admiringly—“Bartley[SEP]caught the wind early, and it has sung in his sails ever since.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Mrs.[SEP]Alexander sat looking into the fire with intent preoccupation, and[SEP]Wilson studied her half-averted face.[SEP]He liked the suggestion of stormy[SEP]possibilities in the proud curve of her lip and nostril.[SEP]Without that,[SEP]he reflected, she would be too cold.[EOT]
[SOT]“I should like to know what he was really like when he was a boy.[SEP]I[SEP]don’t believe he remembers,” she said suddenly.[SEP]“Won’t you smoke, Mr.[SEP]Wilson?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson lit a cigarette.[SEP]“No, I don’t suppose he does.[SEP]He was never[SEP]introspective.[SEP]He was simply the most tremendous response to stimuli I[SEP]have ever known.[SEP]We didn’t know exactly what to do with him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]A servant came in and noiselessly removed the tea-tray.[SEP]Mrs.[SEP]Alexander[SEP]screened her face from the firelight, which was beginning to throw[SEP]wavering bright spots on her dress and hair as the dusk deepened.[EOT]
[SOT]“Of course,” she said, “I now and again hear stories about things that[SEP]happened when he was in college.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But that isn’t what you want.[SEP]” Wilson wrinkled his brows and looked at[SEP]her with the smiling familiarity that had come about so quickly.[SEP]“What[SEP]you want is a picture of him, standing back there at the other end of[SEP]twenty years.[SEP]You want to look down through my memory.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She dropped her hands in her lap.[SEP]“Yes, yes; that’s exactly what I[SEP]want.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]At this moment they heard the front door shut with a jar, and Wilson[SEP]laughed as Mrs.[SEP]Alexander rose quickly.[SEP]“There he is.[SEP]Away with[SEP]perspective![SEP]No past, no future for Bartley; just the fiery moment.[SEP]The[SEP]only moment that ever was or will be in the world![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The door from the hall opened, a voice called “Winifred?[SEP]” hurriedly,[SEP]and a big man came through the drawing-room with a quick, heavy tread,[SEP]bringing with him a smell of cigar smoke and chill out-of-doors air.[SEP]When Alexander reached the library door, he switched on the lights and[SEP]stood six feet and more in the archway, glowing with strength and[SEP]cordiality and rugged, blond good looks.[SEP]There were other[SEP]bridge-builders in the world, certainly, but it was always Alexander’s[SEP]picture that the Sunday Supplement men wanted, because he looked as a[SEP]tamer of rivers ought to look.[SEP]Under his tumbled sandy hair his head[SEP]seemed as hard and powerful as a catapult, and his shoulders looked[SEP]strong enough in themselves to support a span of any one of his ten[SEP]great bridges that cut the air above as many rivers.[EOT]
[SOT]After dinner Alexander took Wilson up to his study.[SEP]It was a large room[SEP]over the library, and looked out upon the black river and the row of[SEP]white lights along the Cambridge Embankment.[SEP]The room was not at all[SEP]what one might expect of an engineer’s study.[SEP]Wilson felt at once the[SEP]harmony of beautiful things that have lived long together without[SEP]obtrusions of ugliness or change.[SEP]It was none of Alexander’s doing, of[SEP]course; those warm consonances of color had been blending and mellowing[SEP]before he was born.[SEP]But the wonder was that he was not out of place[SEP]there,—that it all seemed to glow like the inevitable background for[SEP]his vigor and vehemence.[SEP]He sat before the fire, his shoulders deep in[SEP]the cushions of his chair, his powerful head upright, his hair rumpled[SEP]above his broad forehead.[SEP]He sat heavily, a cigar in his large, smooth[SEP]hand, a flush of after-dinner color in his face, which wind and sun and[SEP]exposure to all sorts of weather had left fair and clear-skinned.[EOT]
[SOT]“You are off for England on Saturday, Bartley, Mrs.[SEP]Alexander tells[SEP]me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, for a few weeks only.[SEP]There’s a meeting of British engineers, and[SEP]I’m doing another bridge in Canada, you know.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, every one knows about that.[SEP]And it was in Canada that you met your[SEP]wife, wasn’t it?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, at Allway.[SEP]She was visiting her great-aunt there.[SEP]A most[SEP]remarkable old lady.[SEP]I was working with MacKeller then, an old Scotch[SEP]engineer who had picked me up in London and taken me back to Quebec[SEP]with him.[SEP]He had the contract for the Allway Bridge, but before he[SEP]began work on it he found out that he was going to die, and he advised[SEP]the committee to turn the job over to me.[SEP]Otherwise I’d never have got[SEP]anything good so early.[SEP]MacKeller was an old friend of Mrs.[SEP]Pemberton,[SEP]Winifred’s aunt.[SEP]He had mentioned me to her, so when I went to Allway[SEP]she asked me to come to see her.[SEP]She was a wonderful old lady.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Like her niece?[SEP]” Wilson queried.[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley laughed.[SEP]“She had been very handsome, but not in Winifred’s[SEP]way.[SEP]When I knew her she was little and fragile, very pink and white,[SEP]with a splendid head and a face like fine old lace, somehow,—but[SEP]perhaps I always think of that because she wore a lace scarf on her[SEP]hair.[SEP]She had such a flavor of life about her.[SEP]She had known Gordon and[SEP]Livingstone and Beaconsfield when she was young,—every one.[SEP]She was the[SEP]first woman of that sort I’d ever known.[SEP]You know how it is in the[SEP]West,—old people are poked out of the way.[SEP]Aunt Eleanor fascinated me[SEP]as few young women have ever done.[SEP]I used to go up from the works to[SEP]have tea with her, and sit talking to her for hours.[SEP]It was very[SEP]stimulating, for she couldn’t tolerate stupidity.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It must have been then that your luck began, Bartley,” said Wilson,[SEP]flicking his cigar ash with his long finger.[SEP]“It’s curious, watching[SEP]boys,” he went on reflectively.[SEP]“I’m sure I did you justice in the[SEP]matter of ability.[SEP]Yet I always used to feel that there was a weak spot[SEP]where some day strain would tell.[SEP]Even after you began to climb, I[SEP]stood down in the crowd and watched you with—well, not with confidence.[SEP]The more dazzling the front you presented, the higher your facade rose,[SEP]the more I expected to see a big crack zigzagging from top to[SEP]bottom,”—he indicated its course in the air with his forefinger,—“then[SEP]a crash and clouds of dust.[SEP]It was curious.[SEP]I had such a clear picture[SEP]of it.[SEP]And another curious thing, Bartley,” Wilson spoke with[SEP]deliberateness and settled deeper into his chair, “is that I don’t feel[SEP]it any longer.[SEP]I am sure of you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander laughed.[SEP]“Nonsense![SEP]It’s not I you feel sure of; it’s[SEP]Winifred.[SEP]People often make that mistake.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, I’m serious, Alexander.[SEP]You’ve changed.[SEP]You have decided to leave[SEP]some birds in the bushes.[SEP]You used to want them all.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander’s chair creaked.[SEP]“I still want a good many,” he said rather[SEP]gloomily.[SEP]“After all, life doesn’t offer a man much.[SEP]You work like the[SEP]devil and think you’re getting on, and suddenly you discover that[SEP]you’ve only been getting yourself tied up.[SEP]A million details drink you[SEP]dry.[SEP]Your life keeps going for things you don’t want, and all the while[SEP]you are being built alive into a social structure you don’t care a rap[SEP]about.[SEP]I sometimes wonder what sort of chap I’d have been if I hadn’t[SEP]been this sort; I want to go and live out his potentialities, too.[SEP]I[SEP]haven’t forgotten that there are birds in the bushes.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley stopped and sat frowning into the fire, his shoulders thrust[SEP]forward as if he were about to spring at something.[SEP]Wilson watched him,[SEP]wondering.[SEP]His old pupil always stimulated him at first, and then[SEP]vastly wearied him.[SEP]The machinery was always pounding away in this man,[SEP]and Wilson preferred companions of a more reflective habit of mind.[SEP]He[SEP]could not help feeling that there were unreasoning and unreasonable[SEP]activities going on in Alexander all the while; that even after dinner,[SEP]when most men achieve a decent impersonality, Bartley had merely closed[SEP]the door of the engine-room and come up for an airing.[SEP]The machinery[SEP]itself was still pounding on.[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley’s abstraction and Wilson’s reflections were cut short by a[SEP]rustle at the door, and almost before they could rise Mrs.[SEP]Alexander[SEP]was standing by the hearth.[SEP]Alexander brought a chair for her, but she[SEP]shook her head.[EOT]
[SOT]“No, dear, thank you.[SEP]I only came in to see whether you and Professor[SEP]Wilson were quite comfortable.[SEP]I am going down to the music-room.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why not practice here?[SEP]Wilson and I are growing very dull.[SEP]We are[SEP]tired of talk.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, I beg you, Mrs.[SEP]Alexander,” Wilson began, but he got no further.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why, certainly, if you won’t find me too noisy.[SEP]I am working on the[SEP]Schumann ‘Carnival,’ and, though I don’t practice a great many hours, I[SEP]am very methodical,” Mrs.[SEP]Alexander explained, as she crossed to an[SEP]upright piano that stood at the back of the room, near the windows.[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson followed, and, having seen her seated, dropped into a chair[SEP]behind her.[SEP]She played brilliantly and with great musical feeling.[SEP]Wilson could not imagine her permitting herself to do anything badly,[SEP]but he was surprised at the cleanness of her execution.[SEP]He wondered how[SEP]a woman with so many duties had managed to keep herself up to a[SEP]standard really professional.[SEP]It must take a great deal of time,[SEP]certainly, and Bartley must take a great deal of time.[SEP]Wilson reflected[SEP]that he had never before known a woman who had been able, for any[SEP]considerable while, to support both a personal and an intellectual[SEP]passion.[SEP]Sitting behind her, he watched her with perplexed admiration,[SEP]shading his eyes with his hand.[SEP]In her dinner dress she looked even[SEP]younger than in street clothes, and, for all her composure and[SEP]self-sufficiency, she seemed to him strangely alert and vibrating, as[SEP]if in her, too, there were something never altogether at rest.[SEP]He felt[SEP]that he knew pretty much what she demanded in people and what she[SEP]demanded from life, and he wondered how she squared Bartley.[SEP]After ten[SEP]years she must know him; and however one took him, however much one[SEP]admired him, one had to admit that he simply wouldn’t square.[SEP]He was a[SEP]natural force, certainly, but beyond that, Wilson felt, he was not[SEP]anything very really or for very long at a time.[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson glanced toward the fire, where Bartley’s profile was still[SEP]wreathed in cigar smoke that curled up more and more slowly.[SEP]His[SEP]shoulders were sunk deep in the cushions and one hand hung large and[SEP]passive over the arm of his chair.[SEP]He had slipped on a purple velvet[SEP]smoking-coat.[SEP]His wife, Wilson surmised, had chosen it.[SEP]She was clearly[SEP]very proud of his good looks and his fine color.[SEP]But, with the glow of[SEP]an immediate interest gone out of it, the engineer’s face looked tired,[SEP]even a little haggard.[SEP]The three lines in his forehead, directly above[SEP]the nose, deepened as he sat thinking, and his powerful head drooped[SEP]forward heavily.[SEP]Although Alexander was only forty-three, Wilson[SEP]thought that beneath his vigorous color he detected the dulling[SEP]weariness of on-coming middle age.[EOT]
[SOT]The next afternoon, at the hour when the river was beginning to redden[SEP]under the declining sun, Wilson again found himself facing Mrs.[SEP]Alexander at the tea-table in the library.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well,” he remarked, when he was bidden to give an account of himself,[SEP]“there was a long morning with the psychologists, luncheon with Bartley[SEP]at his club, more psychologists, and here I am.[SEP]I’ve looked forward to[SEP]this hour all day.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Mrs.[SEP]Alexander smiled at him across the vapor from the kettle.[SEP]“And do[SEP]you remember where we stopped yesterday?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Perfectly.[SEP]I was going to show you a picture.[SEP]But I doubt whether I[SEP]have color enough in me.[SEP]Bartley makes me feel a faded monochrome.[SEP]You[SEP]can’t get at the young Bartley except by means of color.[SEP]” Wilson paused[SEP]and deliberated.[SEP]Suddenly he broke out: “He wasn’t a remarkable[SEP]student, you know, though he was always strong in higher mathematics.[SEP]His work in my own department was quite ordinary.[SEP]It was as a[SEP]powerfully equipped nature that I found him interesting.[SEP]That is the[SEP]most interesting thing a teacher can find.[SEP]It has the fascination of a[SEP]scientific discovery.[SEP]We come across other pleasing and endearing[SEP]qualities so much oftener than we find force.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And, after all,” said Mrs.[SEP]Alexander, “that is the thing we all live[SEP]upon.[SEP]It is the thing that takes us forward.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson thought she spoke a little wistfully.[SEP]“Exactly,” he assented[SEP]warmly.[SEP]“It builds the bridges into the future, over which the feet of[SEP]every one of us will go.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“How interested I am to hear you put it in that way.[SEP]The bridges into[SEP]the future—I often say that to myself.[SEP]Bartley’s bridges always seem to[SEP]me like that.[SEP]Have you ever seen his first suspension bridge in Canada,[SEP]the one he was doing when I first knew him?[SEP]I hope you will see it[SEP]sometime.[SEP]We were married as soon as it was finished, and you will[SEP]laugh when I tell you that it always has a rather bridal look to me.[SEP]It[SEP]is over the wildest river, with mists and clouds always battling about[SEP]it, and it is as delicate as a cobweb hanging in the sky.[SEP]It really was[SEP]a bridge into the future.[SEP]You have only to look at it to feel that it[SEP]meant the beginning of a great career.[SEP]But I have a photograph of it[SEP]here.[SEP]” She drew a portfolio from behind a bookcase.[SEP]“And there, you[SEP]see, on the hill, is my aunt’s house.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson took up the photograph.[SEP]“Bartley was telling me something about[SEP]your aunt last night.[SEP]She must have been a delightful person.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Winifred laughed.[SEP]“The bridge, you see, was just at the foot of the[SEP]hill, and the noise of the engines annoyed her very much at first.[SEP]But[SEP]after she met Bartley she pretended to like it, and said it was a good[SEP]thing to be reminded that there were things going on in the world.[SEP]She[SEP]loved life, and Bartley brought a great deal of it in to her when he[SEP]came to the house.[SEP]Aunt Eleanor was very worldly in a frank,[SEP]Early-Victorian manner.[SEP]She liked men of action, and disliked young men[SEP]who were careful of themselves and who, as she put it, were always[SEP]trimming their wick as if they were afraid of their oil’s giving out.[SEP]MacKeller, Bartley’s first chief, was an old friend of my aunt, and he[SEP]told her that Bartley was a wild, ill-governed youth, which really[SEP]pleased her very much.[SEP]I remember we were sitting alone in the dusk[SEP]after Bartley had been there for the first time.[SEP]I knew that Aunt[SEP]Eleanor had found him much to her taste, but she hadn’t said anything.[SEP]Presently she came out, with a chuckle: ‘MacKeller found him sowing[SEP]wild oats in London, I believe.[SEP]I hope he didn’t stop him too soon.[SEP]Life coquets with dashing fellows.[SEP]The coming men are always like that.[SEP]We must have him to dinner, my dear.[SEP]’ And we did.[SEP]She grew much fonder[SEP]of Bartley than she was of me.[SEP]I had been studying in Vienna, and she[SEP]thought that absurd.[SEP]She was interested in the army and in politics,[SEP]and she had a great contempt for music and art and philosophy.[SEP]She used[SEP]to declare that the Prince Consort had brought all that stuff over out[SEP]of Germany.[SEP]She always sniffed when Bartley asked me to play for him.[SEP]She considered that a newfangled way of making a match of it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]When Alexander came in a few moments later, he found Wilson and his[SEP]wife still confronting the photograph.[SEP]“Oh, let us get that out of the[SEP]way,” he said, laughing.[SEP]“Winifred, Thomas can bring my trunk down.[SEP]I’ve decided to go over to New York to-morrow night and take a fast[SEP]boat.[SEP]I shall save two days.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER II[EOT]
[SOT]On the night of his arrival in London, Alexander went immediately to[SEP]the hotel on the Embankment at which he always stopped, and in the[SEP]lobby he was accosted by an old acquaintance, Maurice Mainhall, who[SEP]fell upon him with effusive cordiality and indicated a willingness to[SEP]dine with him.[SEP]Bartley never dined alone if he could help it, and[SEP]Mainhall was a good gossip who always knew what had been going on in[SEP]town; especially, he knew everything that was not printed in the[SEP]newspapers.[SEP]The nephew of one of the standard Victorian novelists,[SEP]Mainhall bobbed about among the various literary cliques of London and[SEP]its outlying suburbs, careful to lose touch with none of them.[SEP]He had[SEP]written a number of books himself; among them a “History of Dancing,” a[SEP]“History of Costume,” a “Key to Shakespeare’s Sonnets,” a study of “The[SEP]Poetry of Ernest Dowson,” etc.[SEP]Although Mainhall’s enthusiasm was often[SEP]tiresome, and although he was often unable to distinguish between facts[SEP]and vivid figments of his imagination, his imperturbable good nature[SEP]overcame even the people whom he bored most, so that they ended by[SEP]becoming, in a reluctant manner, his friends.[SEP]In appearance, Mainhall[SEP]was astonishingly like the conventional stage-Englishman of American[SEP]drama: tall and thin, with high, hitching shoulders and a small head[SEP]glistening with closely brushed yellow hair.[SEP]He spoke with an extreme[SEP]Oxford accent, and when he was talking well, his face sometimes wore[SEP]the rapt expression of a very emotional man listening to music.[SEP]Mainhall liked Alexander because he was an engineer.[SEP]He had[SEP]preconceived ideas about everything, and his idea about Americans was[SEP]that they should be engineers or mechanics.[SEP]He hated them when they[SEP]presumed to be anything else.[EOT]
[SOT]While they sat at dinner Mainhall acquainted Bartley with the fortunes[SEP]of his old friends in London, and as they left the table he proposed[SEP]that they should go to see Hugh MacConnell’s new comedy, “Bog Lights.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s really quite the best thing MacConnell’s done,” he explained as[SEP]they got into a hansom.[SEP]“It’s tremendously well put on, too.[SEP]Florence[SEP]Merrill and Cyril Henderson.[SEP]But Hilda Burgoyne’s the hit of the piece.[SEP]Hugh’s written a delightful part for her, and she’s quite[SEP]inexpressible.[SEP]It’s been on only two weeks, and I’ve been half a dozen[SEP]times already.[SEP]I happen to have MacConnell’s box for tonight or there’d[SEP]be no chance of our getting places.[SEP]There’s everything in seeing Hilda[SEP]while she’s fresh in a part.[SEP]She’s apt to grow a bit stale after a[SEP]time.[SEP]The ones who have any imagination do.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Hilda Burgoyne![SEP]” Alexander exclaimed mildly.[SEP]“Why, I haven’t heard of[SEP]her for—years.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Mainhall laughed.[SEP]“Then you can’t have heard much at all, my dear[SEP]Alexander.[SEP]It’s only lately, since MacConnell and his set have got hold[SEP]of her, that she’s come up.[SEP]Myself, I always knew she had it in her.[SEP]If[SEP]we had one real critic in London—but what can one expect?[SEP]Do you know,[SEP]Alexander,”—Mainhall looked with perplexity up into the top of the[SEP]hansom and rubbed his pink cheek with his gloved finger,—“do you know,[SEP]I sometimes think of taking to criticism seriously myself.[SEP]In a way, it[SEP]would be a sacrifice; but, dear me, we do need some one.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Just then they drove up to the Duke of York’s, so Alexander did not[SEP]commit himself, but followed Mainhall into the theatre.[SEP]When they[SEP]entered the stage-box on the left the first act was well under way, the[SEP]scene being the interior of a cabin in the south of Ireland.[SEP]As they[SEP]sat down, a burst of applause drew Alexander’s attention to the stage.[SEP]Miss Burgoyne and her donkey were thrusting their heads in at the half[SEP]door.[SEP]“After all,” he reflected, “there’s small probability of her[SEP]recognizing me.[SEP]She doubtless hasn’t thought of me for years.[SEP]” He felt[SEP]the enthusiasm of the house at once, and in a few moments he was caught[SEP]up by the current of MacConnell’s irresistible comedy.[SEP]The audience had[SEP]come forewarned, evidently, and whenever the ragged slip of a[SEP]donkey-girl ran upon the stage there was a deep murmur of approbation,[SEP]every one smiled and glowed, and Mainhall hitched his heavy chair a[SEP]little nearer the brass railing.[EOT]
[SOT]“You see,” he murmured in Alexander’s ear, as the curtain fell on the[SEP]first act, “one almost never sees a part like that done without[SEP]smartness or mawkishness.[SEP]Of course, Hilda is Irish,—the Burgoynes have[SEP]been stage people for generations,—and she has the Irish voice.[SEP]It’s[SEP]delightful to hear it in a London theatre.[SEP]That laugh, now, when she[SEP]doubles over at the hips—who ever heard it out of Galway?[SEP]She saves her[SEP]hand, too.[SEP]She’s at her best in the second act.[SEP]She’s really[SEP]MacConnell’s poetic motif, you see; makes the whole thing a fairy[SEP]tale.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The second act opened before Philly Doyle’s underground still, with[SEP]Peggy and her battered donkey come in to smuggle a load of potheen[SEP]across the bog, and to bring Philly word of what was doing in the world[SEP]without, and of what was happening along the roadsides and ditches with[SEP]the first gleam of fine weather.[SEP]Alexander, annoyed by Mainhall’s sighs[SEP]and exclamations, watched her with keen, half-skeptical interest.[SEP]As[SEP]Mainhall had said, she was the second act; the plot and feeling alike[SEP]depended upon her lightness of foot, her lightness of touch, upon the[SEP]shrewdness and deft fancifulness that played alternately, and sometimes[SEP]together, in her mirthful brown eyes.[SEP]When she began to dance, by way[SEP]of showing the gossoons what she had seen in the fairy rings at night,[SEP]the house broke into a prolonged uproar.[SEP]After her dance she withdrew[SEP]from the dialogue and retreated to the ditch wall back of Philly’s[SEP]burrow, where she sat singing “The Rising of the Moon” and making a[SEP]wreath of primroses for her donkey.[EOT]
[SOT]When the act was over Alexander and Mainhall strolled out into the[SEP]corridor.[SEP]They met a good many acquaintances; Mainhall, indeed, knew[SEP]almost every one, and he babbled on incontinently, screwing his small[SEP]head about over his high collar.[SEP]Presently he hailed a tall, bearded[SEP]man, grim-browed and rather battered-looking, who had his opera cloak[SEP]on his arm and his hat in his hand, and who seemed to be on the point[SEP]of leaving the theatre.[EOT]
[SOT]“MacConnell, let me introduce Mr. Bartley Alexander.[SEP]I say![SEP]It’s going[SEP]famously to-night, Mac.[SEP]And what an audience![SEP]You’ll never do anything[SEP]like this again, mark me.[SEP]A man writes to the top of his bent only[SEP]once.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The playwright gave Mainhall a curious look out of his deep-set faded[SEP]eyes and made a wry face.[SEP]“And have I done anything so fool as that,[SEP]now?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s what I was saying,” Mainhall lounged a little nearer and[SEP]dropped into a tone even more conspicuously confidential.[SEP]“And you’ll[SEP]never bring Hilda out like this again.[SEP]Dear me, Mac, the girl couldn’t[SEP]possibly be better, you know.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]MacConnell grunted.[SEP]“She’ll do well enough if she keeps her pace and[SEP]doesn’t go off on us in the middle of the season, as she’s more than[SEP]like to do.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He nodded curtly and made for the door, dodging acquaintances as he[SEP]went.[EOT]
[SOT]“Poor old Hugh,” Mainhall murmured.[SEP]“He’s hit terribly hard.[SEP]He’s been[SEP]wanting to marry Hilda these three years and more.[SEP]She doesn’t take up[SEP]with anybody, you know.[SEP]Irene Burgoyne, one of her family, told me in[SEP]confidence that there was a romance somewhere back in the beginning.[SEP]One of your countrymen, Alexander, by the way; an American student whom[SEP]she met in Paris, I believe.[SEP]I dare say it’s quite true that there’s[SEP]never been any one else.[SEP]” Mainhall vouched for her constancy with a[SEP]loftiness that made Alexander smile, even while a kind of rapid[SEP]excitement was tingling through him.[SEP]Blinking up at the lights,[SEP]Mainhall added in his luxurious, worldly way: “She’s an elegant little[SEP]person, and quite capable of an extravagant bit of sentiment like that.[SEP]Here comes Sir Harry Towne.[SEP]He’s another who’s awfully keen about her.[SEP]Let me introduce you.[SEP]Sir Harry Towne, Mr. Bartley Alexander, the[SEP]American engineer.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sir Harry Towne bowed and said that he had met Mr. Alexander and his[SEP]wife in Tokyo.[EOT]
[SOT]Mainhall cut in impatiently.[EOT]
[SOT]“I say, Sir Harry, the little girl’s going famously to-night, isn’t[SEP]she?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sir Harry wrinkled his brows judiciously.[SEP]“Do you know, I thought the[SEP]dance a bit conscious to-night, for the first time.[SEP]The fact is, she’s[SEP]feeling rather seedy, poor child.[SEP]Westmere and I were back after the[SEP]first act, and we thought she seemed quite uncertain of herself.[SEP]A[SEP]little attack of nerves, possibly.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He bowed as the warning bell rang, and Mainhall whispered: “You know[SEP]Lord Westmere, of course,—the stooped man with the long gray mustache,[SEP]talking to Lady Dowle.[SEP]Lady Westmere is very fond of Hilda.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]When they reached their box the house was darkened and the orchestra[SEP]was playing “The Cloak of Old Gaul.[SEP]” In a moment Peggy was on the stage[SEP]again, and Alexander applauded vigorously with the rest.[SEP]He even leaned[SEP]forward over the rail a little.[SEP]For some reason he felt pleased and[SEP]flattered by the enthusiasm of the audience.[SEP]In the half-light he[SEP]looked about at the stalls and boxes and smiled a little consciously,[SEP]recalling with amusement Sir Harry’s judicial frown.[SEP]He was beginning[SEP]to feel a keen interest in the slender, barefoot donkey-girl who[SEP]slipped in and out of the play, singing, like some one winding through[SEP]a hilly field.[SEP]He leaned forward and beamed felicitations as warmly as[SEP]Mainhall himself when, at the end of the play, she came again and again[SEP]before the curtain, panting a little and flushed, her eyes dancing and[SEP]her eager, nervous little mouth tremulous with excitement.[EOT]
[SOT]When Alexander returned to his hotel—he shook Mainhall at the door of[SEP]the theatre—he had some supper brought up to his room, and it was late[SEP]before he went to bed.[SEP]He had not thought of Hilda Burgoyne for years;[SEP]indeed, he had almost forgotten her.[SEP]He had last written to her from[SEP]Canada, after he first met Winifred, telling her that everything was[SEP]changed with him—that he had met a woman whom he would marry if he[SEP]could; if he could not, then all the more was everything changed for[SEP]him.[SEP]Hilda had never replied to his letter.[SEP]He felt guilty and unhappy[SEP]about her for a time, but after Winifred promised to marry him he[SEP]really forgot Hilda altogether.[SEP]When he wrote her that everything was[SEP]changed for him, he was telling the truth.[SEP]After he met Winifred[SEP]Pemberton he seemed to himself like a different man.[SEP]One night when he[SEP]and Winifred were sitting together on the bridge, he told her that[SEP]things had happened while he was studying abroad that he was sorry[SEP]for,—one thing in particular,—and he asked her whether she thought she[SEP]ought to know about them.[SEP]She considered a moment and then said “No, I[SEP]think not, though I am glad you ask me.[SEP]You see, one can’t be jealous[SEP]about things in general; but about particular, definite, personal[SEP]things,”—here she had thrown her hands up to his shoulders with a[SEP]quick, impulsive gesture—“oh, about those I should be very jealous.[SEP]I[SEP]should torture myself—I couldn’t help it.[SEP]” After that it was easy to[SEP]forget, actually to forget.[SEP]He wondered to-night, as he poured his[SEP]wine, how many times he had thought of Hilda in the last ten years.[SEP]He[SEP]had been in London more or less, but he had never happened to hear of[SEP]her.[SEP]“All the same,” he lifted his glass, “here’s to you, little Hilda.[SEP]You’ve made things come your way, and I never thought you’d do it.[EOT]
[SOT]“Of course,” he reflected, “she always had that combination of[SEP]something homely and sensible, and something utterly wild and daft.[SEP]But[SEP]I never thought she’d do anything.[SEP]She hadn’t much ambition then, and[SEP]she was too fond of trifles.[SEP]She must care about the theatre a great[SEP]deal more than she used to.[SEP]Perhaps she has me to thank for something,[SEP]after all.[SEP]Sometimes a little jolt like that does one good.[SEP]She was a[SEP]daft, generous little thing.[SEP]I’m glad she’s held her own since.[SEP]After[SEP]all, we were awfully young.[SEP]It was youth and poverty and proximity, and[SEP]everything was young and kindly.[SEP]I shouldn’t wonder if she could laugh[SEP]about it with me now.[SEP]I shouldn’t wonder— But they’ve probably spoiled[SEP]her, so that she’d be tiresome if one met her again.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley smiled and yawned and went to bed.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER III[EOT]
[SOT]The next evening Alexander dined alone at a club, and at about nine[SEP]o’clock he dropped in at the Duke of York’s.[SEP]The house was sold out and[SEP]he stood through the second act.[SEP]When he returned to his hotel he[SEP]examined the new directory, and found Miss Burgoyne’s address still[SEP]given as off Bedford Square, though at a new number.[SEP]He remembered[SEP]that, in so far as she had been brought up at all, she had been brought[SEP]up in Bloomsbury.[SEP]Her father and mother played in the provinces most of[SEP]the year, and she was left a great deal in the care of an old aunt who[SEP]was crippled by rheumatism and who had had to leave the stage[SEP]altogether.[SEP]In the days when Alexander knew her, Hilda always managed[SEP]to have a lodging of some sort about Bedford Square, because she clung[SEP]tenaciously to such scraps and shreds of memories as were connected[SEP]with it.[SEP]The mummy room of the British Museum had been one of the chief[SEP]delights of her childhood.[SEP]That forbidding pile was the goal of her[SEP]truant fancy, and she was sometimes taken there for a treat, as other[SEP]children are taken to the theatre.[SEP]It was long since Alexander had[SEP]thought of any of these things, but now they came back to him quite[SEP]fresh, and had a significance they did not have when they were first[SEP]told him in his restless twenties.[SEP]So she was still in the old[SEP]neighborhood, near Bedford Square.[SEP]The new number probably meant[SEP]increased prosperity.[SEP]He hoped so.[SEP]He would like to know that she was[SEP]snugly settled.[SEP]He looked at his watch.[SEP]It was a quarter past ten; she[SEP]would not be home for a good two hours yet, and he might as well walk[SEP]over and have a look at the place.[SEP]He remembered the shortest way.[EOT]
[SOT]It was a warm, smoky evening, and there was a grimy moon.[SEP]He went[SEP]through Covent Garden to Oxford Street, and as he turned into Museum[SEP]Street he walked more slowly, smiling at his own nervousness as he[SEP]approached the sullen gray mass at the end.[SEP]He had not been inside the[SEP]Museum, actually, since he and Hilda used to meet there; sometimes to[SEP]set out for gay adventures at Twickenham or Richmond, sometimes to[SEP]linger about the place for a while and to ponder by Lord Elgin’s[SEP]marbles upon the lastingness of some things, or, in the mummy room,[SEP]upon the awful brevity of others.[SEP]Since then Bartley had always thought[SEP]of the British Museum as the ultimate repository of mortality, where[SEP]all the dead things in the world were assembled to make one’s hour of[SEP]youth the more precious.[SEP]One trembled lest before he got out it might[SEP]somehow escape him, lest he might drop the glass from over-eagerness[SEP]and see it shivered on the stone floor at his feet.[SEP]How one hid his[SEP]youth under his coat and hugged it![SEP]And how good it was to turn one’s[SEP]back upon all that vaulted cold, to take Hilda’s arm and hurry out of[SEP]the great door and down the steps into the sunlight among the[SEP]pigeons—to know that the warm and vital thing within him was still[SEP]there and had not been snatched away to flush Cæsar’s lean cheek or to[SEP]feed the veins of some bearded Assyrian king.[SEP]They in their day had[SEP]carried the flaming liquor, but to-day was his![SEP]So the song used to run[SEP]in his head those summer mornings a dozen years ago.[SEP]Alexander walked[SEP]by the place very quietly, as if he were afraid of waking some one.[EOT]
[SOT]He crossed Bedford Square and found the number he was looking for.[SEP]The[SEP]house, a comfortable, well-kept place enough, was dark except for the[SEP]four front windows on the second floor, where a low, even light was[SEP]burning behind the white muslin sash curtains.[SEP]Outside there were[SEP]window boxes, painted white and full of flowers.[SEP]Bartley was making a[SEP]third round of the Square when he heard the far-flung hoof-beats of a[SEP]hansom-cab horse, driven rapidly.[SEP]He looked at his watch, and was[SEP]astonished to find that it was a few minutes after twelve.[SEP]He turned[SEP]and walked back along the iron railing as the cab came up to Hilda’s[SEP]number and stopped.[SEP]The hansom must have been one that she employed[SEP]regularly, for she did not stop to pay the driver.[SEP]She stepped out[SEP]quickly and lightly.[SEP]He heard her cheerful “Good-night, cabby,” as she[SEP]ran up the steps and opened the door with a latchkey.[SEP]In a few moments[SEP]the lights flared up brightly behind the white curtains, and as he[SEP]walked away he heard a window raised.[SEP]But he had gone too far to look[SEP]up without turning round.[SEP]He went back to his hotel, feeling that he[SEP]had had a good evening, and he slept well.[EOT]
[SOT]For the next few days Alexander was very busy.[SEP]He took a desk in the[SEP]office of a Scotch engineering firm on Henrietta Street, and was at[SEP]work almost constantly.[SEP]He avoided the clubs and usually dined alone at[SEP]his hotel.[SEP]One afternoon, after he had tea, he started for a walk down[SEP]the Embankment toward Westminster, intending to end his stroll at[SEP]Bedford Square and to ask whether Miss Burgoyne would let him take her[SEP]to the theatre.[SEP]But he did not go so far.[SEP]When he reached the Abbey, he[SEP]turned back and crossed Westminster Bridge and sat down to watch the[SEP]trails of smoke behind the Houses of Parliament catch fire with the[SEP]sunset.[SEP]The slender towers were washed by a rain of golden light and[SEP]licked by little flickering flames; Somerset House and the bleached[SEP]gray pinnacles about Whitehall were floated in a luminous haze.[SEP]The[SEP]yellow light poured through the trees and the leaves seemed to burn[SEP]with soft fires.[SEP]There was a smell of acacias in the air everywhere,[SEP]and the laburnums were dripping gold over the walls of the gardens.[SEP]It[SEP]was a sweet, lonely kind of summer evening.[SEP]Remembering Hilda as she[SEP]used to be, was doubtless more satisfactory than seeing her as she must[SEP]be now—and, after all, Alexander asked himself, what was it but his own[SEP]young years that he was remembering?[EOT]
[SOT]He crossed back to Westminster, went up to the Temple, and sat down to[SEP]smoke in the Middle Temple gardens, listening to the thin voice of the[SEP]fountain and smelling the spice of the sycamores that came out heavily[SEP]in the damp evening air.[SEP]He thought, as he sat there, about a great[SEP]many things: about his own youth and Hilda’s; above all, he thought of[SEP]how glorious it had been, and how quickly it had passed; and, when it[SEP]had passed, how little worth while anything was.[SEP]None of the things he[SEP]had gained in the least compensated.[SEP]In the last six years his[SEP]reputation had become, as the saying is, popular.[SEP]Four years ago he had[SEP]been called to Japan to deliver, at the Emperor’s request, a course of[SEP]lectures at the Imperial University, and had instituted reforms[SEP]throughout the islands, not only in the practice of bridge-building but[SEP]in drainage and road-making.[SEP]On his return he had undertaken the bridge[SEP]at Moorlock, in Canada, the most important piece of bridge-building[SEP]going on in the world,—a test, indeed, of how far the latest practice[SEP]in bridge structure could be carried.[SEP]It was a spectacular undertaking[SEP]by reason of its very size, and Bartley realized that, whatever else he[SEP]might do, he would probably always be known as the engineer who[SEP]designed the great Moorlock Bridge, the longest cantilever in[SEP]existence.[SEP]Yet it was to him the least satisfactory thing he had ever[SEP]done.[SEP]He was cramped in every way by a niggardly commission, and was[SEP]using lighter structural material than he thought proper.[SEP]He had[SEP]vexations enough, too, with his work at home.[SEP]He had several bridges[SEP]under way in the United States, and they were always being held up by[SEP]strikes and delays resulting from a general industrial unrest.[EOT]
[SOT]Though Alexander often told himself he had never put more into his work[SEP]than he had done in the last few years, he had to admit that he had[SEP]never got so little out of it.[SEP]He was paying for success, too, in the[SEP]demands made on his time by boards of civic enterprise and committees[SEP]of public welfare.[SEP]The obligations imposed by his wife’s fortune and[SEP]position were sometimes distracting to a man who followed his[SEP]profession, and he was expected to be interested in a great many worthy[SEP]endeavors on her account as well as on his own.[SEP]His existence was[SEP]becoming a network of great and little details.[SEP]He had expected that[SEP]success would bring him freedom and power; but it had brought only[SEP]power that was in itself another kind of restraint.[SEP]He had always meant[SEP]to keep his personal liberty at all costs, as old MacKeller, his first[SEP]chief, had done, and not, like so many American engineers, to become a[SEP]part of a professional movement, a cautious board member, a Nestor _de[SEP]pontibus_.[SEP]He happened to be engaged in work of public utility, but he[SEP]was not willing to become what is called a public man.[SEP]He found himself[SEP]living exactly the kind of life he had determined to escape.[SEP]What, he[SEP]asked himself, did he want with these genial honors and substantial[SEP]comforts?[SEP]Hardships and difficulties he had carried lightly; overwork[SEP]had not exhausted him; but this dead calm of middle life which[SEP]confronted him,—of that he was afraid.[SEP]He was not ready for it.[SEP]It was[SEP]like being buried alive.[SEP]In his youth he would not have believed such a[SEP]thing possible.[SEP]The one thing he had really wanted all his life was to[SEP]be free; and there was still something unconquered in him, something[SEP]besides the strong work-horse that his profession had made of him.[SEP]He[SEP]felt rich to-night in the possession of that unstultified survival; in[SEP]the light of his experience, it was more precious than honors or[SEP]achievement.[SEP]In all those busy, successful years there had been nothing[SEP]so good as this hour of wild light-heartedness.[SEP]This feeling was the[SEP]only happiness that was real to him, and such hours were the only ones[SEP]in which he could feel his own continuous identity—feel the boy he had[SEP]been in the rough days of the old West, feel the youth who had worked[SEP]his way across the ocean on a cattle-ship and gone to study in Paris[SEP]without a dollar in his pocket.[SEP]The man who sat in his offices in[SEP]Boston was only a powerful machine.[SEP]Under the activities of that[SEP]machine the person who, in such moments as this, he felt to be himself,[SEP]was fading and dying.[SEP]He remembered how, when he was a little boy and[SEP]his father called him in the morning, he used to leap from his bed into[SEP]the full consciousness of himself.[SEP]That consciousness was Life itself.[SEP]Whatever took its place, action, reflection, the power of concentrated[SEP]thought, were only functions of a mechanism useful to society; things[SEP]that could be bought in the market.[SEP]There was only one thing that had[SEP]an absolute value for each individual, and it was just that original[SEP]impulse, that internal heat, that feeling of one’s self in one’s own[SEP]breast.[EOT]
[SOT]When Alexander walked back to his hotel, the red and green lights were[SEP]blinking along the docks on the farther shore, and the soft white stars[SEP]were shining in the wide sky above the river.[EOT]
[SOT]The next night, and the next, Alexander repeated this same foolish[SEP]performance.[SEP]It was always Miss Burgoyne whom he started out to find,[SEP]and he got no farther than the Temple gardens and the Embankment.[SEP]It[SEP]was a pleasant kind of loneliness.[SEP]To a man who was so little given to[SEP]reflection, whose dreams always took the form of definite ideas,[SEP]reaching into the future, there was a seductive excitement in renewing[SEP]old experiences in imagination.[SEP]He started out upon these walks half[SEP]guiltily, with a curious longing and expectancy which were wholly[SEP]gratified by solitude.[SEP]Solitude, but not solitariness; for he walked[SEP]shoulder to shoulder with a shadowy companion—not little Hilda[SEP]Burgoyne, by any means, but some one vastly dearer to him than she had[SEP]ever been—his own young self, the youth who had waited for him upon the[SEP]steps of the British Museum that night, and who, though he had tried to[SEP]pass so quietly, had known him and come down and linked an arm in his.[EOT]
[SOT]It was not until long afterward that Alexander learned that for him[SEP]this youth was the most dangerous of companions.[EOT]
[SOT]One Sunday evening, at Lady Walford’s, Alexander did at last meet Hilda[SEP]Burgoyne.[SEP]Mainhall had told him that she would probably be there.[SEP]He[SEP]looked about for her rather nervously, and finally found her at the[SEP]farther end of the large drawing-room, the centre of a circle of men,[SEP]young and old.[SEP]She was apparently telling them a story.[SEP]They were all[SEP]laughing and bending toward her.[SEP]When she saw Alexander, she rose[SEP]quickly and put out her hand.[SEP]The other men drew back a little to let[SEP]him approach.[EOT]
[SOT]“Mr. Alexander![SEP]I am delighted.[SEP]Have you been in London long?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley bowed, somewhat laboriously, over her hand.[SEP]“Long enough to[SEP]have seen you more than once.[SEP]How fine it all is![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She laughed as if she were pleased.[SEP]“I’m glad you think so.[SEP]I like it.[SEP]Won’t you join us here?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Miss Burgoyne was just telling us about a donkey-boy she had in Galway[SEP]last summer,” Sir Harry Towne explained as the circle closed up again.[SEP]Lord Westmere stroked his long white mustache with his bloodless hand[SEP]and looked at Alexander blankly.[SEP]Hilda was a good story-teller.[SEP]She was[SEP]sitting on the edge of her chair, as if she had alighted there for a[SEP]moment only.[SEP]Her primrose satin gown seemed like a soft sheath for her[SEP]slender, supple figure, and its delicate color suited her white Irish[SEP]skin and brown hair.[SEP]Whatever she wore, people felt the charm of her[SEP]active, girlish body with its slender hips and quick, eager shoulders.[SEP]Alexander heard little of the story, but he watched Hilda intently.[SEP]She[SEP]must certainly, he reflected, be thirty, and he was honestly delighted[SEP]to see that the years had treated her so indulgently.[SEP]If her face had[SEP]changed at all, it was in a slight hardening of the mouth—still eager[SEP]enough to be very disconcerting at times, he felt—and in an added air[SEP]of self-possession and self-reliance.[SEP]She carried her head, too, a[SEP]little more resolutely.[EOT]
[SOT]When the story was finished, Miss Burgoyne turned pointedly to[SEP]Alexander, and the other men drifted away.[EOT]
[SOT]“I thought I saw you in MacConnell’s box with Mainhall one evening, but[SEP]I supposed you had left town before this.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She looked at him frankly and cordially, as if he were indeed merely an[SEP]old friend whom she was glad to meet again.[EOT]
[SOT]“No, I’ve been mooning about here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda laughed gayly.[SEP]“Mooning![SEP]I see you mooning![SEP]You must be the[SEP]busiest man in the world.[SEP]Time and success have done well by you, you[SEP]know.[SEP]You’re handsomer than ever and you’ve gained a grand manner.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander blushed and bowed.[SEP]“Time and success have been good friends[SEP]to both of us.[SEP]Aren’t you tremendously pleased with yourself?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She laughed again and shrugged her shoulders.[SEP]“Oh, so-so.[SEP]But I want to[SEP]hear about you.[SEP]Several years ago I read such a lot in the papers about[SEP]the wonderful things you did in Japan, and how the Emperor decorated[SEP]you.[SEP]What was it, Commander of the Order of the Rising Sun?[SEP]That sounds[SEP]like ‘The Mikado.[SEP]’ And what about your new bridge—in Canada, isn’t it,[SEP]and it’s to be the longest one in the world and has some queer name I[SEP]can’t remember.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley shook his head and smiled drolly.[SEP]“Since when have you been[SEP]interested in bridges?[SEP]Or have you learned to be interested in[SEP]everything?[SEP]And is that a part of success?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why, how absurd![SEP]As if I were not always interested![SEP]” Hilda exclaimed.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, I think we won’t talk about bridges here, at any rate.[SEP]” Bartley[SEP]looked down at the toe of her yellow slipper which was tapping the rug[SEP]impatiently under the hem of her gown.[SEP]“But I wonder whether you’d[SEP]think me impertinent if I asked you to let me come to see you sometime[SEP]and tell you about them?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why should I?[SEP]Ever so many people come on Sunday afternoons.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I know.[SEP]Mainhall offered to take me.[SEP]But you must know that I’ve been[SEP]in London several times within the last few years, and you might very[SEP]well think that just now is a rather inopportune time—”[EOT]
[SOT]She cut him short.[SEP]“Nonsense.[SEP]One of the pleasantest things about[SEP]success is that it makes people want to look one up, if that’s what you[SEP]mean.[SEP]I’m like every one else—more agreeable to meet when things are[SEP]going well with me.[SEP]Don’t you suppose it gives me any pleasure to do[SEP]something that people like?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Does it?[SEP]Oh, how fine it all is, your coming on like this![SEP]But I[SEP]didn’t want you to think it was because of that I wanted to see you.[SEP]”[SEP]He spoke very seriously and looked down at the floor.[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda studied him in wide-eyed astonishment for a moment, and then[SEP]broke into a low, amused laugh.[SEP]“My dear Mr. Alexander, you have[SEP]strange delicacies.[SEP]If you please, that is exactly why you wish to see[SEP]me.[SEP]We understand that, do we not?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley looked ruffled and turned the seal ring on his little finger[SEP]about awkwardly.[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda leaned back in her chair, watching him indulgently out of her[SEP]shrewd eyes.[SEP]“Come, don’t be angry, but don’t try to pose for me, or to[SEP]be anything but what you are.[SEP]If you care to come, it’s yourself I’ll[SEP]be glad to see, and you thinking well of yourself.[SEP]Don’t try to wear a[SEP]cloak of humility; it doesn’t become you.[SEP]Stalk in as you are and don’t[SEP]make excuses.[SEP]I’m not accustomed to inquiring into the motives of my[SEP]guests.[SEP]That would hardly be safe, even for Lady Walford, in a great[SEP]house like this.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Sunday afternoon, then,” said Alexander, as she rose to join her[SEP]hostess.[SEP]“How early may I come?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She gave him her hand and flushed and laughed.[SEP]He bent over it a little[SEP]stiffly.[SEP]She went away on Lady Walford’s arm, and as he stood watching[SEP]her yellow train glide down the long floor he looked rather sullen.[SEP]He[SEP]felt that he had not come out of it very brilliantly.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER IV[EOT]
[SOT]On Sunday afternoon Alexander remembered Miss Burgoyne’s invitation and[SEP]called at her apartment.[SEP]He found it a delightful little place and he[SEP]met charming people there.[SEP]Hilda lived alone, attended by a very pretty[SEP]and competent French servant who answered the door and brought in the[SEP]tea.[SEP]Alexander arrived early, and some twenty-odd people dropped in[SEP]during the course of the afternoon.[SEP]Hugh MacConnell came with his[SEP]sister, and stood about, managing his tea-cup awkwardly and watching[SEP]every one out of his deep-set, faded eyes.[SEP]He seemed to have made a[SEP]resolute effort at tidiness of attire, and his sister, a robust, florid[SEP]woman with a splendid joviality about her, kept eyeing his freshly[SEP]creased clothes apprehensively.[SEP]It was not very long, indeed, before[SEP]his coat hung with a discouraged sag from his gaunt shoulders and his[SEP]hair and beard were rumpled as if he had been out in a gale.[SEP]His dry[SEP]humor went under a cloud of absent-minded kindliness which, Mainhall[SEP]explained, always overtook him here.[SEP]He was never so witty or so sharp[SEP]here as elsewhere, and Alexander thought he behaved as if he were an[SEP]elderly relative come in to a young girl’s party.[EOT]
[SOT]The editor of a monthly review came with his wife, and Lady Kildare,[SEP]the Irish philanthropist, brought her young nephew, Robert Owen, who[SEP]had come up from Oxford, and who was visibly excited and gratified by[SEP]his first introduction to Miss Burgoyne.[SEP]Hilda was very nice to him,[SEP]and he sat on the edge of his chair, flushed with his conversational[SEP]efforts and moving his chin about nervously over his high collar.[SEP]Sarah[SEP]Frost, the novelist, came with her husband, a very genial and placid[SEP]old scholar who had become slightly deranged upon the subject of the[SEP]fourth dimension.[SEP]On other matters he was perfectly rational and he was[SEP]easy and pleasing in conversation.[SEP]He looked very much like Agassiz,[SEP]and his wife, in her old-fashioned black silk dress, overskirted and[SEP]tight-sleeved, reminded Alexander of the early pictures of Mrs.[SEP]Browning.[SEP]Hilda seemed particularly fond of this quaint couple, and[SEP]Bartley himself was so pleased with their mild and thoughtful converse[SEP]that he took his leave when they did, and walked with them over to[SEP]Oxford Street, where they waited for their ‘bus.[SEP]They asked him to come[SEP]to see them in Chelsea, and they spoke very tenderly of Hilda.[SEP]“She’s a[SEP]dear, unworldly little thing,” said the philosopher absently; “more[SEP]like the stage people of my young days—folk of simple manners.[SEP]There[SEP]aren’t many such left.[SEP]American tours have spoiled them, I’m afraid.[SEP]They have all grown very smart.[SEP]Lamb wouldn’t care a great deal about[SEP]many of them, I fancy.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander went back to Bedford Square a second Sunday afternoon.[SEP]He had[SEP]a long talk with MacConnell, but he got no word with Hilda alone, and[SEP]he left in a discontented state of mind.[SEP]For the rest of the week he[SEP]was nervous and unsettled, and kept rushing his work as if he were[SEP]preparing for immediate departure.[SEP]On Thursday afternoon he cut short a[SEP]committee meeting, jumped into a hansom, and drove to Bedford Square.[SEP]He sent up his card, but it came back to him with a message scribbled[SEP]across the front.[EOT]
[SOT]So sorry I can’t see you.[SEP]Will you come and dine with me Sunday[SEP]evening at half-past seven?[EOT]
[SOT]When Bartley arrived at Bedford Square on Sunday evening, Marie, the[SEP]pretty little French girl, met him at the door and conducted him[SEP]upstairs.[SEP]Hilda was writing in her living-room, under the light of a[SEP]tall desk lamp.[SEP]Bartley recognized the primrose satin gown she had worn[SEP]that first evening at Lady Walford’s.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m so pleased that you think me worth that yellow dress, you know,”[SEP]he said, taking her hand and looking her over admiringly from the toes[SEP]of her canary slippers to her smoothly parted brown hair.[SEP]“Yes, it’s[SEP]very, very pretty.[SEP]Every one at Lady Walford’s was looking at it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda curtsied.[SEP]“Is that why you think it pretty?[SEP]I’ve no need for fine[SEP]clothes in Mac’s play this time, so I can afford a few duddies for[SEP]myself.[SEP]It’s owing to that same chance, by the way, that I am able to[SEP]ask you to dinner.[SEP]I don’t need Marie to dress me this season, so she[SEP]keeps house for me, and my little Galway girl has gone home for a[SEP]visit.[SEP]I should never have asked you if Molly had been here, for I[SEP]remember you don’t like English cookery.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander walked about the room, looking at everything.[EOT]
[SOT]“I haven’t had a chance yet to tell you what a jolly little place I[SEP]think this is.[SEP]Where did you get those etchings?[SEP]They’re quite unusual,[SEP]aren’t they?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Lady Westmere sent them to me from Rome last Christmas.[SEP]She is very[SEP]much interested in the American artist who did them.[SEP]They are all[SEP]sketches made about the Villa d’Este, you see.[SEP]He painted that group of[SEP]cypresses for the Salon, and it was bought for the Luxembourg.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander walked over to the bookcases.[SEP]“It’s the air of the whole[SEP]place here that I like.[SEP]You haven’t got anything that doesn’t belong.[SEP]Seems to me it looks particularly well to-night.[SEP]And you have so many[SEP]flowers.[SEP]I like these little yellow irises.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Rooms always look better by lamplight—in London, at least.[SEP]Though[SEP]Marie is clean—really clean, as the French are.[SEP]Why do you look at the[SEP]flowers so critically?[SEP]Marie got them all fresh in Covent Garden market[SEP]yesterday morning.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m glad,” said Alexander simply.[SEP]“I can’t tell you how glad I am to[SEP]have you so pretty and comfortable here, and to hear every one saying[SEP]such nice things about you.[SEP]You’ve got awfully nice friends,” he added[SEP]humbly, picking up a little jade elephant from her desk.[SEP]“Those fellows[SEP]are all very loyal, even Mainhall.[SEP]They don’t talk of any one else as[SEP]they do of you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda sat down on the couch and said seriously: “I’ve a neat little sum[SEP]in the bank, too, now, and I own a mite of a hut in Galway.[SEP]It’s not[SEP]worth much, but I love it.[SEP]I’ve managed to save something every year,[SEP]and that with helping my three sisters now and then, and tiding poor[SEP]Cousin Mike over bad seasons.[SEP]He’s that gifted, you know, but he will[SEP]drink and loses more good engagements than other fellows ever get.[SEP]And[SEP]I’ve traveled a bit, too.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Marie opened the door and smilingly announced that dinner was served.[EOT]
[SOT]“My dining-room,” Hilda explained, as she led the way, “is the tiniest[SEP]place you have ever seen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was a tiny room, hung all round with French prints, above which ran[SEP]a shelf full of china.[SEP]Hilda saw Alexander look up at it.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s not particularly rare,” she said, “but some of it was my[SEP]mother’s.[SEP]Heaven knows how she managed to keep it whole, through all[SEP]our wanderings, or in what baskets and bundles and theatre trunks it[SEP]hasn’t been stowed away.[SEP]We always had our tea out of those blue cups[SEP]when I was a little girl, sometimes in the queerest lodgings, and[SEP]sometimes on a trunk at the theatre—queer theatres, for that matter.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was a wonderful little dinner.[SEP]There was watercress soup, and sole,[SEP]and a delightful omelette stuffed with mushrooms and truffles, and two[SEP]small rare ducklings, and artichokes, and a dry yellow Rhone wine of[SEP]which Bartley had always been very fond.[SEP]He drank it appreciatively and[SEP]remarked that there was still no other he liked so well.[EOT]
[SOT]“I have some champagne for you, too.[SEP]I don’t drink it myself, but I[SEP]like to see it behave when it’s poured.[SEP]There is nothing else that[SEP]looks so jolly.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Thank you.[SEP]But I don’t like it so well as this.[SEP]” Bartley held the[SEP]yellow wine against the light and squinted into it as he turned the[SEP]glass slowly about.[SEP]“You have traveled, you say.[SEP]Have you been in Paris[SEP]much these late years?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda lowered one of the candle-shades carefully.[SEP]“Oh, yes, I go over[SEP]to Paris often.[SEP]There are few changes in the old Quarter.[SEP]Dear old[SEP]Madame Anger is dead—but perhaps you don’t remember her?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Don’t I, though![SEP]I’m so sorry to hear it.[SEP]How did her son turn out?[SEP]I[SEP]remember how she saved and scraped for him, and how he always lay abed[SEP]till ten o’clock.[SEP]He was the laziest fellow at the Beaux Arts; and[SEP]that’s saying a good deal.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, he is still clever and lazy.[SEP]They say he is a good architect[SEP]when he will work.[SEP]He’s a big, handsome creature, and he hates[SEP]Americans as much as ever.[SEP]But Angel—do you remember Angel?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Perfectly.[SEP]Did she ever get back to Brittany and her _bains de mer?[SEP]_”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah, no.[SEP]Poor Angel![SEP]She got tired of cooking and scouring the coppers[SEP]in Madame Anger’s little kitchen, so she ran away with a soldier, and[SEP]then with another soldier.[SEP]Too bad![SEP]She still lives about the Quarter,[SEP]and, though there is always a _soldat_, she has become a _blanchisseuse[SEP]de fin_.[SEP]She did my blouses beautifully the last time I was there, and[SEP]was so delighted to see me again.[SEP]I gave her all my old clothes, even[SEP]my old hats, though she always wears her Breton headdress.[SEP]Her hair is[SEP]still like flax, and her blue eyes are just like a baby’s, and she has[SEP]the same three freckles on her little nose, and talks about going back[SEP]to her _bains de mer_.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley looked at Hilda across the yellow light of the candles and[SEP]broke into a low, happy laugh.[SEP]“How jolly it was being young, Hilda![SEP]Do[SEP]you remember that first walk we took together in Paris?[SEP]We walked down[SEP]to the Place Saint-Michel to buy some lilacs.[SEP]Do you remember how sweet[SEP]they smelled?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Indeed I do.[SEP]Come, we’ll have our coffee in the other room, and you[SEP]can smoke.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda rose quickly, as if she wished to change the drift of their talk,[SEP]but Bartley found it pleasant to continue it.[EOT]
[SOT]“What a warm, soft spring evening that was,” he went on, as they sat[SEP]down in the study with the coffee on a little table between them; “and[SEP]the sky, over the bridges, was just the color of the lilacs.[SEP]We walked[SEP]on down by the river, didn’t we?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda laughed and looked at him questioningly.[SEP]He saw a gleam in her[SEP]eyes that he remembered even better than the episode he was recalling.[EOT]
[SOT]“I think we did,” she answered demurely.[SEP]“It was on the Quai we met[SEP]that woman who was crying so bitterly.[SEP]I gave her a spray of lilac, I[SEP]remember, and you gave her a franc.[SEP]I was frightened at your[SEP]prodigality.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I expect it was the last franc I had.[SEP]What a strong brown face she[SEP]had, and very tragic.[SEP]She looked at us with such despair and longing,[SEP]out from under her black shawl.[SEP]What she wanted from us was neither our[SEP]flowers nor our francs, but just our youth.[SEP]I remember it touched me[SEP]so.[SEP]I would have given her some of mine off my back, if I could.[SEP]I had[SEP]enough and to spare then,” Bartley mused, and looked thoughtfully at[SEP]his cigar.[EOT]
[SOT]They were both remembering what the woman had said when she took the[SEP]money: “God give you a happy love![SEP]” It was not in the ingratiating tone[SEP]of the habitual beggar: it had come out of the depths of the poor[SEP]creature’s sorrow, vibrating with pity for their youth and despair at[SEP]the terribleness of human life; it had the anguish of a voice of[SEP]prophecy.[SEP]Until she spoke, Bartley had not realized that he was in[SEP]love.[SEP]The strange woman, and her passionate sentence that rang out so[SEP]sharply, had frightened them both.[SEP]They went home sadly with the[SEP]lilacs, back to the Rue Saint-Jacques, walking very slowly, arm in arm.[SEP]When they reached the house where Hilda lodged, Bartley went across the[SEP]court with her, and up the dark old stairs to the third landing; and[SEP]there he had kissed her for the first time.[SEP]He had shut his eyes to[SEP]give him the courage, he remembered, and she had trembled so—[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley started when Hilda rang the little bell beside her.[SEP]“Dear me,[SEP]why did you do that?[SEP]I had quite forgotten—I was back there.[SEP]It was[SEP]very jolly,” he murmured lazily, as Marie came in to take away the[SEP]coffee.[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda laughed and went over to the piano.[SEP]“Well, we are neither of us[SEP]twenty now, you know.[SEP]Have I told you about my new play?[SEP]Mac is writing[SEP]one; really for me this time.[SEP]You see, I’m coming on.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ve seen nothing else.[SEP]What kind of a part is it?[SEP]Shall you wear[SEP]yellow gowns?[SEP]I hope so.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He was looking at her round slender figure, as she stood by the piano,[SEP]turning over a pile of music, and he felt the energy in every line of[SEP]it.[EOT]
[SOT]“No, it isn’t a dress-up part.[SEP]He doesn’t seem to fancy me in fine[SEP]feathers.[SEP]He says I ought to be minding the pigs at home, and I suppose[SEP]I ought.[SEP]But he’s given me some good Irish songs.[SEP]Listen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She sat down at the piano and sang.[SEP]When she finished, Alexander shook[SEP]himself out of a reverie.[EOT]
[SOT]“Sing ‘The Harp That Once,’ Hilda.[SEP]You used to sing it so well.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Nonsense.[SEP]Of course I can’t really sing, except the way my mother and[SEP]grandmother did before me.[SEP]Most actresses nowadays learn to sing[SEP]properly, so I tried a master; but he confused me, just![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander laughed.[SEP]“All the same, sing it, Hilda.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda started up from the stool and moved restlessly toward the window.[SEP]“It’s really too warm in this room to sing.[SEP]Don’t you feel it?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander went over and opened the window for her.[SEP]“Aren’t you afraid[SEP]to let the wind low like that on your neck?[SEP]Can’t I get a scarf or[SEP]something?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ask a theatre lady if she’s afraid of drafts![SEP]” Hilda laughed.[SEP]“But[SEP]perhaps, as I’m so warm—give me your handkerchief.[SEP]There, just in[SEP]front.[SEP]” He slipped the corners carefully under her shoulder-straps.[SEP]“There, that will do.[SEP]It looks like a bib.[SEP]” She pushed his hand away[SEP]quickly and stood looking out into the deserted square.[SEP]“Isn’t London a[SEP]tomb on Sunday night?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander caught the agitation in her voice.[SEP]He stood a little behind[SEP]her, and tried to steady himself as he said: “It’s soft and misty.[SEP]See[SEP]how white the stars are.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]For a long time neither Hilda nor Bartley spoke.[SEP]They stood close[SEP]together, looking out into the wan, watery sky, breathing always more[SEP]quickly and lightly, and it seemed as if all the clocks in the world[SEP]had stopped.[SEP]Suddenly he moved the clenched hand he held behind him and[SEP]dropped it violently at his side.[SEP]He felt a tremor run through the[SEP]slender yellow figure in front of him.[EOT]
[SOT]She caught his handkerchief from her throat and thrust it at him[SEP]without turning round.[SEP]“Here, take it.[SEP]You must go now, Bartley.[SEP]Good-night.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley leaned over her shoulder, without touching her, and whispered[SEP]in her ear: “You are giving me a chance?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes.[SEP]Take it and go.[SEP]This isn’t fair, you know.[SEP]Good-night.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander unclenched the two hands at his sides.[SEP]With one he threw down[SEP]the window and with the other—still standing behind her—he drew her[SEP]back against him.[EOT]
[SOT]She uttered a little cry, threw her arms over her head, and drew his[SEP]face down to hers.[SEP]“Are you going to let me love you a little,[SEP]Bartley?[SEP]” she whispered.[EOT]
[SOT]It was the afternoon of the day before Christmas.[SEP]Mrs.[SEP]Alexander had[SEP]been driving about all the morning, leaving presents at the houses of[SEP]her friends.[SEP]She lunched alone, and as she rose from the table she[SEP]spoke to the butler: “Thomas, I am going down to the kitchen now to see[SEP]Norah.[SEP]In half an hour you are to bring the greens up from the cellar[SEP]and put them in the library.[SEP]Mr. Alexander will be home at three to[SEP]hang them himself.[SEP]Don’t forget the stepladder, and plenty of tacks and[SEP]string.[SEP]You may bring the azaleas upstairs.[SEP]Take the white one to Mr.[SEP]Alexander’s study.[SEP]Put the two pink ones in this room, and the red one[SEP]in the drawing-room.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]A little before three o’clock Mrs.[SEP]Alexander went into the library to[SEP]see that everything was ready.[SEP]She pulled the window shades high, for[SEP]the weather was dark and stormy, and there was little light, even in[SEP]the streets.[SEP]A foot of snow had fallen during the morning, and the wide[SEP]space over the river was thick with flying flakes that fell and[SEP]wreathed the masses of floating ice.[SEP]Winifred was standing by the[SEP]window when she heard the front door open.[SEP]She hurried to the hall as[SEP]Alexander came stamping in, covered with snow.[SEP]He kissed her joyfully[SEP]and brushed away the snow that fell on her hair.[EOT]
[SOT]“I wish I had asked you to meet me at the office and walk home with me,[SEP]Winifred.[SEP]The Common is beautiful.[SEP]The boys have swept the snow off the[SEP]pond and are skating furiously.[SEP]Did the cyclamens come?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“An hour ago.[SEP]What splendid ones![SEP]But aren’t you frightfully[SEP]extravagant?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Not for Christmas-time.[SEP]I’ll go upstairs and change my coat.[SEP]I shall[SEP]be down in a moment.[SEP]Tell Thomas to get everything ready.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]When Alexander reappeared, he took his wife’s arm and went with her[SEP]into the library.[SEP]“When did the azaleas get here?[SEP]Thomas has got the[SEP]white one in my room.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I told him to put it there.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But, I say, it’s much the finest of the lot![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s why I had it put there.[SEP]There is too much color in that room[SEP]for a red one, you know.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley began to sort the greens.[SEP]“It looks very splendid there, but I[SEP]feel piggish to have it.[SEP]However, we really spend more time there than[SEP]anywhere else in the house.[SEP]Will you hand me the holly?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He climbed up the stepladder, which creaked under his weight, and began[SEP]to twist the tough stems of the holly into the frame-work of the[SEP]chandelier.[EOT]
[SOT]“I forgot to tell you that I had a letter from Wilson, this morning,[SEP]explaining his telegram.[SEP]He is coming on because an old uncle up in[SEP]Vermont has conveniently died and left Wilson a little money—something[SEP]like ten thousand.[SEP]He’s coming on to settle up the estate.[SEP]Won’t it be[SEP]jolly to have him?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And how fine that he’s come into a little money.[SEP]I can see him posting[SEP]down State Street to the steamship offices.[SEP]He will get a good many[SEP]trips out of that ten thousand.[SEP]What can have detained him?[SEP]I expected[SEP]him here for luncheon.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Those trains from Albany are always late.[SEP]He’ll be along sometime this[SEP]afternoon.[SEP]And now, don’t you want to go upstairs and lie down for an[SEP]hour?[SEP]You’ve had a busy morning and I don’t want you to be tired[SEP]to-night.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]After his wife went upstairs Alexander worked energetically at the[SEP]greens for a few moments.[SEP]Then, as he was cutting off a length of[SEP]string, he sighed suddenly and sat down, staring out of the window at[SEP]the snow.[SEP]The animation died out of his face, but in his eyes there was[SEP]a restless light, a look of apprehension and suspense.[SEP]He kept clasping[SEP]and unclasping his big hands as if he were trying to realize something.[SEP]The clock ticked through the minutes of a half-hour and the afternoon[SEP]outside began to thicken and darken turbidly.[SEP]Alexander, since he first[SEP]sat down, had not changed his position.[SEP]He leaned forward, his hands[SEP]between his knees, scarcely breathing, as if he were holding himself[SEP]away from his surroundings, from the room, and from the very chair in[SEP]which he sat, from everything except the wild eddies of snow above the[SEP]river on which his eyes were fixed with feverish intentness, as if he[SEP]were trying to project himself thither.[SEP]When at last Lucius Wilson was[SEP]announced, Alexander sprang eagerly to his feet and hurried to meet his[SEP]old instructor.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hello, Wilson.[SEP]What luck![SEP]Come into the library.[SEP]We are to have a lot[SEP]of people to dinner to-night, and Winifred’s lying down.[SEP]You will[SEP]excuse her, won’t you?[SEP]And now what about yourself?[SEP]Sit down and tell[SEP]me everything.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I think I’d rather move about, if you don’t mind.[SEP]I’ve been sitting in[SEP]the train for a week, it seems to me.[SEP]” Wilson stood before the fire[SEP]with his hands behind him and looked about the room.[SEP]“You _have_ been[SEP]busy.[SEP]Bartley, if I’d had my choice of all possible places in which to[SEP]spend Christmas, your house would certainly be the place I’d have[SEP]chosen.[SEP]Happy people do a great deal for their friends.[SEP]A house like[SEP]this throws its warmth out.[SEP]I felt it distinctly as I was coming[SEP]through the Berkshires.[SEP]I could scarcely believe that I was to see Mrs.[SEP]Bartley again so soon.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Thank you, Wilson.[SEP]She’ll be as glad to see you.[SEP]Shall we have tea[SEP]now?[SEP]I’ll ring for Thomas to clear away this litter.[SEP]Winifred says I[SEP]always wreck the house when I try to do anything.[SEP]Do you know, I am[SEP]quite tired.[SEP]Looks as if I were not used to work, doesn’t it?[SEP]”[SEP]Alexander laughed and dropped into a chair.[SEP]“You know, I’m sailing the[SEP]day after New Year’s.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Again?[SEP]Why, you’ve been over twice since I was here in the spring,[SEP]haven’t you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, I was in London about ten days in the summer.[SEP]Went to escape the[SEP]hot weather more than anything else.[SEP]I shan’t be gone more than a month[SEP]this time.[SEP]Winifred and I have been up in Canada for most of the[SEP]autumn.[SEP]That Moorlock Bridge is on my back all the time.[SEP]I never had so[SEP]much trouble with a job before.[SEP]” Alexander moved about restlessly and[SEP]fell to poking the fire.[EOT]
[SOT]“Haven’t I seen in the papers that there is some trouble about a[SEP]tidewater bridge of yours in New Jersey?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, that doesn’t amount to anything.[SEP]It’s held up by a steel strike.[SEP]A[SEP]bother, of course, but the sort of thing one is always having to put up[SEP]with.[SEP]But the Moorlock Bridge is a continual anxiety.[SEP]You see, the[SEP]truth is, we are having to build pretty well to the strain limit up[SEP]there.[SEP]They’ve crowded me too much on the cost.[SEP]It’s all very well if[SEP]everything goes well, but these estimates have never been used for[SEP]anything of such length before.[SEP]However, there’s nothing to be done.[SEP]They hold me to the scale I’ve used in shorter bridges.[SEP]The last thing[SEP]a bridge commission cares about is the kind of bridge you build.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]When Bartley had finished dressing for dinner he went into his study,[SEP]where he found his wife arranging flowers on his writing-table.[EOT]
[SOT]“These pink roses just came from Mrs.[SEP]Hastings,” she said, smiling,[SEP]“and I am sure she meant them for you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley looked about with an air of satisfaction at the greens and the[SEP]wreaths in the windows.[SEP]“Have you a moment, Winifred?[SEP]I have just now[SEP]been thinking that this is our twelfth Christmas.[SEP]Can you realize it?[SEP]”[SEP]He went up to the table and took her hands away from the flowers,[SEP]drying them with his pocket handkerchief.[SEP]“They’ve been awfully happy[SEP]ones, all of them, haven’t they?[SEP]” He took her in his arms and bent[SEP]back, lifting her a little and giving her a long kiss.[SEP]“You are happy,[SEP]aren’t you Winifred?[SEP]More than anything else in the world, I want you[SEP]to be happy.[SEP]Sometimes, of late, I’ve thought you looked as if you were[SEP]troubled.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No; it’s only when you are troubled and harassed that I feel worried,[SEP]Bartley.[SEP]I wish you always seemed as you do to-night.[SEP]But you don’t,[SEP]always.[SEP]” She looked earnestly and inquiringly into his eyes.[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander took her two hands from his shoulders and swung them back and[SEP]forth in his own, laughing his big blond laugh.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m growing older, my dear; that’s what you feel.[SEP]Now, may I show you[SEP]something?[SEP]I meant to save them until to-morrow, but I want you to wear[SEP]them to-night.[SEP]” He took a little leather box out of his pocket and[SEP]opened it.[SEP]On the white velvet lay two long pendants of curiously[SEP]worked gold, set with pearls.[SEP]Winifred looked from the box to Bartley[SEP]and exclaimed:—[EOT]
[SOT]“Where did you ever find such gold work, Bartley?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s old Flemish.[SEP]Isn’t it fine?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They are the most beautiful things, dear.[SEP]But, you know, I never wear[SEP]earrings.[SEP]”[EOT]
