[SOT]“Yes, yes, I know.[SEP]But I want you to wear them.[SEP]I have always wanted[SEP]you to.[SEP]So few women can.[SEP]There must be a good ear, to begin with, and[SEP]a nose”—he waved his hand—“above reproach.[SEP]Most women look silly in[SEP]them.[SEP]They go only with faces like yours—very, very proud, and just a[SEP]little hard.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Winifred laughed as she went over to the mirror and fitted the delicate[SEP]springs to the lobes of her ears.[SEP]“Oh, Bartley, that old foolishness[SEP]about my being hard.[SEP]It really hurts my feelings.[SEP]But I must go down[SEP]now.[SEP]People are beginning to come.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley drew her arm about his neck and went to the door with her.[SEP]“Not[SEP]hard to me, Winifred,” he whispered.[SEP]“Never, never hard to me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Left alone, he paced up and down his study.[SEP]He was at home again, among[SEP]all the dear familiar things that spoke to him of so many happy years.[SEP]His house to-night would be full of charming people, who liked and[SEP]admired him.[SEP]Yet all the time, underneath his pleasure and hopefulness[SEP]and satisfaction, he was conscious of the vibration of an unnatural[SEP]excitement.[SEP]Amid this light and warmth and friendliness, he sometimes[SEP]started and shuddered, as if some one had stepped on his grave.[SEP]Something had broken loose in him of which he knew nothing except that[SEP]it was sullen and powerful, and that it wrung and tortured him.[SEP]Sometimes it came upon him softly, in enervating reveries.[SEP]Sometimes it[SEP]battered him like the cannon rolling in the hold of the vessel.[SEP]Always,[SEP]now, it brought with it a sense of quickened life, of stimulating[SEP]danger.[SEP]To-night it came upon him suddenly, as he was walking the[SEP]floor, after his wife left him.[SEP]It seemed impossible; he could not[SEP]believe it.[SEP]He glanced entreatingly at the door, as if to call her[SEP]back.[SEP]He heard voices in the hall below, and knew that he must go down.[SEP]Going over to the window, he looked out at the lights across the river.[SEP]How could this happen here, in his own house, among the things he[SEP]loved?[SEP]What was it that reached in out of the darkness and thrilled[SEP]him?[SEP]As he stood there he had a feeling that he would never escape.[SEP]He[SEP]shut his eyes and pressed his forehead against the cold window glass,[SEP]breathing in the chill that came through it.[SEP]“That this,” he groaned,[SEP]“that this should have happened to _me![SEP]_”[EOT]
[SOT]On New Year’s day a thaw set in, and during the night torrents of rain[SEP]fell.[SEP]In the morning, the morning of Alexander’s departure for England,[SEP]the river was streaked with fog and the rain drove hard against the[SEP]windows of the breakfast-room.[SEP]Alexander had finished his coffee and[SEP]was pacing up and down.[SEP]His wife sat at the table, watching him.[SEP]She[SEP]was pale and unnaturally calm.[SEP]When Thomas brought the letters, Bartley[SEP]sank into his chair and ran them over rapidly.[EOT]
[SOT]“Here’s a note from old Wilson.[SEP]He’s safe back at his grind, and says[SEP]he had a bully time.[SEP]‘The memory of Mrs.[SEP]Bartley will make my whole[SEP]winter fragrant.[SEP]’ Just like him.[SEP]He will go on getting measureless[SEP]satisfaction out of you by his study fire.[SEP]What a man he is for looking[SEP]on at life![SEP]” Bartley sighed, pushed the letters back impatiently, and[SEP]went over to the window.[SEP]“This is a nasty sort of day to sail.[SEP]I’ve a[SEP]notion to call it off.[SEP]Next week would be time enough.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That would only mean starting twice.[SEP]It wouldn’t really help you out[SEP]at all,” Mrs.[SEP]Alexander spoke soothingly.[SEP]“And you’d come back late for[SEP]all your engagements.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley began jingling some loose coins in his pocket.[SEP]“I wish things[SEP]would let me rest.[SEP]I’m tired of work, tired of people, tired of[SEP]trailing about.[SEP]” He looked out at the storm-beaten river.[EOT]
[SOT]Winifred came up behind him and put a hand on his shoulder.[SEP]“That’s[SEP]what you always say, poor Bartley![SEP]At bottom you really like all these[SEP]things.[SEP]Can’t you remember that?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He put his arm about her.[SEP]“All the same, life runs smoothly enough with[SEP]some people, and with me it’s always a messy sort of patchwork.[SEP]It’s[SEP]like the song; peace is where I am not.[SEP]How can you face it all with so[SEP]much fortitude?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She looked at him with that clear gaze which Wilson had so much[SEP]admired, which he had felt implied such high confidence and fearless[SEP]pride.[SEP]“Oh, I faced that long ago, when you were on your first bridge,[SEP]up at old Allway.[SEP]I knew then that your paths were not to be paths of[SEP]peace, but I decided that I wanted to follow them.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley and his wife stood silent for a long time; the fire crackled in[SEP]the grate, the rain beat insistently upon the windows, and the sleepy[SEP]Angora looked up at them curiously.[EOT]
[SOT]Presently Thomas made a discreet sound at the door.[SEP]“Shall Edward bring[SEP]down your trunks, sir?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes; they are ready.[SEP]Tell him not to forget the big portfolio on the[SEP]study table.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Thomas withdrew, closing the door softly.[SEP]Bartley turned away from his[SEP]wife, still holding her hand.[SEP]“It never gets any easier, Winifred.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]They both started at the sound of the carriage on the pavement outside.[SEP]Alexander sat down and leaned his head on his hand.[SEP]His wife bent over[SEP]him.[SEP]“Courage,” she said gayly.[SEP]Bartley rose and rang the bell.[SEP]Thomas[SEP]brought him his hat and stick and ulster.[SEP]At the sight of these, the[SEP]supercilious Angora moved restlessly, quitted her red cushion by the[SEP]fire, and came up, waving her tail in vexation at these ominous[SEP]indications of change.[SEP]Alexander stooped to stroke her, and then[SEP]plunged into his coat and drew on his gloves.[SEP]His wife held his stick,[SEP]smiling.[SEP]Bartley smiled too, and his eyes cleared.[SEP]“I’ll work like the[SEP]devil, Winifred, and be home again before you realize I’ve gone.[SEP]” He[SEP]kissed her quickly several times, hurried out of the front door into[SEP]the rain, and waved to her from the carriage window as the driver was[SEP]starting his melancholy, dripping black horses.[SEP]Alexander sat with his[SEP]hands clenched on his knees.[SEP]As the carriage turned up the hill, he[SEP]lifted one hand and brought it down violently.[SEP]“This time”—he spoke[SEP]aloud and through his set teeth—“this time I’m going to end it![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]On the afternoon of the third day out, Alexander was sitting well to[SEP]the stern, on the windward side where the chairs were few, his rugs[SEP]over him and the collar of his fur-lined coat turned up about his ears.[SEP]The weather had so far been dark and raw.[SEP]For two hours he had been[SEP]watching the low, dirty sky and the beating of the heavy rain upon the[SEP]iron-colored sea.[SEP]There was a long, oily swell that made exercise[SEP]laborious.[SEP]The decks smelled of damp woolens, and the air was so humid[SEP]that drops of moisture kept gathering upon his hair and mustache.[SEP]He[SEP]seldom moved except to brush them away.[SEP]The great open spaces made him[SEP]passive and the restlessness of the water quieted him.[SEP]He intended[SEP]during the voyage to decide upon a course of action, but he held all[SEP]this away from him for the present and lay in a blessed gray oblivion.[SEP]Deep down in him somewhere his resolution was weakening and[SEP]strengthening, ebbing and flowing.[SEP]The thing that perturbed him went on[SEP]as steadily as his pulse, but he was almost unconscious of it.[SEP]He was[SEP]submerged in the vast impersonal grayness about him, and at intervals[SEP]the sidelong roll of the boat measured off time like the ticking of a[SEP]clock.[SEP]He felt released from everything that troubled and perplexed[SEP]him.[SEP]It was as if he had tricked and outwitted torturing memories, had[SEP]actually managed to get on board without them.[SEP]He thought of nothing at[SEP]all.[SEP]If his mind now and again picked a face out of the grayness, it[SEP]was Lucius Wilson’s, or the face of an old schoolmate, forgotten for[SEP]years; or it was the slim outline of a favorite greyhound he used to[SEP]hunt jack-rabbits with when he was a boy.[EOT]
[SOT]Toward six o’clock the wind rose and tugged at the tarpaulin and[SEP]brought the swell higher.[SEP]After dinner Alexander came back to the wet[SEP]deck, piled his damp rugs over him again, and sat smoking, losing[SEP]himself in the obliterating blackness and drowsing in the rush of the[SEP]gale.[SEP]Before he went below a few bright stars were pricked off between[SEP]heavily moving masses of cloud.[EOT]
[SOT]The next morning was bright and mild, with a fresh breeze.[SEP]Alexander[SEP]felt the need of exercise even before he came out of his cabin.[SEP]When he[SEP]went on deck the sky was blue and blinding, with heavy whiffs of white[SEP]cloud, smoke-colored at the edges, moving rapidly across it.[SEP]The water[SEP]was roughish, a cold, clear indigo breaking into whitecaps.[SEP]Bartley[SEP]walked for two hours, and then stretched himself in the sun until[SEP]lunch-time.[EOT]
[SOT]In the afternoon he wrote a long letter to Winifred.[SEP]Later, as he[SEP]walked the deck through a splendid golden sunset, his spirits rose[SEP]continually.[SEP]It was agreeable to come to himself again after several[SEP]days of numbness and torpor.[SEP]He stayed out until the last tinge of[SEP]violet had faded from the water.[SEP]There was literally a taste of life on[SEP]his lips as he sat down to dinner and ordered a bottle of champagne.[SEP]He[SEP]was late in finishing his dinner, and drank rather more wine than he[SEP]had meant to.[SEP]When he went above, the wind had risen and the deck was[SEP]almost deserted.[SEP]As he stepped out of the door a gale lifted his heavy[SEP]fur coat about his shoulders.[SEP]He fought his way up the deck with keen[SEP]exhilaration.[SEP]The moment he stepped, almost out of breath, behind the[SEP]shelter of the stern, the wind was cut off, and he felt, like a rush of[SEP]warm air, a sense of close and intimate companionship.[SEP]He started back[SEP]and tore his coat open as if something warm were actually clinging to[SEP]him beneath it.[SEP]He hurried up the deck and went into the saloon parlor,[SEP]full of women who had retreated thither from the sharp wind.[SEP]He threw[SEP]himself upon them.[SEP]He talked delightfully to the older ones and played[SEP]accompaniments for the younger ones until the last sleepy girl had[SEP]followed her mother below.[SEP]Then he went into the smoking-room.[SEP]He[SEP]played bridge until two o’clock in the morning, and managed to lose a[SEP]considerable sum of money without really noticing that he was doing so.[EOT]
[SOT]After the break of one fine day the weather was pretty consistently[SEP]dull.[SEP]When the low sky thinned a trifle, the pale white spot of a sun[SEP]did no more than throw a bluish lustre on the water, giving it the dark[SEP]brightness of newly cut lead.[SEP]Through one after another of those gray[SEP]days Alexander drowsed and mused, drinking in the grateful moisture.[SEP]But the complete peace of the first part of the voyage was over.[SEP]Sometimes he rose suddenly from his chair as if driven out, and paced[SEP]the deck for hours.[SEP]People noticed his propensity for walking in rough[SEP]weather, and watched him curiously as he did his rounds.[SEP]From his[SEP]abstraction and the determined set of his jaw, they fancied he must be[SEP]thinking about his bridge.[SEP]Every one had heard of the new cantilever[SEP]bridge in Canada.[EOT]
[SOT]But Alexander was not thinking about his work.[SEP]After the fourth night[SEP]out, when his will suddenly softened under his hands, he had been[SEP]continually hammering away at himself.[SEP]More and more often, when he[SEP]first wakened in the morning or when he stepped into a warm place after[SEP]being chilled on the deck, he felt a sudden painful delight at being[SEP]nearer another shore.[SEP]Sometimes when he was most despondent, when he[SEP]thought himself worn out with this struggle, in a flash he was free of[SEP]it and leaped into an overwhelming consciousness of himself.[SEP]On the[SEP]instant he felt that marvelous return of the impetuousness, the intense[SEP]excitement, the increasing expectancy of youth.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER VI[EOT]
[SOT]The last two days of the voyage Bartley found almost intolerable.[SEP]The[SEP]stop at Queenstown, the tedious passage up the Mersey, were things that[SEP]he noted dimly through his growing impatience.[SEP]He had planned to stop[SEP]in Liverpool; but, instead, he took the boat train for London.[EOT]
[SOT]Emerging at Euston at half-past three o’clock in the afternoon,[SEP]Alexander had his luggage sent to the Savoy and drove at once to[SEP]Bedford Square.[SEP]When Marie met him at the door, even her strong sense[SEP]of the proprieties could not restrain her surprise and delight.[SEP]She[SEP]blushed and smiled and fumbled his card in her confusion before she ran[SEP]upstairs.[SEP]Alexander paced up and down the hallway, buttoning and[SEP]unbuttoning his overcoat, until she returned and took him up to Hilda’s[SEP]living-room.[SEP]The room was empty when he entered.[SEP]A coal fire was[SEP]crackling in the grate and the lamps were lit, for it was already[SEP]beginning to grow dark outside.[SEP]Alexander did not sit down.[SEP]He stood[SEP]his ground over by the windows until Hilda came in.[SEP]She called his name[SEP]on the threshold, but in her swift flight across the room she felt a[SEP]change in him and caught herself up so deftly that he could not tell[SEP]just when she did it.[SEP]She merely brushed his cheek with her lips and[SEP]put a hand lightly and joyously on either shoulder.[SEP]“Oh, what a grand[SEP]thing to happen on a raw day![SEP]I felt it in my bones when I woke this[SEP]morning that something splendid was going to turn up.[SEP]I thought it[SEP]might be Sister Kate or Cousin Mike would be happening along.[SEP]I never[SEP]dreamed it would be you, Bartley.[SEP]But why do you let me chatter on like[SEP]this?[SEP]Come over to the fire; you’re chilled through.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She pushed him toward the big chair by the fire, and sat down on a[SEP]stool at the opposite side of the hearth, her knees drawn up to her[SEP]chin, laughing like a happy little girl.[EOT]
[SOT]“When did you come, Bartley, and how did it happen?[SEP]You haven’t spoken[SEP]a word.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I got in about ten minutes ago.[SEP]I landed at Liverpool this morning and[SEP]came down on the boat train.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander leaned forward and warmed his hands before the blaze.[SEP]Hilda[SEP]watched him with perplexity.[EOT]
[SOT]“There’s something troubling you, Bartley.[SEP]What is it?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley bent lower over the fire.[SEP]“It’s the whole thing that troubles[SEP]me, Hilda.[SEP]You and I.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda took a quick, soft breath.[SEP]She looked at his heavy shoulders and[SEP]big, determined head, thrust forward like a catapult in leash.[EOT]
[SOT]“What about us, Bartley?[SEP]” she asked in a thin voice.[EOT]
[SOT]He locked and unlocked his hands over the grate and spread his fingers[SEP]close to the bluish flame, while the coals crackled and the clock[SEP]ticked and a street vendor began to call under the window.[SEP]At last[SEP]Alexander brought out one word:—[EOT]
[SOT]“Everything![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda was pale by this time, and her eyes were wide with fright.[SEP]She[SEP]looked about desperately from Bartley to the door, then to the windows,[SEP]and back again to Bartley.[SEP]She rose uncertainly, touched his hair with[SEP]her hand, then sank back upon her stool.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll do anything you wish me to, Bartley,” she said tremulously.[SEP]“I[SEP]can’t stand seeing you miserable.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I can’t live with myself any longer,” he answered roughly.[EOT]
[SOT]He rose and pushed the chair behind him and began to walk miserably[SEP]about the room, seeming to find it too small for him.[SEP]He pulled up a[SEP]window as if the air were heavy.[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda watched him from her corner, trembling and scarcely breathing,[SEP]dark shadows growing about her eyes.[EOT]
[SOT]“It .[SEP].[SEP].[SEP]it hasn’t always made you miserable, has it?[SEP]” Her eyelids[SEP]fell and her lips quivered.[EOT]
[SOT]“Always.[SEP]But it’s worse now.[SEP]It’s unbearable.[SEP]It tortures me every[SEP]minute.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But why _now?[SEP]_” she asked piteously, wringing her hands.[EOT]
[SOT]He ignored her question.[SEP]“I am not a man who can live two lives,” he[SEP]went on feverishly.[SEP]“Each life spoils the other.[SEP]I get nothing but[SEP]misery out of either.[SEP]The world is all there, just as it used to be,[SEP]but I can’t get at it any more.[SEP]There is this deception between me and[SEP]everything.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]At that word “deception,” spoken with such self-contempt, the color[SEP]flashed back into Hilda’s face as suddenly as if she had been struck by[SEP]a whiplash.[SEP]She bit her lip and looked down at her hands, which were[SEP]clasped tightly in front of her.[EOT]
[SOT]“Could you—could you sit down and talk about it quietly, Bartley, as if[SEP]I were a friend, and not some one who had to be defied?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He dropped back heavily into his chair by the fire.[SEP]“It was myself I[SEP]was defying, Hilda.[SEP]I have thought about it until I am worn out.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He looked at her and his haggard face softened.[SEP]He put out his hand[SEP]toward her as he looked away again into the fire.[EOT]
[SOT]She crept across to him, drawing her stool after her.[SEP]“When did you[SEP]first begin to feel like this, Bartley?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“After the very first.[SEP]The first was—sort of in play, wasn’t it?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda’s face quivered, but she whispered: “Yes, I think it must have[SEP]been.[SEP]But why didn’t you tell me when you were here in the summer?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander groaned.[SEP]“I meant to, but somehow I couldn’t.[SEP]We had only a[SEP]few days, and your new play was just on, and you were so happy.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, I was happy, wasn’t I?[SEP]” She pressed his hand gently in gratitude.[SEP]“Weren’t you happy then, at all?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She closed her eyes and took a deep breath, as if to draw in again the[SEP]fragrance of those days.[SEP]Something of their troubling sweetness came[SEP]back to Alexander, too.[SEP]He moved uneasily and his chair creaked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, I was then.[SEP]You know.[SEP]But afterward.[SEP].[SEP].[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, yes,” she hurried, pulling her hand gently away from him.[SEP]Presently it stole back to his coat sleeve.[SEP]“Please tell me one thing,[SEP]Bartley.[SEP]At least, tell me that you believe I thought I was making you[SEP]happy.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]His hand shut down quickly over the questioning fingers on his sleeves.[SEP]“Yes, Hilda; I know that,” he said simply.[EOT]
[SOT]She leaned her head against his arm and spoke softly:—[EOT]
[SOT]“You see, my mistake was in wanting you to have everything.[SEP]I wanted[SEP]you to eat all the cakes and have them, too.[SEP]I somehow believed that I[SEP]could take all the bad consequences for you.[SEP]I wanted you always to be[SEP]happy and handsome and successful—to have all the things that a great[SEP]man ought to have, and, once in a way, the careless holidays that great[SEP]men are not permitted.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley gave a bitter little laugh, and Hilda looked up and read in the[SEP]deepening lines of his face that youth and Bartley would not much[SEP]longer struggle together.[EOT]
[SOT]“I understand, Bartley.[SEP]I was wrong.[SEP]But I didn’t know.[SEP]You’ve only to[SEP]tell me now.[SEP]What must I do that I’ve not done, or what must I not do?[SEP]”[SEP]She listened intently, but she heard nothing but the creaking of his[SEP]chair.[SEP]“You want me to say it?[SEP]” she whispered.[SEP]“You want to tell me[SEP]that you can only see me like this, as old friends do, or out in the[SEP]world among people?[SEP]I can do that.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I can’t,” he said heavily.[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda shivered and sat still.[SEP]Bartley leaned his head in his hands and[SEP]spoke through his teeth.[SEP]“It’s got to be a clean break, Hilda.[SEP]I can’t[SEP]see you at all, anywhere.[SEP]What I mean is that I want you to promise[SEP]never to see me again, no matter how often I come, no matter how hard I[SEP]beg.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda sprang up like a flame.[SEP]She stood over him with her hands[SEP]clenched at her side, her body rigid.[EOT]
[SOT]“No![SEP]” she gasped.[SEP]“It’s too late to ask that.[SEP]Do you hear me, Bartley?[SEP]It’s too late.[SEP]I won’t promise.[SEP]It’s abominable of you to ask me.[SEP]Keep[SEP]away if you wish; when have I ever followed you?[SEP]But, if you come to[SEP]me, I’ll do as I see fit.[SEP]The shamefulness of your asking me to do[SEP]that![SEP]If you come to me, I’ll do as I see fit.[SEP]Do you understand?[SEP]Bartley, you’re cowardly![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander rose and shook himself angrily.[SEP]“Yes, I know I’m cowardly.[SEP]I’m afraid of myself.[SEP]I don’t trust myself any more.[SEP]I carried it all[SEP]lightly enough at first, but now I don’t dare trifle with it.[SEP]It’s[SEP]getting the better of me.[SEP]It’s different now.[SEP]I’m growing older, and[SEP]you’ve got my young self here with you.[SEP]It’s through him that I’ve come[SEP]to wish for you all and all the time.[SEP]” He took her roughly in his arms.[SEP]“Do you know what I mean?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda held her face back from him and began to cry bitterly.[SEP]“Oh,[SEP]Bartley, what am I to do?[SEP]Why didn’t you let me be angry with you?[SEP]You[SEP]ask me to stay away from you because you want me![SEP]And I’ve got nobody[SEP]but you.[SEP]I will do anything you say—but that![SEP]I will ask the least[SEP]imaginable, but I must have _something![SEP]_”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley turned away and sank down in his chair again.[SEP]Hilda sat on the[SEP]arm of it and put her hands lightly on his shoulders.[EOT]
[SOT]“Just something Bartley.[SEP]I must have you to think of through the months[SEP]and months of loneliness.[SEP]I must see you.[SEP]I must know about you.[SEP]The[SEP]sight of you, Bartley, to see you living and happy and successful—can I[SEP]never make you understand what that means to me?[SEP]” She pressed his[SEP]shoulders gently.[SEP]“You see, loving some one as I love you makes the[SEP]whole world different.[SEP]If I’d met you later, if I hadn’t loved you so[SEP]well—but that’s all over, long ago.[SEP]Then came all those years without[SEP]you, lonely and hurt and discouraged; those decent young fellows and[SEP]poor Mac, and me never heeding—hard as a steel spring.[SEP]And then you[SEP]came back, not caring very much, but it made no difference.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She slid to the floor beside him, as if she were too tired to sit up[SEP]any longer.[SEP]Bartley bent over and took her in his arms, kissing her[SEP]mouth and her wet, tired eyes.[EOT]
[SOT]“Don’t cry, don’t cry,” he whispered.[SEP]“We’ve tortured each other enough[SEP]for tonight.[SEP]Forget everything except that I am here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I think I have forgotten everything but that already,” she murmured.[SEP]“Ah, your dear arms![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER VII[EOT]
[SOT]During the fortnight that Alexander was in London he drove himself[SEP]hard.[SEP]He got through a great deal of personal business and saw a great[SEP]many men who were doing interesting things in his own profession.[SEP]He[SEP]disliked to think of his visits to London as holidays, and when he was[SEP]there he worked even harder than he did at home.[EOT]
[SOT]The day before his departure for Liverpool was a singularly fine one.[SEP]The thick air had cleared overnight in a strong wind which brought in a[SEP]golden dawn and then fell off to a fresh breeze.[SEP]When Bartley looked[SEP]out of his windows from the Savoy, the river was flashing silver and[SEP]the gray stone along the Embankment was bathed in bright, clear[SEP]sunshine.[SEP]London had wakened to life after three weeks of cold and[SEP]sodden rain.[SEP]Bartley breakfasted hurriedly and went over his mail while[SEP]the hotel valet packed his trunks.[SEP]Then he paid his account and walked[SEP]rapidly down the Strand past Charing Cross Station.[SEP]His spirits rose[SEP]with every step, and when he reached Trafalgar Square, blazing in the[SEP]sun, with its fountains playing and its column reaching up into the[SEP]bright air, he signaled to a hansom, and, before he knew what he was[SEP]about, told the driver to go to Bedford Square by way of the British[SEP]Museum.[EOT]
[SOT]When he reached Hilda’s apartment she met him, fresh as the morning[SEP]itself.[SEP]Her rooms were flooded with sunshine and full of the flowers he[SEP]had been sending her.[SEP]She would never let him give her anything else.[EOT]
[SOT]“Are you busy this morning, Hilda?[SEP]” he asked as he sat down, his hat[SEP]and gloves in his hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“Very.[SEP]I’ve been up and about three hours, working at my part.[SEP]We open[SEP]in February, you know.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, then you’ve worked enough.[SEP]And so have I.[SEP]I’ve seen all my men,[SEP]my packing is done, and I go up to Liverpool this evening.[SEP]But this[SEP]morning we are going to have a holiday.[SEP]What do you say to a drive out[SEP]to Kew and Richmond?[SEP]You may not get another day like this all winter.[SEP]It’s like a fine April day at home.[SEP]May I use your telephone?[SEP]I want to[SEP]order the carriage.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, how jolly![SEP]There, sit down at the desk.[SEP]And while you are[SEP]telephoning I’ll change my dress.[SEP]I shan’t be long.[SEP]All the morning[SEP]papers are on the table.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda was back in a few moments wearing a long gray squirrel coat and a[SEP]broad fur hat.[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley rose and inspected her.[SEP]“Why don’t you wear some of those pink[SEP]roses?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“But they came only this morning, and they have not even begun to open.[SEP]I was saving them.[SEP]I am so unconsciously thrifty![SEP]” She laughed as she[SEP]looked about the room.[SEP]“You’ve been sending me far too many flowers,[SEP]Bartley.[SEP]New ones every day.[SEP]That’s too often; though I do love to open[SEP]the boxes, and I take good care of them.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why won’t you let me send you any of those jade or ivory things you[SEP]are so fond of?[SEP]Or pictures?[SEP]I know a good deal about pictures.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda shook her large hat as she drew the roses out of the tall glass.[SEP]“No, there are some things you can’t do.[SEP]There’s the carriage.[SEP]Will you[SEP]button my gloves for me?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bartley took her wrist and began to button the long gray suede glove.[SEP]“How gay your eyes are this morning, Hilda.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s because I’ve been studying.[SEP]It always stirs me up a little.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He pushed the top of the glove up slowly.[SEP]“When did you learn to take[SEP]hold of your parts like that?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“When I had nothing else to think of.[SEP]Come, the carriage is waiting.[SEP]What a shocking while you take.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m in no hurry.[SEP]We’ve plenty of time.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]They found all London abroad.[SEP]Piccadilly was a stream of rapidly moving[SEP]carriages, from which flashed furs and flowers and bright winter[SEP]costumes.[SEP]The metal trappings of the harnesses shone dazzlingly, and[SEP]the wheels were revolving disks that threw off rays of light.[SEP]The parks[SEP]were full of children and nursemaids and joyful dogs that leaped and[SEP]yelped and scratched up the brown earth with their paws.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m not going until to-morrow, you know,” Bartley announced suddenly.[SEP]“I’ll cut off a day in Liverpool.[SEP]I haven’t felt so jolly this long[SEP]while.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda looked up with a smile which she tried not to make too glad.[SEP]“I[SEP]think people were meant to be happy, a little,” she said.[EOT]
[SOT]They had lunch at Richmond and then walked to Twickenham, where they[SEP]had sent the carriage.[SEP]They drove back, with a glorious sunset behind[SEP]them, toward the distant gold-washed city.[SEP]It was one of those rare[SEP]afternoons when all the thickness and shadow of London are changed to a[SEP]kind of shining, pulsing, special atmosphere; when the smoky vapors[SEP]become fluttering golden clouds, nacreous veils of pink and amber; when[SEP]all that bleakness of gray stone and dullness of dirty brick trembles[SEP]in aureate light, and all the roofs and spires, and one great dome, are[SEP]floated in golden haze.[SEP]On such rare afternoons the ugliest of cities[SEP]becomes the most poetic, and months of sodden days are offset by a[SEP]moment of miracle.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s like that with us Londoners, too,” Hilda was saying.[SEP]“Everything[SEP]is awfully grim and cheerless, our weather and our houses and our ways[SEP]of amusing ourselves.[SEP]But we can be happier than anybody.[SEP]We can go mad[SEP]with joy, as the people do out in the fields on a fine Whitsunday.[SEP]We[SEP]make the most of our moment.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She thrust her little chin out defiantly over her gray fur collar, and[SEP]Bartley looked down at her and laughed.[EOT]
[SOT]“You are a plucky one, you.[SEP]” He patted her glove with his hand.[SEP]“Yes,[SEP]you are a plucky one.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda sighed.[SEP]“No, I’m not.[SEP]Not about some things, at any rate.[SEP]It[SEP]doesn’t take pluck to fight for one’s moment, but it takes pluck to go[SEP]without—a lot.[SEP]More than I have.[SEP]I can’t help it,” she added fiercely.[EOT]
[SOT]After miles of outlying streets and little gloomy houses, they reached[SEP]London itself, red and roaring and murky, with a thick dampness coming[SEP]up from the river, that betokened fog again to-morrow.[SEP]The streets were[SEP]full of people who had worked indoors all through the priceless day and[SEP]had now come hungrily out to drink the muddy lees of it.[SEP]They stood in[SEP]long black lines, waiting before the pit entrances of the[SEP]theatres—short-coated boys, and girls in sailor hats, all shivering and[SEP]chatting gayly.[SEP]There was a blurred rhythm in all the dull city[SEP]noises—in the clatter of the cab horses and the rumbling of the busses,[SEP]in the street calls, and in the undulating tramp, tramp of the crowd.[SEP]It was like the deep vibration of some vast underground machinery, and[SEP]like the muffled pulsations of millions of human hearts.[EOT]
[SOT][See “The Barrel Organ by Alfred Noyes.[SEP]Ed.] [I have placed it at the[SEP]end for your convenience][EOT]
[SOT]“Seems good to get back, doesn’t it?[SEP]” Bartley whispered, as they drove[SEP]from Bayswater Road into Oxford Street.[SEP]“London always makes me want to[SEP]live more than any other city in the world.[SEP]You remember our priestess[SEP]mummy over in the mummy-room, and how we used to long to go and bring[SEP]her out on nights like this?[SEP]Three thousand years![SEP]Ugh![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“All the same, I believe she used to feel it when we stood there and[SEP]watched her and wished her well.[SEP]I believe she used to remember,” Hilda[SEP]said thoughtfully.[EOT]
[SOT]“I hope so.[SEP]Now let’s go to some awfully jolly place for dinner before[SEP]we go home.[SEP]I could eat all the dinners there are in London to-night.[SEP]Where shall I tell the driver?[SEP]The Piccadilly Restaurant?[SEP]The music’s[SEP]good there.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“There are too many people there whom one knows.[SEP]Why not that little[SEP]French place in Soho, where we went so often when you were here in the[SEP]summer?[SEP]I love it, and I’ve never been there with any one but you.[SEP]Sometimes I go by myself, when I am particularly lonely.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Very well, the sole’s good there.[SEP]How many street pianos there are[SEP]about to-night![SEP]The fine weather must have thawed them out.[SEP]We’ve had[SEP]five miles of ‘Il Trovatore’ now.[SEP]They always make me feel jaunty.[SEP]Are[SEP]you comfy, and not too tired?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m not tired at all.[SEP]I was just wondering how people can ever die.[SEP]Why did you remind me of the mummy?[SEP]Life seems the strongest and most[SEP]indestructible thing in the world.[SEP]Do you really believe that all those[SEP]people rushing about down there, going to good dinners and clubs and[SEP]theatres, will be dead some day, and not care about anything?[SEP]I don’t[SEP]believe it, and I know I shan’t die, ever![SEP]You see, I feel too—too[SEP]powerful![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The carriage stopped.[SEP]Bartley sprang out and swung her quickly to the[SEP]pavement.[SEP]As he lifted her in his two hands he whispered: “You[SEP]are—powerful![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER VIII[EOT]
[SOT]The last rehearsal was over, a tedious dress rehearsal which had lasted[SEP]all day and exhausted the patience of every one who had to do with it.[SEP]When Hilda had dressed for the street and came out of her[SEP]dressing-room, she found Hugh MacConnell waiting for her in the[SEP]corridor.[EOT]
[SOT]“The fog’s thicker than ever, Hilda.[SEP]There have been a great many[SEP]accidents to-day.[SEP]It’s positively unsafe for you to be out alone.[SEP]Will[SEP]you let me take you home?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“How good of you, Mac.[SEP]If you are going with me, I think I’d rather[SEP]walk.[SEP]I’ve had no exercise to-day, and all this has made me nervous.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I shouldn’t wonder,” said MacConnell dryly.[SEP]Hilda pulled down her veil[SEP]and they stepped out into the thick brown wash that submerged St.[SEP]Martin’s Lane.[SEP]MacConnell took her hand and tucked it snugly under his[SEP]arm.[SEP]“I’m sorry I was such a savage.[SEP]I hope you didn’t think I made an[SEP]ass of myself.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Not a bit of it.[SEP]I don’t wonder you were peppery.[SEP]Those things are[SEP]awfully trying.[SEP]How do you think it’s going?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Magnificently.[SEP]That’s why I got so stirred up.[SEP]We are going to hear[SEP]from this, both of us.[SEP]And that reminds me; I’ve got news for you.[SEP]They[SEP]are going to begin repairs on the theatre about the middle of March,[SEP]and we are to run over to New York for six weeks.[SEP]Bennett told me[SEP]yesterday that it was decided.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda looked up delightedly at the tall gray figure beside her.[SEP]He was[SEP]the only thing she could see, for they were moving through a dense[SEP]opaqueness, as if they were walking at the bottom of the ocean.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, Mac, how glad I am![SEP]And they love your things over there, don’t[SEP]they?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Shall you be glad for—any other reason, Hilda?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]MacConnell put his hand in front of her to ward off some dark object.[SEP]It proved to be only a lamp-post, and they beat in farther from the[SEP]edge of the pavement.[EOT]
[SOT]“What do you mean, Mac?[SEP]” Hilda asked nervously.[EOT]
[SOT]“I was just thinking there might be people over there you’d be glad to[SEP]see,” he brought out awkwardly.[SEP]Hilda said nothing, and as they walked[SEP]on MacConnell spoke again, apologetically: “I hope you don’t mind my[SEP]knowing about it, Hilda.[SEP]Don’t stiffen up like that.[SEP]No one else knows,[SEP]and I didn’t try to find out anything.[SEP]I felt it, even before I knew[SEP]who he was.[SEP]I knew there was somebody, and that it wasn’t I.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]They crossed Oxford Street in silence, feeling their way.[SEP]The busses[SEP]had stopped running and the cab-drivers were leading their horses.[SEP]When[SEP]they reached the other side, MacConnell said suddenly, “I hope you are[SEP]happy.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Terribly, dangerously happy, Mac,”—Hilda spoke quietly, pressing the[SEP]rough sleeve of his greatcoat with her gloved hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’ve always thought me too old for you, Hilda,—oh, of course you’ve[SEP]never said just that,—and here this fellow is not more than eight years[SEP]younger than I.[SEP]I’ve always felt that if I could get out of my old case[SEP]I might win you yet.[SEP]It’s a fine, brave youth I carry inside me, only[SEP]he’ll never be seen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Nonsense, Mac.[SEP]That has nothing to do with it.[SEP]It’s because you seem[SEP]too close to me, too much my own kind.[SEP]It would be like marrying Cousin[SEP]Mike, almost.[SEP]I really tried to care as you wanted me to, away back in[SEP]the beginning.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, here we are, turning out of the Square.[SEP]You are not angry with[SEP]me, Hilda?[SEP]Thank you for this walk, my dear.[SEP]Go in and get dry things[SEP]on at once.[SEP]You’ll be having a great night to-morrow.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She put out her hand.[SEP]“Thank you, Mac, for everything.[SEP]Good-night.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]MacConnell trudged off through the fog, and she went slowly upstairs.[SEP]Her slippers and dressing gown were waiting for her before the fire.[SEP]“I[SEP]shall certainly see him in New York.[SEP]He will see by the papers that we[SEP]are coming.[SEP]Perhaps he knows it already,” Hilda kept thinking as she[SEP]undressed.[SEP]“Perhaps he will be at the dock.[SEP]No, scarcely that; but I[SEP]may meet him in the street even before he comes to see me.[SEP]” Marie[SEP]placed the tea-table by the fire and brought Hilda her letters.[SEP]She[SEP]looked them over, and started as she came to one in a handwriting that[SEP]she did not often see; Alexander had written to her only twice before,[SEP]and he did not allow her to write to him at all.[SEP]“Thank you, Marie.[SEP]You[SEP]may go now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda sat down by the table with the letter in her hand, still[SEP]unopened.[SEP]She looked at it intently, turned it over, and felt its[SEP]thickness with her fingers.[SEP]She believed that she sometimes had a kind[SEP]of second-sight about letters, and could tell before she read them[SEP]whether they brought good or evil tidings.[SEP]She put this one down on the[SEP]table in front of her while she poured her tea.[SEP]At last, with a little[SEP]shiver of expectancy, she tore open the envelope and read:—[EOT]
[SOT]BOSTON, February —[EOT]
[SOT]MY DEAR HILDA:—[EOT]
[SOT]It is after twelve o’clock.[SEP]Every one else is in bed and I am sitting[SEP]alone in my study.[SEP]I have been happier in this room than anywhere else[SEP]in the world.[SEP]Happiness like that makes one insolent.[SEP]I used to think[SEP]these four walls could stand against anything.[SEP]And now I scarcely know[SEP]myself here.[SEP]Now I know that no one can build his security upon the[SEP]nobleness of another person.[SEP]Two people, when they love each other,[SEP]grow alike in their tastes and habits and pride, but their moral[SEP]natures (whatever we may mean by that canting expression) are never[SEP]welded.[SEP]The base one goes on being base, and the noble one noble, to[SEP]the end.[EOT]
[SOT]The last week has been a bad one; I have been realizing how things used[SEP]to be with me.[SEP]Sometimes I get used to being dead inside, but lately it[SEP]has been as if a window beside me had suddenly opened, and as if all[SEP]the smells of spring blew in to me.[SEP]There is a garden out there, with[SEP]stars overhead, where I used to walk at night when I had a single[SEP]purpose and a single heart.[SEP]I can remember how I used to feel there,[SEP]how beautiful everything about me was, and what life and power and[SEP]freedom I felt in myself.[SEP]When the window opens I know exactly how it[SEP]would feel to be out there.[SEP]But that garden is closed to me.[SEP]How is it,[SEP]I ask myself, that everything can be so different with me when nothing[SEP]here has changed?[SEP]I am in my own house, in my own study, in the midst[SEP]of all these quiet streets where my friends live.[SEP]They are all safe and[SEP]at peace with themselves.[SEP]But I am never at peace.[SEP]I feel always on the[SEP]edge of danger and change.[EOT]
[SOT]I keep remembering locoed horses I used to see on the range when I was[SEP]a boy.[SEP]They changed like that.[SEP]We used to catch them and put them up in[SEP]the corral, and they developed great cunning.[SEP]They would pretend to eat[SEP]their oats like the other horses, but we knew they were always scheming[SEP]to get back at the loco.[EOT]
[SOT]It seems that a man is meant to live only one life in this world.[SEP]When[SEP]he tries to live a second, he develops another nature.[SEP]I feel as if a[SEP]second man had been grafted into me.[SEP]At first he seemed only a[SEP]pleasure-loving simpleton, of whose company I was rather ashamed, and[SEP]whom I used to hide under my coat when I walked the Embankment, in[SEP]London.[SEP]But now he is strong and sullen, and he is fighting for his[SEP]life at the cost of mine.[SEP]That is his one activity: to grow strong.[SEP]No[SEP]creature ever wanted so much to live.[SEP]Eventually, I suppose, he will[SEP]absorb me altogether.[SEP]Believe me, you will hate me then.[EOT]
[SOT]And what have you to do, Hilda, with this ugly story?[SEP]Nothing at all.[SEP]The little boy drank of the prettiest brook in the forest and he became[SEP]a stag.[SEP]I write all this because I can never tell it to you, and[SEP]because it seems as if I could not keep silent any longer.[SEP]And because[SEP]I suffer, Hilda.[SEP]If any one I loved suffered like this, I’d want to[SEP]know it.[SEP]Help me, Hilda![EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER IX[EOT]
[SOT]On the last Saturday in April, the New York “Times” published an[SEP]account of the strike complications which were delaying Alexander’s New[SEP]Jersey bridge, and stated that the engineer himself was in town and at[SEP]his office on West Tenth Street.[EOT]
[SOT]On Sunday, the day after this notice appeared, Alexander worked all day[SEP]at his Tenth Street rooms.[SEP]His business often called him to New York,[SEP]and he had kept an apartment there for years, subletting it when he[SEP]went abroad for any length of time.[SEP]Besides his sleeping-room and bath,[SEP]there was a large room, formerly a painter’s studio, which he used as a[SEP]study and office.[SEP]It was furnished with the cast-off possessions of his[SEP]bachelor days and with odd things which he sheltered for friends of his[SEP]who followed itinerant and more or less artistic callings.[SEP]Over the[SEP]fireplace there was a large old-fashioned gilt mirror.[SEP]Alexander’s big[SEP]work-table stood in front of one of the three windows, and above the[SEP]couch hung the one picture in the room, a big canvas of charming color[SEP]and spirit, a study of the Luxembourg Gardens in early spring, painted[SEP]in his youth by a man who had since become a portrait-painter of[SEP]international renown.[SEP]He had done it for Alexander when they were[SEP]students together in Paris.[EOT]
[SOT]Sunday was a cold, raw day and a fine rain fell continuously.[SEP]When[SEP]Alexander came back from dinner he put more wood on his fire, made[SEP]himself comfortable, and settled down at his desk, where he began[SEP]checking over estimate sheets.[SEP]It was after nine o’clock and he was[SEP]lighting a second pipe, when he thought he heard a sound at his door.[SEP]He started and listened, holding the burning match in his hand; again[SEP]he heard the same sound, like a firm, light tap.[SEP]He rose and crossed[SEP]the room quickly.[SEP]When he threw open the door he recognized the figure[SEP]that shrank back into the bare, dimly lit hallway.[SEP]He stood for a[SEP]moment in awkward constraint, his pipe in his hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“Come in,” he said to Hilda at last, and closed the door behind her.[SEP]He[SEP]pointed to a chair by the fire and went back to his worktable.[SEP]“Won’t[SEP]you sit down?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He was standing behind the table, turning over a pile of blueprints[SEP]nervously.[SEP]The yellow light from the student’s lamp fell on his hands[SEP]and the purple sleeves of his velvet smoking-jacket, but his flushed[SEP]face and big, hard head were in the shadow.[SEP]There was something about[SEP]him that made Hilda wish herself at her hotel again, in the street[SEP]below, anywhere but where she was.[EOT]
[SOT]“Of course I know, Bartley,” she said at last, “that after this you[SEP]won’t owe me the least consideration.[SEP]But we sail on Tuesday.[SEP]I saw[SEP]that interview in the paper yesterday, telling where you were, and I[SEP]thought I had to see you.[SEP]That’s all.[SEP]Good-night; I’m going now.[SEP]” She[SEP]turned and her hand closed on the door-knob.[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander hurried toward her and took her gently by the arm.[SEP]“Sit down,[SEP]Hilda; you’re wet through.[SEP]Let me take off your coat—and your boots;[SEP]they’re oozing water.[SEP]” He knelt down and began to unlace her shoes,[SEP]while Hilda shrank into the chair.[SEP]“Here, put your feet on this stool.[SEP]You don’t mean to say you walked down—and without overshoes![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda hid her face in her hands.[SEP]“I was afraid to take a cab.[SEP]Can’t you[SEP]see, Bartley, that I’m terribly frightened?[SEP]I’ve been through this a[SEP]hundred times to-day.[SEP]Don’t be any more angry than you can help.[SEP]I was[SEP]all right until I knew you were in town.[SEP]If you’d sent me a note, or[SEP]telephoned me, or anything![SEP]But you won’t let me write to you, and I[SEP]had to see you after that letter, that terrible letter you wrote me[SEP]when you got home.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander faced her, resting his arm on the mantel behind him, and[SEP]began to brush the sleeve of his jacket.[SEP]“Is this the way you mean to[SEP]answer it, Hilda?[SEP]” he asked unsteadily.[EOT]

[SOT]Alexander sat down on the arm of her chair.[SEP]“Did you think I had[SEP]forgotten you were in town, Hilda?[SEP]Do you think I kept away by[SEP]accident?[SEP]Did you suppose I didn’t know you were sailing on Tuesday?[SEP]There is a letter for you there, in my desk drawer.[SEP]It was to have[SEP]reached you on the steamer.[SEP]I was all the morning writing it.[SEP]I told[SEP]myself that if I were really thinking of you, and not of myself, a[SEP]letter would be better than nothing.[SEP]Marks on paper mean something to[SEP]you.[SEP]” He paused.[SEP]“They never did to me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda smiled up at him beautifully and put her hand on his sleeve.[SEP]“Oh,[SEP]Bartley![SEP]Did you write to me?[SEP]Why didn’t you telephone me to let me[SEP]know that you had?[SEP]Then I wouldn’t have come.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander slipped his arm about her.[SEP]“I didn’t know it before, Hilda,[SEP]on my honor I didn’t, but I believe it was because, deep down in me[SEP]somewhere, I was hoping I might drive you to do just this.[SEP]I’ve watched[SEP]that door all day.[SEP]I’ve jumped up if the fire crackled.[SEP]I think I have[SEP]felt that you were coming.[SEP]” He bent his face over her hair.[EOT]

[SOT]“No, you weren’t mistaken.[SEP]I’ve been up in Canada with my bridge, and I[SEP]arranged not to come to New York until after you had gone.[SEP]Then, when[SEP]your manager added two more weeks, I was already committed.[SEP]” He dropped[SEP]upon the stool in front of her and sat with his hands hanging between[SEP]his knees.[SEP]“What am I to do, Hilda?[SEP]”[EOT]

[SOT]“Oh, it doesn’t matter much![SEP]One of them.[SEP]Only not Mac.[SEP]I’m too fond of[SEP]him.[SEP]”[EOT]
