[SOT]“Yes, I know very well.[SEP]I’ve thought about it a great deal, and I’ve[SEP]quite decided.[SEP]I never used to understand how women did things like[SEP]that, but I know now.[SEP]It’s because they can’t be at the mercy of the[SEP]man they love any longer.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander flushed angrily.[SEP]“So it’s better to be at the mercy of a man[SEP]you don’t love?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Under such circumstances, infinitely![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]There was a flash in her eyes that made Alexander’s fall.[SEP]He got up and[SEP]went over to the window, threw it open, and leaned out.[SEP]He heard Hilda[SEP]moving about behind him.[SEP]When he looked over his shoulder she was[SEP]lacing her boots.[SEP]He went back and stood over her.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hilda you’d better think a while longer before you do that.[SEP]I don’t[SEP]know what I ought to say, but I don’t believe you’d be happy; truly I[SEP]don’t.[SEP]Aren’t you trying to frighten me?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She tied the knot of the last lacing and put her boot-heel down firmly.[SEP]“No; I’m telling you what I’ve made up my mind to do.[SEP]I suppose I would[SEP]better do it without telling you.[SEP]But afterward I shan’t have an[SEP]opportunity to explain, for I shan’t be seeing you again.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander started to speak, but caught himself.[SEP]When Hilda rose he sat[SEP]down on the arm of her chair and drew her back into it.[EOT]
[SOT]“I wouldn’t be so much alarmed if I didn’t know how utterly reckless[SEP]you _can_ be.[SEP]Don’t do anything like that rashly.[SEP]” His face grew[SEP]troubled.[SEP]“You wouldn’t be happy.[SEP]You are not that kind of woman.[SEP]I’d[SEP]never have another hour’s peace if I helped to make you do a thing like[SEP]that.[SEP]” He took her face between his hands and looked down into it.[SEP]“You[SEP]see, you are different, Hilda.[SEP]Don’t you know you are?[SEP]” His voice grew[SEP]softer, his touch more and more tender.[SEP]“Some women can do that sort of[SEP]thing, but you—you can love as queens did, in the old time.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda had heard that soft, deep tone in his voice only once before.[SEP]She[SEP]closed her eyes; her lips and eyelids trembled.[SEP]“Only one, Bartley.[SEP]Only one.[SEP]And he threw it back at me a second time.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She felt the strength leap in the arms that held her so lightly.[EOT]
[SOT]“Try him again, Hilda.[SEP]Try him once again.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She looked up into his eyes, and hid her face in her hands.[EOT]
[SOT]On Tuesday afternoon a Boston lawyer, who had been trying a case in[SEP]Vermont, was standing on the siding at White River Junction when the[SEP]Canadian Express pulled by on its northward journey.[SEP]As the day-coaches[SEP]at the rear end of the long train swept by him, the lawyer noticed at[SEP]one of the windows a man’s head, with thick rumpled hair.[SEP]“Curious,” he[SEP]thought; “that looked like Alexander, but what would he be doing back[SEP]there in the daycoaches?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was, indeed, Alexander.[EOT]
[SOT]That morning a telegram from Moorlock had reached him, telling him that[SEP]there was serious trouble with the bridge and that he was needed there[SEP]at once, so he had caught the first train out of New York.[SEP]He had taken[SEP]a seat in a day-coach to avoid the risk of meeting any one he knew, and[SEP]because he did not wish to be comfortable.[SEP]When the telegram arrived,[SEP]Alexander was at his rooms on Tenth Street, packing his bag to go to[SEP]Boston.[SEP]On Monday night he had written a long letter to his wife, but[SEP]when morning came he was afraid to send it, and the letter was still in[SEP]his pocket.[SEP]Winifred was not a woman who could bear disappointment.[SEP]She[SEP]demanded a great deal of herself and of the people she loved; and she[SEP]never failed herself.[SEP]If he told her now, he knew, it would be[SEP]irretrievable.[SEP]There would be no going back.[SEP]He would lose the thing he[SEP]valued most in the world; he would be destroying himself and his own[SEP]happiness.[SEP]There would be nothing for him afterward.[SEP]He seemed to see[SEP]himself dragging out a restless existence on the Continent—Cannes,[SEP]Hyères, Algiers, Cairo—among smartly dressed, disabled men of every[SEP]nationality; forever going on journeys that led nowhere; hurrying to[SEP]catch trains that he might just as well miss; getting up in the morning[SEP]with a great bustle and splashing of water, to begin a day that had no[SEP]purpose and no meaning; dining late to shorten the night, sleeping late[SEP]to shorten the day.[EOT]
[SOT]And for what?[SEP]For a mere folly, a masquerade, a little thing that he[SEP]could not let go.[SEP]_And he could even let it go_, he told himself.[SEP]But[SEP]he had promised to be in London at mid-summer, and he knew that he[SEP]would go.[SEP].[SEP].[SEP].[SEP]It was impossible to live like this any longer.[EOT]
[SOT]And this, then, was to be the disaster that his old professor had[SEP]foreseen for him: the crack in the wall, the crash, the cloud of dust.[SEP]And he could not understand how it had come about.[SEP]He felt that he[SEP]himself was unchanged, that he was still there, the same man he had[SEP]been five years ago, and that he was sitting stupidly by and letting[SEP]some resolute offshoot of himself spoil his life for him.[SEP]This new[SEP]force was not he, it was but a part of him.[SEP]He would not even admit[SEP]that it was stronger than he; but it was more active.[SEP]It was by its[SEP]energy that this new feeling got the better of him.[SEP]His wife was the[SEP]woman who had made his life, gratified his pride, given direction to[SEP]his tastes and habits.[SEP]The life they led together seemed to him[SEP]beautiful.[SEP]Winifred still was, as she had always been, Romance for him,[SEP]and whenever he was deeply stirred he turned to her.[SEP]When the grandeur[SEP]and beauty of the world challenged him—as it challenges even the most[SEP]self-absorbed people—he always answered with her name.[SEP]That was his[SEP]reply to the question put by the mountains and the stars; to all the[SEP]spiritual aspects of life.[SEP]In his feeling for his wife there was all[SEP]the tenderness, all the pride, all the devotion of which he was[SEP]capable.[SEP]There was everything but energy; the energy of youth which[SEP]must register itself and cut its name before it passes.[SEP]This new[SEP]feeling was so fresh, so unsatisfied and light of foot.[SEP]It ran and was[SEP]not wearied, anticipated him everywhere.[SEP]It put a girdle round the[SEP]earth while he was going from New York to Moorlock.[SEP]At this moment, it[SEP]was tingling through him, exultant, and live as quicksilver,[SEP]whispering, “In July you will be in England.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Already he dreaded the long, empty days at sea, the monotonous Irish[SEP]coast, the sluggish passage up the Mersey, the flash of the boat train[SEP]through the summer country.[SEP]He closed his eyes and gave himself up to[SEP]the feeling of rapid motion and to swift, terrifying thoughts.[SEP]He was[SEP]sitting so, his face shaded by his hand, when the Boston lawyer saw him[SEP]from the siding at White River Junction.[EOT]
[SOT]When at last Alexander roused himself, the afternoon had waned to[SEP]sunset.[SEP]The train was passing through a gray country and the sky[SEP]overhead was flushed with a wide flood of clear color.[SEP]There was a[SEP]rose-colored light over the gray rocks and hills and meadows.[SEP]Off to[SEP]the left, under the approach of a weather-stained wooden bridge, a[SEP]group of boys were sitting around a little fire.[SEP]The smell of the wood[SEP]smoke blew in at the window.[SEP]Except for an old farmer, jogging along[SEP]the highroad in his box-wagon, there was not another living creature to[SEP]be seen.[SEP]Alexander looked back wistfully at the boys, camped on the[SEP]edge of a little marsh, crouching under their shelter and looking[SEP]gravely at their fire.[SEP]They took his mind back a long way, to a[SEP]campfire on a sandbar in a Western river, and he wished he could go[SEP]back and sit down with them.[SEP]He could remember exactly how the world[SEP]had looked then.[EOT]
[SOT]It was quite dark and Alexander was still thinking of the boys, when it[SEP]occurred to him that the train must be nearing Allway.[SEP]In going to his[SEP]new bridge at Moorlock he had always to pass through Allway.[SEP]The train[SEP]stopped at Allway Mills, then wound two miles up the river, and then[SEP]the hollow sound under his feet told Bartley that he was on his first[SEP]bridge again.[SEP]The bridge seemed longer than it had ever seemed before,[SEP]and he was glad when he felt the beat of the wheels on the solid[SEP]roadbed again.[SEP]He did not like coming and going across that bridge, or[SEP]remembering the man who built it.[SEP]And was he, indeed, the same man who[SEP]used to walk that bridge at night, promising such things to himself and[SEP]to the stars?[SEP]And yet, he could remember it all so well: the quiet[SEP]hills sleeping in the moonlight, the slender skeleton of the bridge[SEP]reaching out into the river, and up yonder, alone on the hill, the big[SEP]white house; upstairs, in Winifred’s window, the light that told him[SEP]she was still awake and still thinking of him.[SEP]And after the light went[SEP]out he walked alone, taking the heavens into his confidence, unable to[SEP]tear himself away from the white magic of the night, unwilling to sleep[SEP]because longing was so sweet to him, and because, for the first time[SEP]since first the hills were hung with moonlight, there was a lover in[SEP]the world.[SEP]And always there was the sound of the rushing water[SEP]underneath, the sound which, more than anything else, meant death; the[SEP]wearing away of things under the impact of physical forces which men[SEP]could direct but never circumvent or diminish.[SEP]Then, in the exaltation[SEP]of love, more than ever it seemed to him to mean death, the only other[SEP]thing as strong as love.[SEP]Under the moon, under the cold, splendid[SEP]stars, there were only those two things awake and sleepless; death and[SEP]love, the rushing river and his burning heart.[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander sat up and looked about him.[SEP]The train was tearing on through[SEP]the darkness.[SEP]All his companions in the day-coach were either dozing or[SEP]sleeping heavily, and the murky lamps were turned low.[SEP]How came he here[SEP]among all these dirty people?[SEP]Why was he going to London?[SEP]What did it[SEP]mean—what was the answer?[SEP]How could this happen to a man who had lived[SEP]through that magical spring and summer, and who had felt that the stars[SEP]themselves were but flaming particles in the far-away infinitudes of[SEP]his love?[EOT]
[SOT]What had he done to lose it?[SEP]How could he endure the baseness of life[SEP]without it?[SEP]And with every revolution of the wheels beneath him, the[SEP]unquiet quicksilver in his breast told him that at midsummer he would[SEP]be in London.[SEP]He remembered his last night there: the red foggy[SEP]darkness, the hungry crowds before the theatres, the hand-organs, the[SEP]feverish rhythm of the blurred, crowded streets, and the feeling of[SEP]letting himself go with the crowd.[SEP]He shuddered and looked about him at[SEP]the poor unconscious companions of his journey, unkempt and[SEP]travel-stained, now doubled in unlovely attitudes, who had come to[SEP]stand to him for the ugliness he had brought into the world.[EOT]
[SOT]And those boys back there, beginning it all just as he had begun it; he[SEP]wished he could promise them better luck.[SEP]Ah, if one could promise any[SEP]one better luck, if one could assure a single human being of happiness![SEP]He had thought he could do so, once; and it was thinking of that that[SEP]he at last fell asleep.[SEP]In his sleep, as if it had nothing fresher to[SEP]work upon, his mind went back and tortured itself with something years[SEP]and years away, an old, long-forgotten sorrow of his childhood.[EOT]
[SOT]When Alexander awoke in the morning, the sun was just rising through[SEP]pale golden ripples of cloud, and the fresh yellow light was vibrating[SEP]through the pine woods.[SEP]The white birches, with their little unfolding[SEP]leaves, gleamed in the lowlands, and the marsh meadows were already[SEP]coming to life with their first green, a thin, bright color which had[SEP]run over them like fire.[SEP]As the train rushed along the trestles,[SEP]thousands of wild birds rose screaming into the light.[SEP]The sky was[SEP]already a pale blue and of the clearness of crystal.[SEP]Bartley caught up[SEP]his bag and hurried through the Pullman coaches until he found the[SEP]conductor.[SEP]There was a stateroom unoccupied, and he took it and set[SEP]about changing his clothes.[SEP]Last night he would not have believed that[SEP]anything could be so pleasant as the cold water he dashed over his head[SEP]and shoulders and the freshness of clean linen on his body.[EOT]
[SOT]After he had dressed, Alexander sat down at the window and drew into[SEP]his lungs deep breaths of the pine-scented air.[SEP]He had awakened with[SEP]all his old sense of power.[SEP]He could not believe that things were as[SEP]bad with him as they had seemed last night, that there was no way to[SEP]set them entirely right.[SEP]Even if he went to London at midsummer, what[SEP]would that mean except that he was a fool?[SEP]And he had been a fool[SEP]before.[SEP]That was not the reality of his life.[SEP]Yet he knew that he would[SEP]go to London.[EOT]
[SOT]Half an hour later the train stopped at Moorlock.[SEP]Alexander sprang to[SEP]the platform and hurried up the siding, waving to Philip Horton, one of[SEP]his assistants, who was anxiously looking up at the windows of the[SEP]coaches.[SEP]Bartley took his arm and they went together into the station[SEP]buffet.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll have my coffee first, Philip.[SEP]Have you had yours?[SEP]And now, what[SEP]seems to be the matter up here?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The young man, in a hurried, nervous way, began his explanation.[EOT]
[SOT]But Alexander cut him short.[SEP]“When did you stop work?[SEP]” he asked[SEP]sharply.[EOT]
[SOT]The young engineer looked confused.[SEP]“I haven’t stopped work yet, Mr.[SEP]Alexander.[SEP]I didn’t feel that I could go so far without definite[SEP]authorization from you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Then why didn’t you say in your telegram exactly what you thought, and[SEP]ask for your authorization?[SEP]You’d have got it quick enough.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, really, Mr. Alexander, I couldn’t be absolutely sure, you know,[SEP]and I didn’t like to take the responsibility of making it public.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander pushed back his chair and rose.[SEP]“Anything I do can be made[SEP]public, Phil.[SEP]You say that you believe the lower chords are showing[SEP]strain, and that even the workmen have been talking about it, and yet[SEP]you’ve gone on adding weight.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m sorry, Mr. Alexander, but I had counted on your getting here[SEP]yesterday.[SEP]My first telegram missed you somehow.[SEP]I sent one Sunday[SEP]evening, to the same address, but it was returned to me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Have you a carriage out there?[SEP]I must stop to send a wire.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander went up to the telegraph-desk and penciled the following[SEP]message to his wife:—[EOT]
[SOT]I may have to be here for some time.[SEP]Can you come up at once?[SEP]Urgent.[EOT]
[SOT]The Moorlock Bridge lay three miles above the town.[SEP]When they were[SEP]seated in the carriage, Alexander began to question his assistant[SEP]further.[SEP]If it were true that the compression members showed strain,[SEP]with the bridge only two thirds done, then there was nothing to do but[SEP]pull the whole structure down and begin over again.[SEP]Horton kept[SEP]repeating that he was sure there could be nothing wrong with the[SEP]estimates.[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander grew impatient.[SEP]“That’s all true, Phil, but we never were[SEP]justified in assuming that a scale that was perfectly safe for an[SEP]ordinary bridge would work with anything of such length.[SEP]It’s all very[SEP]well on paper, but it remains to be seen whether it can be done in[SEP]practice.[SEP]I should have thrown up the job when they crowded me.[SEP]It’s[SEP]all nonsense to try to do what other engineers are doing when you know[SEP]they’re not sound.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But just now, when there is such competition,” the younger man[SEP]demurred.[SEP]“And certainly that’s the new line of development.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander shrugged his shoulders and made no reply.[EOT]
[SOT]When they reached the bridge works, Alexander began his examination[SEP]immediately.[SEP]An hour later he sent for the superintendent.[SEP]“I think you[SEP]had better stop work out there at once, Dan.[SEP]I should say that the[SEP]lower chord here might buckle at any moment.[SEP]I told the Commission that[SEP]we were using higher unit stresses than any practice has established,[SEP]and we’ve put the dead load at a low estimate.[SEP]Theoretically it worked[SEP]out well enough, but it had never actually been tried.[SEP]” Alexander put[SEP]on his overcoat and took the superintendent by the arm.[SEP]“Don’t look so[SEP]chopfallen, Dan.[SEP]It’s a jolt, but we’ve got to face it.[SEP]It isn’t the[SEP]end of the world, you know.[SEP]Now we’ll go out and call the men off[SEP]quietly.[SEP]They’re already nervous, Horton tells me, and there’s no use[SEP]alarming them.[SEP]I’ll go with you, and we’ll send the end riveters in[SEP]first.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Alexander and the superintendent picked their way out slowly over the[SEP]long span.[SEP]They went deliberately, stopping to see what each gang was[SEP]doing, as if they were on an ordinary round of inspection.[SEP]When they[SEP]reached the end of the river span, Alexander nodded to the[SEP]superintendent, who quietly gave an order to the foreman.[SEP]The men in[SEP]the end gang picked up their tools and, glancing curiously at each[SEP]other, started back across the bridge toward the river-bank.[SEP]Alexander[SEP]himself remained standing where they had been working, looking about[SEP]him.[SEP]It was hard to believe, as he looked back over it, that the whole[SEP]great span was incurably disabled, was already as good as condemned,[SEP]because something was out of line in the lower chord of the cantilever[SEP]arm.[EOT]
[SOT]The end riveters had reached the bank and were dispersing among the[SEP]tool-houses, and the second gang had picked up their tools and were[SEP]starting toward the shore.[SEP]Alexander, still standing at the end of the[SEP]river span, saw the lower chord of the cantilever arm give a little,[SEP]like an elbow bending.[SEP]He shouted and ran after the second gang, but by[SEP]this time every one knew that the big river span was slowly settling.[SEP]There was a burst of shouting that was immediately drowned by the[SEP]scream and cracking of tearing iron, as all the tension work began to[SEP]pull asunder.[SEP]Once the chords began to buckle, there were thousands of[SEP]tons of ironwork, all riveted together and lying in midair without[SEP]support.[SEP]It tore itself to pieces with roaring and grinding and noises[SEP]that were like the shrieks of a steam whistle.[SEP]There was no shock of[SEP]any kind; the bridge had no impetus except from its own weight.[SEP]It[SEP]lurched neither to right nor left, but sank almost in a vertical line,[SEP]snapping and breaking and tearing as it went, because no integral part[SEP]could bear for an instant the enormous strain loosed upon it.[SEP]Some of[SEP]the men jumped and some ran, trying to make the shore.[EOT]
[SOT]At the first shriek of the tearing iron, Alexander jumped from the[SEP]downstream side of the bridge.[SEP]He struck the water without injury and[SEP]disappeared.[SEP]He was under the river a long time and had great[SEP]difficulty in holding his breath.[SEP]When it seemed impossible, and his[SEP]chest was about to heave, he thought he heard his wife telling him that[SEP]he could hold out a little longer.[SEP]An instant later his face cleared[SEP]the water.[SEP]For a moment, in the depths of the river, he had realized[SEP]what it would mean to die a hypocrite, and to lie dead under the last[SEP]abandonment of her tenderness.[SEP]But once in the light and air, he knew[SEP]he should live to tell her and to recover all he had lost.[SEP]Now, at[SEP]last, he felt sure of himself.[SEP]He was not startled.[SEP]It seemed to him[SEP]that he had been through something of this sort before.[SEP]There was[SEP]nothing horrible about it.[SEP]This, too, was life, and life was activity,[SEP]just as it was in Boston or in London.[SEP]He was himself, and there was[SEP]something to be done; everything seemed perfectly natural.[SEP]Alexander[SEP]was a strong swimmer, but he had gone scarcely a dozen strokes when the[SEP]bridge itself, which had been settling faster and faster, crashed into[SEP]the water behind him.[SEP]Immediately the river was full of drowning men.[SEP]A[SEP]gang of French Canadians fell almost on top of him.[SEP]He thought he had[SEP]cleared them, when they began coming up all around him, clutching at[SEP]him and at each other.[SEP]Some of them could swim, but they were either[SEP]hurt or crazed with fright.[SEP]Alexander tried to beat them off, but there[SEP]were too many of them.[SEP]One caught him about the neck, another gripped[SEP]him about the middle, and they went down together.[SEP]When he sank, his[SEP]wife seemed to be there in the water beside him, telling him to keep[SEP]his head, that if he could hold out the men would drown and release[SEP]him.[SEP]There was something he wanted to tell his wife, but he could not[SEP]think clearly for the roaring in his ears.[SEP]Suddenly he remembered what[SEP]it was.[SEP]He caught his breath, and then she let him go.[EOT]
[SOT]The work of recovering the dead went on all day and all the following[SEP]night.[SEP]By the next morning forty-eight bodies had been taken out of the[SEP]river, but there were still twenty missing.[SEP]Many of the men had fallen[SEP]with the bridge and were held down under the debris.[SEP]Early on the[SEP]morning of the second day a closed carriage was driven slowly along the[SEP]river-bank and stopped a little below the works, where the river boiled[SEP]and churned about the great iron carcass which lay in a straight line[SEP]two thirds across it.[SEP]The carriage stood there hour after hour, and[SEP]word soon spread among the crowds on the shore that its occupant was[SEP]the wife of the Chief Engineer; his body had not yet been found.[SEP]The[SEP]widows of the lost workmen, moving up and down the bank with shawls[SEP]over their heads, some of them carrying babies, looked at the rusty[SEP]hired hack many times that morning.[SEP]They drew near it and walked about[SEP]it, but none of them ventured to peer within.[SEP]Even half-indifferent[SEP]sightseers dropped their voices as they told a newcomer: “You see that[SEP]carriage over there?[SEP]That’s Mrs.[SEP]Alexander.[SEP]They haven’t found him yet.[SEP]She got off the train this morning.[SEP]Horton met her.[SEP]She heard it in[SEP]Boston yesterday—heard the newsboys crying it in the street.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]At noon Philip Horton made his way through the crowd with a tray and a[SEP]tin coffee-pot from the camp kitchen.[SEP]When he reached the carriage he[SEP]found Mrs.[SEP]Alexander just as he had left her in the early morning,[SEP]leaning forward a little, with her hand on the lowered window, looking[SEP]at the river.[SEP]Hour after hour she had been watching the water, the[SEP]lonely, useless stone towers, and the convulsed mass of iron wreckage[SEP]over which the angry river continually spat up its yellow foam.[EOT]
[SOT]“Those poor women out there, do they blame him very much?[SEP]” she asked,[SEP]as she handed the coffee-cup back to Horton.[EOT]
[SOT]“Nobody blames him, Mrs.[SEP]Alexander.[SEP]If any one is to blame, I’m afraid[SEP]it’s I.[SEP]I should have stopped work before he came.[SEP]He said so as soon[SEP]as I met him.[SEP]I tried to get him here a day earlier, but my telegram[SEP]missed him, somehow.[SEP]He didn’t have time really to explain to me.[SEP]If[SEP]he’d got here Monday, he’d have had all the men off at once.[SEP]But, you[SEP]see, Mrs.[SEP]Alexander, such a thing never happened before.[SEP]According to[SEP]all human calculations, it simply couldn’t happen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Horton leaned wearily against the front wheel of the cab.[SEP]He had not[SEP]had his clothes off for thirty hours, and the stimulus of violent[SEP]excitement was beginning to wear off.[EOT]
[SOT]“Don’t be afraid to tell me the worst, Mr. Horton.[SEP]Don’t leave me to[SEP]the dread of finding out things that people may be saying.[SEP]If he is[SEP]blamed, if he needs any one to speak for him,”—for the first time her[SEP]voice broke and a flush of life, tearful, painful, and confused, swept[SEP]over her rigid pallor,—“if he needs any one, tell me, show me what to[SEP]do.[SEP]” She began to sob, and Horton hurried away.[EOT]
[SOT]When he came back at four o’clock in the afternoon he was carrying his[SEP]hat in his hand, and Winifred knew as soon as she saw him that they had[SEP]found Bartley.[SEP]She opened the carriage door before he reached her and[SEP]stepped to the ground.[EOT]
[SOT]Horton put out his hand as if to hold her back and spoke pleadingly:[SEP]“Won’t you drive up to my house, Mrs.[SEP]Alexander?[SEP]They will take him up[SEP]there.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Take me to him now, please.[SEP]I shall not make any trouble.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The group of men down under the riverbank fell back when they saw a[SEP]woman coming, and one of them threw a tarpaulin over the stretcher.[SEP]They took off their hats and caps as Winifred approached, and although[SEP]she had pulled her veil down over her face they did not look up at her.[SEP]She was taller than Horton, and some of the men thought she was the[SEP]tallest woman they had ever seen.[SEP]“As tall as himself,” some one[SEP]whispered.[SEP]Horton motioned to the men, and six of them lifted the[SEP]stretcher and began to carry it up the embankment.[SEP]Winifred followed[SEP]them the half-mile to Horton’s house.[SEP]She walked quietly, without once[SEP]breaking or stumbling.[SEP]When the bearers put the stretcher down in[SEP]Horton’s spare bedroom, she thanked them and gave her hand to each in[SEP]turn.[SEP]The men went out of the house and through the yard with their[SEP]caps in their hands.[SEP]They were too much confused to say anything as[SEP]they went down the hill.[EOT]
[SOT]Horton himself was almost as deeply perplexed.[SEP]“Mamie,” he said to his[SEP]wife, when he came out of the spare room half an hour later, “will you[SEP]take Mrs.[SEP]Alexander the things she needs?[SEP]She is going to do everything[SEP]herself.[SEP]Just stay about where you can hear her and go in if she wants[SEP]you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Everything happened as Alexander had foreseen in that moment of[SEP]prescience under the river.[SEP]With her own hands she washed him clean of[SEP]every mark of disaster.[SEP]All night he was alone with her in the still[SEP]house, his great head lying deep in the pillow.[SEP]In the pocket of his[SEP]coat Winifred found the letter that he had written her the night before[SEP]he left New York, water-soaked and illegible, but because of its[SEP]length, she knew it had been meant for her.[EOT]
[SOT]For Alexander death was an easy creditor.[SEP]Fortune, which had smiled[SEP]upon him consistently all his life, did not desert him in the end.[SEP]His[SEP]harshest critics did not doubt that, had he lived, he would have[SEP]retrieved himself.[SEP]Even Lucius Wilson did not see in this accident the[SEP]disaster he had once foretold.[EOT]
[SOT]When a great man dies in his prime there is no surgeon who can say[SEP]whether he did well; whether or not the future was his, as it seemed to[SEP]be.[SEP]The mind that society had come to regard as a powerful and reliable[SEP]machine, dedicated to its service, may for a long time have been sick[SEP]within itself and bent upon its own destruction.[EOT]
[SOT]Professor Wilson had been living in London for six years and he was[SEP]just back from a visit to America.[SEP]One afternoon, soon after his[SEP]return, he put on his frock-coat and drove in a hansom to pay a call[SEP]upon Hilda Burgoyne, who still lived at her old number, off Bedford[SEP]Square.[SEP]He and Miss Burgoyne had been fast friends for a long time.[SEP]He[SEP]had first noticed her about the corridors of the British Museum, where[SEP]he read constantly.[SEP]Her being there so often had made him feel that he[SEP]would like to know her, and as she was not an inaccessible person, an[SEP]introduction was not difficult.[SEP]The preliminaries once over, they came[SEP]to depend a great deal upon each other, and Wilson, after his day’s[SEP]reading, often went round to Bedford Square for his tea.[SEP]They had much[SEP]more in common than their memories of a common friend.[SEP]Indeed, they[SEP]seldom spoke of him.[SEP]They saved that for the deep moments which do not[SEP]come often, and then their talk of him was mostly silence.[SEP]Wilson knew[SEP]that Hilda had loved him; more than this he had not tried to know.[EOT]
[SOT]It was late when Wilson reached Hilda’s apartment on this particular[SEP]December afternoon, and he found her alone.[SEP]She sent for fresh tea and[SEP]made him comfortable, as she had such a knack of making people[SEP]comfortable.[EOT]
[SOT]“How good you were to come back before Christmas![SEP]I quite dreaded the[SEP]Holidays without you.[SEP]You’ve helped me over a good many Christmases.[SEP]”[SEP]She smiled at him gayly.[EOT]
[SOT]“As if you needed me for that![SEP]But, at any rate, I needed _you_.[SEP]How[SEP]well you are looking, my dear, and how rested.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He peered up at her from his low chair, balancing the tips of his long[SEP]fingers together in a judicial manner which had grown on him with[SEP]years.[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda laughed as she carefully poured his cream.[SEP]“That means that I was[SEP]looking very seedy at the end of the season, doesn’t it?[SEP]Well, we must[SEP]show wear at last, you know.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson took the cup gratefully.[SEP]“Ah, no need to remind a man of[SEP]seventy, who has just been home to find that he has survived all his[SEP]contemporaries.[SEP]I was most gently treated—as a sort of precious relic.[SEP]But, do you know, it made me feel awkward to be hanging about still.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Seventy?[SEP]Never mention it to me.[SEP]” Hilda looked appreciatively at the[SEP]Professor’s alert face, with so many kindly lines about the mouth and[SEP]so many quizzical ones about the eyes.[SEP]“You’ve got to hang about for[SEP]me, you know.[SEP]I can’t even let you go home again.[SEP]You must stay put,[SEP]now that I have you back.[SEP]You’re the realest thing I have.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson chuckled.[SEP]“Dear me, am I?[SEP]Out of so many conquests and the[SEP]spoils of conquered cities![SEP]You’ve really missed me?[SEP]Well, then, I[SEP]shall hang.[SEP]Even if you have at last to put ME in the mummy-room with[SEP]the others.[SEP]You’ll visit me often, won’t you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Every day in the calendar.[SEP]Here, your cigarettes are in this drawer,[SEP]where you left them.[SEP]” She struck a match and lit one for him.[SEP]“But you[SEP]did, after all, enjoy being at home again?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, yes.[SEP]I found the long railway journeys trying.[SEP]People live a[SEP]thousand miles apart.[SEP]But I did it thoroughly; I was all over the[SEP]place.[SEP]It was in Boston I lingered longest.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah, you saw Mrs.[SEP]Alexander?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Often.[SEP]I dined with her, and had tea there a dozen different times, I[SEP]should think.[SEP]Indeed, it was to see her that I lingered on and on.[SEP]I[SEP]found that I still loved to go to the house.[SEP]It always seemed as if[SEP]Bartley were there, somehow, and that at any moment one might hear his[SEP]heavy tramp on the stairs.[SEP]Do you know, I kept feeling that he must be[SEP]up in his study.[SEP]” The Professor looked reflectively into the grate.[SEP]“I[SEP]should really have liked to go up there.[SEP]That was where I had my last[SEP]long talk with him.[SEP]But Mrs.[SEP]Alexander never suggested it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson was a little startled by her tone, and he turned his head so[SEP]quickly that his cuff-link caught the string of his nose-glasses and[SEP]pulled them awry.[SEP]“Why?[SEP]Why, dear me, I don’t know.[SEP]She probably never[SEP]thought of it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda bit her lip.[SEP]“I don’t know what made me say that.[SEP]I didn’t mean[SEP]to interrupt.[SEP]Go on please, and tell me how it was.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, it was like that.[SEP]Almost as if he were there.[SEP]In a way, he[SEP]really is there.[SEP]She never lets him go.[SEP]It’s the most beautiful and[SEP]dignified sorrow I’ve ever known.[SEP]It’s so beautiful that it has its[SEP]compensations, I should think.[SEP]Its very completeness is a compensation.[SEP]It gives her a fixed star to steer by.[SEP]She doesn’t drift.[SEP]We sat there[SEP]evening after evening in the quiet of that magically haunted room, and[SEP]watched the sunset burn on the river, and felt him.[SEP]Felt him with a[SEP]difference, of course.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda leaned forward, her elbow on her knee, her chin on her hand.[SEP]“With a difference?[SEP]Because of her, you mean?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson’s brow wrinkled.[SEP]“Something like that, yes.[SEP]Of course, as time[SEP]goes on, to her he becomes more and more their simple personal[SEP]relation.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda studied the droop of the Professor’s head intently.[SEP]“You didn’t[SEP]altogether like that?[SEP]You felt it wasn’t wholly fair to him?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson shook himself and readjusted his glasses.[SEP]“Oh, fair enough.[SEP]More[SEP]than fair.[SEP]Of course, I always felt that my image of him was just a[SEP]little different from hers.[SEP]No relation is so complete that it can hold[SEP]absolutely all of a person.[SEP]And I liked him just as he was; his[SEP]deviations, too; the places where he didn’t square.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda considered vaguely.[SEP]“Has she grown much older?[SEP]” she asked at[SEP]last.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, and no.[SEP]In a tragic way she is even handsomer.[SEP]But colder.[SEP]Cold[SEP]for everything but him.[SEP]‘Forget thyself to marble’; I kept thinking of[SEP]that.[SEP]Her happiness was a happiness _à deux_, not apart from the world,[SEP]but actually against it.[SEP]And now her grief is like that.[SEP]She saves[SEP]herself for it and doesn’t even go through the form of seeing people[SEP]much.[SEP]I’m sorry.[SEP]It would be better for her, and might be so good for[SEP]them, if she could let other people in.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps she’s afraid of letting him out a little, of sharing him with[SEP]somebody.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson put down his cup and looked up with vague alarm.[SEP]“Dear me, it[SEP]takes a woman to think of that, now![SEP]I don’t, you know, think we ought[SEP]to be hard on her.[SEP]More, even, than the rest of us she didn’t choose[SEP]her destiny.[SEP]She underwent it.[SEP]And it has left her chilled.[SEP]As to her[SEP]not wishing to take the world into her confidence—well, it is a pretty[SEP]brutal and stupid world, after all, you know.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hilda leaned forward.[SEP]“Yes, I know, I know.[SEP]Only I can’t help being[SEP]glad that there was something for him even in stupid and vulgar people.[SEP]My little Marie worshiped him.[SEP]When she is dusting I always know when[SEP]she has come to his picture.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Wilson nodded.[SEP]“Oh, yes![SEP]He left an echo.[SEP]The ripples go on in all of[SEP]us.[SEP]He belonged to the people who make the play, and most of us are[SEP]only onlookers at the best.[SEP]We shouldn’t wonder too much at Mrs.[SEP]Alexander.[SEP]She must feel how useless it would be to stir about, that[SEP]she may as well sit still; that nothing can happen to her after[SEP]Bartley.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes,” said Hilda softly, “nothing can happen to one after Bartley.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]They both sat looking into the fire.[EOT]
[SOT]THE BARREL ORGAN by Alfred Noyes[EOT]
[SOT]There’s a barrel-organ caroling across a golden street,[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks low;[SEP]And the music’s not immortal; but the world has made it sweet[SEP]And fulfilled it with the sunset glow;[SEP]And it pulses through the pleasures of the City and the pain[SEP]That surround the singing organ like a large eternal light;[SEP]And they’ve given it a glory and a part to play again[SEP]In the Symphony that rules the day and the night.[EOT]
[SOT]And now it’s marching onward through the realms of old romance,[SEP]And trolling out a fond familiar tune,[SEP]And now it’s roaring cannon down to fight the King of France,[SEP]And now it’s prattling softly to the moon,[SEP]And all around the organ there’s a sea without a shore[SEP]Of human joys and wonders and regrets;[SEP]To remember and to recompense the music evermore[SEP]For what the cold machinery forgets.[SEP].[SEP].[SEP].[EOT]
[SOT]Yes; as the music changes,[SEP]Like a prismatic glass,[SEP]It takes the light and ranges[SEP]Through all the moods that pass;[SEP]Dissects the common carnival[SEP]Of passions and regrets,[SEP]And gives the world a glimpse of all[SEP]The colors it forgets.[EOT]
[SOT]And there _La Traviata_ sights[SEP]Another sadder song;[SEP]And there _Il Trovatore_ cries[SEP]A tale of deeper wrong;[SEP]And bolder knights to battle go[SEP]With sword and shield and lance,[SEP]Than ever here on earth below[SEP]Have whirled into—_a dance![SEP]_—[EOT]
[SOT]Go down to Kew in lilac time; in lilac time; in lilac time;[SEP]Go down to Kew in lilac time; (it isn’t far from London![SEP])[SEP]And you shall wander hand in hand with love in summer’s wonderland;[SEP]Go down to Kew in lilac time; (it isn’t far from London![SEP])[EOT]
[SOT]The cherry-trees are seas of bloom and soft perfume and sweet perfume,[SEP]The cherry-trees are seas of bloom (and oh, so near to London![SEP])[SEP]And there they say, when dawn is high and all the world’s a blaze of[SEP]sky[SEP]The cuckoo, though he’s very shy, will sing a song for London.[EOT]
[SOT]The nightingale is rather rare and yet they say you’ll hear him there[SEP]At Kew, at Kew in lilac time (and oh, so near to London![SEP])[SEP]The linnet and the throstle, too, and after dark the long halloo[SEP]And golden-eyed _tu-whit, tu whoo_ of owls that ogle London.[EOT]
[SOT]For Noah hardly knew a bird of any kind that isn’t heard[SEP]At Kew, at Kew in lilac time (and oh, so near to London![SEP])[SEP]And when the rose begins to pout and all the chestnut spires are out[SEP]You’ll hear the rest without a doubt, all chorusing for London:—[EOT]
[SOT]_Come down to Kew in lilac time; in lilac time; in lilac time;[SEP]Come down to Kew in lilac time;_ (_it isn’t far from London![SEP]_)[SEP]_And you shall wander hand in hand with love in summer’s wonderland;[SEP]Come down to Kew in lilac time;_ (_it isn’t far from London![SEP]_)[EOT]
[SOT]And then the troubadour begins to thrill the golden street,[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks low;[SEP]And in all the gaudy busses there are scores of weary feet[SEP]Marking time, sweet time, with a dull mechanic beat,[SEP]And a thousand hearts are plunging to a love they’ll never meet,[SEP]Through the meadows of the sunset, through the poppies and the wheat,[SEP]In the land where the dead dreams go.[EOT]
[SOT]Verdi, Verdi, when you wrote _Il Trovatore_ did you dream[SEP]Of the City when the sun sinks low[SEP]Of the organ and the monkey and the many-colored stream[SEP]On the Piccadilly pavement, of the myriad eyes that seem[SEP]To be litten for a moment with a wild Italian gleam[SEP]As _A che la morte_ parodies the world’s eternal theme[SEP]And pulses with the sunset glow?[EOT]
[SOT]There’s a thief, perhaps, that listens with a face of frozen stone[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks low;[SEP]There’s a portly man of business with a balance of his own,[SEP]There’s a clerk and there’s a butcher of a soft reposeful tone,[SEP]And they’re all them returning to the heavens they have known:[SEP]They are crammed and jammed in busses and—they’re each of them alone[SEP]In the land where the dead dreams go.[EOT]
[SOT]There’s a very modish woman and her smile is very bland[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks low;[SEP]And her hansom jingles onward, but her little jeweled hand[SEP]Is clenched a little tighter and she cannot understand[SEP]What she wants or why she wanders to that undiscovered land,[SEP]For the parties there are not at all the sort of thing she planned,[SEP]In the land where the dead dreams go.[EOT]
[SOT]There’s an Oxford man that listens and his heart is crying out[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks low;[SEP]For the barge the eight, the Isis, and the coach’s whoop and shout,[SEP]For the minute gun, the counting and the long disheveled rout,[SEP]For the howl along the tow-path and a fate that’s still in doubt,[SEP]For a roughened oar to handle and a race to think about[SEP]In the land where the dead dreams go.[EOT]
[SOT]There’s a laborer that listen to the voices of the dead[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks low;[SEP]And his hand begins to tremble and his face is rather red[SEP]As he sees a loafer watching him and—there he turns his head[SEP]And stares into the sunset where his April love is fled,[SEP]For he hears her softly singing and his lonely soul is led[SEP]Through the land where the dead dreams go.[EOT]
[SOT]There’s and old and hardened demi-rep, it’s ringing in her ears,[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks low;[SEP]With the wild and empty sorrow of the love that blights and sears,[SEP]Oh, and if she hurries onward, then be sure, be sure she hears,[SEP]Hears and bears the bitter burden of the unforgotten years,[SEP]And her laugh’s a little harsher and her eyes are brimmed with tears[SEP]For the land where the dead dreams go.[EOT]
[SOT]There’s a barrel-organ caroling across a golden street,[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks low;[SEP]Though the music’s only Verdi there’s a world to make it sweet[SEP]Just as yonder yellow sunset where the earth and heaven meet[SEP]Mellows all the sooty City![SEP]Hark, a hundred thousand feet[SEP]Are marching on to glory through the poppies and the wheat[SEP]In the land where the dead dreams go.[EOT]
[SOT]So it’s Jeremiah, Jeremiah,[SEP]What have you to say[SEP]When you meet the garland girls[SEP]Tripping on their way?[EOT]
[SOT]All around my gala hat[SEP]I wear a wreath of roses[SEP](A long and lonely year it is[SEP]I’ve waited for the May![SEP])[EOT]
[SOT]If any one should ask you,[SEP]The reason why I wear it is,[SEP]My own love, my true love, is coming home to-day.[EOT]
[SOT]It’s buy a bunch of violets for the lady[SEP](_It’s lilac time in London; it’s lilac time in London![SEP]_)[SEP]Buy a bunch of violets for the lady;[SEP]While the sky burns blue above:[EOT]
[SOT]On the other side of the street you’ll find it shady[SEP](_It’s lilac time in London; it’s lilac time in London![SEP]_)[SEP]But buy a bunch of violets for the lady;[SEP]And tell her she’s your own true love.[EOT]
[SOT]There’s a barrel-organ caroling across a golden street,[SEP]In the City as the sun sinks glittering and slow;[SEP]And the music’s not immortal, but the world has made it sweet[SEP]And enriched it with the harmonies that make a song complete[SEP]In the deeper heavens of music where the night and morning meet,[SEP]As it dies into the sunset glow;[EOT]
[SOT]And it pulses through the pleasures of the City and the pain[SEP]That surround the singing organ like a large eternal light,[SEP]And they’ve given it a glory and a part of play again[SEP]In the Symphony that rules the day and night.[EOT]
[SOT]And there, as the music changes,[SEP]The song runs round again;[SEP]Once more it turns and ranges[SEP]Through all its joy and pain:[SEP]Dissects the common carnival[SEP]Of passions and regrets;[SEP]And the wheeling world remembers all[SEP]The wheeling song forgets.[EOT]
[SOT]Once more _La Traviata_ sighs[SEP]Another sadder song:[SEP]Once more _Il Trovatore_ cries[SEP]A tale of deeper wrong;[SEP]Once more the knights to battle go[SEP]With sword and shield and lance,[SEP]Till once, once more, the shattered foe[SEP]Has whirled into—_a dance![SEP]_—[EOT]
[SOT]_Come down to Kew in lilac time; in lilac time; in lilac time;[SEP]Come down to Kew in lilac time;_ (_it isn’t far from London![SEP]_)[SEP]_And you shall wander hand in hand with love in summer’s wonderland;[SEP]Come down to Kew in lilac time;_ (_it isn’t far from London![SEP]_)[EOT]

[SOT]The Rassendylls--With a Word on the Elphbergs[EOT]
[SOT]“I wonder when in the world you’re going to do anything, Rudolf?[SEP]” said[SEP]my brother’s wife.[EOT]
[SOT]“My dear Rose,” I answered, laying down my egg-spoon, “why in the world[SEP]should I do anything?[SEP]My position is a comfortable one.[SEP]I have an[SEP]income nearly sufficient for my wants (no one’s income is ever quite[SEP]sufficient, you know), I enjoy an enviable social position: I am[SEP]brother to Lord Burlesdon, and brother-in-law to that charming lady, his[SEP]countess.[SEP]Behold, it is enough![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You are nine-and-twenty,” she observed, “and you’ve done nothing but--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Knock about?[SEP]It is true.[SEP]Our family doesn’t need to do things.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]This remark of mine rather annoyed Rose, for everybody knows (and[SEP]therefore there can be no harm in referring to the fact) that, pretty[SEP]and accomplished as she herself is, her family is hardly of the same[SEP]standing as the Rassendylls.[SEP]Besides her attractions, she possessed a[SEP]large fortune, and my brother Robert was wise enough not to mind about[SEP]her ancestry.[SEP]Ancestry is, in fact, a matter concerning which the next[SEP]observation of Rose’s has some truth.[EOT]
[SOT]“Good families are generally worse than any others,” she said.[EOT]
[SOT]Upon this I stroked my hair: I knew quite well what she meant.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m so glad Robert’s is black![SEP]” she cried.[EOT]
[SOT]At this moment Robert (who rises at seven and works before breakfast)[SEP]came in.[SEP]He glanced at his wife: her cheek was slightly flushed; he[SEP]patted it caressingly.[EOT]
[SOT]“What’s the matter, my dear?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“She objects to my doing nothing and having red hair,” said I, in an[SEP]injured tone.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh![SEP]of course he can’t help his hair,” admitted Rose.[EOT]
[SOT]“It generally crops out once in a generation,” said my brother.[SEP]“So does[SEP]the nose.[SEP]Rudolf has got them both.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I wish they didn’t crop out,” said Rose, still flushed.[EOT]
[SOT]“I rather like them myself,” said I, and, rising, I bowed to the[SEP]portrait of Countess Amelia.[EOT]
[SOT]My brother’s wife uttered an exclamation of impatience.[EOT]
[SOT]“I wish you’d take that picture away, Robert,” said she.[EOT]
[SOT]“My dear![SEP]” he cried.[EOT]
[SOT]“Good heavens![SEP]” I added.[EOT]
[SOT]“Then it might be forgotten,” she continued.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hardly--with Rudolf about,” said Robert, shaking his head.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why should it be forgotten?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Rudolf![SEP]” exclaimed my brother’s wife, blushing very prettily.[EOT]
[SOT]I laughed, and went on with my egg.[SEP]At least I had shelved the question[SEP]of what (if anything) I ought to do.[SEP]And, by way of closing the[SEP]discussion--and also, I must admit, of exasperating my strict little[SEP]sister-in-law a trifle more--I observed:[EOT]
[SOT]“I rather like being an Elphberg myself.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]When I read a story, I skip the explanations; yet the moment I begin to[SEP]write one, I find that I must have an explanation.[SEP]For it is manifest[SEP]that I must explain why my sister-in-law was vexed with my nose and[SEP]hair, and why I ventured to call myself an Elphberg.[SEP]For eminent as,[SEP]I must protest, the Rassendylls have been for many generations, yet[SEP]participation in their blood of course does not, at first sight, justify[SEP]the boast of a connection with the grander stock of the Elphbergs or[SEP]a claim to be one of that Royal House.[SEP]For what relationship is there[SEP]between Ruritania and Burlesdon, between the Palace at Strelsau or the[SEP]Castle of Zenda and Number 305 Park Lane, W.[SEP]?[EOT]
[SOT]Well then--and I must premise that I am going, perforce, to rake up the[SEP]very scandal which my dear Lady Burlesdon wishes forgotten--in the year[SEP]1733, George II.[SEP]sitting then on the throne, peace reigning for[SEP]the moment, and the King and the Prince of Wales being not yet at[SEP]loggerheads, there came on a visit to the English Court a certain[SEP]prince, who was afterwards known to history as Rudolf the Third of[SEP]Ruritania.[SEP]The prince was a tall, handsome young fellow, marked (maybe[SEP]marred, it is not for me to say) by a somewhat unusually long, sharp and[SEP]straight nose, and a mass of dark-red hair--in fact, the nose and the[SEP]hair which have stamped the Elphbergs time out of mind.[SEP]He stayed some[SEP]months in England, where he was most courteously received; yet, in[SEP]the end, he left rather under a cloud.[SEP]For he fought a duel (it was[SEP]considered highly well bred of him to waive all question of his rank)[SEP]with a nobleman, well known in the society of the day, not only for his[SEP]own merits, but as the husband of a very beautiful wife.[SEP]In that duel[SEP]Prince Rudolf received a severe wound, and, recovering therefrom, was[SEP]adroitly smuggled off by the Ruritanian ambassador, who had found him[SEP]a pretty handful.[SEP]The nobleman was not wounded in the duel; but the[SEP]morning being raw and damp on the occasion of the meeting, he contracted[SEP]a severe chill, and, failing to throw it off, he died some six months[SEP]after the departure of Prince Rudolf, without having found leisure to[SEP]adjust his relations with his wife--who, after another two months, bore[SEP]an heir to the title and estates of the family of Burlesdon.[SEP]This lady[SEP]was the Countess Amelia, whose picture my sister-in-law wished to remove[SEP]from the drawing-room in Park Lane; and her husband was James, fifth[SEP]Earl of Burlesdon and twenty-second Baron Rassendyll, both in the[SEP]peerage of England, and a Knight of the Garter.[SEP]As for Rudolf, he went[SEP]back to Ruritania, married a wife, and ascended the throne, whereon his[SEP]progeny in the direct line have sat from then till this very hour--with[SEP]one short interval.[SEP]And, finally, if you walk through the picture[SEP]galleries at Burlesdon, among the fifty portraits or so of the last[SEP]century and a half, you will find five or six, including that of the[SEP]sixth earl, distinguished by long, sharp, straight noses and a quantity[SEP]of dark-red hair; these five or six have also blue eyes, whereas among[SEP]the Rassendylls dark eyes are the commoner.[EOT]
[SOT]That is the explanation, and I am glad to have finished it: the[SEP]blemishes on honourable lineage are a delicate subject, and certainly[SEP]this heredity we hear so much about is the finest scandalmonger in the[SEP]world; it laughs at discretion, and writes strange entries between the[SEP]lines of the “Peerages”.[EOT]
[SOT]It will be observed that my sister-in-law, with a want of logic that[SEP]must have been peculiar to herself (since we are no longer allowed to[SEP]lay it to the charge of her sex), treated my complexion almost as an[SEP]offence for which I was responsible, hastening to assume from that[SEP]external sign inward qualities of which I protest my entire innocence;[SEP]and this unjust inference she sought to buttress by pointing to the[SEP]uselessness of the life I had led.[SEP]Well, be that as it may, I had picked[SEP]up a good deal of pleasure and a good deal of knowledge.[SEP]I had been to[SEP]a German school and a German university, and spoke German as readily[SEP]and perfectly as English; I was thoroughly at home in French; I had a[SEP]smattering of Italian and enough Spanish to swear by.[SEP]I was, I believe,[SEP]a strong, though hardly fine swordsman and a good shot.[SEP]I could ride[SEP]anything that had a back to sit on; and my head was as cool a one as you[SEP]could find, for all its flaming cover.[SEP]If you say that I ought to have[SEP]spent my time in useful labour, I am out of Court and have nothing[SEP]to say, save that my parents had no business to leave me two thousand[SEP]pounds a year and a roving disposition.[EOT]
[SOT]“The difference between you and Robert,” said my sister-in-law, who[SEP]often (bless her![SEP]) speaks on a platform, and oftener still as if she[SEP]were on one, “is that he recognizes the duties of his position, and you[SEP]see the opportunities of yours.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“To a man of spirit, my dear Rose,” I answered, “opportunities are[SEP]duties.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Nonsense![SEP]” said she, tossing her head; and after a moment she went on:[SEP]“Now, here’s Sir Jacob Borrodaile offering you exactly what you might be[SEP]equal to.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“A thousand thanks![SEP]” I murmured.[EOT]
[SOT]“He’s to have an Embassy in six months, and Robert says he is sure that[SEP]he’ll take you as an attaché.[SEP]Do take it, Rudolf--to please me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Now, when my sister-in-law puts the matter in that way, wrinkling her[SEP]pretty brows, twisting her little hands, and growing wistful in the[SEP]eyes, all on account of an idle scamp like myself, for whom she has[SEP]no natural responsibility, I am visited with compunction.[SEP]Moreover, I[SEP]thought it possible that I could pass the time in the position suggested[SEP]with some tolerable amusement.[SEP]Therefore I said:[EOT]
[SOT]“My dear sister, if in six months’ time no unforeseen obstacle has[SEP]arisen, and Sir Jacob invites me, hang me if I don’t go with Sir Jacob![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, Rudolf, how good of you![SEP]I am glad![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Where’s he going to?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He doesn’t know yet; but it’s sure to be a good Embassy.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Madame,” said I, “for your sake I’ll go, if it’s no more than a[SEP]beggarly Legation.[SEP]When I do a thing, I don’t do it by halves.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]My promise, then, was given; but six months are six months, and seem an[SEP]eternity, and, inasmuch as they stretched between me and my prospective[SEP]industry (I suppose attachés are industrious; but I know not, for I[SEP]never became attaché to Sir Jacob or anybody else), I cast about for[SEP]some desirable mode of spending them.[SEP]And it occurred to me suddenly[SEP]that I would visit Ruritania.[SEP]It may seem strange that I had never[SEP]visited that country yet; but my father (in spite of a sneaking fondness[SEP]for the Elphbergs, which led him to give me, his second son, the famous[SEP]Elphberg name of Rudolf) had always been averse from my going, and,[SEP]since his death, my brother, prompted by Rose, had accepted the family[SEP]tradition which taught that a wide berth was to be given to that[SEP]country.[SEP]But the moment Ruritania had come into my head I was eaten up[SEP]with a curiosity to see it.[SEP]After all, red hair and long noses are[SEP]not confined to the House of Elphberg, and the old story seemed[SEP]a preposterously insufficient reason for debarring myself from[SEP]acquaintance with a highly interesting and important kingdom, one which[SEP]had played no small part in European history, and might do the like[SEP]again under the sway of a young and vigorous ruler, such as the new[SEP]King was rumoured to be.[SEP]My determination was clinched by reading in _The[SEP]Times_ that Rudolf the Fifth was to be crowned at Strelsau in the course[SEP]of the next three weeks, and that great magnificence was to mark[SEP]the occasion.[SEP]At once I made up my mind to be present, and began my[SEP]preparations.[SEP]But, inasmuch as it has never been my practice to furnish[SEP]my relatives with an itinerary of my journeys and in this case I[SEP]anticipated opposition to my wishes, I gave out that I was going for a[SEP]ramble in the Tyrol--an old haunt of mine--and propitiated Rose’s wrath[SEP]by declaring that I intended to study the political and social problems[SEP]of the interesting community which dwells in that neighbourhood.[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps,” I hinted darkly, “there may be an outcome of the expedition.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What do you mean?[SEP]” she asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well,” said I carelessly, “there seems a gap that might be filled by an[SEP]exhaustive work on--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh![SEP]will you write a book?[SEP]” she cried, clapping her hands.[SEP]“That would[SEP]be splendid, wouldn’t it, Robert?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s the best of introductions to political life nowadays,” observed my[SEP]brother, who has, by the way, introduced himself in this manner several[SEP]times over.[SEP]_Burlesdon on Ancient Theories and Modern Facts_ and _The[SEP]Ultimate Outcome, by a Political Student_, are both works of recognized[SEP]eminence.[EOT]
[SOT]“I believe you are right, Bob, my boy,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Now promise you’ll do it,” said Rose earnestly.[EOT]
[SOT]“No, I won’t promise; but if I find enough material, I will.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s fair enough,” said Robert.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, material doesn’t matter![SEP]” she said, pouting.[EOT]
[SOT]But this time she could get no more than a qualified promise out of me.[SEP]To tell the truth, I would have wagered a handsome sum that the story[SEP]of my expedition that summer would stain no paper and spoil not a single[SEP]pen.[SEP]And that shows how little we know what the future holds; for here I[SEP]am, fulfilling my qualified promise, and writing, as I never thought[SEP]to write, a book--though it will hardly serve as an introduction to[SEP]political life, and has not a jot to do with the Tyrol.[EOT]
[SOT]Neither would it, I fear, please Lady Burlesdon, if I were to submit it[SEP]to her critical eye--a step which I have no intention of taking.[EOT]
[SOT]Concerning the Colour of Men’s Hair[EOT]
[SOT]It was a maxim of my Uncle William’s that no man should pass through[SEP]Paris without spending four-and-twenty hours there.[SEP]My uncle spoke out[SEP]of a ripe experience of the world, and I honoured his advice by putting[SEP]up for a day and a night at “The Continental” on my way to--the Tyrol.[SEP]I called on George Featherly at the Embassy, and we had a bit of dinner[SEP]together at Durand’s, and afterwards dropped in to the Opera; and[SEP]after that we had a little supper, and after that we called on Bertram[SEP]Bertrand, a versifier of some repute and Paris correspondent to _The[SEP]Critic_.[SEP]He had a very comfortable suite of rooms, and we found some[SEP]pleasant fellows smoking and talking.[SEP]It struck me, however, that[SEP]Bertram himself was absent and in low spirits, and when everybody except[SEP]ourselves had gone, I rallied him on his moping preoccupation.[SEP]He[SEP]fenced with me for a while, but at last, flinging himself on a sofa, he[SEP]exclaimed:[EOT]
[SOT]“Very well; have it your own way.[SEP]I am in love--infernally in love![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, you’ll write the better poetry,” said I, by way of consolation.[EOT]
[SOT]He ruffled his hair with his hand and smoked furiously.[SEP]George[SEP]Featherly, standing with his back to the mantelpiece, smiled unkindly.[EOT]
[SOT]“If it’s the old affair,” said he, “you may as well throw it up, Bert.[SEP]She’s leaving Paris tomorrow.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I know that,” snapped Bertram.[EOT]
[SOT]“Not that it would make any difference if she stayed,” pursued the[SEP]relentless George.[SEP]“She flies higher than the paper trade, my boy![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Hang her![SEP]” said Bertram.[EOT]
[SOT]“It would make it more interesting for me,” I ventured to observe, “if I[SEP]knew who you were talking about.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Antoinette Mauban,” said George.[EOT]
[SOT]“De Mauban,” growled Bertram.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oho![SEP]” said I, passing by the question of the `de’.[SEP]“You don’t mean to[SEP]say, Bert--?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Can’t you let me alone?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Where’s she going to?[SEP]” I asked, for the lady was something of a[SEP]celebrity.[EOT]
[SOT]George jingled his money, smiled cruelly at poor Bertram, and answered[SEP]pleasantly:[EOT]
[SOT]“Nobody knows.[SEP]By the way, Bert, I met a great man at her house the[SEP]other night--at least, about a month ago.[SEP]Did you ever meet him--the[SEP]Duke of Strelsau?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, I did,” growled Bertram.[EOT]
[SOT]“An extremely accomplished man, I thought him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was not hard to see that George’s references to the duke were[SEP]intended to aggravate poor Bertram’s sufferings, so that I drew the[SEP]inference that the duke had distinguished Madame de Mauban by his[SEP]attentions.[SEP]She was a widow, rich, handsome, and, according to repute,[SEP]ambitious.[SEP]It was quite possible that she, as George put it, was flying[SEP]as high as a personage who was everything he could be, short of enjoying[SEP]strictly royal rank: for the duke was the son of the late King of[SEP]Ruritania by a second and morganatic marriage, and half-brother to the[SEP]new King.[SEP]He had been his father’s favourite, and it had occasioned[SEP]some unfavourable comment when he had been created a duke, with a title[SEP]derived from no less a city than the capital itself.[SEP]His mother had been[SEP]of good, but not exalted, birth.[EOT]
[SOT]“He’s not in Paris now, is he?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh no![SEP]He’s gone back to be present at the King’s coronation; a[SEP]ceremony which, I should say, he’ll not enjoy much.[SEP]But, Bert, old man,[SEP]don’t despair![SEP]He won’t marry the fair Antoinette--at least, not unless[SEP]another plan comes to nothing.[SEP]Still perhaps she--” He paused and added,[SEP]with a laugh: “Royal attentions are hard to resist--you know that, don’t[SEP]you, Rudolf?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Confound you![SEP]” said I; and rising, I left the hapless Bertram in[SEP]George’s hands and went home to bed.[EOT]
[SOT]The next day George Featherly went with me to the station, where I took[SEP]a ticket for Dresden.[EOT]
[SOT]“Going to see the pictures?[SEP]” asked George, with a grin.[EOT]
[SOT]George is an inveterate gossip, and had I told him that I was off to[SEP]Ruritania, the news would have been in London in three days and in Park[SEP]Lane in a week.[SEP]I was, therefore, about to return an evasive answer,[SEP]when he saved my conscience by leaving me suddenly and darting across[SEP]the platform.[SEP]Following him with my eyes, I saw him lift his hat and[SEP]accost a graceful, fashionably dressed woman who had just appeared from[SEP]the booking-office.[SEP]She was, perhaps, a year or two over thirty, tall,[SEP]dark, and of rather full figure.[SEP]As George talked, I saw her glance at[SEP]me, and my vanity was hurt by the thought that, muffled in a fur coat[SEP]and a neck-wrapper (for it was a chilly April day) and wearing a soft[SEP]travelling hat pulled down to my ears, I must be looking very far from[SEP]my best.[SEP]A moment later, George rejoined me.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’ve got a charming travelling companion,” he said.[SEP]“That’s poor Bert[SEP]Bertrand’s goddess, Antoinette de Mauban, and, like you, she’s going to[SEP]Dresden--also, no doubt, to see the pictures.[SEP]It’s very queer, though,[SEP]that she doesn’t at present desire the honour of your acquaintance.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I didn’t ask to be introduced,” I observed, a little annoyed.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, I offered to bring you to her; but she said, ‘Another time.[SEP]’[SEP]Never mind, old fellow, perhaps there’ll be a smash, and you’ll have a[SEP]chance of rescuing her and cutting out the Duke of Strelsau![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]No smash, however, happened, either to me or to Madame de Mauban.[SEP]I can[SEP]speak for her as confidently as for myself; for when, after a night’s[SEP]rest in Dresden, I continued my journey, she got into the same train.[SEP]Understanding that she wished to be let alone, I avoided her carefully,[SEP]but I saw that she went the same way as I did to the very end of my[SEP]journey, and I took opportunities of having a good look at her, when I[SEP]could do so unobserved.[EOT]
[SOT]As soon as we reached the Ruritanian frontier (where the old officer who[SEP]presided over the Custom House favoured me with such a stare that I felt[SEP]surer than before of my Elphberg physiognomy), I bought the papers, and[SEP]found in them news which affected my movements.[SEP]For some reason, which[SEP]was not clearly explained, and seemed to be something of a mystery, the[SEP]date of the coronation had been suddenly advanced, and the ceremony was[SEP]to take place on the next day but one.[SEP]The whole country seemed in a[SEP]stir about it, and it was evident that Strelsau was thronged.[SEP]Rooms were[SEP]all let and hotels overflowing; there would be very little chance of my[SEP]obtaining a lodging, and I should certainly have to pay an exorbitant[SEP]charge for it.[SEP]I made up my mind to stop at Zenda, a small town fifty[SEP]miles short of the capital, and about ten from the frontier.[SEP]My train[SEP]reached there in the evening; I would spend the next day, Tuesday, in a[SEP]wander over the hills, which were said to be very fine, and in taking[SEP]a glance at the famous Castle, and go over by train to Strelsau on the[SEP]Wednesday morning, returning at night to sleep at Zenda.[EOT]
[SOT]Accordingly at Zenda I got out, and as the train passed where I stood on[SEP]the platform, I saw my friend Madame de Mauban in her place; clearly she[SEP]was going through to Strelsau, having, with more providence than I could[SEP]boast, secured apartments there.[SEP]I smiled to think how surprised[SEP]George Featherly would have been to know that she and I had been fellow[SEP]travellers for so long.[EOT]
[SOT]I was very kindly received at the hotel--it was really no more than an[SEP]inn--kept by a fat old lady and her two daughters.[SEP]They were good,[SEP]quiet people, and seemed very little interested in the great doings at[SEP]Strelsau.[SEP]The old lady’s hero was the duke, for he was now, under the[SEP]late King’s will, master of the Zenda estates and of the Castle, which[SEP]rose grandly on its steep hill at the end of the valley a mile or so[SEP]from the inn.[SEP]The old lady, indeed, did not hesitate to express regret[SEP]that the duke was not on the throne, instead of his brother.[EOT]
[SOT]“We know Duke Michael,” said she.[SEP]“He has always lived among us; every[SEP]Ruritanian knows Duke Michael.[SEP]But the King is almost a stranger; he has[SEP]been so much abroad, not one in ten knows him even by sight.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And now,” chimed in one of the young women, “they say he has shaved off[SEP]his beard, so that no one at all knows him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Shaved his beard![SEP]” exclaimed her mother.[SEP]“Who says so?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Johann, the duke’s keeper.[SEP]He has seen the King.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah, yes.[SEP]The King, sir, is now at the duke’s hunting-lodge in the[SEP]forest here; from here he goes to Strelsau to be crowned on Wednesday[SEP]morning.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I was interested to hear this, and made up my mind to walk next day in[SEP]the direction of the lodge, on the chance of coming across the King.[SEP]The[SEP]old lady ran on garrulously:[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah, and I wish he would stay at his hunting--that and wine (and one[SEP]thing more) are all he loves, they say--and suffer our duke to be[SEP]crowned on Wednesday.[SEP]That I wish, and I don’t care who knows it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Hush, mother![SEP]” urged the daughters.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, there’s many to think as I do![SEP]” cried the old woman stubbornly.[EOT]
[SOT]I threw myself back in my deep armchair, and laughed at her zeal.[EOT]
[SOT]“For my part,” said the younger and prettier of the two daughters, a[SEP]fair, buxom, smiling wench, “I hate Black Michael![SEP]A red Elphberg for[SEP]me, mother![SEP]The King, they say, is as red as a fox or as--”[EOT]
[SOT]And she laughed mischievously as she cast a glance at me, and tossed her[SEP]head at her sister’s reproving face.[EOT]
[SOT]“Many a man has cursed their red hair before now,” muttered the old[SEP]lady--and I remembered James, fifth Earl of Burlesdon.[EOT]
[SOT]“But never a woman![SEP]” cried the girl.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, and women, when it was too late,” was the stern answer, reducing[SEP]the girl to silence and blushes.[EOT]
[SOT]“How comes the King here?[SEP]” I asked, to break an embarrassed silence.[SEP]“It[SEP]is the duke’s land here, you say.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The duke invited him, sir, to rest here till Wednesday.[SEP]The duke is at[SEP]Strelsau, preparing the King’s reception.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Then they’re friends?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“None better,” said the old lady.[EOT]
[SOT]But my rosy damsel tossed her head again; she was not to be repressed[SEP]for long, and she broke out again:[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, they love one another as men do who want the same place and the[SEP]same wife![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The old woman glowered; but the last words pricked my curiosity, and I[SEP]interposed before she could begin scolding:[EOT]
[SOT]“What, the same wife, too![SEP]How’s that, young lady?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“All the world knows that Black Michael--well then, mother, the[SEP]duke--would give his soul to marry his cousin, the Princess Flavia, and[SEP]that she is to be the queen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Upon my word,” said I, “I begin to be sorry for your duke.[SEP]But if a man[SEP]will be a younger son, why he must take what the elder leaves, and be[SEP]as thankful to God as he can;” and, thinking of myself, I shrugged my[SEP]shoulders and laughed.[SEP]And then I thought also of Antoinette de Mauban[SEP]and her journey to Strelsau.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s little dealing Black Michael has with--” began the girl, braving[SEP]her mother’s anger; but as she spoke a heavy step sounded on the floor,[SEP]and a gruff voice asked in a threatening tone:[EOT]
[SOT]“Who talks of ‘Black Michael’ in his Highness’s own burgh?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The girl gave a little shriek, half of fright--half, I think, of[SEP]amusement.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’ll not tell of me, Johann?[SEP]” she said.[EOT]
[SOT]“See where your chatter leads,” said the old lady.[EOT]
[SOT]The man who had spoken came forward.[EOT]
[SOT]“We have company, Johann,” said my hostess, and the fellow plucked off[SEP]his cap.[SEP]A moment later he saw me, and, to my amazement, he started back[SEP]a step, as though he had seen something wonderful.[EOT]
[SOT]“What ails you, Johann?[SEP]” asked the elder girl.[SEP]“This is a gentleman on[SEP]his travels, come to see the coronation.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The man had recovered himself, but he was staring at me with an intense,[SEP]searching, almost fierce glance.[EOT]
[SOT]“Good evening to you,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Good evening, sir,” he muttered, still scrutinizing me, and the merry[SEP]girl began to laugh as she called--[EOT]
[SOT]“See, Johann, it is the colour you love![SEP]He started to see your hair,[SEP]sir.[SEP]It’s not the colour we see most of here in Zenda.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I crave your pardon, sir,” stammered the fellow, with puzzled eyes.[SEP]“I[SEP]expected to see no one.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Give him a glass to drink my health in; and I’ll bid you good night,[SEP]and thanks to you, ladies, for your courtesy and pleasant conversation.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]So speaking, I rose to my feet, and with a slight bow turned to the[SEP]door.[SEP]The young girl ran to light me on the way, and the man fell back[SEP]to let me pass, his eyes still fixed on me.[SEP]The moment I was by, he[SEP]started a step forward, asking:[EOT]
[SOT]“Pray, sir, do you know our King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I never saw him,” said I.[SEP]“I hope to do so on Wednesday.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He said no more, but I felt his eyes following me till the door closed[SEP]behind me.[SEP]My saucy conductor, looking over her shoulder at me as she[SEP]preceded me upstairs, said:[EOT]
[SOT]“There’s no pleasing Master Johann for one of your colour, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He prefers yours, maybe?[SEP]” I suggested.[EOT]
[SOT]“I meant, sir, in a man,” she answered, with a coquettish glance.[EOT]
[SOT]“What,” asked I, taking hold of the other side of the candlestick, “does[SEP]colour matter in a man?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Nay, but I love yours--it’s the Elphberg red.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Colour in a man,” said I, “is a matter of no more moment than[SEP]that![SEP]”--and I gave her something of no value.[EOT]
[SOT]“God send the kitchen door be shut![SEP]” said she.[EOT]
[SOT]“Amen![SEP]” said I, and left her.[EOT]
[SOT]In fact, however, as I now know, colour is sometimes of considerable[SEP]moment to a man.[EOT]
[SOT]A Merry Evening with a Distant Relative[EOT]
[SOT]I was not so unreasonable as to be prejudiced against the duke’s keeper[SEP]because he disliked my complexion; and if I had been, his most civil[SEP]and obliging conduct (as it seemed to me to be) next morning would have[SEP]disarmed me.[SEP]Hearing that I was bound for Strelsau, he came to see[SEP]me while I was breakfasting, and told me that a sister of his who had[SEP]married a well-to-do tradesman and lived in the capital, had invited[SEP]him to occupy a room in her house.[SEP]He had gladly accepted, but now found[SEP]that his duties would not permit of his absence.[SEP]He begged therefore[SEP]that, if such humble (though, as he added, clean and comfortable)[SEP]lodgings would satisfy me, I would take his place.[SEP]He pledged his[SEP]sister’s acquiescence, and urged the inconvenience and crowding to which[SEP]I should be subject in my journeys to and from Strelsau the next day.[SEP]I accepted his offer without a moment’s hesitation, and he went off to[SEP]telegraph to his sister, while I packed up and prepared to take the next[SEP]train.[SEP]But I still hankered after the forest and the hunting-lodge, and[SEP]when my little maid told me that I could, by walking ten miles or so[SEP]through the forest, hit the railway at a roadside station, I decided to[SEP]send my luggage direct to the address which Johann had given, take my[SEP]walk, and follow to Strelsau myself.[SEP]Johann had gone off and was not[SEP]aware of the change in my plans; but, as its only effect was to delay[SEP]my arrival at his sister’s for a few hours, there was no reason for[SEP]troubling to inform him of it.[SEP]Doubtless the good lady would waste no[SEP]anxiety on my account.[EOT]
[SOT]I took an early luncheon, and, having bidden my kind entertainers[SEP]farewell, promising to return to them on my way home, I set out to climb[SEP]the hill that led to the Castle, and thence to the forest of Zenda.[SEP]Half an hour’s leisurely walking brought me to the Castle.[SEP]It had been[SEP]a fortress in old days, and the ancient keep was still in good[SEP]preservation and very imposing.[SEP]Behind it stood another portion of the[SEP]original castle, and behind that again, and separated from it by a deep[SEP]and broad moat, which ran all round the old buildings, was a handsome[SEP]modern chateau, erected by the last king, and now forming the country[SEP]residence of the Duke of Strelsau.[SEP]The old and the new portions were[SEP]connected by a drawbridge, and this indirect mode of access formed the[SEP]only passage between the old building and the outer world; but leading[SEP]to the modern chateau there was a broad and handsome avenue.[SEP]It was an[SEP]ideal residence: when “Black Michael” desired company, he could dwell in[SEP]his chateau; if a fit of misanthropy seized him, he had merely to cross[SEP]the bridge and draw it up after him (it ran on rollers), and nothing[SEP]short of a regiment and a train of artillery could fetch him out.[SEP]I went[SEP]on my way, glad that poor Black Michael, though he could not have the[SEP]throne or the princess, had, at least, as fine a residence as any prince[SEP]in Europe.[EOT]
[SOT]Soon I entered the forest, and walked on for an hour or more in its cool[SEP]sombre shade.[SEP]The great trees enlaced with one another over my head, and[SEP]the sunshine stole through in patches as bright as diamonds, and[SEP]hardly bigger.[SEP]I was enchanted with the place, and, finding a felled[SEP]tree-trunk, propped my back against it, and stretching my legs out gave[SEP]myself up to undisturbed contemplation of the solemn beauty of the woods[SEP]and to the comfort of a good cigar.[SEP]And when the cigar was finished and[SEP]I had (I suppose) inhaled as much beauty as I could, I went off into[SEP]the most delightful sleep, regardless of my train to Strelsau and of[SEP]the fast-waning afternoon.[SEP]To remember a train in such a spot would[SEP]have been rank sacrilege.[SEP]Instead of that, I fell to dreaming that I[SEP]was married to the Princess Flavia and dwelt in the Castle of Zenda, and[SEP]beguiled whole days with my love in the glades of the forest--which made[SEP]a very pleasant dream.[SEP]In fact, I was just impressing a fervent kiss on[SEP]the charming lips of the princess, when I heard (and the voice seemed at[SEP]first a part of the dream) someone exclaim, in rough strident tones.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why, the devil’s in it![SEP]Shave him, and he’d be the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The idea seemed whimsical enough for a dream: by the sacrifice of my[SEP]heavy moustache and carefully pointed imperial, I was to be transformed[SEP]into a monarch![SEP]I was about to kiss the princess again, when I arrived[SEP](very reluctantly) at the conclusion that I was awake.[EOT]
[SOT]I opened my eyes, and found two men regarding me with much curiosity.[SEP]Both wore shooting costumes and carried guns.[SEP]One was rather short[SEP]and very stoutly built, with a big bullet-shaped head, a bristly grey[SEP]moustache, and small pale-blue eyes, a trifle bloodshot.[SEP]The other was a[SEP]slender young fellow, of middle height, dark in complexion, and bearing[SEP]himself with grace and distinction.[SEP]I set the one down as an old[SEP]soldier: the other for a gentleman accustomed to move in good society,[SEP]but not unused to military life either.[SEP]It turned out afterwards that my[SEP]guess was a good one.[EOT]
[SOT]The elder man approached me, beckoning the younger to follow.[SEP]He did so,[SEP]courteously raising his hat.[SEP]I rose slowly to my feet.[EOT]
[SOT]“He’s the height, too![SEP]” I heard the elder murmur, as he surveyed my six[SEP]feet two inches of stature.[SEP]Then, with a cavalier touch of the cap, he[SEP]addressed me:[EOT]
[SOT]“May I ask your name?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“As you have taken the first step in the acquaintance, gentlemen,” said[SEP]I, with a smile, “suppose you give me a lead in the matter of names.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The young man stepped forward with a pleasant smile.[EOT]
[SOT]“This,” said he, “is Colonel Sapt, and I am called Fritz von Tarlenheim:[SEP]we are both in the service of the King of Ruritania.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I bowed and, baring my head, answered:[EOT]
[SOT]“I am Rudolf Rassendyll.[SEP]I am a traveller from England; and once for a[SEP]year or two I held a commission from her Majesty the Queen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Then we are all brethren of the sword,” answered Tarlenheim, holding[SEP]out his hand, which I took readily.[EOT]
[SOT]“Rassendyll, Rassendyll![SEP]” muttered Colonel Sapt; then a gleam of[SEP]intelligence flitted across his face.[EOT]
[SOT]“By Heaven![SEP]” he cried, “you’re of the Burlesdons?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“My brother is now Lord Burlesdon,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Thy head betrayeth thee,” he chuckled, pointing to my uncovered poll.[SEP]“Why, Fritz, you know the story?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The young man glanced apologetically at me.[SEP]He felt a delicacy which[SEP]my sister-in-law would have admired.[SEP]To put him at his ease, I remarked[SEP]with a smile:[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah![SEP]the story is known here as well as among us, it seems.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Known![SEP]” cried Sapt.[SEP]“If you stay here, the deuce a man in all Ruritania[SEP]will doubt of it--or a woman either.[SEP]”[EOT]
