[SOT]I began to feel uncomfortable.[SEP]Had I realized what a very plainly[SEP]written pedigree I carried about with me, I should have thought long[SEP]before I visited Ruritania.[SEP]However, I was in for it now.[EOT]
[SOT]At this moment a ringing voice sounded from the wood behind us:[EOT]
[SOT]“Fritz, Fritz![SEP]where are you, man?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Tarlenheim started, and said hastily:[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Old Sapt chuckled again.[EOT]
[SOT]Then a young man jumped out from behind the trunk of a tree and stood[SEP]beside us.[SEP]As I looked at him, I uttered an astonished cry; and he,[SEP]seeing me, drew back in sudden wonder.[SEP]Saving the hair on my face and[SEP]a manner of conscious dignity which his position gave him, saving also[SEP]that he lacked perhaps half an inch--nay, less than that, but still[SEP]something--of my height, the King of Ruritania might have been Rudolf[SEP]Rassendyll, and I, Rudolf, the King.[EOT]
[SOT]For an instant we stood motionless, looking at one another.[SEP]Then I bared[SEP]my head again and bowed respectfully.[SEP]The King found his voice, and[SEP]asked in bewilderment:[EOT]
[SOT]“Colonel--Fritz--who is this gentleman?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I was about to answer, when Colonel Sapt stepped between the King and[SEP]me, and began to talk to his Majesty in a low growl.[SEP]The King towered[SEP]over Sapt, and, as he listened, his eyes now and again sought mine.[SEP]I looked at him long and carefully.[SEP]The likeness was certainly[SEP]astonishing, though I saw the points of difference also.[SEP]The King’s face[SEP]was slightly more fleshy than mine, the oval of its contour the least[SEP]trifle more pronounced, and, as I fancied, his mouth lacking something[SEP]of the firmness (or obstinacy) which was to be gathered from[SEP]my close-shutting lips.[SEP]But, for all that, and above all minor[SEP]distinctions, the likeness rose striking, salient, wonderful.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt ceased speaking, and the King still frowned.[SEP]Then, gradually, the[SEP]corners of his mouth began to twitch, his nose came down (as mine[SEP]does when I laugh), his eyes twinkled, and, behold![SEP]he burst into the[SEP]merriest fit of irrepressible laughter, which rang through the woods and[SEP]proclaimed him a jovial soul.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well met, cousin![SEP]” he cried, stepping up to me, clapping me on the[SEP]back, and laughing still.[SEP]“You must forgive me if I was taken aback.[SEP]A[SEP]man doesn’t expect to see double at this time of day, eh, Fritz?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I must pray pardon, sire, for my presumption,” said I.[SEP]“I trust it will[SEP]not forfeit your Majesty’s favour.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“By Heaven![SEP]you’ll always enjoy the King’s countenance,” he laughed,[SEP]“whether I like it or not; and, sir, I shall very gladly add to it what[SEP]services I can.[SEP]Where are you travelling to?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“To Strelsau, sire--to the coronation.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The King looked at his friends: he still smiled, though his expression[SEP]hinted some uneasiness.[SEP]But the humorous side of the matter caught him[SEP]again.[EOT]
[SOT]“Fritz, Fritz![SEP]” he cried, “a thousand crowns for a sight of brother[SEP]Michael’s face when he sees a pair of us![SEP]” and the merry laugh rang out[SEP]again.[EOT]
[SOT]“Seriously,” observed Fritz von Tarlenheim, “I question Mr. Rassendyll’s[SEP]wisdom in visiting Strelsau just now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The King lit a cigarette.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, Sapt?[SEP]” said he, questioningly.[EOT]
[SOT]“He mustn’t go,” growled the old fellow.[EOT]
[SOT]“Come, colonel, you mean that I should be in Mr. Rassendyll’s debt,[SEP]if--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, ay![SEP]wrap it up in the right way,” said Sapt, hauling a great pipe[SEP]out of his pocket.[EOT]
[SOT]“Enough, sire,” said I.[SEP]“I’ll leave Ruritania today.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, by thunder, you shan’t--and that’s sans phrase, as Sapt likes it.[SEP]For you shall dine with me tonight, happen what will afterwards.[SEP]Come,[SEP]man, you don’t meet a new relation every day![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We dine sparingly tonight,” said Fritz von Tarlenheim.[EOT]
[SOT]“Not we--with our new cousin for a guest![SEP]” cried the King; and, as Fritz[SEP]shrugged his shoulders, he added: “Oh![SEP]I’ll remember our early start,[SEP]Fritz.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“So will I--tomorrow morning,” said old Sapt, pulling at his pipe.[EOT]
[SOT]“O wise old Sapt![SEP]” cried the King.[SEP]“Come, Mr. Rassendyll--by the way,[SEP]what name did they give you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Your Majesty’s,” I answered, bowing.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, that shows they weren’t ashamed of us,” he laughed.[SEP]“Come, then,[SEP]cousin Rudolf; I’ve got no house of my own here, but my dear brother[SEP]Michael lends us a place of his, and we’ll make shift to entertain you[SEP]there;” and he put his arm through mine and, signing to the others to[SEP]accompany us, walked me off, westerly, through the forest.[EOT]
[SOT]We walked for more than half an hour, and the King smoked cigarettes[SEP]and chattered incessantly.[SEP]He was full of interest in my family, laughed[SEP]heartily when I told him of the portraits with Elphberg hair in our[SEP]galleries, and yet more heartily when he heard that my expedition to[SEP]Ruritania was a secret one.[EOT]
[SOT]“You have to visit your disreputable cousin on the sly, have you?[SEP]” said[SEP]he.[EOT]
[SOT]Suddenly emerging from the wood, we came on a small and rude[SEP]hunting-lodge.[SEP]It was a one-storey building, a sort of bungalow, built[SEP]entirely of wood.[SEP]As we approached it, a little man in a plain livery[SEP]came out to meet us.[SEP]The only other person I saw about the place was[SEP]a fat elderly woman, whom I afterwards discovered to be the mother of[SEP]Johann, the duke’s keeper.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, is dinner ready, Josef?[SEP]” asked the King.[EOT]
[SOT]The little servant informed us that it was, and we soon sat down to a[SEP]plentiful meal.[SEP]The fare was plain enough: the King ate heartily, Fritz[SEP]von Tarlenheim delicately, old Sapt voraciously.[SEP]I played a good[SEP]knife and fork, as my custom is; the King noticed my performance with[SEP]approval.[EOT]
[SOT]“We’re all good trenchermen, we Elphbergs,” said he.[SEP]“But what?[SEP]--we’re[SEP]eating dry![SEP]Wine, Josef![SEP]wine, man![SEP]Are we beasts, to eat without[SEP]drinking?[SEP]Are we cattle, Josef?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]At this reproof Josef hastened to load the table with bottles.[EOT]
[SOT]“Remember tomorrow![SEP]” said Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay--tomorrow![SEP]” said old Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]The King drained a bumper to his “Cousin Rudolf,” as he was gracious--or[SEP]merry--enough to call me; and I drank its fellow to the “Elphberg Red,”[SEP]whereat he laughed loudly.[EOT]
[SOT]Now, be the meat what it might, the wine we drank was beyond all price[SEP]or praise, and we did it justice.[SEP]Fritz ventured once to stay the King’s[SEP]hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“What?[SEP]” cried the King.[SEP]“Remember you start before I do, Master[SEP]Fritz--you must be more sparing by two hours than I.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz saw that I did not understand.[EOT]
[SOT]“The colonel and I,” he explained, “leave here at six: we ride down to[SEP]Zenda and return with the guard of honour to fetch the King at eight,[SEP]and then we all ride together to the station.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Hang that same guard![SEP]” growled Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh![SEP]it’s very civil of my brother to ask the honour for his regiment,”[SEP]said the King.[SEP]“Come, cousin, you need not start early.[SEP]Another bottle,[SEP]man![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I had another bottle--or, rather, a part of one, for the larger half[SEP]travelled quickly down his Majesty’s throat.[SEP]Fritz gave up his attempts[SEP]at persuasion: from persuading, he fell to being persuaded, and soon we[SEP]were all of us as full of wine as we had any right to be.[SEP]The King began[SEP]talking of what he would do in the future, old Sapt of what he had[SEP]done in the past, Fritz of some beautiful girl or other, and I of the[SEP]wonderful merits of the Elphberg dynasty.[SEP]We all talked at once, and[SEP]followed to the letter Sapt’s exhortation to let the morrow take care of[SEP]itself.[EOT]
[SOT]At last the King set down his glass and leant back in his chair.[EOT]
[SOT]“I have drunk enough,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]“Far be it from me to contradict the King,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]Indeed, his remark was most absolutely true--so far as it went.[EOT]
[SOT]While I yet spoke, Josef came and set before the King a marvellous old[SEP]wicker-covered flagon.[SEP]It had lain so long in some darkened cellar that[SEP]it seemed to blink in the candlelight.[EOT]
[SOT]“His Highness the Duke of Strelsau bade me set this wine before the[SEP]King, when the King was weary of all other wines, and pray the King to[SEP]drink, for the love that he bears his brother.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well done, Black Michael![SEP]” said the King.[SEP]“Out with the cork, Josef.[SEP]Hang him![SEP]Did he think I’d flinch from his bottle?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The bottle was opened, and Josef filled the King’s glass.[SEP]The King[SEP]tasted it.[SEP]Then, with a solemnity born of the hour and his own[SEP]condition, he looked round on us:[EOT]
[SOT]“Gentlemen, my friends--Rudolf, my cousin [‘tis a scandalous story,[SEP]Rudolf, on my honour![SEP]), everything is yours to the half of Ruritania.[SEP]But ask me not for a single drop of this divine bottle, which I will[SEP]drink to the health of that--that sly knave, my brother, Black Michael.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And the King seized the bottle and turned it over his mouth, and drained[SEP]it and flung it from him, and laid his head on his arms on the table.[EOT]
[SOT]And we drank pleasant dreams to his Majesty--and that is all I remember[SEP]of the evening.[SEP]Perhaps it is enough.[EOT]
[SOT]The King Keeps His Appointment[EOT]
[SOT]Whether I had slept a minute or a year I knew not.[SEP]I awoke with a start[SEP]and a shiver; my face, hair and clothes dripped water, and opposite me[SEP]stood old Sapt, a sneering smile on his face and an empty bucket in his[SEP]hand.[SEP]On the table by him sat Fritz von Tarlenheim, pale as a ghost and[SEP]black as a crow under the eyes.[EOT]
[SOT]I leapt to my feet in anger.[EOT]
[SOT]“Your joke goes too far, sir![SEP]” I cried.[EOT]
[SOT]“Tut, man, we’ve no time for quarrelling.[SEP]Nothing else would rouse you.[SEP]It’s five o’clock.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll thank you, Colonel Sapt--” I began again, hot in spirit, though I[SEP]was uncommonly cold in body.[EOT]
[SOT]“Rassendyll,” interrupted Fritz, getting down from the table and taking[SEP]my arm, “look here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The King lay full length on the floor.[SEP]His face was red as his hair,[SEP]and he breathed heavily.[SEP]Sapt, the disrespectful old dog, kicked him[SEP]sharply.[SEP]He did not stir, nor was there any break in his breathing.[SEP]I[SEP]saw that his face and head were wet with water, as were mine.[EOT]
[SOT]“We’ve spent half an hour on him,” said Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“He drank three times what either of you did,” growled Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]I knelt down and felt his pulse.[SEP]It was alarmingly languid and slow.[SEP]We[SEP]three looked at one another.[EOT]
[SOT]“Was it drugged--that last bottle?[SEP]” I asked in a whisper.[EOT]
[SOT]“I don’t know,” said Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“We must get a doctor.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“There’s none within ten miles, and a thousand doctors wouldn’t take[SEP]him to Strelsau today.[SEP]I know the look of it.[SEP]He’ll not move for six or[SEP]seven hours yet.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But the coronation![SEP]” I cried in horror.[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz shrugged his shoulders, as I began to see was his habit on most[SEP]occasions.[EOT]
[SOT]“We must send word that he’s ill,” he said.[EOT]
[SOT]“I suppose so,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]Old Sapt, who seemed as fresh as a daisy, had lit his pipe and was[SEP]puffing hard at it.[EOT]
[SOT]“If he’s not crowned today,” said he, “I’ll lay a crown he’s never[SEP]crowned.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But heavens, why?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The whole nation’s there to meet him; half the army--ay, and Black[SEP]Michael at the head.[SEP]Shall we send word that the King’s drunk?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That he’s ill,” said I, in correction.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ill![SEP]” echoed Sapt, with a scornful laugh.[SEP]“They know his illnesses too[SEP]well.[SEP]He’s been ‘ill’ before![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, we must chance what they think,” said Fritz helplessly.[SEP]“I’ll[SEP]carry the news and make the best of it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt raised his hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“Tell me,” said he.[SEP]“Do you think the King was drugged?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I do,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“And who drugged him?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That damned hound, Black Michael,” said Fritz between his teeth.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay,” said Sapt, “that he might not come to be crowned.[SEP]Rassendyll here[SEP]doesn’t know our pretty Michael.[SEP]What think you, Fritz, has Michael no[SEP]king ready?[SEP]Has half Strelsau no other candidate?[SEP]As God’s alive, man,[SEP]the throne’s lost if the King show himself not in Strelsau today.[SEP]I know[SEP]Black Michael.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We could carry him there,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“And a very pretty picture he makes,” sneered Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz von Tarlenheim buried his face in his hands.[SEP]The King breathed[SEP]loudly and heavily.[SEP]Sapt stirred him again with his foot.[EOT]
[SOT]“The drunken dog![SEP]” he said; “but he’s an Elphberg and the son of his[SEP]father, and may I rot in hell before Black Michael sits in his place![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]For a moment or two we were all silent; then Sapt, knitting his bushy[SEP]grey brows, took his pipe from his mouth and said to me:[EOT]
[SOT]“As a man grows old he believes in Fate.[SEP]Fate sent you here.[SEP]Fate sends[SEP]you now to Strelsau.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I staggered back, murmuring “Good God![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz looked up with an eager, bewildered gaze.[EOT]
[SOT]“Impossible![SEP]” I muttered.[SEP]“I should be known.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s a risk--against a certainty,” said Sapt.[SEP]“If you shave, I’ll wager[SEP]you’ll not be known.[SEP]Are you afraid?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Come, lad, there, there; but it’s your life, you know, if you’re[SEP]known--and mine--and Fritz’s here.[SEP]But, if you don’t go, I swear to you[SEP]Black Michael will sit tonight on the throne, and the King lie in prison[SEP]or his grave.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The King would never forgive it,” I stammered.[EOT]
[SOT]“Are we women?[SEP]Who cares for his forgiveness?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The clock ticked fifty times, and sixty and seventy times, as I stood in[SEP]thought.[SEP]Then I suppose a look came over my face, for old Sapt caught me[SEP]by the hand, crying:[EOT]
[SOT]“You’ll go?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, I’ll go,” said I, and I turned my eyes on the prostrate figure of[SEP]the King on the floor.[EOT]
[SOT]“Tonight,” Sapt went on in a hasty whisper, “we are to lodge in the[SEP]Palace.[SEP]The moment they leave us you and I will mount our horses--Fritz[SEP]must stay there and guard the King’s room--and ride here at a gallop.[SEP]The King will be ready--Josef will tell him--and he must ride back with[SEP]me to Strelsau, and you ride as if the devil were behind you to the[SEP]frontier.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I took it all in in a second, and nodded my head.[EOT]
[SOT]“There’s a chance,” said Fritz, with his first sign of hopefulness.[EOT]
[SOT]“If I escape detection,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“If we’re detected,” said Sapt.[SEP]“I’ll send Black Michael down below[SEP]before I go myself, so help me heaven![SEP]Sit in that chair, man.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I obeyed him.[EOT]
[SOT]He darted from the room, calling “Josef![SEP]Josef![SEP]” In three minutes he was[SEP]back, and Josef with him.[SEP]The latter carried a jug of hot water, soap[SEP]and razors.[SEP]He was trembling as Sapt told him how the land lay, and bade[SEP]him shave me.[EOT]
[SOT]Suddenly Fritz smote on his thigh:[EOT]
[SOT]“But the guard![SEP]They’ll know![SEP]they’ll know![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Pooh![SEP]We shan’t wait for the guard.[SEP]We’ll ride to Hofbau and catch a[SEP]train there.[SEP]When they come, the bird’ll be flown.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But the King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The King will be in the wine-cellar.[SEP]I’m going to carry him there now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“If they find him?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They won’t.[SEP]How should they?[SEP]Josef will put them off.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt stamped his foot.[EOT]
[SOT]“We’re not playing,” he roared.[SEP]“My God![SEP]don’t I know the risk?[SEP]If[SEP]they do find him, he’s no worse off than if he isn’t crowned today in[SEP]Strelsau.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]So speaking, he flung the door open and, stooping, put forth a strength[SEP]I did not dream he had, and lifted the King in his hands.[SEP]And as he did[SEP]so, the old woman, Johann the keeper’s mother, stood in the doorway.[SEP]For a moment she stood, then she turned on her heel, without a sign of[SEP]surprise, and clattered down the passage.[EOT]
[SOT]“Has she heard?[SEP]” cried Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll shut her mouth![SEP]” said Sapt grimly, and he bore off the King in his[SEP]arms.[EOT]
[SOT]For me, I sat down in an armchair, and as I sat there, half-dazed, Josef[SEP]clipped and scraped me till my moustache and imperial were things of the[SEP]past and my face was as bare as the King’s.[SEP]And when Fritz saw me thus[SEP]he drew a long breath and exclaimed:--[EOT]
[SOT]“By Jove, we shall do it![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was six o’clock now, and we had no time to lose.[SEP]Sapt hurried me into[SEP]the King’s room, and I dressed myself in the uniform of a colonel of the[SEP]Guard, finding time as I slipped on the King’s boots to ask Sapt what he[SEP]had done with the old woman.[EOT]
[SOT]“She swore she’d heard nothing,” said he; “but to make sure I tied her[SEP]legs together and put a handkerchief in her mouth and bound her hands,[SEP]and locked her up in the coal-cellar, next door to the King.[SEP]Josef will[SEP]look after them both later on.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then I burst out laughing, and even old Sapt grimly smiled.[EOT]
[SOT]“I fancy,” said he, “that when Josef tells them the King is gone they’ll[SEP]think it is because we smelt a rat.[SEP]For you may swear Black Michael[SEP]doesn’t expect to see him in Strelsau today.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I put the King’s helmet on my head.[SEP]Old Sapt handed me the King’s sword,[SEP]looking at me long and carefully.[EOT]
[SOT]“Thank God, he shaved his beard![SEP]” he exclaimed.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why did he?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Because Princess Flavia said he grazed her cheek when he was graciously[SEP]pleased to give her a cousinly kiss.[SEP]Come though, we must ride.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Is all safe here?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Nothing’s safe anywhere,” said Sapt, “but we can make it no safer.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz now rejoined us in the uniform of a captain in the same regiment[SEP]as that to which my dress belonged.[SEP]In four minutes Sapt had arrayed[SEP]himself in his uniform.[SEP]Josef called that the horses were ready.[SEP]We[SEP]jumped on their backs and started at a rapid trot.[SEP]The game had begun.[SEP]What would the issue of it be?[EOT]
[SOT]The cool morning air cleared my head, and I was able to take in all[SEP]Sapt said to me.[SEP]He was wonderful.[SEP]Fritz hardly spoke, riding like a man[SEP]asleep, but Sapt, without another word for the King, began at once to[SEP]instruct me most minutely in the history of my past life, of my family,[SEP]of my tastes, pursuits, weaknesses, friends, companions, and servants.[SEP]He told me the etiquette of the Ruritanian Court, promising to be[SEP]constantly at my elbow to point out everybody whom I ought to know, and[SEP]give me hints with what degree of favour to greet them.[EOT]
[SOT]“By the way,” he said, “you’re a Catholic, I suppose?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Not I,” I answered.[EOT]
[SOT]“Lord, he’s a heretic![SEP]” groaned Sapt, and forthwith he fell to a[SEP]rudimentary lesson in the practices and observances of the Romish faith.[EOT]
[SOT]“Luckily,” said he, “you won’t be expected to know much, for the King’s[SEP]notoriously lax and careless about such matters.[SEP]But you must be as[SEP]civil as butter to the Cardinal.[SEP]We hope to win him over, because he and[SEP]Michael have a standing quarrel about their precedence.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We were by now at the station.[SEP]Fritz had recovered nerve enough to[SEP]explain to the astonished station master that the King had changed his[SEP]plans.[SEP]The train steamed up.[SEP]We got into a first-class carriage, and[SEP]Sapt, leaning back on the cushions, went on with his lesson.[SEP]I looked at[SEP]my watch--the King’s watch it was, of course.[SEP]It was just eight.[EOT]
[SOT]“I wonder if they’ve gone to look for us,” I said.[EOT]
[SOT]“I hope they won’t find the King,” said Fritz nervously, and this time[SEP]it was Sapt who shrugged his shoulders.[EOT]
[SOT]The train travelled well, and at half-past nine, looking out of the[SEP]window, I saw the towers and spires of a great city.[EOT]
[SOT]“Your capital, my liege,” grinned old Sapt, with a wave of his hand,[SEP]and, leaning forward, he laid his finger on my pulse.[SEP]“A little too[SEP]quick,” said he, in his grumbling tone.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m not made of stone![SEP]” I exclaimed.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’ll do,” said he, with a nod.[SEP]“We must say Fritz here has caught the[SEP]ague.[SEP]Drain your flask, Fritz, for heaven’s sake, boy![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz did as he was bid.[EOT]
[SOT]“We’re an hour early,” said Sapt.[SEP]“We’ll send word forward for your[SEP]Majesty’s arrival, for there’ll be no one here to meet us yet.[SEP]And[SEP]meanwhile--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Meanwhile,” said I, “the King’ll be hanged if he doesn’t have some[SEP]breakfast.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Old Sapt chuckled, and held out his hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’re an Elphberg, every inch of you,” said he.[SEP]Then he paused, and[SEP]looking at us, said quietly, “God send we may be alive tonight![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Amen![SEP]” said Fritz von Tarlenheim.[EOT]
[SOT]The train stopped.[SEP]Fritz and Sapt leapt out, uncovered, and held the[SEP]door for me.[SEP]I choked down a lump that rose in my throat, settled my[SEP]helmet firmly on my head, and (I’m not ashamed to say it) breathed a[SEP]short prayer to God.[SEP]Then I stepped on the platform of the station at[SEP]Strelsau.[EOT]
[SOT]A moment later, all was bustle and confusion: men hurrying up, hats[SEP]in hand, and hurrying off again; men conducting me to the buffet; men[SEP]mounting and riding in hot haste to the quarters of the troops, to the[SEP]Cathedral, to the residence of Duke Michael.[SEP]Even as I swallowed the[SEP]last drop of my cup of coffee, the bells throughout all the city broke[SEP]out into a joyful peal, and the sound of a military band and of men[SEP]cheering smote upon my ear.[EOT]
[SOT]King Rudolf the Fifth was in his good city of Strelsau![SEP]And they shouted[SEP]outside--[EOT]
[SOT]“God save the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Old Sapt’s mouth wrinkled into a smile.[EOT]
[SOT]“God save ’em both![SEP]” he whispered.[SEP]“Courage, lad![SEP]” and I felt his hand[SEP]press my knee.[EOT]
[SOT]The Adventures of an Understudy[EOT]
[SOT]With Fritz von Tarlenheim and Colonel Sapt close behind me, I stepped[SEP]out of the buffet on to the platform.[SEP]The last thing I did was to feel[SEP]if my revolver were handy and my sword loose in the scabbard.[SEP]A gay[SEP]group of officers and high dignitaries stood awaiting me, at their head[SEP]a tall old man, covered with medals, and of military bearing.[SEP]He wore[SEP]the yellow and red ribbon of the Red Rose of Ruritania--which, by the[SEP]way, decorated my unworthy breast also.[EOT]
[SOT]“Marshal Strakencz,” whispered Sapt, and I knew that I was in the[SEP]presence of the most famous veteran of the Ruritanian army.[EOT]
[SOT]Just behind the Marshal stood a short spare man, in flowing robes of[SEP]black and crimson.[EOT]
[SOT]“The Chancellor of the Kingdom,” whispered Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]The Marshal greeted me in a few loyal words, and proceeded to deliver[SEP]an apology from the Duke of Strelsau.[SEP]The duke, it seemed, had been[SEP]afflicted with a sudden indisposition which made it impossible for him[SEP]to come to the station, but he craved leave to await his Majesty at the[SEP]Cathedral.[SEP]I expressed my concern, accepted the Marshal’s excuses very[SEP]suavely, and received the compliments of a large number of distinguished[SEP]personages.[SEP]No one betrayed the least suspicion, and I felt my nerve[SEP]returning and the agitated beating of my heart subsiding.[SEP]But Fritz[SEP]was still pale, and his hand shook like a leaf as he extended it to the[SEP]Marshal.[EOT]
[SOT]Presently we formed procession and took our way to the door of the[SEP]station.[SEP]Here I mounted my horse, the Marshal holding my stirrup.[SEP]The[SEP]civil dignitaries went off to their carriages, and I started to ride[SEP]through the streets with the Marshal on my right and Sapt (who, as my[SEP]chief aide-de-camp, was entitled to the place) on my left.[SEP]The city of[SEP]Strelsau is partly old and partly new.[SEP]Spacious modern boulevards and[SEP]residential quarters surround and embrace the narrow, tortuous, and[SEP]picturesque streets of the original town.[SEP]In the outer circles the upper[SEP]classes live; in the inner the shops are situated; and, behind their[SEP]prosperous fronts, lie hidden populous but wretched lanes and alleys,[SEP]filled with a poverty-stricken, turbulent, and (in large measure)[SEP]criminal class.[SEP]These social and local divisions corresponded, as I knew[SEP]from Sapt’s information, to another division more important to me.[SEP]The[SEP]New Town was for the King; but to the Old Town Michael of Strelsau was a[SEP]hope, a hero, and a darling.[EOT]
[SOT]The scene was very brilliant as we passed along the Grand Boulevard and[SEP]on to the great square where the Royal Palace stood.[SEP]Here I was in[SEP]the midst of my devoted adherents.[SEP]Every house was hung with red and[SEP]bedecked with flags and mottoes.[SEP]The streets were lined with raised[SEP]seats on each side, and I passed along, bowing this way and that, under[SEP]a shower of cheers, blessings, and waving handkerchiefs.[SEP]The balconies[SEP]were full of gaily dressed ladies, who clapped their hands and curtsied[SEP]and threw their brightest glances at me.[SEP]A torrent of red roses fell on[SEP]me; one bloom lodged in my horse’s mane, and I took it and stuck it in[SEP]my coat.[SEP]The Marshal smiled grimly.[SEP]I had stolen some glances at his[SEP]face, but he was too impassive to show me whether his sympathies were[SEP]with me or not.[EOT]
[SOT]“The red rose for the Elphbergs, Marshal,” said I gaily, and he nodded.[EOT]
[SOT]I have written “gaily,” and a strange word it must seem.[SEP]But the truth[SEP]is, that I was drunk with excitement.[SEP]At that moment I believed--I[SEP]almost believed--that I was in very truth the King; and, with a look of[SEP]laughing triumph, I raised my eyes to the beauty-laden balconies again[SEP].[SEP].[SEP].[SEP]and then I started.[SEP]For, looking down on me, with her handsome[SEP]face and proud smile, was the lady who had been my fellow[SEP]traveller--Antoinette de Mauban; and I saw her also start, and her lips[SEP]moved, and she leant forward and gazed at me.[SEP]And I, collecting myself,[SEP]met her eyes full and square, while again I felt my revolver.[SEP]Suppose[SEP]she had cried aloud, “That’s not the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Well, we went by; and then the Marshal, turning round in his saddle,[SEP]waved his hand, and the Cuirassiers closed round us, so that the crowd[SEP]could not come near me.[SEP]We were leaving my quarter and entering Duke[SEP]Michael’s, and this action of the Marshal’s showed me more clearly than[SEP]words what the state of feeling in the town must be.[SEP]But if Fate made me[SEP]a King, the least I could do was to play the part handsomely.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why this change in our order, Marshal?[SEP]” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]The Marshal bit his white moustache.[EOT]
[SOT]“It is more prudent, sire,” he murmured.[EOT]
[SOT]I drew rein.[EOT]
[SOT]“Let those in front ride on,” said I, “till they are fifty yards ahead.[SEP]But do you, Marshal, and Colonel Sapt and my friends, wait here till[SEP]I have ridden fifty yards.[SEP]And see that no one is nearer to me.[SEP]I will[SEP]have my people see that their King trusts them.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt laid his hand on my arm.[SEP]I shook him off.[SEP]The Marshal hesitated.[EOT]
[SOT]“Am I not understood?[SEP]” said I; and, biting his moustache again, he gave[SEP]the orders.[SEP]I saw old Sapt smiling into his beard, but he shook his[SEP]head at me.[SEP]If I had been killed in open day in the streets of Strelsau,[SEP]Sapt’s position would have been a difficult one.[EOT]
[SOT]Perhaps I ought to say that I was dressed all in white, except my boots.[SEP]I wore a silver helmet with gilt ornaments, and the broad ribbon of the[SEP]Rose looked well across my chest.[SEP]I should be paying a poor compliment[SEP]to the King if I did not set modesty aside and admit that I made a very[SEP]fine figure.[SEP]So the people thought; for when I, riding alone, entered[SEP]the dingy, sparsely decorated, sombre streets of the Old Town, there[SEP]was first a murmur, then a cheer, and a woman, from a window above a[SEP]cookshop, cried the old local saying:[EOT]
[SOT]“If he’s red, he’s right![SEP]” whereat I laughed and took off my helmet that[SEP]she might see that I was of the right colour and they cheered me again[SEP]at that.[EOT]
[SOT]It was more interesting riding thus alone, for I heard the comments of[SEP]the crowd.[EOT]
[SOT]“He looks paler than his wont,” said one.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’d look pale if you lived as he does,” was the highly disrespectful[SEP]retort.[EOT]
[SOT]“He’s a bigger man than I thought,” said another.[EOT]
[SOT]“So he had a good jaw under that beard after all,” commented a third.[EOT]
[SOT]“The pictures of him aren’t handsome enough,” declared a pretty girl,[SEP]taking great care that I should hear.[SEP]No doubt it was mere flattery.[EOT]
[SOT]But, in spite of these signs of approval and interest, the mass of[SEP]the people received me in silence and with sullen looks, and my dear[SEP]brother’s portrait ornamented most of the windows--which was an ironical[SEP]sort of greeting to the King.[SEP]I was quite glad that he had been spared[SEP]the unpleasant sight.[SEP]He was a man of quick temper, and perhaps he would[SEP]not have taken it so placidly as I did.[EOT]
[SOT]At last we were at the Cathedral.[SEP]Its great grey front, embellished[SEP]with hundreds of statues and boasting a pair of the finest oak doors in[SEP]Europe, rose for the first time before me, and the sudden sense of my[SEP]audacity almost overcame me.[SEP]Everything was in a mist as I dismounted.[SEP]I[SEP]saw the Marshal and Sapt dimly, and dimly the throng of gorgeously robed[SEP]priests who awaited me.[SEP]And my eyes were still dim as I walked up the[SEP]great nave, with the pealing of the organ in my ears.[SEP]I saw nothing of[SEP]the brilliant throng that filled it, I hardly distinguished the stately[SEP]figure of the Cardinal as he rose from the archiepiscopal throne to[SEP]greet me.[SEP]Two faces only stood out side by side clearly before my[SEP]eyes--the face of a girl, pale and lovely, surmounted by a crown of the[SEP]glorious Elphberg hair (for in a woman it is glorious), and the face[SEP]of a man, whose full-blooded red cheeks, black hair, and dark deep eyes[SEP]told me that at last I was in the presence of my brother, Black Michael.[SEP]And[SEP]when he saw me his red cheeks went pale all in a moment, and his helmet[SEP]fell with a clatter on the floor.[SEP]Till that moment I believe that he had[SEP]not realized that the King was in very truth come to Strelsau.[EOT]
[SOT]Of what followed next I remember nothing.[SEP]I knelt before the altar and[SEP]the Cardinal anointed my head.[SEP]Then I rose to my feet, and stretched out[SEP]my hand and took from him the crown of Ruritania and set it on my head,[SEP]and I swore the old oath of the King; and (if it were a sin, may it be[SEP]forgiven me) I received the Holy Sacrament there before them all.[SEP]Then[SEP]the great organ pealed out again, the Marshal bade the heralds proclaim[SEP]me, and Rudolf the Fifth was crowned King; of which imposing ceremony an[SEP]excellent picture hangs now in my dining-room.[SEP]The portrait of the King[SEP]is very good.[EOT]
[SOT]Then the lady with the pale face and the glorious hair, her train held[SEP]by two pages, stepped from her place and came to where I stood.[SEP]And a[SEP]herald cried:[EOT]
[SOT]“Her Royal Highness the Princess Flavia![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She curtsied low, and put her hand under mine and raised my hand and[SEP]kissed it.[SEP]And for an instant I thought what I had best do.[SEP]Then I[SEP]drew her to me and kissed her twice on the cheek, and she blushed red,[SEP]and--then his Eminence the Cardinal Archbishop slipped in front of Black[SEP]Michael, and kissed my hand and presented me with a letter from the[SEP]Pope--the first and last which I have received from that exalted[SEP]quarter![EOT]
[SOT]And then came the Duke of Strelsau.[SEP]His step trembled, I swear, and[SEP]he looked to the right and to the left, as a man looks who thinks on[SEP]flight; and his face was patched with red and white, and his hand shook[SEP]so that it jumped under mine, and I felt his lips dry and parched.[SEP]And[SEP]I glanced at Sapt, who was smiling again into his beard, and, resolutely[SEP]doing my duty in that station of life to which I had been marvellously[SEP]called, I took my dear Michael by both hands and kissed him on the[SEP]cheek.[SEP]I think we were both glad when that was over![EOT]
[SOT]But neither in the face of the princess nor in that of any other did I[SEP]see the least doubt or questioning.[SEP]Yet, had I and the King stood side[SEP]by side, she could have told us in an instant, or, at least, on a little[SEP]consideration.[SEP]But neither she nor anyone else dreamed or imagined that[SEP]I could be other than the King.[SEP]So the likeness served, and for an hour[SEP]I stood there, feeling as weary and blase as though I had been a king[SEP]all my life; and everybody kissed my hand, and the ambassadors paid me[SEP]their respects, among them old Lord Topham, at whose house in Grosvenor[SEP]Square I had danced a score of times.[SEP]Thank heaven, the old man was as[SEP]blind as a bat, and did not claim my acquaintance.[EOT]
[SOT]Then back we went through the streets to the Palace, and I heard them[SEP]cheering Black Michael; but he, Fritz told me, sat biting his nails like[SEP]a man in a reverie, and even his own friends said that he should have[SEP]made a braver show.[SEP]I was in a carriage now, side by side with the[SEP]Princess Flavia, and a rough fellow cried out:[EOT]
[SOT]“And when’s the wedding?[SEP]” and as he spoke another struck him in the[SEP]face, crying “Long live Duke Michael![SEP]” and the princess coloured--it was[SEP]an admirable tint--and looked straight in front of her.[EOT]
[SOT]Now I felt in a difficulty, because I had forgotten to ask Sapt the[SEP]state of my affections, or how far matters had gone between the princess[SEP]and myself.[SEP]Frankly, had I been the King, the further they had gone the[SEP]better should I have been pleased.[SEP]For I am not a slow-blooded man, and[SEP]I had not kissed Princess Flavia’s cheek for nothing.[SEP]These thoughts[SEP]passed through my head, but, not being sure of my ground, I said[SEP]nothing; and in a moment or two the princess, recovering her equanimity,[SEP]turned to me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Do you know, Rudolf,” said she, “you look somehow different today?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The fact was not surprising, but the remark was disquieting.[EOT]
[SOT]“You look,” she went on, “more sober, more sedate; you’re almost[SEP]careworn, and I declare you’re thinner.[SEP]Surely it’s not possible that[SEP]you’ve begun to take anything seriously?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The princess seemed to hold of the King much the same opinion that Lady[SEP]Burlesdon held of me.[EOT]
[SOT]I braced myself up to the conversation.[EOT]
[SOT]“Would that please you?[SEP]” I asked softly.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, you know my views,” said she, turning her eyes away.[EOT]
[SOT]“Whatever pleases you I try to do,” I said; and, as I saw her smile and[SEP]blush, I thought that I was playing the King’s hand very well for him.[SEP]So I continued and what I said was perfectly true:[EOT]
[SOT]“I assure you, my dear cousin, that nothing in my life has affected me[SEP]more than the reception I’ve been greeted with today.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She smiled brightly, but in an instant grew grave again, and whispered:[EOT]
[SOT]“Did you notice Michael?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes,” said I, adding, “he wasn’t enjoying himself.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Do be careful![SEP]” she went on.[SEP]“You don’t--indeed you don’t--keep enough[SEP]watch on him.[SEP]You know--”[EOT]
[SOT]“I know,” said I, “that he wants what I’ve got.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes.[SEP]Hush![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then--and I can’t justify it, for I committed the King far beyond what I[SEP]had a right to do--I suppose she carried me off my feet--I went on:[EOT]
[SOT]“And perhaps also something which I haven’t got yet, but hope to win[SEP]some day.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]This was my answer.[SEP]Had I been the King, I should have thought it[SEP]encouraging:[EOT]
[SOT]“Haven’t you enough responsibilities on you for one day, cousin?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Bang, bang![SEP]Blare, blare![SEP]We were at the Palace.[SEP]Guns were firing[SEP]and trumpets blowing.[SEP]Rows of lackeys stood waiting, and, handing the[SEP]princess up the broad marble staircase, I took formal possession, as[SEP]a crowned King, of the House of my ancestors, and sat down at my own[SEP]table, with my cousin on my right hand, on her other side Black Michael,[SEP]and on my left his Eminence the Cardinal.[SEP]Behind my chair stood Sapt;[SEP]and at the end of the table, I saw Fritz von Tarlenheim drain to the[SEP]bottom his glass of champagne rather sooner than he decently should.[EOT]
[SOT]I wondered what the King of Ruritania was doing.[EOT]
[SOT]The Secret of a Cellar[EOT]
[SOT]We were in the King’s dressing-room--Fritz von Tarlenheim, Sapt, and I.[SEP]I flung myself exhausted into an armchair.[SEP]Sapt lit his pipe.[SEP]He uttered[SEP]no congratulations on the marvellous success of our wild risk, but his[SEP]whole bearing was eloquent of satisfaction.[SEP]The triumph, aided perhaps[SEP]by good wine, had made a new man of Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“What a day for you to remember![SEP]” he cried.[SEP]“Gad, I’d like to be King[SEP]for twelve hours myself![SEP]But, Rassendyll, you mustn’t throw your heart[SEP]too much into the part.[SEP]I don’t wonder Black Michael looked blacker than[SEP]ever--you and the princess had so much to say to one another.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“How beautiful she is![SEP]” I exclaimed.[EOT]
[SOT]“Never mind the woman,” growled Sapt.[SEP]“Are you ready to start?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes,” said I, with a sigh.[EOT]
[SOT]It was five o’clock, and at twelve I should be no more than Rudolf[SEP]Rassendyll.[SEP]I remarked on it in a joking tone.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’ll be lucky,” observed Sapt grimly, “if you’re not the late Rudolf[SEP]Rassendyll.[SEP]By Heaven![SEP]I feel my head wobbling on my shoulders every[SEP]minute you’re in the city.[SEP]Do you know, friend, that Michael has had[SEP]news from Zenda?[SEP]He went into a room alone to read it--and he came out[SEP]looking like a man dazed.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m ready,” said I, this news making me none the more eager to linger.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt sat down.[EOT]
[SOT]“I must write us an order to leave the city.[SEP]Michael’s Governor, you[SEP]know, and we must be prepared for hindrances.[SEP]You must sign the order.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“My dear colonel, I’ve not been bred a forger![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Out of his pocket Sapt produced a piece of paper.[EOT]
[SOT]“There’s the King’s signature,” he said, “and here,” he went on, after[SEP]another search in his pocket, “is some tracing paper.[SEP]If you can’t[SEP]manage a ‘Rudolf’ in ten minutes, why--I can.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Your education has been more comprehensive than mine,” said I.[SEP]“You[SEP]write it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And a very tolerable forgery did this versatile hero produce.[EOT]
[SOT]“Now, Fritz,” said he, “the King goes to bed.[SEP]He is upset.[SEP]No one is to[SEP]see him till nine o’clock tomorrow.[SEP]You understand--no one?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I understand,” answered Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“Michael may come, and claim immediate audience.[SEP]You’ll answer that only[SEP]princes of the blood are entitled to it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That’ll annoy Michael,” laughed Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“You quite understand?[SEP]” asked Sapt again.[SEP]“If the door of this room is[SEP]opened while we’re away, you’re not to be alive to tell us about it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I need no schooling, colonel,” said Fritz, a trifle haughtily.[EOT]
[SOT]“Here, wrap yourself in this big cloak,” Sapt continued to me, “and[SEP]put on this flat cap.[SEP]My orderly rides with me to the hunting-lodge[SEP]tonight.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“There’s an obstacle,” I observed.[SEP]“The horse doesn’t live that can[SEP]carry me forty miles.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, yes, he does--two of him: one here--one at the lodge.[SEP]Now, are you[SEP]ready?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m ready,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz held out his hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“In case,” said he; and we shook hands heartily.[EOT]
[SOT]“Damn your sentiment![SEP]” growled Sapt.[SEP]“Come along.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He went, not to the door, but to a panel in the wall.[EOT]
[SOT]“In the old King’s time,” said he, “I knew this way well.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I followed him, and we walked, as I should estimate, near two hundred[SEP]yards along a narrow passage.[SEP]Then we came to a stout oak door.[SEP]Sapt[SEP]unlocked it.[SEP]We passed through, and found ourselves in a quiet street[SEP]that ran along the back of the Palace gardens.[SEP]A man was waiting for us[SEP]with two horses.[SEP]One was a magnificent bay, up to any weight; the other[SEP]a sturdy brown.[SEP]Sapt signed to me to mount the bay.[SEP]Without a word[SEP]to the man, we mounted and rode away.[SEP]The town was full of noise and[SEP]merriment, but we took secluded ways.[SEP]My cloak was wrapped over half[SEP]my face; the capacious flat cap hid every lock of my tell-tale hair.[SEP]By[SEP]Sapt’s directions, I crouched on my saddle, and rode with such a round[SEP]back as I hope never to exhibit on a horse again.[SEP]Down a long narrow[SEP]lane we went, meeting some wanderers and some roisterers; and, as we[SEP]rode, we heard the Cathedral bells still clanging out their welcome to[SEP]the King.[SEP]It was half-past six, and still light.[SEP]At last we came to the[SEP]city wall and to a gate.[EOT]
[SOT]“Have your weapon ready,” whispered Sapt.[SEP]“We must stop his mouth, if he[SEP]talks.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I put my hand on my revolver.[SEP]Sapt hailed the doorkeeper.[SEP]The stars[SEP]fought for us![SEP]A little girl of fourteen tripped out.[EOT]
[SOT]“Please, sir, father’s gone to see the King.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He’d better have stayed here,” said Sapt to me, grinning.[EOT]
[SOT]“But he said I wasn’t to open the gate, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Did he, my dear?[SEP]” said Sapt, dismounting.[SEP]“Then give me the key.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The key was in the child’s hand.[SEP]Sapt gave her a crown.[EOT]
[SOT]“Here’s an order from the King.[SEP]Show it to your father.[SEP]Orderly, open[SEP]the gate![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I leapt down.[SEP]Between us we rolled back the great gate, led our horses[SEP]out, and closed it again.[EOT]
[SOT]“I shall be sorry for the doorkeeper if Michael finds out that he wasn’t[SEP]there.[SEP]Now then, lad, for a canter.[SEP]We mustn’t go too fast while we’re[SEP]near the town.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Once, however, outside the city, we ran little danger, for everybody[SEP]else was inside, merry-making; and as the evening fell we quickened our[SEP]pace, my splendid horse bounding along under me as though I had been a[SEP]feather.[SEP]It was a fine night, and presently the moon appeared.[SEP]We talked[SEP]little on the way, and chiefly about the progress we were making.[EOT]
[SOT]“I wonder what the duke’s despatches told him,” said I, once.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, I wonder![SEP]” responded Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]We stopped for a draught of wine and to bait our horses, losing half an[SEP]hour thus.[SEP]I dared not go into the inn, and stayed with the horses[SEP]in the stable.[SEP]Then we went ahead again, and had covered some[SEP]five-and-twenty miles, when Sapt abruptly stopped.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hark![SEP]” he cried.[EOT]
[SOT]I listened.[SEP]Away, far behind us, in the still of the evening--it was[SEP]just half-past nine--we heard the beat of horses’ hoofs.[SEP]The wind[SEP]blowing strong behind us, carried the sound.[SEP]I glanced at Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“Come on![SEP]” he cried, and spurred his horse into a gallop.[SEP]When we next[SEP]paused to listen, the hoof-beats were not audible, and we relaxed our[SEP]pace.[SEP]Then we heard them again.[SEP]Sapt jumped down and laid his ear to the[SEP]ground.[EOT]
[SOT]“There are two,” he said.[SEP]“They’re only a mile behind.[SEP]Thank God the[SEP]road curves in and out, and the wind’s our way.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We galloped on.[SEP]We seemed to be holding our own.[SEP]We had entered the[SEP]outskirts of the forest of Zenda, and the trees, closing in behind us as[SEP]the track zigged and zagged, prevented us seeing our pursuers, and them[SEP]from seeing us.[EOT]
[SOT]Another half-hour brought us to a divide of the road.[SEP]Sapt drew rein.[EOT]
[SOT]“To the right is our road,” he said.[SEP]“To the left, to the Castle.[SEP]Each[SEP]about eight miles.[SEP]Get down.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But they’ll be on us![SEP]” I cried.[EOT]
[SOT]“Get down![SEP]” he repeated brusquely; and I obeyed.[SEP]The wood was dense up[SEP]to the very edge of the road.[SEP]We led our horses into the covert, bound[SEP]handkerchiefs over their eyes, and stood beside them.[EOT]
[SOT]“You want to see who they are?[SEP]” I whispered.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, and where they’re going,” he answered.[EOT]
[SOT]I saw that his revolver was in his hand.[EOT]
[SOT]Nearer and nearer came the hoofs.[SEP]The moon shone out now clear and full,[SEP]so that the road was white with it.[SEP]The ground was hard, and we had left[SEP]no traces.[EOT]
[SOT]“Here they come![SEP]” whispered Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s the duke![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I thought so,” he answered.[EOT]
[SOT]It was the duke; and with him a burly fellow whom I knew well, and who[SEP]had cause to know me afterwards--Max Holf, brother to Johann the keeper,[SEP]and body-servant to his Highness.[SEP]They were up to us: the duke reined[SEP]up.[SEP]I saw Sapt’s finger curl lovingly towards the trigger.[SEP]I believe[SEP]he would have given ten years of his life for a shot; and he could have[SEP]picked off Black Michael as easily as I could a barn-door fowl in a[SEP]farmyard.[SEP]I laid my hand on his arm.[SEP]He nodded reassuringly: he was[SEP]always ready to sacrifice inclination to duty.[EOT]
[SOT]“Which way?[SEP]” asked Black Michael.[EOT]
[SOT]“To the Castle, your Highness,” urged his companion.[SEP]“There we shall[SEP]learn the truth.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]For an instant the duke hesitated.[EOT]
[SOT]“I thought I heard hoofs,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]“I think not, your Highness.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why shouldn’t we go to the lodge?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I fear a trap.[SEP]If all is well, why go to the lodge?[SEP]If not, it’s a[SEP]snare to trap us.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Suddenly the duke’s horse neighed.[SEP]In an instant we folded our cloaks[SEP]close round our horses’ heads, and, holding them thus, covered the duke[SEP]and his attendant with our revolvers.[SEP]If they had found us, they had[SEP]been dead men, or our prisoners.[EOT]
[SOT]Michael waited a moment longer.[SEP]Then he cried:[EOT]
[SOT]“To Zenda, then![SEP]” and setting spurs to his horse, galloped on.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt raised his weapon after him, and there was such an expression[SEP]of wistful regret on his face that I had much ado not to burst out[SEP]laughing.[EOT]
[SOT]For ten minutes we stayed where we were.[EOT]
[SOT]“You see,” said Sapt, “they’ve sent him news that all is well.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What does that mean?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“God knows,” said Sapt, frowning heavily.[SEP]“But it’s brought him from[SEP]Strelsau in a rare puzzle.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then we mounted, and rode as fast as our weary horses could lay their[SEP]feet to the ground.[SEP]For those last eight miles we spoke no more.[SEP]Our[SEP]minds were full of apprehension.[SEP]“All is well.[SEP]” What did it mean?[SEP]Was[SEP]all well with the King?[EOT]
[SOT]At last the lodge came in sight.[SEP]Spurring our horses to a last gallop,[SEP]we rode up to the gate.[SEP]All was still and quiet.[SEP]Not a soul came to meet[SEP]us.[SEP]We dismounted in haste.[SEP]Suddenly Sapt caught me by the arm.[EOT]
[SOT]“Look there![SEP]” he said, pointing to the ground.[EOT]
[SOT]I looked down.[SEP]At my feet lay five or six silk handkerchiefs, torn and[SEP]slashed and rent.[SEP]I turned to him questioningly.[EOT]
[SOT]“They’re what I tied the old woman up with,” said he.[SEP]“Fasten the[SEP]horses, and come along.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The handle of the door turned without resistance.[SEP]We passed into the[SEP]room which had been the scene of last night’s bout.[SEP]It was still strewn[SEP]with the remnants of our meal and with empty bottles.[EOT]
[SOT]“Come on,” cried Sapt, whose marvellous composure had at last almost[SEP]given way.[EOT]
[SOT]We rushed down the passage towards the cellars.[SEP]The door of the[SEP]coal-cellar stood wide open.[EOT]
[SOT]“They found the old woman,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“You might have known that from the handkerchiefs,” he said.[EOT]
[SOT]Then we came opposite the door of the wine-cellar.[SEP]It was shut.[SEP]It[SEP]looked in all respects as it had looked when we left it that morning.[EOT]
[SOT]“Come, it’s all right,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]A loud oath from Sapt rang out.[SEP]His face turned pale, and he pointed[SEP]again at the floor.[SEP]From under the door a red stain had spread over the[SEP]floor of the passage and dried there.[SEP]Sapt sank against the opposite[SEP]wall.[SEP]I tried the door.[SEP]It was locked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Where’s Josef?[SEP]” muttered Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“Where’s the King?[SEP]” I responded.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt took out a flask and put it to his lips.[SEP]I ran back to the[SEP]dining-room, and seized a heavy poker from the fireplace.[SEP]In my terror[SEP]and excitement I rained blows on the lock of the door, and I fired a[SEP]cartridge into it.[SEP]It gave way, and the door swung open.[EOT]
[SOT]“Give me a light,” said I; but Sapt still leant against the wall.[EOT]
[SOT]He was, of course, more moved than I, for he loved his master.[SEP]Afraid[SEP]for himself he was not--no man ever saw him that; but to think what[SEP]might lie in that dark cellar was enough to turn any man’s face pale.[SEP]I went myself, and took a silver candlestick from the dining-table and[SEP]struck a light, and, as I returned, I felt the hot wax drip on my naked[SEP]hand as the candle swayed to and fro; so that I cannot afford to despise[SEP]Colonel Sapt for his agitation.[EOT]
[SOT]I came to the door of the cellar.[SEP]The red stain turning more and more to[SEP]a dull brown, stretched inside.[SEP]I walked two yards into the cellar, and[SEP]held the candle high above my head.[SEP]I saw the full bins of wine; I saw[SEP]spiders crawling on the walls; I saw, too, a couple of empty bottles[SEP]lying on the floor; and then, away in the corner, I saw the body of a[SEP]man, lying flat on his back, with his arms stretched wide, and a crimson[SEP]gash across his throat.[SEP]I walked to him and knelt down beside him, and[SEP]commended to God the soul of a faithful man.[SEP]For it was the body of[SEP]Josef, the little servant, slain in guarding the King.[EOT]
[SOT]I felt a hand on my shoulders, and, turning, saw Sapt, eyes glaring and[SEP]terror-struck, beside me.[EOT]
[SOT]“The King?[SEP]My God![SEP]the King?[SEP]” he whispered hoarsely.[EOT]
[SOT]I threw the candle’s gleam over every inch of the cellar.[EOT]
[SOT]“The King is not here,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]His Majesty Sleeps in Strelsau[EOT]
[SOT]I put my arm round Sapt’s waist and supported him out of the cellar,[SEP]drawing the battered door close after me.[SEP]For ten minutes or more we sat[SEP]silent in the dining-room.[SEP]Then old Sapt rubbed his knuckles into his[SEP]eyes, gave one great gasp, and was himself again.[SEP]As the clock on the[SEP]mantelpiece struck one he stamped his foot on the floor, saying:[EOT]
[SOT]“They’ve got the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes,” said I, “‘all’s well![SEP]’ as Black Michael’s despatch said.[SEP]What[SEP]a moment it must have been for him when the royal salutes fired at[SEP]Strelsau this morning![SEP]I wonder when he got the message?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It must have been sent in the morning,” said Sapt.[SEP]“They must have sent[SEP]it before news of your arrival at Strelsau reached Zenda--I suppose it[SEP]came from Zenda.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And he’s carried it about all day![SEP]” I exclaimed.[SEP]“Upon my honour, I’m[SEP]not the only man who’s had a trying day![SEP]What did he think, Sapt?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What does that matter?[SEP]What does he think, lad, now?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I rose to my feet.[EOT]
[SOT]“We must get back,” I said, “and rouse every soldier in Strelsau.[SEP]We[SEP]ought to be in pursuit of Michael before midday.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Old Sapt pulled out his pipe and carefully lit it from the candle which[SEP]guttered on the table.[EOT]
[SOT]“The King may be murdered while we sit here![SEP]” I urged.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt smoked on for a moment in silence.[EOT]
[SOT]“That cursed old woman![SEP]” he broke out.[SEP]“She must have attracted their[SEP]attention somehow.[SEP]I see the game.[SEP]They came up to kidnap the King,[SEP]and--as I say--somehow they found him.[SEP]If you hadn’t gone to Strelsau,[SEP]you and I and Fritz had been in heaven by now![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And the King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Who knows where the King is now?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Come, let’s be off![SEP]” said I; but he sat still.[SEP]And suddenly he burst[SEP]into one of his grating chuckles:[EOT]
[SOT]“By Jove, we’ve shaken up Black Michael![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Come, come![SEP]” I repeated impatiently.[EOT]
[SOT]“And we’ll shake him up a bit more,” he added, a cunning smile[SEP]broadening on his wrinkled, weather-beaten face, and his teeth working[SEP]on an end of his grizzled moustache.[SEP]“Ay, lad, we’ll go back to[SEP]Strelsau.[SEP]The King shall be in his capital again tomorrow.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The crowned King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You’re mad![SEP]” I cried.[EOT]
[SOT]“If we go back and tell the trick we played, what would you give for our[SEP]lives?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Just what they’re worth,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“And for the King’s throne?[SEP]Do you think that the nobles and the people[SEP]will enjoy being fooled as you’ve fooled them?[SEP]Do you think they’ll love[SEP]a King who was too drunk to be crowned, and sent a servant to personate[SEP]him?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He was drugged--and I’m no servant.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Mine will be Black Michael’s version.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He rose, came to me, and laid his hand on my shoulder.[EOT]
[SOT]“Lad,” he said, “if you play the man, you may save the King yet.[SEP]Go back[SEP]and keep his throne warm for him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But the duke knows--the villains he has employed know--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, but they can’t speak![SEP]” roared Sapt in grim triumph.[EOT]
[SOT]“We’ve got ’em![SEP]How can they denounce you without denouncing themselves?[SEP]This is not the King, because we kidnapped the King and murdered his[SEP]servant.[SEP]Can they say that?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The position flashed on me.[SEP]Whether Michael knew me or not, he could not[SEP]speak.[SEP]Unless he produced the King, what could he do?[SEP]And if he produced[SEP]the King, where was he?[SEP]For a moment I was carried away headlong; but in[SEP]an instant the difficulties came strong upon me.[EOT]
[SOT]“I must be found out,” I urged.[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps; but every hour’s something.[SEP]Above all, we must have a King in[SEP]Strelsau, or the city will be Michael’s in four-and-twenty hours, and[SEP]what would the King’s life be worth then--or his throne?[SEP]Lad, you must[SEP]do it![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Suppose they kill the King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They’ll kill him, if you don’t.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Sapt, suppose they have killed the King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Then, by heaven, you’re as good an Elphberg as Black Michael, and you[SEP]shall reign in Ruritania![SEP]But I don’t believe they have; nor will they[SEP]kill him if you’re on the throne.[SEP]Will they kill him, to put you in?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was a wild plan--wilder even and more hopeless than the trick we[SEP]had already carried through; but as I listened to Sapt I saw the strong[SEP]points in our game.[SEP]And then I was a young man and I loved action, and I[SEP]was offered such a hand in such a game as perhaps never man played yet.[EOT]
[SOT]“I shall be found out,” I said.[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps,” said Sapt.[SEP]“Come![SEP]to Strelsau![SEP]We shall be caught like rats[SEP]in a trap if we stay here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Sapt,” I cried, “I’ll try it![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well played![SEP]” said he.[SEP]“I hope they’ve left us the horses.[SEP]I’ll go and[SEP]see.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We must bury that poor fellow,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“No time,” said Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll do it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Hang you![SEP]” he grinned.[SEP]“I make you a King, and--Well, do it.[SEP]Go and[SEP]fetch him, while I look to the horses.[SEP]He can’t lie very deep, but I[SEP]doubt if he’ll care about that.[SEP]Poor little Josef![SEP]He was an honest bit[SEP]of a man.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He went out, and I went to the cellar.[SEP]I raised poor Josef in my arms[SEP]and bore him into the passage and thence towards the door of the house.[SEP]Just inside I laid him down, remembering that I must find spades for our[SEP]task.[SEP]At this instant Sapt came up.[EOT]
[SOT]“The horses are all right; there’s the own brother to the one that[SEP]brought you here.[SEP]But you may save yourself that job.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll not go before he’s buried.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, you will.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Not I, Colonel Sapt; not for all Ruritania.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You fool![SEP]” said he.[SEP]“Come here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He drew me to the door.[SEP]The moon was sinking, but about three hundred[SEP]yards away, coming along the road from Zenda, I made out a party of men.[SEP]There were seven or eight of them; four were on horseback and the rest[SEP]were walking, and I saw that they carried long implements, which I[SEP]guessed to be spades and mattocks, on their shoulders.[EOT]
[SOT]“They’ll save you the trouble,” said Sapt.[SEP]“Come along.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He was right.[SEP]The approaching party must, beyond doubt, be Duke[SEP]Michael’s men, come to remove the traces of their evil work.[SEP]I hesitated[SEP]no longer, but an irresistible desire seized me.[EOT]
[SOT]Pointing to the corpse of poor little Josef, I said to Sapt:[EOT]
[SOT]“Colonel, we ought to strike a blow for him![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You’d like to give him some company, eh![SEP]But it’s too risky work, your[SEP]Majesty.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I must have a slap at ’em,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt wavered.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well,” said he, “it’s not business, you know; but you’ve been a good[SEP]boy--and if we come to grief, why, hang me, it’ll save us lot of[SEP]thinking![SEP]I’ll show you how to touch them.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He cautiously closed the open chink of the door.[EOT]
[SOT]Then we retreated through the house and made our way to the back[SEP]entrance.[SEP]Here our horses were standing.[SEP]A carriage-drive swept all[SEP]round the lodge.[EOT]
[SOT]“Revolver ready?[SEP]” asked Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“No; steel for me,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Gad, you’re thirsty tonight,” chuckled Sapt.[SEP]“So be it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We mounted, drawing our swords, and waited silently for a minute or two.[SEP]Then we heard the tramp of men on the drive the other side of the house.[SEP]They came to a stand, and one cried:[EOT]
[SOT]“Now then, fetch him out![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Now![SEP]” whispered Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]Driving the spurs into our horses, we rushed at a gallop round the[SEP]house, and in a moment we were among the ruffians.[SEP]Sapt told me[SEP]afterwards that he killed a man, and I believe him; but I saw no more of[SEP]him.[SEP]With a cut, I split the head of a fellow on a brown horse, and he[SEP]fell to the ground.[SEP]Then I found myself opposite a big man, and I was[SEP]half conscious of another to my right.[SEP]It was too warm to stay, and with[SEP]a simultaneous action I drove my spurs into my horse again and my sword[SEP]full into the big man’s breast.[SEP]His bullet whizzed past my ear--I could[SEP]almost swear it touched it.[SEP]I wrenched at the sword, but it would not[SEP]come, and I dropped it and galloped after Sapt, whom I now saw about[SEP]twenty yards ahead.[SEP]I waved my hand in farewell, and dropped it a second[SEP]later with a yell, for a bullet had grazed my finger and I felt the[SEP]blood.[SEP]Old Sapt turned round in the saddle.[SEP]Someone fired again, but[SEP]they had no rifles, and we were out of range.[SEP]Sapt fell to laughing.[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s one to me and two to you, with decent luck,” said he.[SEP]“Little[SEP]Josef will have company.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, they’ll be a _parti carrée_,” said I.[SEP]My blood was up, and I[SEP]rejoiced to have killed them.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, a pleasant night’s work to the rest![SEP]” said he.[SEP]“I wonder if they[SEP]noticed you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The big fellow did; as I stuck him I heard him cry, ‘The King![SEP]’”[EOT]
[SOT]“Good![SEP]good![SEP]Oh, we’ll give Black Michael some work before we’ve done![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Pausing an instant, we made a bandage for my wounded finger, which was[SEP]bleeding freely and ached severely, the bone being much bruised.[SEP]Then we[SEP]rode on, asking of our good horses all that was in them.[SEP]The excitement[SEP]of the fight and of our great resolve died away, and we rode in gloomy[SEP]silence.[SEP]Day broke clear and cold.[SEP]We found a farmer just up, and made[SEP]him give us sustenance for ourselves and our horses.[SEP]I, feigning a[SEP]toothache, muffled my face closely.[SEP]Then ahead again, till Strelsau lay[SEP]before us.[SEP]It was eight o’clock or nearing nine, and the gates were all[SEP]open, as they always were save when the duke’s caprice or intrigues shut[SEP]them.[SEP]We rode in by the same way as we had come out the evening before,[SEP]all four of us--the men and the horses--wearied and jaded.[SEP]The streets[SEP]were even quieter than when we had gone: everyone was sleeping off last[SEP]night’s revelry, and we met hardly a soul till we reached the little[SEP]gate of the Palace.[SEP]There Sapt’s old groom was waiting for us.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is all well, sir?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“All’s well,” said Sapt, and the man, coming to me, took my hand to[SEP]kiss.[EOT]
[SOT]“The King’s hurt![SEP]” he cried.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s nothing,” said I, as I dismounted; “I caught my finger in the[SEP]door.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Remember--silence![SEP]” said Sapt.[SEP]“Ah![SEP]but, my good Freyler, I do not need[SEP]to tell you that![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The old fellow shrugged his shoulders.[EOT]
[SOT]“All young men like to ride abroad now and again, why not the King?[SEP]”[SEP]said he; and Sapt’s laugh left his opinion of my motives undisturbed.[EOT]
[SOT]“You should always trust a man,” observed Sapt, fitting the key in the[SEP]lock, “just as far as you must.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We went in and reached the dressing-room.[SEP]Flinging open the door, we saw[SEP]Fritz von Tarlenheim stretched, fully dressed, on the sofa.[SEP]He seemed to[SEP]have been sleeping, but our entry woke him.[SEP]He leapt to his feet, gave[SEP]one glance at me, and with a joyful cry, threw himself on his knees[SEP]before me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Thank God, sire![SEP]thank God, you’re safe![SEP]” he cried, stretching his hand[SEP]up to catch hold of mine.[EOT]
[SOT]I confess that I was moved.[SEP]This King, whatever his faults, made people[SEP]love him.[SEP]For a moment I could not bear to speak or break the poor[SEP]fellow’s illusion.[SEP]But tough old Sapt had no such feeling.[SEP]He slapped[SEP]his hand on his thigh delightedly.[EOT]
[SOT]“Bravo, lad![SEP]” cried he.[SEP]“We shall do![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz looked up in bewilderment.[SEP]I held out my hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’re wounded, sire![SEP]” he exclaimed.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s only a scratch,” said I, “but--” I paused.[EOT]
[SOT]He rose to his feet with a bewildered air.[SEP]Holding my hand, he looked[SEP]me up and down, and down and up.[SEP]Then suddenly he dropped my hand and[SEP]reeled back.[EOT]
[SOT]“Where’s the King?[SEP]Where’s the King?[SEP]” he cried.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hush, you fool![SEP]” hissed Sapt.[SEP]“Not so loud![SEP]Here’s the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]A knock sounded on the door.[SEP]Sapt seized me by the hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“Here, quick, to the bedroom![SEP]Off with your cap and boots.[SEP]Get into bed.[SEP]Cover everything up.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I did as I was bid.[SEP]A moment later Sapt looked in, nodded, grinned, and[SEP]introduced an extremely smart and deferential young gentleman, who came[SEP]up to my bedside, bowing again and again, and informed me that he was[SEP]of the household of the Princess Flavia, and that her Royal Highness[SEP]had sent him especially to enquire how the King’s health was after the[SEP]fatigues which his Majesty had undergone yesterday.[EOT]
[SOT]“My best thanks, sir, to my cousin,” said I; “and tell her Royal[SEP]Highness that I was never better in my life.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The King,” added old Sapt (who, I began to find, loved a good lie for[SEP]its own sake), “has slept without a break all night.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The young gentleman (he reminded me of “Osric” in Hamlet) bowed himself[SEP]out again.[SEP]The farce was over, and Fritz von Tarlenheim’s pale face[SEP]recalled us to reality--though, in faith, the farce had to be reality[SEP]for us now.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is the King dead?[SEP]” he whispered.[EOT]
[SOT]“Please God, no,” said I.[SEP]“But he’s in the hands of Black Michael![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]A Fair Cousin and a Dark Brother[EOT]
[SOT]A real king’s life is perhaps a hard one; but a pretended king’s is,[SEP]I warrant, much harder.[SEP]On the next day, Sapt instructed me in my[SEP]duties--what I ought to do and what I ought to know--for three hours;[SEP]then I snatched breakfast, with Sapt still opposite me, telling me that[SEP]the King always took white wine in the morning and was known to detest[SEP]all highly seasoned dishes.[SEP]Then came the Chancellor, for another three[SEP]hours; and to him I had to explain that the hurt to my finger (we turned[SEP]that bullet to happy account) prevented me from writing--whence arose[SEP]great to-do, hunting of precedents and so forth, ending in my “making[SEP]my mark,” and the Chancellor attesting it with a superfluity of solemn[SEP]oaths.[SEP]Then the French ambassador was introduced, to present his[SEP]credentials; here my ignorance was of no importance, as the King would[SEP]have been equally raw to the business (we worked through the whole _corps[SEP]diplomatique_ in the next few days, a demise of the Crown necessitating[SEP]all this bother).[EOT]
[SOT]Then, at last, I was left alone.[SEP]I called my new servant (we had chosen,[SEP]to succeed poor Josef, a young man who had never known the King), had a[SEP]brandy-and-soda brought to me, and observed to Sapt that I trusted that[SEP]I might now have a rest.[SEP]Fritz von Tarlenheim was standing by.[EOT]
[SOT]“By heaven![SEP]” he cried, “we waste time.[SEP]Aren’t we going to throw Black[SEP]Michael by the heels?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Gently, my son, gently,” said Sapt, knitting his brows.[SEP]“It would be[SEP]a pleasure, but it might cost us dear.[SEP]Would Michael fall and leave the[SEP]King alive?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And,” I suggested, “while the King is here in Strelsau, on his throne,[SEP]what grievance has he against his dear brother Michael?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Are we to do nothing, then?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We’re to do nothing stupid,” growled Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“In fact, Fritz,” said I, “I am reminded of a situation in one of our[SEP]English plays--The Critic--have you heard of it?[SEP]Or, if you like, of two[SEP]men, each covering the other with a revolver.[SEP]For I can’t expose Michael[SEP]without exposing myself--”[EOT]
[SOT]“And the King,” put in Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“And, hang me if Michael won’t expose himself, if he tries to expose[SEP]me![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s very pretty,” said old Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“If I’m found out,” I pursued, “I will make a clean breast of it, and[SEP]fight it out with the duke; but at present I’m waiting for a move from[SEP]him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He’ll kill the King,” said Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“Not he,” said Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“Half of the Six are in Strelsau,” said Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“Only half?[SEP]You’re sure?[SEP]” asked Sapt eagerly.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes--only half.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Then the King’s alive, for the other three are guarding him![SEP]” cried[SEP]Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes--you’re right![SEP]” exclaimed Fritz, his face brightening.[SEP]“If the[SEP]King were dead and buried, they’d all be here with Michael.[SEP]You know[SEP]Michael’s back, colonel?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I know, curse him![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Gentlemen, gentlemen,” said I, “who are the Six?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I think you’ll make their acquaintance soon,” said Sapt.[SEP]“They are six[SEP]gentlemen whom Michael maintains in his household: they belong to him[SEP]body and soul.[SEP]There are three Ruritanians; then there’s a Frenchman, a[SEP]Belgian, and one of your countrymen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They’d all cut a throat if Michael told them,” said Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps they’ll cut mine,” I suggested.[EOT]
[SOT]“Nothing more likely,” agreed Sapt.[SEP]“Who are here, Fritz?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“De Gautet, Bersonin, and Detchard.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The foreigners![SEP]It’s as plain as a pikestaff.[SEP]He’s brought them, and[SEP]left the Ruritanians with the King; that’s because he wants to commit[SEP]the Ruritanians as deep as he can.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They were none of them among our friends at the lodge, then?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“I wish they had been,” said Sapt wistfully.[SEP]“They had been, not six,[SEP]but four, by now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I had already developed one attribute of royalty--a feeling that I need[SEP]not reveal all my mind or my secret designs even to my intimate friends.[SEP]I had fully resolved on my course of action.[SEP]I meant to make myself[SEP]as popular as I could, and at the same time to show no disfavour to[SEP]Michael.[SEP]By these means I hoped to allay the hostility of his adherents,[SEP]and make it appear, if an open conflict came about, that he was[SEP]ungrateful and not oppressed.[EOT]
[SOT]Yet an open conflict was not what I hoped for.[EOT]
[SOT]The King’s interest demanded secrecy; and while secrecy lasted, I had[SEP]a fine game to play in Strelsau, Michael should not grow stronger for[SEP]delay![EOT]
[SOT]I ordered my horse, and, attended by Fritz von Tarlenheim, rode in the[SEP]grand new avenue of the Royal Park, returning all the salutes which I[SEP]received with punctilious politeness.[SEP]Then I rode through a few of the[SEP]streets, stopped and bought flowers of a pretty girl, paying her with[SEP]a piece of gold; and then, having attracted the desired amount of[SEP]attention (for I had a trail of half a thousand people after me), I rode[SEP]to the residence of the Princess Flavia, and asked if she would[SEP]receive me.[SEP]This step created much interest, and was met with shouts of[SEP]approval.[SEP]The princess was very popular, and the Chancellor himself had[SEP]not scrupled to hint to me that the more I pressed my suit, and the more[SEP]rapidly I brought it to a prosperous conclusion, the stronger should I[SEP]be in the affection of my subjects.[SEP]The Chancellor, of course, did not[SEP]understand the difficulties which lay in the way of following his loyal[SEP]and excellent advice.[SEP]However, I thought I could do no harm by calling;[SEP]and in this view Fritz supported me with a cordiality that surprised me,[SEP]until he confessed that he also had his motives for liking a visit to[SEP]the princess’s house, which motive was no other than a great desire to[SEP]see the princess’s lady-in-waiting and bosom friend, the Countess Helga[SEP]von Strofzin.[EOT]
[SOT]Etiquette seconded Fritz’s hopes.[SEP]While I was ushered into the[SEP]princess’s room, he remained with the countess in the ante-chamber: in[SEP]spite of the people and servants who were hanging about, I doubt not[SEP]that they managed a _tête-à-tête;_ but I had no leisure to think of them,[SEP]for I was playing the most delicate move in all my difficult game.[SEP]I had[SEP]to keep the princess devoted to me--and yet indifferent to me: I had to[SEP]show affection for her--and not feel it.[SEP]I had to make love for another,[SEP]and that to a girl who--princess or no princess--was the most beautiful[SEP]I had ever seen.[SEP]Well, I braced myself to the task, made no easier by[SEP]the charming embarrassment with which I was received.[SEP]How I succeeded in[SEP]carrying out my programme will appear hereafter.[EOT]
[SOT]“You are gaining golden laurels,” she said.[SEP]“You are like the prince in[SEP]Shakespeare who was transformed by becoming king.[SEP]But I’m forgetting you[SEP]are King, sire.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I ask you to speak nothing but what your heart tells you--and to call[SEP]me nothing but my name.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She looked at me for a moment.[EOT]
[SOT]“Then I’m glad and proud, Rudolf,” said she.[SEP]“Why, as I told you, your[SEP]very face is changed.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I acknowledged the compliment, but I disliked the topic; so I said:[EOT]
[SOT]“My brother is back, I hear.[SEP]He made an excursion, didn’t he?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, he is here,” she said, frowning a little.[EOT]
[SOT]“He can’t stay long from Strelsau, it seems,” I observed, smiling.[SEP]“Well, we are all glad to see him.[SEP]The nearer he is, the better.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The princess glanced at me with a gleam of amusement in her eyes.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why, cousin?[SEP]Is it that you can--?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“See better what he’s doing?[SEP]Perhaps,” said I.[SEP]“And why are you glad?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I didn’t say I was glad,” she answered.[EOT]
[SOT]“Some people say so for you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“There are many insolent people,” she said, with delightful haughtiness.[EOT]
[SOT]“Possibly you mean that I am one?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Your Majesty could not be,” she said, curtseying in feigned deference,[SEP]but adding, mischievously, after a pause: “Unless, that is--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, unless what?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Unless you tell me that I mind a snap of my fingers where the Duke of[SEP]Strelsau is.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Really, I wished that I had been the King.[EOT]
[SOT]“You don’t care where cousin Michael--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah, cousin Michael![SEP]I call him the Duke of Strelsau.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You call him Michael when you meet him?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes--by the orders of your father.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I see.[SEP]And now by mine?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“If those are your orders.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, decidedly![SEP]We must all be pleasant to our dear Michael.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You order me to receive his friends, too, I suppose?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The Six?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You call them that, too?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“To be in the fashion, I do.[SEP]But I order you to receive no one unless[SEP]you like.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Except yourself?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I pray for myself.[SEP]I could not order.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]As I spoke, there came a cheer from the street.[SEP]The princess ran to the[SEP]window.[EOT]
[SOT]“It is he![SEP]” she cried.[SEP]“It is--the Duke of Strelsau![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I smiled, but said nothing.[SEP]She returned to her seat.[SEP]For a few moments[SEP]we sat in silence.[SEP]The noise outside subsided, but I heard the tread of[SEP]feet in the ante-room.[SEP]I began to talk on general subjects.[SEP]This went on[SEP]for some minutes.[SEP]I wondered what had become of Michael, but it did[SEP]not seem to be for me to interfere.[SEP]All at once, to my great surprise,[SEP]Flavia, clasping her hands asked in an agitated voice:[EOT]
[SOT]“Are you wise to make him angry?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What?[SEP]Who?[SEP]How am I making him angry?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why, by keeping him waiting.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“My dear cousin, I don’t want to keep him--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, then, is he to come in?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Of course, if you wish it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She looked at me curiously.[EOT]
[SOT]“How funny you are,” she said.[SEP]“Of course no one could be announced[SEP]while I was with you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Here was a charming attribute of royalty![EOT]
[SOT]“An excellent etiquette![SEP]” I cried.[SEP]“But I had clean forgotten it; and if[SEP]I were alone with someone else, couldn’t you be announced?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You know as well as I do.[SEP]I could be, because I am of the Blood;” and[SEP]she still looked puzzled.[EOT]
[SOT]“I never could remember all these silly rules,” said I, rather feebly,[SEP]as I inwardly cursed Fritz for not posting me up.[SEP]“But I’ll repair my[SEP]fault.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I jumped up, flung open the door, and advanced into the ante-room.[SEP]Michael was sitting at a table, a heavy frown on his face.[SEP]Everyone[SEP]else was standing, save that impudent young dog Fritz, who was lounging[SEP]easily in an armchair, and flirting with the Countess Helga.[SEP]He leapt up[SEP]as I entered, with a deferential alacrity that lent point to his former[SEP]nonchalance.[SEP]I had no difficulty in understanding that the duke might[SEP]not like young Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]I held out my hand, Michael took it, and I embraced him.[SEP]Then I drew him[SEP]with me into the inner room.[EOT]
[SOT]“Brother,” I said, “if I had known you were here, you should not have[SEP]waited a moment before I asked the princess to permit me to bring you to[SEP]her.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He thanked me, but coldly.[SEP]The man had many qualities, but he could not[SEP]hide his feelings.[SEP]A mere stranger could have seen that he hated me, and[SEP]hated worse to see me with Princess Flavia; yet I am persuaded that he[SEP]tried to conceal both feelings, and, further, that he tried to persuade[SEP]me that he believed I was verily the King.[SEP]I did not know, of course;[SEP]but, unless the King were an impostor, at once cleverer and more[SEP]audacious than I (and I began to think something of myself in that[SEP]role), Michael could not believe that.[SEP]And, if he didn’t, how he must[SEP]have loathed paying me deference, and hearing my “Michael” and my[SEP]“Flavia![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Your hand is hurt, sire,” he observed, with concern.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, I was playing a game with a mongrel dog” (I meant to stir him),[SEP]“and you know, brother, such have uncertain tempers.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He smiled sourly, and his dark eyes rested on me for a moment.[EOT]
[SOT]“But is there no danger from the bite?[SEP]” cried Flavia anxiously.[EOT]
[SOT]“None from this,” said I.[SEP]“If I gave him a chance to bite deeper, it[SEP]would be different, cousin.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But surely he has been destroyed?[SEP]” said she.[EOT]
[SOT]“Not yet.[SEP]We’re waiting to see if his bite is harmful.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And if it is?[SEP]” asked Michael, with his sour smile.[EOT]
[SOT]“He’ll be knocked on the head, brother,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“You won’t play with him any more?[SEP]” urged Flavia.[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps I shall.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He might bite again.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Doubtless he’ll try,” said I, smiling.[EOT]
