[SOT]Then, fearing Michael would say something which I must appear to[SEP]resent (for, though I might show him my hate, I must seem to be full of[SEP]favour), I began to compliment him on the magnificent condition of his[SEP]regiment, and of their loyal greeting to me on the day of my coronation.[SEP]Thence I passed to a rapturous description of the hunting-lodge which[SEP]he had lent me.[SEP]But he rose suddenly to his feet.[SEP]His temper was failing[SEP]him, and, with an excuse, he said farewell.[SEP]However, as he reached the[SEP]door he stopped, saying:[EOT]
[SOT]“Three friends of mine are very anxious to have the honour of being[SEP]presented to you, sire.[SEP]They are here in the ante-chamber.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I joined him directly, passing my arm through his.[SEP]The look on his[SEP]face was honey to me.[SEP]We entered the ante-chamber in fraternal fashion.[SEP]Michael beckoned, and three men came forward.[EOT]
[SOT]“These gentlemen,” said Michael, with a stately courtesy which, to[SEP]do him justice, he could assume with perfect grace and ease, “are the[SEP]loyalest and most devoted of your Majesty’s servants, and are my very[SEP]faithful and attached friends.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“On the last ground as much as the first,” said I, “I am very pleased to[SEP]see them.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]They came one by one and kissed my hand--De Gautet, a tall lean fellow,[SEP]with hair standing straight up and waxed moustache; Bersonin, the[SEP]Belgian, a portly man of middle height with a bald head (though he was[SEP]not far past thirty); and last, the Englishman, Detchard, a narrow-faced[SEP]fellow, with close-cut fair hair and a bronzed complexion.[SEP]He was a[SEP]finely made man, broad in the shoulder and slender in the hips.[SEP]A good[SEP]fighter, but a crooked customer, I put him down for.[SEP]I spoke to him in[SEP]English, with a slight foreign accent, and I swear the fellow smiled,[SEP]though he hid the smile in an instant.[EOT]
[SOT]“So Mr. Detchard is in the secret,” thought I.[EOT]
[SOT]Having got rid of my dear brother and his friends, I returned to make my[SEP]adieu to my cousin.[SEP]She was standing at the door.[SEP]I bade her farewell,[SEP]taking her hand in mine.[EOT]
[SOT]“Rudolf,” she said, very low, “be careful, won’t you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Of what?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You know--I can’t say.[SEP]But think what your life is to--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well to--?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“To Ruritania.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Was I right to play the part, or wrong to play the part?[SEP]I know not:[SEP]evil lay both ways, and I dared not tell her the truth.[EOT]
[SOT]“Only to Ruritania?[SEP]” I asked softly.[EOT]
[SOT]A sudden flush spread over her incomparable face.[EOT]
[SOT]“To your friends, too,” she said.[EOT]
[SOT]“Friends?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And to your cousin,” she whispered, “and loving servant.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I could not speak.[SEP]I kissed her hand, and went out cursing myself.[EOT]
[SOT]Outside I found Master Fritz, quite reckless of the footmen, playing at[SEP]cat’s-cradle with the Countess Helga.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hang it![SEP]” said he, “we can’t always be plotting.[SEP]Love claims his[SEP]share.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m inclined to think he does,” said I; and Fritz, who had been by my[SEP]side, dropped respectfully behind.[EOT]
[SOT]A New Use for a Tea-table[EOT]
[SOT]If I were to detail the ordinary events of my daily life at this time,[SEP]they might prove instructive to people who are not familiar with the[SEP]inside of palaces; if I revealed some of the secrets I learnt, they[SEP]might prove of interest to the statesmen of Europe.[SEP]I intend to do[SEP]neither of these things.[SEP]I should be between the Scylla of dullness and[SEP]the Charybdis of indiscretion, and I feel that I had far better confine[SEP]myself strictly to the underground drama which was being played beneath[SEP]the surface of Ruritanian politics.[SEP]I need only say that the secret of[SEP]my imposture defied detection.[SEP]I made mistakes.[SEP]I had bad minutes: it[SEP]needed all the tact and graciousness whereof I was master to smooth over[SEP]some apparent lapses of memory and unmindfulness of old acquaintances of[SEP]which I was guilty.[SEP]But I escaped, and I attribute my escape, as I have[SEP]said before, most of all, to the very audacity of the enterprise.[SEP]It is[SEP]my belief that, given the necessary physical likeness, it was far easier[SEP]to pretend to be King of Ruritania than it would have been to personate[SEP]my next-door neighbour.[SEP]One day Sapt came into my room.[SEP]He threw me a[SEP]letter, saying:[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s for you--a woman’s hand, I think.[SEP]But I’ve some news for you[SEP]first.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What’s that?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The King’s at the Castle of Zenda,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]“How do you know?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Because the other half of Michael’s Six are there.[SEP]I had enquiries[SEP]made, and they’re all there--Lauengram, Krafstein, and young Rupert[SEP]Hentzau: three rogues, too, on my honour, as fine as live in Ruritania.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, Fritz wants you to march to the Castle with horse, foot, and[SEP]artillery.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And drag the moat?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“That would be about it,” grinned Sapt, “and we shouldn’t find the[SEP]King’s body then.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You think it’s certain he’s there?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Very probable.[SEP]Besides the fact of those three being there, the[SEP]drawbridge is kept up, and no one goes in without an order from young[SEP]Hentzau or Black Michael himself.[SEP]We must tie Fritz up.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll go to Zenda,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’re mad.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Some day.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, perhaps.[SEP]You’ll very likely stay there though, if you do.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That may be, my friend,” said I carelessly.[EOT]
[SOT]“His Majesty looks sulky,” observed Sapt.[SEP]“How’s the love affair?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Damn you, hold your tongue![SEP]” I said.[EOT]
[SOT]He looked at me for a moment, then he lit his pipe.[SEP]It was quite true[SEP]that I was in a bad temper, and I went on perversely:[EOT]
[SOT]“Wherever I go, I’m dogged by half a dozen fellows.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I know you are; I send ’em,” he replied composedly.[EOT]
[SOT]“What for?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well,” said Sapt, puffing away, “it wouldn’t be exactly inconvenient[SEP]for Black Michael if you disappeared.[SEP]With you gone, the old game that[SEP]we stopped would be played--or he’d have a shot at it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I can take care of myself.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“De Gautet, Bersonin, and Detchard are in Strelsau; and any one of[SEP]them, lad, would cut your throat as readily--as readily as I would Black[SEP]Michael’s, and a deal more treacherously.[SEP]What’s the letter?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I opened it and read it aloud:[EOT]
[SOT]“If the King desires to know what it deeply concerns the King to know,[SEP]let him do as this letter bids him.[SEP]At the end of the New Avenue there[SEP]stands a house in large grounds.[SEP]The house has a portico, with a statue[SEP]of a nymph on it.[SEP]A wall encloses the garden; there is a gate in the[SEP]wall at the back.[SEP]At twelve o’clock tonight, if the King enters alone[SEP]by that gate, turns to the right, and walks twenty yards, he will find[SEP]a summerhouse, approached by a flight of six steps.[SEP]If he mounts and[SEP]enters, he will find someone who will tell him what touches most dearly[SEP]his life and his throne.[SEP]This is written by a faithful friend.[SEP]He must[SEP]be alone.[SEP]If he neglects the invitation his life will be in danger.[SEP]Let[SEP]him show this to no one, or he will ruin a woman who loves him: Black[SEP]Michael does not pardon.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No,” observed Sapt, as I ended, “but he can dictate a very pretty[SEP]letter.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I had arrived at the same conclusion, and was about to throw the letter[SEP]away, when I saw there was more writing on the other side.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hallo![SEP]there’s some more.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“If you hesitate,” the writer continued, “consult Colonel Sapt--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Eh,” exclaimed that gentleman, genuinely astonished.[SEP]“Does she take me[SEP]for a greater fool than you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I waved to him to be silent.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ask him what woman would do most to prevent the duke from marrying his[SEP]cousin, and therefore most to prevent him becoming king?[SEP]And ask if her[SEP]name begins with--A?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I sprang to my feet.[SEP]Sapt laid down his pipe.[EOT]
[SOT]“Antoinette de Mauban, by heaven![SEP]” I cried.[EOT]
[SOT]“How do you know?[SEP]” asked Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]I told him what I knew of the lady, and how I knew it.[SEP]He nodded.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s so far true that she’s had a great row with Michael,” said he,[SEP]thoughtfully.[EOT]
[SOT]“If she would, she could be useful,” I said.[EOT]
[SOT]“I believe, though, that Michael wrote that letter.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“So do I, but I mean to know for certain.[SEP]I shall go, Sapt.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, I shall go,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]“You may go as far as the gate.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I shall go to the summer-house.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m hanged if you shall![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I rose and leant my back against the mantelpiece.[EOT]
[SOT]“Sapt, I believe in that woman, and I shall go.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I don’t believe in any woman,” said Sapt, “and you shan’t go.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I either go to the summer-house or back to England,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt began to know exactly how far he could lead or drive, and when he[SEP]must follow.[EOT]
[SOT]“We’re playing against time,” I added.[SEP]“Every day we leave the King[SEP]where he is there is fresh risk.[SEP]Every day I masquerade like this, there[SEP]is fresh risk.[SEP]Sapt, we must play high; we must force the game.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“So be it,” he said, with a sigh.[EOT]
[SOT]To cut the story short, at half-past eleven that night Sapt and I[SEP]mounted our horses.[SEP]Fritz was again left on guard, our destination not[SEP]being revealed to him.[SEP]It was a very dark night.[SEP]I wore no sword, but I[SEP]carried a revolver, a long knife, and a bull’s-eye lantern.[SEP]We arrived[SEP]outside the gate.[SEP]I dismounted.[SEP]Sapt held out his hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“I shall wait here,” he said.[SEP]“If I hear a shot, I’ll--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Stay where you are; it’s the King’s only chance.[SEP]You mustn’t come to[SEP]grief too.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You’re right, lad.[SEP]Good luck![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I pressed the little gate.[SEP]It yielded, and I found myself in a wild sort[SEP]of shrubbery.[SEP]There was a grass-grown path and, turning to the right as[SEP]I had been bidden, I followed it cautiously.[SEP]My lantern was closed, the[SEP]revolver was in my hand.[SEP]I heard not a sound.[SEP]Presently a large dark[SEP]object loomed out of the gloom ahead of me.[SEP]It was the summer-house.[SEP]Reaching the steps, I mounted them and found myself confronted by a[SEP]weak, rickety wooden door, which hung upon the latch.[SEP]I pushed it open[SEP]and walked in.[SEP]A woman flew to me and seized my hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“Shut the door,” she whispered.[EOT]
[SOT]I obeyed and turned the light of my lantern on her.[SEP]She was in evening[SEP]dress, arrayed very sumptuously, and her dark striking beauty was[SEP]marvellously displayed in the glare of the bull’s-eye.[SEP]The summer-house[SEP]was a bare little room, furnished only with a couple of chairs and a[SEP]small iron table, such as one sees in a tea garden or an open-air cafe.[EOT]
[SOT]“Don’t talk,” she said.[SEP]“We’ve no time.[SEP]Listen![SEP]I know you, Mr.[SEP]Rassendyll.[SEP]I wrote that letter at the duke’s orders.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“So I thought,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“In twenty minutes three men will be here to kill you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Three--the three?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes.[SEP]You must be gone by then.[SEP]If not, tonight you’ll be killed--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Or they will.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Listen, listen![SEP]When you’re killed, your body will be taken to a low[SEP]quarter of the town.[SEP]It will be found there.[SEP]Michael will at once[SEP]arrest all your friends--Colonel Sapt and Captain von Tarlenheim[SEP]first--proclaim a state of siege in Strelsau, and send a messenger to[SEP]Zenda.[SEP]The other three will murder the King in the Castle, and the duke[SEP]will proclaim either himself or the princess--himself, if he is strong[SEP]enough.[SEP]Anyhow, he’ll marry her, and become king in fact, and soon in[SEP]name.[SEP]Do you see?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s a pretty plot.[SEP]But why, madame, do you--?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Say I’m a Christian--or say I’m jealous.[SEP]My God![SEP]shall I see him marry[SEP]her?[SEP]Now go; but remember--this is what I have to tell you--that never,[SEP]by night or by day, are you safe.[SEP]Three men follow you as a guard.[SEP]Is it[SEP]not so?[SEP]Well, three follow them; Michael’s three are never two hundred[SEP]yards from you.[SEP]Your life is not worth a moment if ever they find you[SEP]alone.[SEP]Now go.[SEP]Stay, the gate will be guarded by now.[SEP]Go down softly, go[SEP]past the summer-house, on for a hundred yards, and you’ll find a ladder[SEP]against the wall.[SEP]Get over it, and fly for your life.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And you?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“I have my game to play too.[SEP]If he finds out what I have done, we shall[SEP]not meet again.[SEP]If not, I may yet--But never mind.[SEP]Go at once.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But what will you tell him?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That you never came--that you saw through the trick.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I took her hand and kissed it.[EOT]
[SOT]“Madame,” said I, “you have served the King well tonight.[SEP]Where is he in[SEP]the Castle?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She sank her voice to a fearful whisper.[SEP]I listened eagerly.[EOT]
[SOT]“Across the drawbridge you come to a heavy door; behind that lies--Hark![SEP]What’s that?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]There were steps outside.[EOT]
[SOT]“They’re coming![SEP]They’re too soon![SEP]Heavens![SEP]they’re too soon![SEP]” and she[SEP]turned pale as death.[EOT]
[SOT]“They seem to me,” said I, “to be in the nick of time.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Close your lantern.[SEP]See, there’s a chink in the door.[SEP]Can you see[SEP]them?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I put my eye to the chink.[SEP]On the lowest step I saw three dim figures.[SEP]I[SEP]cocked my revolver.[SEP]Antoinette hastily laid her hand on mine.[EOT]
[SOT]“You may kill one,” said she.[SEP]“But what then?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]A voice came from outside--a voice that spoke perfect English.[EOT]
[SOT]“Mr. Rassendyll,” it said.[EOT]
[SOT]I made no answer.[EOT]
[SOT]“We want to talk to you.[SEP]Will you promise not to shoot till we’ve done?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Have I the pleasure of addressing Mr. Detchard?[SEP]” I said.[EOT]
[SOT]“Never mind names.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Then let mine alone.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“All right, sire.[SEP]I’ve an offer for you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I still had my eye to the chink.[SEP]The three had mounted two steps more;[SEP]three revolvers pointed full at the door.[EOT]
[SOT]“Will you let us in?[SEP]We pledge our honour to observe the truce.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Don’t trust them,” whispered Antoinette.[EOT]
[SOT]“We can speak through the door,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“But you might open it and fire,” objected Detchard; “and though we[SEP]should finish you, you might finish one of us.[SEP]Will you give your honour[SEP]not to fire while we talk?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Don’t trust them,” whispered Antoinette again.[EOT]
[SOT]A sudden idea struck me.[SEP]I considered it for a moment.[SEP]It seemed[SEP]feasible.[EOT]
[SOT]“I give my honour not to fire before you do,” said I; “but I won’t let[SEP]you in.[SEP]Stand outside and talk.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s sensible,” he said.[EOT]
[SOT]The three mounted the last step, and stood just outside the door.[SEP]I[SEP]laid my ear to the chink.[SEP]I could hear no words, but Detchard’s head was[SEP]close to that of the taller of his companions (De Gautet, I guessed).[EOT]
[SOT]“H’m![SEP]Private communications,” thought I.[SEP]Then I said aloud:[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, gentlemen, what’s the offer?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“A safe-conduct to the frontier, and fifty thousand pounds English.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, no,” whispered Antoinette in the lowest of whispers.[SEP]“They are[SEP]treacherous.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That seems handsome,” said I, reconnoitering through the chink.[SEP]They[SEP]were all close together, just outside the door now.[EOT]
[SOT]I had probed the hearts of the ruffians, and I did not need Antoinette’s[SEP]warning.[SEP]They meant to “rush” me as soon as I was engaged in talk.[EOT]
[SOT]“Give me a minute to consider,” said I; and I thought I heard a laugh[SEP]outside.[EOT]
[SOT]I turned to Antoinette.[EOT]
[SOT]“Stand up close to the wall, out of the line of fire from the door,” I[SEP]whispered.[EOT]
[SOT]“What are you going to do?[SEP]” she asked in fright.[EOT]
[SOT]“You’ll see,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]I took up the little iron table.[SEP]It was not very heavy for a man of my[SEP]strength, and I held it by the legs.[SEP]The top, protruding in front of[SEP]me, made a complete screen for my head and body.[SEP]I fastened my closed[SEP]lantern to my belt and put my revolver in a handy pocket.[SEP]Suddenly I saw[SEP]the door move ever so slightly--perhaps it was the wind, perhaps it was[SEP]a hand trying it outside.[EOT]
[SOT]I drew back as far as I could from the door, holding the table in the[SEP]position that I have described.[SEP]Then I called out:[EOT]
[SOT]“Gentlemen, I accept your offer, relying on your honour.[SEP]If you will[SEP]open the door--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Open it yourself,” said Detchard.[EOT]
[SOT]“It opens outwards,” said I.[SEP]“Stand back a little, gentlemen, or I shall[SEP]hit you when I open it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I went and fumbled with the latch.[SEP]Then I stole back to my place on[SEP]tiptoe.[EOT]
[SOT]“I can’t open it![SEP]” I cried.[SEP]“The latch has caught.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Tut![SEP]I’ll open it![SEP]” cried Detchard.[SEP]“Nonsense, Bersonin, why not?[SEP]Are[SEP]you afraid of one man?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I smiled to myself.[SEP]An instant later the door was flung back.[SEP]The gleam[SEP]of a lantern showed me the three close together outside, their[SEP]revolvers levelled.[SEP]With a shout, I charged at my utmost pace across the[SEP]summer-house and through the doorway.[SEP]Three shots rang out and battered[SEP]into my shield.[SEP]Another moment, and I leapt out and the table caught[SEP]them full and square, and in a tumbling, swearing, struggling mass, they[SEP]and I and that brave table, rolled down the steps of the summerhouse to[SEP]the ground below.[SEP]Antoinette de Mauban shrieked, but I rose to my feet,[SEP]laughing aloud.[EOT]
[SOT]De Gautet and Bersonin lay like men stunned.[SEP]Detchard was under the[SEP]table, but, as I rose, he pushed it from him and fired again.[SEP]I raised[SEP]my revolver and took a snap shot; I heard him curse, and then I ran like[SEP]a hare, laughing as I went, past the summer-house and along by the wall.[SEP]I heard steps behind me, and turning round I fired again for luck.[SEP]The[SEP]steps ceased.[EOT]
[SOT]“Please God,” said I, “she told me the truth about the ladder![SEP]” for the[SEP]wall was high and topped with iron spikes.[EOT]
[SOT]Yes, there it was.[SEP]I was up and over in a minute.[SEP]Doubling back, I saw[SEP]the horses; then I heard a shot.[SEP]It was Sapt.[SEP]He had heard us, and was[SEP]battling and raging with the locked gate, hammering it and firing into[SEP]the keyhole like a man possessed.[SEP]He had quite forgotten that he was[SEP]not to take part in the fight.[SEP]Whereat I laughed again, and said, as I[SEP]clapped him on the shoulder:[EOT]
[SOT]“Come home to bed, old chap.[SEP]I’ve got the finest tea-table story that[SEP]ever you heard![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He started and cried: “You’re safe![SEP]” and wrung my hand.[SEP]But a moment[SEP]later he added:[EOT]
[SOT]“And what the devil are you laughing at?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Four gentlemen round a tea-table,” said I, laughing still, for it had[SEP]been uncommonly ludicrous to see the formidable three altogether routed[SEP]and scattered with no more deadly weapon than an ordinary tea-table.[EOT]
[SOT]Moreover, you will observe that I had honourably kept my word, and not[SEP]fired till they did.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 10[EOT]
[SOT]A Great Chance for a Villain[EOT]
[SOT]It was the custom that the Prefect of Police should send every afternoon[SEP]a report to me on the condition of the capital and the feeling of the[SEP]people: the document included also an account of the movements of any[SEP]persons whom the police had received instructions to watch.[SEP]Since I had[SEP]been in Strelsau, Sapt had been in the habit of reading the report and[SEP]telling me any items of interest which it might contain.[SEP]On the day[SEP]after my adventure in the summer-house, he came in as I was playing a[SEP]hand of _écarté_ with Fritz von Tarlenheim.[EOT]
[SOT]“The report is rather full of interest this afternoon,” he observed,[SEP]sitting down.[EOT]
[SOT]“Do you find,” I asked, “any mention of a certain fracas?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He shook his head with a smile.[EOT]
[SOT]“I find this first,” he said: “‘His Highness the Duke of Strelsau left[SEP]the city (so far as it appears, suddenly), accompanied by several of his[SEP]household.[SEP]His destination is believed to be the Castle of Zenda, but[SEP]the party travelled by road and not by train.[SEP]MM De Gautet, Bersonin,[SEP]and Detchard followed an hour later, the last-named carrying his arm in[SEP]a sling.[SEP]The cause of his wound is not known, but it is suspected that[SEP]he has fought a duel, probably incidental to a love affair.[SEP]’”[EOT]
[SOT]“That is remotely true,” I observed, very well pleased to find that I[SEP]had left my mark on the fellow.[EOT]
[SOT]“Then we come to this,” pursued Sapt: “‘Madame de Mauban, whose[SEP]movements have been watched according to instructions, left by train at[SEP]midday.[SEP]She took a ticket for Dresden--’”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s an old habit of hers,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“‘The Dresden train stops at Zenda.[SEP]’ An acute fellow, this.[SEP]And finally[SEP]listen to this: ‘The state of feeling in the city is not satisfactory.[SEP]The King is much criticized’ (you know, he’s told to be quite frank)[SEP]‘for taking no steps about his marriage.[SEP]From enquiries among the[SEP]entourage of the Princess Flavia, her Royal Highness is believed to be[SEP]deeply offended by the remissness of his Majesty.[SEP]The common people are[SEP]coupling her name with that of the Duke of Strelsau, and the duke gains[SEP]much popularity from the suggestion.[SEP]’ I have caused the announcement that[SEP]the King gives a ball tonight in honour of the princess to be widely[SEP]diffused, and the effect is good.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“That is news to me,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, the preparations are all made![SEP]” laughed Fritz.[SEP]“I’ve seen to that.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt turned to me and said, in a sharp, decisive voice:[EOT]
[SOT]“You must make love to her tonight, you know.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I think it is very likely I shall, if I see her alone,” said I.[SEP]“Hang[SEP]it, Sapt, you don’t suppose I find it difficult?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz whistled a bar or two; then he said: “You’ll find it only too[SEP]easy.[SEP]Look here, I hate telling you this, but I must.[SEP]The Countess Helga[SEP]told me that the princess had become most attached to the King.[SEP]Since[SEP]the coronation, her feelings have undergone a marked development.[SEP]It’s[SEP]quite true that she is deeply wounded by the King’s apparent neglect.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Here’s a kettle of fish![SEP]” I groaned.[EOT]
[SOT]“Tut, tut![SEP]” said Sapt.[SEP]“I suppose you’ve made pretty speeches to a girl[SEP]before now?[SEP]That’s all she wants.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Fritz, himself a lover, understood better my distress.[SEP]He laid his hand[SEP]on my shoulder, but said nothing.[EOT]
[SOT]“I think, though,” pursued that cold-blooded old Sapt, “that you’d[SEP]better make your offer tonight.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Good heavens![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Or, any rate, go near it: and I shall send a ‘semi-official’ to the[SEP]papers.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll do nothing of the sort--no more will you![SEP]” said I.[SEP]“I utterly[SEP]refuse to take part in making a fool of the princess.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt looked at me with his small keen eyes.[SEP]A slow cunning smile passed[SEP]over his face.[EOT]
[SOT]“All right, lad, all right,” said he.[SEP]“We mustn’t press you too hard.[SEP]Soothe her down a bit, if you can, you know.[SEP]Now for Michael![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, damn Michael![SEP]” said I.[SEP]“He’ll do tomorrow.[SEP]Here, Fritz, come for a[SEP]stroll in the garden.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt at once yielded.[SEP]His rough manner covered a wonderful tact--and[SEP]as I came to recognize more and more, a remarkable knowledge of human[SEP]nature.[SEP]Why did he urge me so little about the princess?[SEP]Because he[SEP]knew that her beauty and my ardour would carry me further than all his[SEP]arguments--and that the less I thought about the thing, the more likely[SEP]was I to do it.[SEP]He must have seen the unhappiness he might bring on the[SEP]princess; but that went for nothing with him.[SEP]Can I say, confidently,[SEP]that he was wrong?[SEP]If the King were restored, the princess must turn to[SEP]him, either knowing or not knowing the change.[SEP]And if the King were not[SEP]restored to us?[SEP]It was a subject that we had never yet spoken of.[SEP]But I[SEP]had an idea that, in such a case, Sapt meant to seat me on the throne of[SEP]Ruritania for the term of my life.[SEP]He would have set Satan himself there[SEP]sooner than that pupil of his, Black Michael.[EOT]
[SOT]The ball was a sumptuous affair.[SEP]I opened it by dancing a quadrille[SEP]with Flavia: then I waltzed with her.[SEP]Curious eyes and eager whispers[SEP]attended us.[SEP]We went in to supper; and, half way through, I, half mad by[SEP]then, for her glance had answered mine, and her quick breathing met my[SEP]stammered sentences--I rose in my place before all the brilliant crowd,[SEP]and taking the Red Rose that I wore, flung the ribbon with its jewelled[SEP]badge round her neck.[SEP]In a tumult of applause I sat down: I saw Sapt[SEP]smiling over his wine, and Fritz frowning.[SEP]The rest of the meal passed[SEP]in silence; neither Flavia nor I could speak.[SEP]Fritz touched me on the[SEP]shoulder, and I rose, gave her my arm, and walked down the hall into a[SEP]little room, where coffee was served to us.[SEP]The gentlemen and ladies in[SEP]attendance withdrew, and we were alone.[EOT]
[SOT]The little room had French windows opening on the gardens.[SEP]The night was[SEP]fine, cool, and fragrant.[SEP]Flavia sat down, and I stood opposite her.[SEP]I[SEP]was struggling with myself: if she had not looked at me, I believe that[SEP]even then I should have won my fight.[SEP]But suddenly, involuntarily, she[SEP]gave me one brief glance--a glance of question, hurriedly turned aside;[SEP]a blush that the question had ever come spread over her cheek, and she[SEP]caught her breath.[SEP]Ah, if you had seen her![SEP]I forgot the King in Zenda.[SEP]I forgot the King in Strelsau.[SEP]She was a princess--and I an impostor.[SEP]Do you think I remembered that?[SEP]I threw myself on my knee and seized[SEP]her hands in mine.[SEP]I said nothing.[SEP]Why should I?[SEP]The soft sounds of the[SEP]night set my wooing to a wordless melody, as I pressed my kisses on her[SEP]lips.[EOT]
[SOT]She pushed me from her, crying suddenly:[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah![SEP]is it true?[SEP]or is it only because you must?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s true![SEP]” I said, in low smothered tones--“true that I love you more[SEP]than life--or truth--or honour![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She set no meaning to my words, treating them as one of love’s sweet[SEP]extravagances.[SEP]She came close to me, and whispered:[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, if you were not the King![SEP]Then I could show you how I love you![SEP]How[SEP]is it that I love you now, Rudolf?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes--just lately.[SEP]I--I never did before.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Pure triumph filled me.[SEP]It was I--Rudolf Rassendyll--who had won her![SEP]I[SEP]caught her round the waist.[EOT]
[SOT]“You didn’t love me before?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]She looked up into my face, smiling, as she whispered:[EOT]
[SOT]“It must have been your Crown.[SEP]I felt it first on the Coronation Day.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Never before?[SEP]” I asked eagerly.[EOT]
[SOT]She laughed low.[EOT]
[SOT]“You speak as if you would be pleased to hear me say ‘Yes’ to that,” she[SEP]said.[EOT]
[SOT]“Would ‘Yes’ be true?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes,” I just heard her breathe, and she went on in an instant: “Be[SEP]careful, Rudolf; be careful, dear.[SEP]He will be mad now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What, Michael?[SEP]If Michael were the worst--”[EOT]
[SOT]“What worse is there?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]There was yet a chance for me.[SEP]Controlling myself with a mighty effort,[SEP]I took my hands off her and stood a yard or two away.[SEP]I remember now the[SEP]note of the wind in the elm trees outside.[EOT]
[SOT]“If I were not the King,” I began, “if I were only a private[SEP]gentleman--”[EOT]
[SOT]Before I could finish, her hand was in mine.[EOT]
[SOT]“If you were a convict in the prison of Strelsau, you would be my King,”[SEP]she said.[EOT]
[SOT]And under my breath I groaned, “God forgive me![SEP]” and, holding her hand[SEP]in mine, I said again:[EOT]
[SOT]“If I were not the King--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Hush, hush![SEP]” she whispered.[SEP]“I don’t deserve it--I don’t deserve to be[SEP]doubted.[SEP]Ah, Rudolf![SEP]does a woman who marries without love look on the[SEP]man as I look on you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And she hid her face from me.[EOT]
[SOT]For more than a minute we stood there together; and I, even with my arm[SEP]about her, summoned up what honour and conscience her beauty and the[SEP]toils that I was in had left me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Flavia,” I said, in a strange dry voice that seemed not my own, “I am[SEP]not--”[EOT]
[SOT]As I spoke--as she raised her eyes to me--there was a heavy step on the[SEP]gravel outside, and a man appeared at the window.[SEP]A little cry burst[SEP]from Flavia, as she sprang back from me.[SEP]My half-finished sentence died[SEP]on my lips.[SEP]Sapt stood there, bowing low, but with a stern frown on his[SEP]face.[EOT]
[SOT]“A thousand pardons, sire,” said he, “but his Eminence the Cardinal has[SEP]waited this quarter of an hour to offer his respectful adieu to your[SEP]Majesty.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I met his eye full and square; and I read in it an angry warning.[SEP]How[SEP]long he had been a listener I knew not, but he had come in upon us in[SEP]the nick of time.[EOT]
[SOT]“We must not keep his Eminence waiting,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]But Flavia, in whose love there lay no shame, with radiant eyes and[SEP]blushing face, held out her hand to Sapt.[SEP]She said nothing, but no[SEP]man could have missed her meaning, who had ever seen a woman in the[SEP]exultation of love.[SEP]A sour, yet sad, smile passed over the old soldier’s[SEP]face, and there was tenderness in his voice, as bending to kiss her[SEP]hand, he said:[EOT]
[SOT]“In joy and sorrow, in good times and bad, God save your Royal[SEP]Highness![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He paused and added, glancing at me and drawing himself up to military[SEP]erectness:[EOT]
[SOT]“But, before all comes the King--God save the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And Flavia caught at my hand and kissed it, murmuring:[EOT]
[SOT]“Amen![SEP]Good God, Amen![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We went into the ballroom again.[SEP]Forced to receive adieus, I was[SEP]separated from Flavia: everyone, when they left me, went to her.[SEP]Sapt[SEP]was out and in of the throng, and where he had been, glances, smiles,[SEP]and whispers were rife.[SEP]I doubted not that, true to his relentless[SEP]purpose, he was spreading the news that he had learnt.[SEP]To uphold[SEP]the Crown and beat Black Michael--that was his one resolve.[SEP]Flavia,[SEP]myself--ay, and the real King in Zenda, were pieces in his game; and[SEP]pawns have no business with passions.[SEP]Not even at the walls of the[SEP]Palace did he stop; for when at last I handed Flavia down the broad[SEP]marble steps and into her carriage, there was a great crowd awaiting[SEP]us, and we were welcomed with deafening cheers.[SEP]What could I do?[SEP]Had I[SEP]spoken then, they would have refused to believe that I was not the King;[SEP]they might have believed that the King had run mad.[SEP]By Sapt’s devices[SEP]and my own ungoverned passion I had been forced on, and the way back had[SEP]closed behind me; and the passion still drove me in the same direction[SEP]as the devices seduced me.[SEP]I faced all Strelsau that night as the King[SEP]and the accepted suitor of the Princess Flavia.[EOT]
[SOT]At last, at three in the morning, when the cold light of dawning day[SEP]began to steal in, I was in my dressing-room, and Sapt alone was with[SEP]me.[SEP]I sat like a man dazed, staring into the fire; he puffed at his[SEP]pipe; Fritz was gone to bed, having almost refused to speak to me.[SEP]On[SEP]the table by me lay a rose; it had been in Flavia’s dress, and, as we[SEP]parted, she had kissed it and given it to me.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt advanced his hand towards the rose, but, with a quick movement, I[SEP]shut mine down upon it.[EOT]
[SOT]“That’s mine,” I said, “not yours--nor the King’s either.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We struck a good blow for the King tonight,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]I turned on him fiercely.[EOT]
[SOT]“What’s to prevent me striking a blow for myself?[SEP]” I said.[EOT]
[SOT]He nodded his head.[EOT]
[SOT]“I know what’s in your mind,” he said.[SEP]“Yes, lad; but you’re bound in[SEP]honour.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Have you left me any honour?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, come, to play a little trick on a girl--”[EOT]
[SOT]“You can spare me that.[SEP]Colonel Sapt, if you would not have me utterly a[SEP]villain--if you would not have your King rot in Zenda, while Michael and[SEP]I play for the great stake outside--You follow me?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, I follow you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We must act, and quickly![SEP]You saw tonight--you heard--tonight--”[EOT]
[SOT]“I did,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]“Your cursed acuteness told you what I should do.[SEP]Well, leave me here a[SEP]week--and there’s another problem for you.[SEP]Do you find the answer?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, I find it,” he answered, frowning heavily.[SEP]“But if you did that,[SEP]you’d have to fight me first--and kill me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, and if I had--or a score of men?[SEP]I tell you, I could raise all[SEP]Strelsau on you in an hour, and choke you with your lies--yes, your mad[SEP]lies--in your mouth.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s gospel truth,” he said--“thanks to my advice you could.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I could marry the princess, and send Michael and his brother together[SEP]to--”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m not denying it, lad,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]“Then, in God’s name,” I cried, stretching out my hands to him, “let us[SEP]go to Zenda and crush this Michael and bring the King back to his own[SEP]again.[SEP]” The old fellow stood and looked at me for full a minute.[EOT]
[SOT]“And the princess?[SEP]” he said.[EOT]
[SOT]I bowed my head to meet my hands, and crushed the rose between my[SEP]fingers and my lips.[EOT]
[SOT]I felt his hand on my shoulder, and his voice sounded husky as he[SEP]whispered low in my ear:[EOT]
[SOT]“Before God, you’re the finest Elphberg of them all.[SEP]But I have eaten[SEP]of the King’s bread, and I am the King’s servant.[SEP]Come, we will go to[SEP]Zenda![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And I looked up and caught him by the hand.[SEP]And the eyes of both of us[SEP]were wet.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 11[EOT]
[SOT]Hunting a Very Big Boar[EOT]
[SOT]The terrible temptation which was assailing me will now be understood.[SEP]I would so force Michael’s hand that he must kill the King.[SEP]I was in a[SEP]position to bid him defiance and tighten my grasp on the crown--not for[SEP]its own sake, but because the King of Ruritania was to wed the Princess[SEP]Flavia.[SEP]What of Sapt and Fritz?[SEP]Ah![SEP]but a man cannot be held to write[SEP]down in cold blood the wild and black thoughts that storm his brain when[SEP]an uncontrolled passion has battered a breach for them.[SEP]Yet, unless[SEP]he sets up as a saint, he need not hate himself for them.[SEP]He is better[SEP]employed, as it humbly seems to me, in giving thanks that power to[SEP]resist was vouchsafed to him, than in fretting over wicked impulses[SEP]which come unsought and extort an unwilling hospitality from the[SEP]weakness of our nature.[EOT]
[SOT]It was a fine bright morning when I walked, unattended, to the[SEP]princess’s house, carrying a nosegay in my hand.[SEP]Policy made excuses[SEP]for love, and every attention that I paid her, while it riveted my own[SEP]chains, bound closer to me the people of the great city, who worshipped[SEP]her.[SEP]I found Fritz’s _inamorata_, the Countess Helga, gathering blooms in[SEP]the garden for her mistress’s wear, and prevailed on her to take mine in[SEP]their place.[SEP]The girl was rosy with happiness, for Fritz, in his turn,[SEP]had not wasted his evening, and no dark shadow hung over his wooing,[SEP]save the hatred which the Duke of Strelsau was known to bear him.[EOT]
[SOT]“And that,” she said, with a mischievous smile, “your Majesty has made[SEP]of no moment.[SEP]Yes, I will take the flowers; shall I tell you, sire, what[SEP]is the first thing the princess does with them?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We were talking on a broad terrace that ran along the back of the house,[SEP]and a window above our heads stood open.[EOT]
[SOT]“Madame![SEP]” cried the countess merrily, and Flavia herself looked out.[SEP]I[SEP]bared my head and bowed.[SEP]She wore a white gown, and her hair was loosely[SEP]gathered in a knot.[SEP]She kissed her hand to me, crying:[EOT]
[SOT]“Bring the King up, Helga; I’ll give him some coffee.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The countess, with a gay glance, led the way, and took me into Flavia’s[SEP]morning-room.[SEP]And, left alone, we greeted one another as lovers are[SEP]wont.[SEP]Then the princess laid two letters before me.[SEP]One was from Black[SEP]Michael--a most courteous request that she would honour him by spending[SEP]a day at his Castle of Zenda, as had been her custom once a year in the[SEP]summer, when the place and its gardens were in the height of their great[SEP]beauty.[SEP]I threw the letter down in disgust, and Flavia laughed at me.[SEP]Then, growing grave again, she pointed to the other sheet.[EOT]
[SOT]“I don’t know who that comes from,” she said.[SEP]“Read it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I knew in a moment.[SEP]There was no signature at all this time, but the[SEP]handwriting was the same as that which had told me of the snare in the[SEP]summer-house: it was Antoinette de Mauban’s.[EOT]
[SOT]“I have no cause to love you,” it ran, “but God forbid that you should[SEP]fall into the power of the duke.[SEP]Accept no invitations of his.[SEP]Go[SEP]nowhere without a large guard--a regiment is not too much to make you[SEP]safe.[SEP]Show this, if you can, to him who reigns in Strelsau.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why doesn’t it say ‘the King’?[SEP]” asked Flavia, leaning over my shoulder,[SEP]so that the ripple of her hair played on my cheek.[SEP]“Is it a hoax?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“As you value life, and more than life, my queen,” I said, “obey it to[SEP]the very letter.[SEP]A regiment shall camp round your house today.[SEP]See that[SEP]you do not go out unless well guarded.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“An order, sire?[SEP]” she asked, a little rebellious.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, an order, madame--if you love me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah![SEP]” she cried; and I could not but kiss her.[EOT]
[SOT]“You know who sent it?[SEP]” she asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“I guess,” said I.[SEP]“It is from a good friend--and I fear, an unhappy[SEP]woman.[SEP]You must be ill, Flavia, and unable to go to Zenda.[SEP]Make your[SEP]excuses as cold and formal as you like.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“So you feel strong enough to anger Michael?[SEP]” she said, with a proud[SEP]smile.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m strong enough for anything, while you are safe,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]Soon I tore myself away from her, and then, without consulting Sapt, I[SEP]took my way to the house of Marshal Strakencz.[SEP]I had seen something[SEP]of the old general, and I liked and trusted him.[SEP]Sapt was less[SEP]enthusiastic, but I had learnt by now that Sapt was best pleased when[SEP]he could do everything, and jealousy played some part in his views.[SEP]As[SEP]things were now, I had more work than Sapt and Fritz could manage, for[SEP]they must come with me to Zenda, and I wanted a man to guard what I[SEP]loved most in all the world, and suffer me to set about my task of[SEP]releasing the King with a quiet mind.[EOT]
[SOT]The Marshal received me with most loyal kindness.[SEP]To some extent, I took[SEP]him into my confidence.[SEP]I charged him with the care of the princess,[SEP]looking him full and significantly in the face as I bade him let no one[SEP]from her cousin the duke approach her, unless he himself were there and[SEP]a dozen of his men with him.[EOT]
[SOT]“You may be right, sire,” said he, shaking his grey head sadly.[SEP]“I have[SEP]known better men than the duke do worse things than that for love.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I could quite appreciate the remark, but I said:[EOT]
[SOT]“There’s something beside love, Marshal.[SEP]Love’s for the heart; is there[SEP]nothing my brother might like for his head?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I pray that you wrong him, sire.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Marshal, I’m leaving Strelsau for a few days.[SEP]Every evening I will[SEP]send a courier to you.[SEP]If for three days none comes, you will publish an[SEP]order which I will give you, depriving Duke Michael of the governorship[SEP]of Strelsau and appointing you in his place.[SEP]You will declare a state of[SEP]siege.[SEP]Then you will send word to Michael that you demand an audience of[SEP]the King--You follow me?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, sire.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“--In twenty-four hours.[SEP]If he does not produce the King” (I laid my[SEP]hand on his knee), “then the King is dead, and you will proclaim the[SEP]next heir.[SEP]You know who that is?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The Princess Flavia.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And swear to me, on your faith and honour and by the fear of the living[SEP]God, that you will stand by her to the death, and kill that reptile, and[SEP]seat her where I sit now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“On my faith and honour, and by the fear of God, I swear it![SEP]And may[SEP]Almighty God preserve your Majesty, for I think that you go on an errand[SEP]of danger.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I hope that no life more precious than mine may be demanded,” said I,[SEP]rising.[SEP]Then I held out my hand to him.[EOT]
[SOT]“Marshal,” I said, “in days to come, it may be--I know not--that you[SEP]will hear strange things of the man who speaks to you now.[SEP]Let him be[SEP]what he may, and who he may, what say you of the manner in which he has[SEP]borne himself as King in Strelsau?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The old man, holding my hand, spoke to me, man to man.[EOT]
[SOT]“I have known many of the Elphbergs,” said he, “and I have seen you.[SEP]And, happen what may, you have borne yourself as a wise King and a brave[SEP]man; ay, and you have proved as courteous a gentleman and as gallant a[SEP]lover as any that have been of the House.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Be that my epitaph,” said I, “when the time comes that another sits on[SEP]the throne of Ruritania.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“God send a far day, and may I not see it![SEP]” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]I was much moved, and the Marshal’s worn face twitched.[SEP]I sat down and[SEP]wrote my order.[EOT]
[SOT]“I can hardly yet write,” said I; “my finger is stiff still.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was, in fact, the first time that I had ventured to write more than[SEP]a signature; and in spite of the pains I had taken to learn the King’s[SEP]hand, I was not yet perfect in it.[EOT]
[SOT]“Indeed, sire,” he said, “it differs a little from your ordinary[SEP]handwriting.[SEP]It is unfortunate, for it may lead to a suspicion of[SEP]forgery.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Marshal,” said I, with a laugh, “what use are the guns of Strelsau, if[SEP]they can’t assuage a little suspicion?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He smiled grimly, and took the paper.[EOT]
[SOT]“Colonel Sapt and Fritz von Tarlenheim go with me,” I continued.[EOT]
[SOT]“You go to seek the duke?[SEP]” he asked in a low tone.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, the duke, and someone else of whom I have need, and who is at[SEP]Zenda,” I replied.[EOT]
[SOT]“I wish I could go with you,” he cried, tugging at his white moustache.[SEP]“I’d like to strike a blow for you and your crown.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I leave you what is more than my life and more than my crown,” said I,[SEP]“because you are the man I trust more than all other in Ruritania.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I will deliver her to you safe and sound,” said he, “and, failing that,[SEP]I will make her queen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We parted, and I returned to the Palace and told Sapt and Fritz what[SEP]I had done.[SEP]Sapt had a few faults to find and a few grumbles to[SEP]utter.[SEP]This was merely what I expected, for Sapt liked to be consulted[SEP]beforehand, not informed afterwards; but on the whole he approved of my[SEP]plans, and his spirits rose high as the hour of action drew nearer and[SEP]nearer.[SEP]Fritz, too, was ready; though he, poor fellow, risked more than[SEP]Sapt did, for he was a lover, and his happiness hung in the scale.[SEP]Yet[SEP]how I envied him![SEP]For the triumphant issue which would crown him with[SEP]happiness and unite him to his mistress, the success for which we were[SEP]bound to hope and strive and struggle, meant to me sorrow more certain[SEP]and greater than if I were doomed to fail.[SEP]He understood something of[SEP]this, for when we were alone (save for old Sapt, who was smoking at the[SEP]other end of the room) he passed his arm through mine, saying:[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s hard for you.[SEP]Don’t think I don’t trust you; I know you have[SEP]nothing but true thoughts in your heart.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]But I turned away from him, thankful that he could not see what my heart[SEP]held, but only be witness to the deeds that my hands were to do.[EOT]
[SOT]Yet even he did not understand, for he had not dared to lift his eyes to[SEP]the Princess Flavia, as I had lifted mine.[EOT]
[SOT]Our plans were now all made, even as we proceeded to carry them out, and[SEP]as they will hereafter appear.[SEP]The next morning we were to start on the[SEP]hunting excursion.[SEP]I had made all arrangements for being absent, and[SEP]now there was only one thing left to do--the hardest, the most[SEP]heart-breaking.[SEP]As evening fell, I drove through the busy streets to[SEP]Flavia’s residence.[SEP]I was recognized as I went and heartily cheered.[SEP]I[SEP]played my part, and made shift to look the happy lover.[SEP]In spite of my[SEP]depression, I was almost amused at the coolness and delicate hauteur[SEP]with which my sweet lover received me.[SEP]She had heard that the King was[SEP]leaving Strelsau on a hunting expedition.[EOT]
[SOT]“I regret that we cannot amuse your Majesty here in Strelsau,” she said,[SEP]tapping her foot lightly on the floor.[SEP]“I would have offered you more[SEP]entertainment, but I was foolish enough to think--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, what?[SEP]” I asked, leaning over her.[EOT]
[SOT]“That just for a day or two after--after last night--you might be happy[SEP]without much gaiety;” and she turned pettishly from me, as she added, “I[SEP]hope the boars will be more engrossing.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m going after a very big boar,” said I; and, because I could not help[SEP]it, I began to play with her hair, but she moved her head away.[EOT]
[SOT]“Are you offended with me?[SEP]” I asked, in feigned surprise, for I could[SEP]not resist tormenting her a little.[SEP]I had never seen her angry, and[SEP]every fresh aspect of her was a delight to me.[EOT]
[SOT]“What right have I to be offended?[SEP]True, you said last night that every[SEP]hour away from me was wasted.[SEP]But a very big boar![SEP]that’s a different[SEP]thing.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps the boar will hunt me,” I suggested.[SEP]“Perhaps, Flavia, he’ll[SEP]catch me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She made no answer.[EOT]
[SOT]“You are not touched even by that danger?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Still she said nothing; and I, stealing round, found her eyes full of[SEP]tears.[EOT]
[SOT]“You weep for my danger?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then she spoke very low:[EOT]
[SOT]“This is like what you used to be; but not like the King--the King I--I[SEP]have come to love![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]With a sudden great groan, I caught her to my heart.[EOT]
[SOT]“My darling![SEP]” I cried, forgetting everything but her, “did you dream[SEP]that I left you to go hunting?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What then, Rudolf?[SEP]Ah![SEP]you’re not going--?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, it is hunting.[SEP]I go to seek Michael in his lair.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She had turned very pale.[EOT]
[SOT]“So, you see, sweet, I was not so poor a lover as you thought me.[SEP]I[SEP]shall not be long gone.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You will write to me, Rudolf?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I was weak, but I could not say a word to stir suspicion in her.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll send you all my heart every day,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“And you’ll run no danger?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“None that I need not.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And when will you be back?[SEP]Ah, how long will it be![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“When shall I be back?[SEP]” I repeated.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, yes![SEP]Don’t be long, dear, don’t be long.[SEP]I shan’t sleep while[SEP]you’re away.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I don’t know when I shall be back,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Soon, Rudolf, soon?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“God knows, my darling.[SEP]But, if never--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Hush, hush![SEP]” and she pressed her lips to mine.[EOT]
[SOT]“If never,” I whispered, “you must take my place; you’ll be the only one[SEP]of the House then.[SEP]You must reign, and not weep for me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]For a moment she drew herself up like a very queen.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, I will![SEP]” she said.[SEP]“I will reign.[SEP]I will do my part though all my[SEP]life will be empty and my heart dead; yet I’ll do it![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She paused, and sinking against me again, wailed softly.[EOT]
[SOT]“Come soon![SEP]come soon![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Carried away, I cried loudly:[EOT]
[SOT]“As God lives, I--yes, I myself--will see you once more before I die![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What do you mean?[SEP]” she exclaimed, with wondering eyes; but I had no[SEP]answer for her, and she gazed at me with her wondering eyes.[EOT]
[SOT]I dared not ask her to forget, she would have found it an insult.[SEP]I[SEP]could not tell her then who and what I was.[SEP]She was weeping, and I had[SEP]but to dry her tears.[EOT]
[SOT]“Shall a man not come back to the loveliest lady in all the wide world?[SEP]”[SEP]said I.[SEP]“A thousand Michaels should not keep me from you![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She clung to me, a little comforted.[EOT]
[SOT]“You won’t let Michael hurt you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, sweetheart.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Or keep you from me?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, sweetheart.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Nor anyone else?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And again I answered:[EOT]
[SOT]“No, sweetheart.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Yet there was one--not Michael--who, if he lived, must keep me from[SEP]her; and for whose life I was going forth to stake my own.[SEP]And his[SEP]figure--the lithe, buoyant figure I had met in the woods of Zenda--the[SEP]dull, inert mass I had left in the cellar of the hunting-lodge--seemed[SEP]to rise, double-shaped, before me, and to come between us, thrusting[SEP]itself in even where she lay, pale, exhausted, fainting, in my arms, and[SEP]yet looking up at me with those eyes that bore such love as I have never[SEP]seen, and haunt me now, and will till the ground closes over me--and[SEP](who knows?[SEP]) perhaps beyond.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 12[EOT]
[SOT]I Receive a Visitor and Bait a Hook[EOT]
[SOT]About five miles from Zenda--on the opposite side from that on which[SEP]the Castle is situated, there lies a large tract of wood.[SEP]It is rising[SEP]ground, and in the centre of the demesne, on the top of the hill, stands[SEP]a fine modern chateau, the property of a distant kinsman of Fritz’s, the[SEP]Count Stanislas von Tarlenheim.[SEP]Count Stanislas himself was a student[SEP]and a recluse.[SEP]He seldom visited the house, and had, on Fritz’s request,[SEP]very readily and courteously offered me its hospitality for myself and[SEP]my party.[SEP]This, then, was our destination; chosen ostensibly for the[SEP]sake of the boar-hunting (for the wood was carefully preserved, and[SEP]boars, once common all over Ruritania, were still to be found there[SEP]in considerable numbers), really because it brought us within striking[SEP]distance of the Duke of Strelsau’s more magnificent dwelling on the[SEP]other side of the town.[SEP]A large party of servants, with horses and[SEP]luggage, started early in the morning; we followed at midday, travelling[SEP]by train for thirty miles, and then mounting our horses to ride the[SEP]remaining distance to the chateau.[EOT]
[SOT]We were a gallant party.[SEP]Besides Sapt and Fritz, I was accompanied by[SEP]ten gentlemen: every one of them had been carefully chosen, and no less[SEP]carefully sounded, by my two friends, and all were devotedly attached to[SEP]the person of the King.[SEP]They were told a part of the truth; the attempt[SEP]on my life in the summer-house was revealed to them, as a spur to their[SEP]loyalty and an incitement against Michael.[SEP]They were also informed that[SEP]a friend of the King’s was suspected to be forcibly confined within the[SEP]Castle of Zenda.[SEP]His rescue was one of the objects of the expedition;[SEP]but, it was added, the King’s main desire was to carry into effect[SEP]certain steps against his treacherous brother, as to the precise nature[SEP]of which they could not at present be further enlightened.[SEP]Enough that[SEP]the King commanded their services, and would rely on their devotion when[SEP]occasion arose to call for it.[SEP]Young, well-bred, brave, and loyal, they[SEP]asked no more: they were ready to prove their dutiful obedience, and[SEP]prayed for a fight as the best and most exhilarating mode of showing it.[EOT]
[SOT]Thus the scene was shifted from Strelsau to the chateau of Tarlenheim[SEP]and Castle of Zenda, which frowned at us across the valley.[SEP]I tried to[SEP]shift my thoughts also, to forget my love, and to bend all my energies[SEP]to the task before me.[SEP]It was to get the King out of the Castle alive.[SEP]Force was useless: in some trick lay the chance; and I had already an[SEP]inkling of what we must do.[SEP]But I was terribly hampered by the publicity[SEP]which attended my movements.[SEP]Michael must know by now of my expedition;[SEP]and I knew Michael too well to suppose that his eyes would be blinded by[SEP]the feint of the boar-hunt.[SEP]He would understand very well what the real[SEP]quarry was.[SEP]That, however, must be risked--that and all it might mean;[SEP]for Sapt, no less than myself, recognized that the present state of[SEP]things had become unendurable.[SEP]And there was one thing that I dared to[SEP]calculate on--not, as I now know, without warrant.[SEP]It was this--that[SEP]Black Michael would not believe that I meant well by the King.[SEP]He could[SEP]not appreciate--I will not say an honest man, for the thoughts of my[SEP]own heart have been revealed--but a man acting honestly.[SEP]He saw[SEP]my opportunity as I had seen it, as Sapt had seen it; he knew the[SEP]princess--nay (and I declare that a sneaking sort of pity for him[SEP]invaded me), in his way he loved her; he would think that Sapt and Fritz[SEP]could be bribed, so the bribe was large enough.[SEP]Thinking thus, would he[SEP]kill the King, my rival and my danger?[SEP]Ay, verily, that he would, with[SEP]as little compunction as he would kill a rat.[SEP]But he would kill Rudolf[SEP]Rassendyll first, if he could; and nothing but the certainty of being[SEP]utterly damned by the release of the King alive and his restoration to[SEP]the throne would drive him to throw away the trump card which he held in[SEP]reserve to baulk the supposed game of the impudent impostor Rassendyll.[SEP]Musing on all this as I rode along, I took courage.[EOT]
[SOT]Michael knew of my coming, sure enough.[SEP]I had not been in the house an[SEP]hour, when an imposing Embassy arrived from him.[SEP]He did not quite reach[SEP]the impudence of sending my would-be assassins, but he sent the other[SEP]three of his famous Six--the three Ruritanian gentlemen--Lauengram,[SEP]Krafstein, and Rupert Hentzau.[SEP]A fine, strapping trio they were,[SEP]splendidly horsed and admirably equipped.[SEP]Young Rupert, who looked[SEP]a dare-devil, and could not have been more than twenty-two or[SEP]twenty-three, took the lead, and made us the neatest speech, wherein[SEP]my devoted subject and loving brother Michael of Strelsau, prayed me to[SEP]pardon him for not paying his addresses in person, and, further, for not[SEP]putting his Castle at my disposal; the reason for both of these apparent[SEP]derelictions being that he and several of his servants lay sick of[SEP]scarlet fever, and were in a very sad, and also a very infectious state.[SEP]So declared young Rupert with an insolent smile on his curling upper lip[SEP]and a toss of his thick hair--he was a handsome villain, and the gossip[SEP]ran that many a lady had troubled her heart for him already.[EOT]
[SOT]“If my brother has scarlet fever,” said I, “he is nearer my complexion[SEP]than he is wont to be, my lord.[SEP]I trust he does not suffer?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He is able to attend to his affairs, sire.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I hope all beneath your roof are not sick.[SEP]What of my good friends, De[SEP]Gautet, Bersonin, and Detchard?[SEP]I heard the last had suffered a hurt.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Lauengram and Krafstein looked glum and uneasy, but young Rupert’s smile[SEP]grew broader.[EOT]
[SOT]“He hopes soon to find a medicine for it, sire,” he answered.[EOT]
[SOT]And I burst out laughing, for I knew what medicine Detchard longed[SEP]for--it is called Revenge.[EOT]
[SOT]“You will dine with us, gentlemen?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]Young Rupert was profuse in apologies.[SEP]They had urgent duties at the[SEP]Castle.[EOT]
[SOT]“Then,” said I, with a wave of my hand, “to our next meeting, gentlemen.[SEP]May it make us better acquainted.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We will pray your Majesty for an early opportunity,” quoth Rupert[SEP]airily; and he strode past Sapt with such jeering scorn on his face that[SEP]I saw the old fellow clench his fist and scowl black as night.[EOT]
[SOT]For my part, if a man must needs be a knave, I would have him a debonair[SEP]knave, and I liked Rupert Hentzau better than his long-faced, close-eyed[SEP]companions.[SEP]It makes your sin no worse, as I conceive, to do it _à la[SEP]mode_ and stylishly.[EOT]
[SOT]Now it was a curious thing that on this first night, instead of eating[SEP]the excellent dinner my cooks had prepared for me, I must needs leave my[SEP]gentlemen to eat it alone, under Sapt’s presiding care, and ride myself[SEP]with Fritz to the town of Zenda and a certain little inn that I knew[SEP]of.[SEP]There was little danger in the excursion; the evenings were long and[SEP]light, and the road this side of Zenda well frequented.[SEP]So off we rode,[SEP]with a groom behind us.[SEP]I muffled myself up in a big cloak.[EOT]
[SOT]“Fritz,” said I, as we entered the town, “there’s an uncommonly pretty[SEP]girl at this inn.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“How do you know?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Because I’ve been there,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Since--?[SEP]” he began.[EOT]
[SOT]“No. Before,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“But they’ll recognize you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, of course they will.[SEP]Now, don’t argue, my good fellow, but listen[SEP]to me.[SEP]We’re two gentlemen of the King’s household, and one of us has a[SEP]toothache.[SEP]The other will order a private room and dinner, and, further,[SEP]a bottle of the best wine for the sufferer.[SEP]And if he be as clever a[SEP]fellow as I take him for, the pretty girl and no other will wait on us.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What if she won’t?[SEP]” objected Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“My dear Fritz,” said I, “if she won’t for you, she will for me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We were at the inn.[SEP]Nothing of me but my eyes was visible as I walked[SEP]in.[SEP]The landlady received us; two minutes later, my little friend (ever,[SEP]I fear me, on the look-out for such guests as might prove amusing) made[SEP]her appearance.[SEP]Dinner and the wine were ordered.[SEP]I sat down in the[SEP]private room.[SEP]A minute later Fritz came in.[EOT]
[SOT]“She’s coming,” he said.[EOT]
[SOT]“If she were not, I should have to doubt the Countess Helga’s taste.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She came in.[SEP]I gave her time to set the wine down--I didn’t want it[SEP]dropped.[SEP]Fritz poured out a glass and gave it to me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is the gentleman in great pain?[SEP]” the girl asked, sympathetically.[EOT]
[SOT]“The gentleman is no worse than when he saw you last,” said I, throwing[SEP]away my cloak.[EOT]
[SOT]She started, with a little shriek.[SEP]Then she cried:[EOT]
[SOT]“It was the King, then![SEP]I told mother so the moment I saw his picture.[SEP]Oh, sir, forgive me![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Faith, you gave me nothing that hurt much,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“But the things we said![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I forgive them for the thing you did.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I must go and tell mother.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Stop,” said I, assuming a graver air.[SEP]“We are not here for sport[SEP]tonight.[SEP]Go and bring dinner, and not a word of the King being here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She came back in a few minutes, looking grave, yet very curious.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, how is Johann?[SEP]” I asked, beginning my dinner.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, that fellow, sir--my lord King, I mean![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“‘Sir’ will do, please.[SEP]How is he?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We hardly see him now, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And why not?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I told him he came too often, sir,” said she, tossing her head.[EOT]
[SOT]“So he sulks and stays away?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But you could bring him back?[SEP]” I suggested with a smile.[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps I could,” said she.[EOT]
[SOT]“I know your powers, you see,” said I, and she blushed with pleasure.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s not only that, sir, that keeps him away.[SEP]He’s very busy at the[SEP]Castle.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“But there’s no shooting on now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, sir; but he’s in charge of the house.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Johann turned housemaid?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The little girl was brimming over with gossip.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, there are no others,” said she.[SEP]“There’s not a woman there--not[SEP]as a servant, I mean.[SEP]They do say--but perhaps it’s false, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Let’s have it for what it’s worth,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Indeed, I’m ashamed to tell you, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, see, I’m looking at the ceiling.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They do say there is a lady there, sir; but, except for her, there’s[SEP]not a woman in the place.[SEP]And Johann has to wait on the gentlemen.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Poor Johann![SEP]He must be overworked.[SEP]Yet I’m sure he could find half an[SEP]hour to come and see you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It would depend on the time, sir, perhaps.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Do you love him?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Not I, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And you wish to serve the King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Then tell him to meet you at the second milestone out of Zenda tomorrow[SEP]evening at ten o’clock.[SEP]Say you’ll be there and will walk home with[SEP]him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Do you mean him harm, sir?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Not if he will do as I bid him.[SEP]But I think I’ve told you enough, my[SEP]pretty maid.[SEP]See that you do as I bid you.[SEP]And, mind, no one is to know[SEP]that the King has been here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I spoke a little sternly, for there is seldom harm in infusing a little[SEP]fear into a woman’s liking for you, and I softened the effect by giving[SEP]her a handsome present.[SEP]Then we dined, and, wrapping my cloak about[SEP]my face, with Fritz leading the way, we went downstairs to our horses[SEP]again.[EOT]
[SOT]It was but half-past eight, and hardly yet dark; the streets were full[SEP]for such a quiet little place, and I could see that gossip was all agog.[SEP]With the King on one side and the duke on the other, Zenda felt itself[SEP]the centre of all Ruritania.[SEP]We jogged gently through the town, but set[SEP]our horses to a sharper pace when we reached the open country.[EOT]
[SOT]“You want to catch this fellow Johann?[SEP]” asked Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, and I fancy I’ve baited the hook right.[SEP]Our little Delilah will[SEP]bring our Samson.[SEP]It is not enough, Fritz, to have no women in a house,[SEP]though brother Michael shows some wisdom there.[SEP]If you want safety, you[SEP]must have none within fifty miles.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“None nearer than Strelsau, for instance,” said poor Fritz, with a[SEP]lovelorn sigh.[EOT]
[SOT]We reached the avenue of the chateau, and were soon at the house.[SEP]As the[SEP]hoofs of our horses sounded on the gravel, Sapt rushed out to meet us.[EOT]
[SOT]“Thank God, you’re safe![SEP]” he cried.[SEP]“Have you seen anything of them?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Of whom?[SEP]” I asked, dismounting.[EOT]
[SOT]He drew us aside, that the grooms might not hear.[EOT]
[SOT]“Lad,” he said to me, “you must not ride about here, unless with half a[SEP]dozen of us.[SEP]You know among our men a tall young fellow, Bernenstein by[SEP]name?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I knew him.[SEP]He was a fine strapping young man, almost of my height, and[SEP]of light complexion.[EOT]
[SOT]“He lies in his room upstairs, with a bullet through his arm.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The deuce he does![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“After dinner he strolled out alone, and went a mile or so into the[SEP]wood; and as he walked, he thought he saw three men among the trees;[SEP]and one levelled a gun at him.[SEP]He had no weapon, and he started at a run[SEP]back towards the house.[SEP]But one of them fired, and he was hit, and had[SEP]much ado to reach here before he fainted.[SEP]By good luck, they feared to[SEP]pursue him nearer the house.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He paused and added:[EOT]
[SOT]“Lad, the bullet was meant for you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It is very likely,” said I, “and it’s first blood to brother Michael.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I wonder which three it was,” said Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, Sapt,” I said, “I went out tonight for no idle purpose, as you[SEP]shall hear.[SEP]But there’s one thing in my mind.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What’s that?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why this,” I answered.[SEP]“That I shall ill requite the very great honours[SEP]Ruritania has done me if I depart from it leaving one of those Six[SEP]alive--neither with the help of God, will I.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And Sapt shook my hand on that.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 13[EOT]
[SOT]An Improvement on Jacob’s Ladder[EOT]
[SOT]In the morning of the day after that on which I swore my oath against[SEP]the Six, I gave certain orders, and then rested in greater contentment[SEP]than I had known for some time.[SEP]I was at work; and work, though it[SEP]cannot cure love, is yet a narcotic to it; so that Sapt, who grew[SEP]feverish, marvelled to see me sprawling in an armchair in the sunshine,[SEP]listening to one of my friends who sang me amorous songs in a mellow[SEP]voice and induced in me a pleasing melancholy.[SEP]Thus was I engaged when[SEP]young Rupert Hentzau, who feared neither man nor devil, and rode through[SEP]the demesne--where every tree might hide a marksman, for all he knew--as[SEP]though it had been the park at Strelsau, cantered up to where I lay,[SEP]bowing with burlesque deference, and craving private speech with me[SEP]in order to deliver a message from the Duke of Strelsau.[SEP]I made all[SEP]withdraw, and then he said, seating himself by me:[EOT]
[SOT]“The King is in love, it seems?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Not with life, my lord,” said I, smiling.[EOT]
[SOT]“It is well,” he rejoined.[SEP]“Come, we are alone, Rassendyll--”[EOT]
[SOT]I rose to a sitting posture.[EOT]
[SOT]“What’s the matter?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“I was about to call one of my gentlemen to bring your horse, my lord.[SEP]If you do not know how to address the King, my brother must find another[SEP]messenger.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why keep up the farce?[SEP]” he asked, negligently dusting his boot with his[SEP]glove.[EOT]
[SOT]“Because it is not finished yet; and meanwhile I’ll choose my own name.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, so be it![SEP]Yet I spoke in love for you; for indeed you are a man[SEP]after my own heart.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Saving my poor honesty,” said I, “maybe I am.[SEP]But that I keep faith[SEP]with men, and honour with women, maybe I am, my lord.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He darted a glance at me--a glance of anger.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is your mother dead?[SEP]” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, she’s dead.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“She may thank God,” said I, and I heard him curse me softly.[SEP]“Well,[SEP]what’s the message?[SEP]” I continued.[EOT]
[SOT]I had touched him on the raw, for all the world knew he had broken his[SEP]mother’s heart and flaunted his mistresses in her house; and his airy[SEP]manner was gone for the moment.[EOT]
[SOT]“The duke offers you more than I would,” he growled.[SEP]“A halter for[SEP]you, sire, was my suggestion.[SEP]But he offers you safe-conduct across the[SEP]frontier and a million crowns.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I prefer your offer, my lord, if I am bound to one.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You refuse?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Of course.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I told Michael you would;” and the villain, his temper restored,[SEP]gave me the sunniest of smiles.[SEP]“The fact is, between ourselves,” he[SEP]continued, “Michael doesn’t understand a gentleman.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I began to laugh.[EOT]
[SOT]“And you?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“I do,” he said.[SEP]“Well, well, the halter be it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m sorry you won’t live to see it,” I observed.[EOT]
[SOT]“Has his Majesty done me the honour to fasten a particular quarrel on[SEP]me?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I would you were a few years older, though.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, God gives years, but the devil gives increase,” laughed he.[SEP]“I can[SEP]hold my own.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“How is your prisoner?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“The K--?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Your prisoner.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I forgot your wishes, sire.[SEP]Well, he is alive.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He rose to his feet; I imitated him.[SEP]Then, with a smile, he said:[EOT]
[SOT]“And the pretty princess?[SEP]Faith, I’ll wager the next Elphberg will be[SEP]red enough, for all that Black Michael will be called his father.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I sprang a step towards him, clenching my hand.[SEP]He did not move an inch,[SEP]and his lip curled in insolent amusement.[EOT]
[SOT]“Go, while your skin’s whole![SEP]” I muttered.[SEP]He had repaid me with[SEP]interest my hit about his mother.[EOT]
[SOT]Then came the most audacious thing I have known in my life.[SEP]My friends[SEP]were some thirty yards away.[SEP]Rupert called to a groom to bring him his[SEP]horse, and dismissed the fellow with a crown.[SEP]The horse stood near.[SEP]I[SEP]stood still, suspecting nothing.[SEP]Rupert made as though to mount; then[SEP]he suddenly turned to me: his left hand resting in his belt, his right[SEP]outstretched: “Shake hands,” he said.[EOT]
[SOT]I bowed, and did as he had foreseen--I put my hands behind me.[SEP]Quicker[SEP]than thought, his left hand darted out at me, and a small dagger flashed[SEP]in the air; he struck me in the left shoulder--had I not swerved, it[SEP]had been my heart.[SEP]With a cry, I staggered back.[SEP]Without touching the[SEP]stirrup, he leapt upon his horse and was off like an arrow, pursued by[SEP]cries and revolver shots--the last as useless as the first--and I[SEP]sank into my chair, bleeding profusely, as I watched the devil’s brat[SEP]disappear down the long avenue.[SEP]My friends surrounded me, and then I[SEP]fainted.[EOT]
[SOT]I suppose that I was put to bed, and there lay, unconscious, or half[SEP]conscious, for many hours; for it was night when I awoke to my full[SEP]mind, and found Fritz beside me.[SEP]I was weak and weary, but he bade me be[SEP]of good cheer, saying that my wound would soon heal, and that meanwhile[SEP]all had gone well, for Johann, the keeper, had fallen into the snare we[SEP]had laid for him, and was even now in the house.[EOT]
[SOT]“And the queer thing is,” pursued Fritz, “that I fancy he’s not[SEP]altogether sorry to find himself here.[SEP]He seems to think that when[SEP]Black Michael has brought off his coup, witnesses of how it was[SEP]effected--saving, of course, the Six themselves--will not be at a[SEP]premium.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]This idea argued a shrewdness in our captive which led me to build[SEP]hopes on his assistance.[SEP]I ordered him to be brought in at once.[SEP]Sapt[SEP]conducted him, and set him in a chair by my bedside.[SEP]He was sullen, and[SEP]afraid; but, to say truth, after young Rupert’s exploit, we also had[SEP]our fears, and, if he got as far as possible from Sapt’s formidable[SEP]six-shooter, Sapt kept him as far as he could from me.[SEP]Moreover, when he[SEP]came in his hands were bound, but that I would not suffer.[EOT]
[SOT]I need not stay to recount the safeguards and rewards we promised the[SEP]fellow--all of which were honourably observed and paid, so that he lives[SEP]now in prosperity (though where I may not mention); and we were the more[SEP]free inasmuch as we soon learnt that he was rather a weak man than a[SEP]wicked, and had acted throughout this matter more from fear of the duke[SEP]and of his own brother Max than for any love of what was done.[SEP]But[SEP]he had persuaded all of his loyalty; and though not in their secret[SEP]counsels, was yet, by his knowledge of their dispositions within the[SEP]Castle, able to lay bare before us the very heart of their devices.[SEP]And[SEP]here, in brief, is his story:[EOT]
[SOT]Below the level of the ground in the Castle, approached by a flight of[SEP]stone steps which abutted on the end of the drawbridge, were situated[SEP]two small rooms, cut out of the rock itself.[SEP]The outer of the two had no[SEP]windows, but was always lighted with candles; the inner had one square[SEP]window, which gave upon the moat.[SEP]In the outer room there lay always,[SEP]day and night, three of the Six; and the instructions of Duke Michael[SEP]were, that on any attack being made on the outer room, the three were to[SEP]defend the door of it so long as they could without risk to themselves.[SEP]But, so soon as the door should be in danger of being forced, then[SEP]Rupert Hentzau or Detchard (for one of these two was always there)[SEP]should leave the others to hold it as long as they could, and himself[SEP]pass into the inner room, and, without more ado, kill the King who[SEP]lay there, well-treated indeed, but without weapons, and with his arms[SEP]confined in fine steel chains, which did not allow him to move his elbow[SEP]more than three inches from his side.[SEP]Thus, before the outer door were[SEP]stormed, the King would be dead.[SEP]And his body?[SEP]For his body would be[SEP]evidence as damning as himself.[EOT]
[SOT]“Nay, sir,” said Johann, “his Highness has thought of that.[SEP]While the[SEP]two hold the outer room, the one who has killed the King unlocks the[SEP]bars in the square window (they turn on a hinge).[SEP]The window now gives[SEP]no light, for its mouth is choked by a great pipe of earthenware; and[SEP]this pipe, which is large enough to let pass through it the body of[SEP]a man, passes into the moat, coming to an end immediately above the[SEP]surface of the water, so that there is no perceptible interval between[SEP]water and pipe.[SEP]The King being dead, his murderer swiftly ties a weight[SEP]to the body, and, dragging it to the window, raises it by a pulley (for,[SEP]lest the weight should prove too great, Detchard has provided one) till[SEP]it is level with the mouth of the pipe.[SEP]He inserts the feet in the pipe,[SEP]and pushes the body down.[SEP]Silently, without splash or sound, it falls[SEP]into the water and thence to the bottom of the moat, which is twenty[SEP]feet deep thereabouts.[SEP]This done, the murderer cries loudly, ‘All’s[SEP]well![SEP]’ and himself slides down the pipe; and the others, if they can and[SEP]the attack is not too hot, run to the inner room and, seeking a moment’s[SEP]delay, bar the door, and in their turn slide down.[SEP]And though the King[SEP]rises not from the bottom, they rise and swim round to the other side,[SEP]where the orders are for men to wait them with ropes, to haul them out,[SEP]and horses.[SEP]And here, if things go ill, the duke will join them and seek[SEP]safety by riding; but if all goes well, they will return to the Castle,[SEP]and have their enemies in a trap.[SEP]That, sir, is the plan of his Highness[SEP]for the disposal of the King in case of need.[SEP]But it is not to be used[SEP]till the last; for, as we all know, he is not minded to kill the King[SEP]unless he can, before or soon after, kill you also, sir.[SEP]Now, sir, I[SEP]have spoken the truth, as God is my witness, and I pray you to shield me[SEP]from the vengeance of Duke Michael; for if, after he knows what I have[SEP]done, I fall into his hands, I shall pray for one thing out of all the[SEP]world--a speedy death, and that I shall not obtain from him![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The fellow’s story was rudely told, but our questions supplemented[SEP]his narrative.[SEP]What he had told us applied to an armed attack; but if[SEP]suspicions were aroused, and there came overwhelming force--such, for[SEP]instance, as I, the King, could bring--the idea of resistance would be[SEP]abandoned; the King would be quietly murdered and slid down the pipe.[SEP]And--here comes an ingenious touch--one of the Six would take his[SEP]place in the cell, and, on the entrance of the searchers, loudly demand[SEP]release and redress; and Michael, being summoned, would confess to hasty[SEP]action, but he would say the man had angered him by seeking the favour[SEP]of a lady in the Castle (this was Antoinette de Mauban) and he had[SEP]confined him there, as he conceived he, as Lord of Zenda, had right to[SEP]do.[SEP]But he was now, on receiving his apology, content to let him go,[SEP]and so end the gossip which, to his Highness’s annoyance, had arisen[SEP]concerning a prisoner in Zenda, and had given his visitors the trouble[SEP]of this enquiry.[SEP]The visitors, baffled, would retire, and Michael could,[SEP]at his leisure, dispose of the body of the King.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt, Fritz, and I in my bed, looked round on one another in horror and[SEP]bewilderment at the cruelty and cunning of the plan.[SEP]Whether I went[SEP]in peace or in war, openly at the head of a corps, or secretly by a[SEP]stealthy assault, the King would be dead before I could come near him.[SEP]If Michael were stronger and overcame my party, there would be an end.[SEP]But if I were stronger, I should have no way to punish him, no means of[SEP]proving any guilt in him without proving my own guilt also.[SEP]On the other[SEP]hand, I should be left as King (ah![SEP]for a moment my pulse quickened) and[SEP]it would be for the future to witness the final struggle between him and[SEP]me.[SEP]He seemed to have made triumph possible and ruin impossible.[SEP]At[SEP]the worst, he would stand as well as he had stood before I crossed[SEP]his path--with but one man between him and the throne, and that man an[SEP]impostor; at best, there would be none left to stand against him.[SEP]I had[SEP]begun to think that Black Michael was over fond of leaving the fighting[SEP]to his friends; but now I acknowledged that the brains, if not the arms,[SEP]of the conspiracy were his.[EOT]
[SOT]“Does the King know this?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
