[SOT]“I and my brother,” answered Johann, “put up the pipe, under the orders[SEP]of my Lord of Hentzau.[SEP]He was on guard that day, and the King asked my[SEP]lord what it meant.[SEP]‘Faith,’ he answered, with his airy laugh, ‘it’s a[SEP]new improvement on the ladder of Jacob, whereby, as you have read, sire,[SEP]men pass from the earth to heaven.[SEP]We thought it not meet that your[SEP]Majesty should go, in case, sire, you must go, by the common route.[SEP]So[SEP]we have made you a pretty private passage where the vulgar cannot stare[SEP]at you or incommode your passage.[SEP]That, sire, is the meaning of[SEP]that pipe.[SEP]’ And he laughed and bowed, and prayed the King’s leave to[SEP]replenish the King’s glass--for the King was at supper.[SEP]And the King,[SEP]though he is a brave man, as are all of his House, grew red and then[SEP]white as he looked on the pipe and at the merry devil who mocked him.[SEP]Ah, sir” (and the fellow shuddered), “it is not easy to sleep quiet in[SEP]the Castle of Zenda, for all of them would as soon cut a man’s throat[SEP]as play a game at cards; and my Lord Rupert would choose it sooner for[SEP]a pastime than any other--ay, sooner than he would ruin a woman, though[SEP]that he loves also.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The man ceased, and I bade Fritz take him away and have him carefully[SEP]guarded; and, turning to him, I added:[EOT]
[SOT]“If anyone asks you if there is a prisoner in Zenda, you may answer[SEP]‘Yes.[SEP]’ But if any asks who the prisoner is, do not answer.[SEP]For all my[SEP]promises will not save you if any man here learns from you the truth as[SEP]to the prisoner of Zenda.[SEP]I’ll kill you like a dog if the thing be so[SEP]much as breathed within the house![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then, when he was gone, I looked at Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s a hard nut![SEP]” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“So hard,” said he, shaking his grizzled head, “that as I think, this[SEP]time next year is like to find you still King of Ruritania![SEP]” and he[SEP]broke out into curses on Michael’s cunning.[EOT]
[SOT]I lay back on my pillows.[EOT]
[SOT]“There seems to me,” I observed, “to be two ways by which the King can[SEP]come out of Zenda alive.[SEP]One is by treachery in the duke’s followers.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You can leave that out,” said Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“I hope not,” I rejoined, “because the other I was about to mention[SEP]is--by a miracle from heaven![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 14[EOT]
[SOT]A Night Outside the Castle[EOT]
[SOT]It would have surprised the good people of Ruritania to know of the[SEP]foregoing talk; for, according to the official reports, I had suffered a[SEP]grievous and dangerous hurt from an accidental spear-thrust, received in[SEP]the course of my sport.[SEP]I caused the bulletins to be of a very serious[SEP]character, and created great public excitement, whereby three things[SEP]occurred: first, I gravely offended the medical faculty of Strelsau by[SEP]refusing to summon to my bedside any of them, save a young man, a friend[SEP]of Fritz’s, whom we could trust; secondly, I received word from Marshal[SEP]Strakencz that my orders seemed to have no more weight than his, and[SEP]that the Princess Flavia was leaving for Tarlenheim under his unwilling[SEP]escort (news whereat I strove not to be glad and proud); and thirdly, my[SEP]brother, the Duke of Strelsau, although too well informed to believe the[SEP]account of the origin of my sickness, was yet persuaded by the reports[SEP]and by my seeming inactivity that I was in truth incapable of action,[SEP]and that my life was in some danger.[SEP]This I learnt from the man Johann,[SEP]whom I was compelled to trust and send back to Zenda, where, by the way,[SEP]Rupert Hentzau had him soundly flogged for daring to smirch the morals[SEP]of Zenda by staying out all night in the pursuits of love.[SEP]This, from[SEP]Rupert, Johann deeply resented, and the duke’s approval of it did more[SEP]to bind the keeper to my side than all my promises.[EOT]
[SOT]On Flavia’s arrival I cannot dwell.[SEP]Her joy at finding me up and well,[SEP]instead of on my back and fighting with death, makes a picture that[SEP]even now dances before my eyes till they grow too dim to see it; and her[SEP]reproaches that I had not trusted even her must excuse the means I took[SEP]to quiet them.[SEP]In truth, to have her with me once more was like a taste[SEP]of heaven to a damned soul, the sweeter for the inevitable doom that[SEP]was to follow; and I rejoiced in being able to waste two whole days with[SEP]her.[SEP]And when I had wasted two days, the Duke of Strelsau arranged a[SEP]hunting-party.[EOT]
[SOT]The stroke was near now.[SEP]For Sapt and I, after anxious consultations,[SEP]had resolved that we must risk a blow, our resolution being clinched[SEP]by Johann’s news that the King grew peaked, pale, and ill, and that his[SEP]health was breaking down under his rigorous confinement.[SEP]Now a man--be[SEP]he king or no king--may as well die swiftly and as becomes a gentleman,[SEP]from bullet or thrust, as rot his life out in a cellar![SEP]That thought[SEP]made prompt action advisable in the interests of the King; from my own[SEP]point of view, it grew more and more necessary.[SEP]For Strakencz urged on[SEP]me the need of a speedy marriage, and my own inclinations seconded him[SEP]with such terrible insistence that I feared for my resolution.[SEP]I do not[SEP]believe that I should have done the deed I dreamt of; but I might have[SEP]come to flight, and my flight would have ruined the cause.[SEP]And--yes, I[SEP]am no saint (ask my little sister-in-law), and worse still might have[SEP]happened.[EOT]
[SOT]It is perhaps as strange a thing as has ever been in the history of a[SEP]country that the King’s brother and the King’s personator, in a time of[SEP]profound outward peace, near a placid, undisturbed country town, under[SEP]semblance of amity, should wage a desperate war for the person and life[SEP]of the King.[SEP]Yet such was the struggle that began now between Zenda and[SEP]Tarlenheim.[SEP]When I look back on the time, I seem to myself to have been[SEP]half mad.[SEP]Sapt has told me that I suffered no interference and listened[SEP]to no remonstrances; and if ever a King of Ruritania ruled like a[SEP]despot, I was, in those days, the man.[SEP]Look where I would, I saw nothing[SEP]that made life sweet to me, and I took my life in my hand and carried it[SEP]carelessly as a man dangles an old glove.[SEP]At first they strove to guard[SEP]me, to keep me safe, to persuade me not to expose myself; but when they[SEP]saw how I was set, there grew up among them--whether they knew the truth[SEP]or not--a feeling that Fate ruled the issue, and that I must be left to[SEP]play my game with Michael my own way.[EOT]
[SOT]Late next night I rose from table, where Flavia had sat by me, and[SEP]conducted her to the door of her apartments.[SEP]There I kissed her hand,[SEP]and bade her sleep sound and wake to happy days.[SEP]Then I changed my[SEP]clothes and went out.[SEP]Sapt and Fritz were waiting for me with six men[SEP]and the horses.[SEP]Over his saddle Sapt carried a long coil of rope, and[SEP]both were heavily armed.[SEP]I had with me a short stout cudgel and a long[SEP]knife.[SEP]Making a circuit, we avoided the town, and in an hour found[SEP]ourselves slowly mounting the hill that led to the Castle of Zenda.[SEP]The[SEP]night was dark and very stormy; gusts of wind and spits of rain caught[SEP]us as we breasted the incline, and the great trees moaned and sighed.[SEP]When we came to a thick clump, about a quarter of a mile from the[SEP]Castle, we bade our six friends hide there with the horses.[SEP]Sapt had a[SEP]whistle, and they could rejoin us in a few moments if danger came: but,[SEP]up to now, we had met no one.[SEP]I hoped that Michael was still off his[SEP]guard, believing me to be safe in bed.[SEP]However that might be, we gained[SEP]the top of the hill without accident, and found ourselves on the edge of[SEP]the moat where it sweeps under the road, separating the Old Castle[SEP]from it.[SEP]A tree stood on the edge of the bank, and Sapt, silently and[SEP]diligently, set to make fast the rope.[SEP]I stripped off my boots, took a[SEP]pull at a flask of brandy, loosened the knife in its sheath, and took[SEP]the cudgel between my teeth.[SEP]Then I shook hands with my friends, not[SEP]heeding a last look of entreaty from Fritz, and laid hold of the rope.[SEP]I[SEP]was going to have a look at “Jacob’s Ladder.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Gently I lowered myself into the water.[SEP]Though the night was wild, the[SEP]day had been warm and bright, and the water was not cold.[SEP]I struck out,[SEP]and began to swim round the great walls which frowned above me.[SEP]I could[SEP]see only three yards ahead; I had then good hopes of not being seen,[SEP]as I crept along close under the damp, moss-grown masonry.[SEP]There were[SEP]lights from the new part of the Castle on the other side, and now and[SEP]again I heard laughter and merry shouts.[SEP]I fancied I recognized young[SEP]Rupert Hentzau’s ringing tones, and pictured him flushed with wine.[SEP]Recalling my thoughts to the business in hand, I rested a moment.[SEP]If[SEP]Johann’s description were right, I must be near the window now.[SEP]Very[SEP]slowly I moved; and out of the darkness ahead loomed a shape.[SEP]It was[SEP]the pipe, curving from the window to the water: about four feet of its[SEP]surface were displayed; it was as big round as two men.[SEP]I was about to[SEP]approach it, when I saw something else, and my heart stood still.[SEP]The nose of a boat protruded beyond the pipe on the other side; and[SEP]listening intently, I heard a slight shuffle--as of a man shifting his[SEP]position.[SEP]Who was the man who guarded Michael’s invention?[SEP]Was he awake[SEP]or was he asleep?[SEP]I felt if my knife were ready, and trod water; as[SEP]I did so, I found bottom under my feet.[SEP]The foundations of the Castle[SEP]extended some fifteen inches, making a ledge; and I stood on it, out of[SEP]water from my armpits upwards.[SEP]Then I crouched and peered through the[SEP]darkness under the pipe, where, curving, it left a space.[EOT]
[SOT]There was a man in the boat.[SEP]A rifle lay by him--I saw the gleam of[SEP]the barrel.[SEP]Here was the sentinel![SEP]He sat very still.[SEP]I listened; he[SEP]breathed heavily, regularly, monotonously.[SEP]By heaven, he slept![SEP]Kneeling[SEP]on the shelf, I drew forward under the pipe till my face was within two[SEP]feet of his.[SEP]He was a big man, I saw.[SEP]It was Max Holf, the brother of[SEP]Johann.[SEP]My hand stole to my belt, and I drew out my knife.[SEP]Of all the[SEP]deeds of my life, I love the least to think of this, and whether it were[SEP]the act of a man or a traitor I will not ask.[SEP]I said to myself: “It is[SEP]war--and the King’s life is the stake.[SEP]” And I raised myself from beneath[SEP]the pipe and stood up by the boat, which lay moored by the ledge.[SEP]Holding my breath, I marked the spot and raised my arm.[SEP]The great fellow[SEP]stirred.[SEP]He opened his eyes--wide, wider.[SEP]He gasped in terror at my[SEP]face and clutched at his rifle.[SEP]I struck home.[SEP]And I heard the chorus of[SEP]a love-song from the opposite bank.[EOT]
[SOT]Leaving him where he lay, a huddled mass, I turned to “Jacob’s Ladder.[SEP]”[SEP]My time was short.[SEP]This fellow’s turn of watching might be over[SEP]directly, and relief would come.[SEP]Leaning over the pipe, I examined it,[SEP]from the end near the water to the topmost extremity where it passed, or[SEP]seemed to pass, through the masonry of the wall.[SEP]There was no break[SEP]in it, no chink.[SEP]Dropping on my knees, I tested the under side.[SEP]And[SEP]my breath went quick and fast, for on this lower side, where the pipe[SEP]should have clung close to the masonry, there was a gleam of light![SEP]That[SEP]light must come from the cell of the King![SEP]I set my shoulder against the[SEP]pipe and exerted my strength.[SEP]The chink widened a very, very little,[SEP]and hastily I desisted; I had done enough to show that the pipe was not[SEP]fixed in the masonry at the lower side.[EOT]
[SOT]Then I heard a voice--a harsh, grating voice:[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, sire, if you have had enough of my society, I will leave you to[SEP]repose; but I must fasten the little ornaments first.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was Detchard![SEP]I caught the English accent in a moment.[EOT]
[SOT]“Have you anything to ask, sire, before we part?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The King’s voice followed.[SEP]It was his, though it was faint and[SEP]hollow--different from the merry tones I had heard in the glades of the[SEP]forest.[EOT]
[SOT]“Pray my brother,” said the King, “to kill me.[SEP]I am dying by inches[SEP]here.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The duke does not desire your death, sire--yet,” sneered Detchard;[SEP]“when he does behold your path to heaven![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The King answered:[EOT]
[SOT]“So be it![SEP]And now, if your orders allow it, pray leave me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“May you dream of paradise![SEP]” said the ruffian.[EOT]
[SOT]The light disappeared.[SEP]I heard the bolts of the door run home.[SEP]And then[SEP]I heard the sobs of the King.[SEP]He was alone, as he thought.[SEP]Who dares[SEP]mock at him?[EOT]
[SOT]I did not venture to speak to him.[SEP]The risk of some exclamation escaping[SEP]him in surprise was too great.[SEP]I dared do nothing that night; and my[SEP]task now was to get myself away in safety, and to carry off the carcass[SEP]of the dead man.[SEP]To leave him there would tell too much.[SEP]Casting loose[SEP]the boat, I got in.[SEP]The wind was blowing a gale now, and there was[SEP]little danger of oars being heard.[SEP]I rowed swiftly round to where my[SEP]friends waited.[SEP]I had just reached the spot, when a loud whistle sounded[SEP]over the moat behind me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hullo, Max![SEP]” I heard shouted.[EOT]
[SOT]I hailed Sapt in a low tone.[SEP]The rope came down.[SEP]I tied it round the[SEP]corpse, and then went up it myself.[EOT]
[SOT]“Whistle you too,” I whispered, “for our men, and haul in the line.[SEP]No[SEP]talk now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]They hauled up the body.[SEP]Just as it reached the road, three men on[SEP]horseback swept round from the front of the Castle.[SEP]We saw them; but,[SEP]being on foot ourselves, we escaped their notice.[SEP]But we heard our men[SEP]coming up with a shout.[EOT]
[SOT]“The devil, but it’s dark![SEP]” cried a ringing voice.[EOT]
[SOT]It was young Rupert.[SEP]A moment later, shots rang out.[SEP]Our people had met[SEP]them.[SEP]I started forward at a run, Sapt and Fritz following me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Thrust, thrust![SEP]” cried Rupert again, and a loud groan following told[SEP]that he himself was not behind-hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m done, Rupert![SEP]” cried a voice.[SEP]“They’re three to one.[SEP]Save[SEP]yourself![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I ran on, holding my cudgel in my hand.[SEP]Suddenly a horse came towards[SEP]me.[SEP]A man was on it, leaning over his shoulder.[EOT]
[SOT]“Are you cooked too, Krafstein?[SEP]” he cried.[EOT]
[SOT]There was no answer.[EOT]
[SOT]I sprang to the horse’s head.[SEP]It was Rupert Hentzau.[EOT]
[SOT]“At last![SEP]” I cried.[EOT]
[SOT]For we seemed to have him.[SEP]He had only his sword in his hand.[SEP]My men[SEP]were hot upon him; Sapt and Fritz were running up.[SEP]I had outstripped[SEP]them; but if they got close enough to fire, he must die or surrender.[EOT]
[SOT]“At last![SEP]” I cried.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s the play-actor![SEP]” cried he, slashing at my cudgel.[SEP]He cut it clean[SEP]in two; and, judging discretion better than death, I ducked my head[SEP]and (I blush to tell it) scampered for my life.[SEP]The devil was in Rupert[SEP]Hentzau; for he put spurs to his horse, and I, turning to look, saw him[SEP]ride, full gallop, to the edge of the moat and leap in, while the shots[SEP]of our party fell thick round him like hail.[SEP]With one gleam of moonlight[SEP]we should have riddled him with balls; but, in the darkness, he won to[SEP]the corner of the Castle, and vanished from our sight.[EOT]
[SOT]“The deuce take him![SEP]” grinned Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s a pity,” said I, “that he’s a villain.[SEP]Whom have we got?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We had Lauengram and Krafstein: they lay dead; and, concealment being[SEP]no longer possible, we flung them, with Max, into the moat; and, drawing[SEP]together in a compact body, rode off down the hill.[SEP]And, in our midst,[SEP]went the bodies of three gallant gentlemen.[SEP]Thus we travelled home,[SEP]heavy at heart for the death of our friends, sore uneasy concerning[SEP]the King, and cut to the quick that young Rupert had played yet another[SEP]winning hand with us.[EOT]
[SOT]For my own part, I was vexed and angry that I had killed no man in open[SEP]fight, but only stabbed a knave in his sleep.[SEP]And I did not love to hear[SEP]Rupert call me a play-actor.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 15[EOT]
[SOT]I Talk with a Tempter[EOT]
[SOT]Ruritania is not England, or the quarrel between Duke Michael and myself[SEP]could not have gone on, with the extraordinary incidents which marked[SEP]it, without more public notice being directed to it.[SEP]Duels were frequent[SEP]among all the upper classes, and private quarrels between great men[SEP]kept the old habit of spreading to their friends and dependents.[SEP]Nevertheless, after the affray which I have just related, such reports[SEP]began to circulate that I felt it necessary to be on my guard.[SEP]The death[SEP]of the gentlemen involved could not be hidden from their relatives.[SEP]I[SEP]issued a stern order, declaring that duelling had attained unprecedented[SEP]licence (the Chancellor drew up the document for me, and very well he[SEP]did it), and forbidding it save in the gravest cases.[SEP]I sent a public[SEP]and stately apology to Michael, and he returned a deferential and[SEP]courteous reply to me; for our one point of union was--and it underlay[SEP]all our differences and induced an unwilling harmony between our[SEP]actions--that we could neither of us afford to throw our cards on the[SEP]table.[SEP]He, as well as I, was a “play-actor”, and, hating one another, we[SEP]combined to dupe public opinion.[SEP]Unfortunately, however, the necessity[SEP]for concealment involved the necessity of delay: the King might die[SEP]in his prison, or even be spirited off somewhere else; it could not be[SEP]helped.[SEP]For a little while I was compelled to observe a truce, and[SEP]my only consolation was that Flavia most warmly approved of my edict[SEP]against duelling, and, when I expressed delight at having won her[SEP]favour, prayed me, if her favour were any motive to me, to prohibit the[SEP]practice altogether.[EOT]
[SOT]“Wait till we are married,” said I, smiling.[EOT]
[SOT]Not the least peculiar result of the truce and of the secrecy which[SEP]dictated it was that the town of Zenda became in the day-time--I would[SEP]not have trusted far to its protection by night--a sort of neutral zone,[SEP]where both parties could safely go; and I, riding down one day with[SEP]Flavia and Sapt, had an encounter with an acquaintance, which presented[SEP]a ludicrous side, but was at the same time embarrassing.[SEP]As I rode[SEP]along, I met a dignified looking person driving in a two-horsed[SEP]carriage.[SEP]He stopped his horses, got out, and approached me, bowing low.[SEP]I recognized the Head of the Strelsau Police.[EOT]
[SOT]“Your Majesty’s ordinance as to duelling is receiving our best[SEP]attention,” he assured me.[EOT]
[SOT]If the best attention involved his presence in Zenda, I determined at[SEP]once to dispense with it.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is that what brings you to Zenda, Prefect?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why no, sire; I am here because I desired to oblige the British[SEP]Ambassador.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What’s the British Ambassador doing _dans cette galère_?[SEP]” said I,[SEP]carelessly.[EOT]
[SOT]“A young countryman of his, sire--a man of some position--is missing.[SEP]His friends have not heard from him for two months, and there is reason[SEP]to believe that he was last seen in Zenda.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Flavia was paying little attention.[SEP]I dared not look at Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“What reason?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“A friend of his in Paris--a certain M.[SEP]Featherly--has given us[SEP]information which makes it possible that he came here, and the officials[SEP]of the railway recollect his name on some luggage.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What was his name?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Rassendyll, sire,” he answered; and I saw that the name meant nothing[SEP]to him.[SEP]But, glancing at Flavia, he lowered his voice, as he went on:[SEP]“It is thought that he may have followed a lady here.[SEP]Has your Majesty[SEP]heard of a certain Madame de Mauban?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Why, yes,” said I, my eye involuntarily travelling towards the Castle.[EOT]
[SOT]“She arrived in Ruritania about the same time as this Rassendyll.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I caught the Prefect’s glance; he was regarding me with enquiry writ[SEP]large on his face.[EOT]
[SOT]“Sapt,” said I, “I must speak a word to the Prefect.[SEP]Will you ride on[SEP]a few paces with the princess?[SEP]” And I added to the Prefect: “Come, sir,[SEP]what do you mean?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He drew close to me, and I bent in the saddle.[EOT]
[SOT]“If he were in love with the lady?[SEP]” he whispered.[SEP]“Nothing has been[SEP]heard of him for two months;” and this time it was the eye of the[SEP]Prefect which travelled towards the Castle.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, the lady is there,” I said quietly.[SEP]“But I don’t suppose Mr.[SEP]Rassendyll--is that the name?[SEP]--is.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The duke,” he whispered, “does not like rivals, sire.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You’re right there,” said I, with all sincerity.[SEP]“But surely you hint[SEP]at a very grave charge?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He spread his hands out in apology.[SEP]I whispered in his ear:[EOT]
[SOT]“This is a grave matter.[SEP]Go back to Strelsau--”[EOT]
[SOT]“But, sire, if I have a clue here?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Go back to Strelsau,” I repeated.[SEP]“Tell the Ambassador that you have a[SEP]clue, but that you must be left alone for a week or two.[SEP]Meanwhile, I’ll[SEP]charge myself with looking into the matter.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“The Ambassador is very pressing, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You must quiet him.[SEP]Come, sir; you see that if your suspicions are[SEP]correct, it is an affair in which we must move with caution.[SEP]We can have[SEP]no scandal.[SEP]Mind you return tonight.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He promised to obey me, and I rode on to rejoin my companions, a little[SEP]easier in my mind.[SEP]Enquiries after me must be stopped at all hazards for[SEP]a week or two; and this clever official had come surprisingly near the[SEP]truth.[SEP]His impression might be useful some day, but if he acted on it[SEP]now it might mean the worse to the King.[SEP]Heartily did I curse George[SEP]Featherly for not holding his tongue.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well,” asked Flavia, “have you finished your business?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Most satisfactorily,” said I.[SEP]“Come, shall we turn round?[SEP]We are almost[SEP]trenching on my brother’s territory.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We were, in fact, at the extreme end of the town, just where the hills[SEP]begin to mount towards the Castle.[SEP]We cast our eyes up, admiring the[SEP]massive beauty of the old walls, and we saw a _cortège_ winding slowly[SEP]down the hill.[SEP]On it came.[EOT]
[SOT]“Let us go back,” said Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]“I should like to stay,” said Flavia; and I reined my horse beside hers.[EOT]
[SOT]We could distinguish the approaching party now.[SEP]There came first two[SEP]mounted servants in black uniforms, relieved only by a silver badge.[SEP]These were followed by a car drawn by four horses: on it, under a[SEP]heavy pall, lay a coffin; behind it rode a man in plain black clothes,[SEP]carrying his hat in his hand.[SEP]Sapt uncovered, and we stood waiting,[SEP]Flavia keeping by me and laying her hand on my arm.[EOT]
[SOT]“It is one of the gentlemen killed in the quarrel, I expect,” she said.[EOT]
[SOT]I beckoned to a groom.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ride and ask whom they escort,” I ordered.[EOT]
[SOT]He rode up to the servants, and I saw him pass on to the gentleman who[SEP]rode behind.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s Rupert of Hentzau,” whispered Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]Rupert it was, and directly afterwards, waving to the procession to[SEP]stand still, Rupert trotted up to me.[SEP]He was in a frock-coat, tightly[SEP]buttoned, and trousers.[SEP]He wore an aspect of sadness, and he bowed with[SEP]profound respect.[SEP]Yet suddenly he smiled, and I smiled too, for old[SEP]Sapt’s hand lay in his left breast-pocket, and Rupert and I both guessed[SEP]what lay in the hand inside the pocket.[EOT]
[SOT]“Your Majesty asks whom we escort,” said Rupert.[SEP]“It is my dear friend,[SEP]Albert of Lauengram.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Sir,” said I, “no one regrets the unfortunate affair more than I.[SEP]My[SEP]ordinance, which I mean to have obeyed, is witness to it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Poor fellow![SEP]” said Flavia softly, and I saw Rupert’s eyes flash at her.[SEP]Whereat I grew red; for, if I had my way, Rupert Hentzau should not[SEP]have defiled her by so much as a glance.[SEP]Yet he did it and dared to let[SEP]admiration be seen in his look.[EOT]
[SOT]“Your Majesty’s words are gracious,” he said.[SEP]“I grieve for my friend.[SEP]Yet, sire, others must soon lie as he lies now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It is a thing we all do well to remember, my lord,” I rejoined.[EOT]
[SOT]“Even kings, sire,” said Rupert, in a moralizing tone; and old Sapt[SEP]swore softly by my side.[EOT]
[SOT]“It is true,” said I.[SEP]“How fares my brother, my lord?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He is better, sire.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I am rejoiced.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He hopes soon to leave for Strelsau, when his health is secured.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He is only convalescent then?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“There remain one or two small troubles,” answered the insolent fellow,[SEP]in the mildest tone in the world.[EOT]
[SOT]“Express my earnest hope,” said Flavia, “that they may soon cease to[SEP]trouble him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Your Royal Highness’s wish is, humbly, my own,” said Rupert, with a[SEP]bold glance that brought a blush to Flavia’s cheek.[EOT]
[SOT]I bowed; and Rupert, bowing lower, backed his horse and signed to his[SEP]party to proceed.[SEP]With a sudden impulse, I rode after him.[SEP]He turned[SEP]swiftly, fearing that, even in the presence of the dead and before a[SEP]lady’s eyes, I meant him mischief.[EOT]
[SOT]“You fought as a brave man the other night,” I said.[SEP]“Come, you are[SEP]young, sir.[SEP]If you will deliver your prisoner alive to me, you shall[SEP]come to no hurt.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He looked at me with a mocking smile; but suddenly he rode nearer to me.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m unarmed,” he said; “and our old Sapt there could pick me off in a[SEP]minute.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’m not afraid,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“No, curse you![SEP]” he answered.[SEP]“Look here, I made you a proposal from the[SEP]duke once.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll hear nothing from Black Michael,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Then hear one from me.[SEP]” He lowered his voice to a whisper.[SEP]“Attack the[SEP]Castle boldly.[SEP]Let Sapt and Tarlenheim lead.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Go on,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Arrange the time with me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I have such confidence in you, my lord![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Tut![SEP]I’m talking business now.[SEP]Sapt there and Fritz will fall; Black[SEP]Michael will fall--”[EOT]
[SOT]“--Black Michael will fall, like the dog he is; the prisoner, as you[SEP]call him, will go by ‘Jacob’s Ladder’--ah, you know that![SEP]--to hell![SEP]Two[SEP]men will be left--I, Rupert Hentzau, and you, the King of Ruritania.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He paused, and then, in a voice that quivered with eagerness, added:[EOT]
[SOT]“Isn’t that a hand to play?[SEP]--a throne and your princess![SEP]And for me, say[SEP]a competence and your Majesty’s gratitude.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Surely,” I exclaimed, “while you’re above ground, hell wants its[SEP]master![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, think it over,” he said.[SEP]“And, look you, it would take more than[SEP]a scruple or two to keep me from yonder girl,” and his evil eye flashed[SEP]again at her I loved.[EOT]
[SOT]“Get out of my reach![SEP]” said I; and yet in a moment I began to laugh for[SEP]the very audacity of it.[EOT]
[SOT]“Would you turn against your master?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]He swore at Michael for being what the offspring of a legal, though[SEP]morganatic, union should not be called, and said to me in an almost[SEP]confidential and apparently friendly tone:[EOT]
[SOT]“He gets in my way, you know.[SEP]He’s a jealous brute![SEP]Faith, I nearly[SEP]stuck a knife into him last night; he came most cursedly _mal àpropos_![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]My temper was well under control now; I was learning something.[EOT]
[SOT]“A lady?[SEP]” I asked negligently.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, and a beauty,” he nodded.[SEP]“But you’ve seen her.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah![SEP]was it at a tea-party, when some of your friends got on the wrong[SEP]side of the table?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What can you expect of fools like Detchard and De Gautet?[SEP]I wish I’d[SEP]been there.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And the duke interferes?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well,” said Rupert meditatively, “that’s hardly a fair way of putting[SEP]it, perhaps.[SEP]I want to interfere.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And she prefers the duke?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, the silly creature![SEP]Ah, well, you think about my plan,” and, with a[SEP]bow, he pricked his horse and trotted after the body of his friend.[EOT]
[SOT]I went back to Flavia and Sapt, pondering on the strangeness of the man.[SEP]Wicked men I have known in plenty, but Rupert Hentzau remains unique in[SEP]my experience.[SEP]And if there be another anywhere, let him be caught and[SEP]hanged out of hand.[SEP]So say I![EOT]
[SOT]“He’s very handsome, isn’t he?[SEP]” said Flavia.[EOT]
[SOT]Well, of course, she didn’t know him as I did; yet I was put out, for I[SEP]thought his bold glances would have made her angry.[SEP]But my dear Flavia[SEP]was a woman, and so--she was not put out.[SEP]On the contrary, she thought[SEP]young Rupert very handsome--as, beyond question, the ruffian was.[EOT]
[SOT]“And how sad he looked at his friend’s death![SEP]” said she.[EOT]
[SOT]“He’ll have better reason to be sad at his own,” observed Sapt, with a[SEP]grim smile.[EOT]
[SOT]As for me, I grew sulky; unreasonable it was perhaps, for what better[SEP]business had I to look at her with love than had even Rupert’s lustful[SEP]eyes?[SEP]And sulky I remained till, as evening fell and we rode up to[SEP]Tarlenheim, Sapt having fallen behind in case anyone should be[SEP]following us, Flavia, riding close beside me, said softly, with a little[SEP]half-ashamed laugh:[EOT]
[SOT]“Unless you smile, Rudolf, I cry.[SEP]Why are you angry?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It was something that fellow said to me,” said I, but I was smiling as[SEP]we reached the door and dismounted.[EOT]
[SOT]There a servant handed me a note: it was unaddressed.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is it for me?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, sire; a boy brought it.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I tore it open:[EOT]
[SOT]Johann carries this for me.[SEP]I warned you once.[SEP]In the name of God, and[SEP]if you are a man, rescue me from this den of murderers![SEP]--A.[SEP]de M.[EOT]
[SOT]I handed it to Sapt; but all that the tough old soul said in reply to[SEP]this piteous appeal was:[EOT]
[SOT]“Whose fault brought her there?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Nevertheless, not being faultless myself, I took leave to pity[SEP]Antoinette de Mauban.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 16[EOT]
[SOT]A Desperate Plan[EOT]
[SOT]As I had ridden publicly in Zenda, and had talked there with Rupert[SEP]Hentzau, of course all pretence of illness was at an end.[SEP]I marked the[SEP]effect on the garrison of Zenda: they ceased to be seen abroad; and any[SEP]of my men who went near the Castle reported that the utmost vigilance[SEP]prevailed there.[SEP]Touched as I was by Madame de Mauban’s appeal, I seemed[SEP]as powerless to befriend her as I had proved to help the King.[SEP]Michael[SEP]bade me defiance; and although he too had been seen outside the walls,[SEP]with more disregard for appearances than he had hitherto shown, he did[SEP]not take the trouble to send any excuse for his failure to wait on the[SEP]King.[SEP]Time ran on in inactivity, when every moment was pressing; for[SEP]not only was I faced with the new danger which the stir about my[SEP]disappearance brought on me, but great murmurs had arisen in Strelsau at[SEP]my continued absence from the city.[SEP]They had been greater, but for the[SEP]knowledge that Flavia was with me; and for this reason I suffered her to[SEP]stay, though I hated to have her where danger was, and though every[SEP]day of our present sweet intercourse strained my endurance almost to[SEP]breaking.[SEP]As a final blow, nothing would content my advisers, Strakencz[SEP]and the Chancellor (who came out from Strelsau to make an urgent[SEP]representation to me), save that I should appoint a day for the public[SEP]solemnization of my betrothal, a ceremony which in Ruritania is well[SEP]nigh as binding and great a thing as the marriage itself.[SEP]And this--with[SEP]Flavia sitting by me--I was forced to do, setting a date a fortnight[SEP]ahead, and appointing the Cathedral in Strelsau as the place.[SEP]And this[SEP]formal act being published far and wide, caused great joy throughout the[SEP]kingdom, and was the talk of all tongues; so that I reckoned there were[SEP]but two men who chafed at it--I mean Black Michael and myself; and but[SEP]one who did not know of it--that one the man whose name I bore, the King[SEP]of Ruritania.[EOT]
[SOT]In truth, I heard something of the way the news was received in the[SEP]Castle; for after an interval of three days, the man Johann, greedy for[SEP]more money, though fearful for his life, again found means to visit us.[SEP]He had been waiting on the duke when the tidings came.[SEP]Black Michael’s[SEP]face had grown blacker still, and he had sworn savagely; nor was he[SEP]better pleased when young Rupert took oath that I meant to do as I[SEP]said, and turning to Madame de Mauban, wished her joy on a rival gone.[SEP]Michael’s hand stole towards his sword (said Johann), but not a bit did[SEP]Rupert care; for he rallied the duke on having made a better King than[SEP]had reigned for years past in Ruritania.[SEP]“And,” said he, with a meaning[SEP]bow to his exasperated master, “the devil sends the princess a finer man[SEP]than heaven had marked out for her, by my soul, it does![SEP]” Then Michael[SEP]harshly bade him hold his tongue, and leave them; but Rupert must needs[SEP]first kiss madame’s hand, which he did as though he loved her, while[SEP]Michael glared at him.[EOT]
[SOT]This was the lighter side of the fellow’s news; but more serious came[SEP]behind, and it was plain that if time pressed at Tarlenheim, it pressed[SEP]none the less fiercely at Zenda.[SEP]For the King was very sick: Johann had[SEP]seen him, and he was wasted and hardly able to move.[SEP]“There could be no[SEP]thought of taking another for him now.[SEP]” So alarmed were they, that they[SEP]had sent for a physician from Strelsau; and the physician having been[SEP]introduced into the King’s cell, had come forth pale and trembling, and[SEP]urgently prayed the duke to let him go back and meddle no more in the[SEP]affair; but the duke would not, and held him there a prisoner, telling[SEP]him his life was safe if the King lived while the duke desired and died[SEP]when the duke desired--not otherwise.[SEP]And, persuaded by the physician,[SEP]they had allowed Madame de Mauban to visit the King and give him such[SEP]attendance as his state needed, and as only a woman can give.[SEP]Yet his[SEP]life hung in the balance; and I was still strong and whole and free.[SEP]Wherefore great gloom reigned at Zenda; and save when they quarrelled,[SEP]to which they were very prone, they hardly spoke.[SEP]But the deeper the[SEP]depression of the rest, young Rupert went about Satan’s work with a[SEP]smile in his eye and a song on his lip; and laughed “fit to burst” (said[SEP]Johann) because the duke always set Detchard to guard the King when[SEP]Madame de Mauban was in the cell--which precaution was, indeed, not[SEP]unwise in my careful brother.[SEP]Thus Johann told his tale and seized his[SEP]crowns.[SEP]Yet he besought us to allow him to stay with us in Tarlenheim,[SEP]and not venture his head again in the lion’s den; but we had need of him[SEP]there, and, although I refused to constrain him, I prevailed on him by[SEP]increased rewards to go back and carry tidings to Madame de Mauban that[SEP]I was working for her, and that, if she could, she should speak one[SEP]word of comfort to the King.[SEP]For while suspense is bad for the sick, yet[SEP]despair is worse still, and it might be that the King lay dying of mere[SEP]hopelessness, for I could learn of no definite disease that afflicted[SEP]him.[EOT]
[SOT]“And how do they guard the King now?[SEP]” I asked, remembering that two of[SEP]the Six were dead, and Max Holf also.[EOT]
[SOT]“Detchard and Bersonin watch by night, Rupert Hentzau and De Gautet by[SEP]day, sir,” he answered.[EOT]
[SOT]“Only two at a time?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, sir; but the others rest in a room just above, and are within sound[SEP]of a cry or a whistle.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“A room just above?[SEP]I didn’t know of that.[SEP]Is there any communication[SEP]between it and the room where they watch?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, sir.[SEP]You must go down a few stairs and through the door by the[SEP]drawbridge, and so to where the King is lodged.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And that door is locked?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Only the four lords have keys, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I drew nearer to him.[EOT]
[SOT]“And have they keys of the grating?[SEP]” I asked in a low whisper.[EOT]
[SOT]“I think, sir, only Detchard and Rupert.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Where does the duke lodge?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“In the chateau, on the first floor.[SEP]His apartments are on the right as[SEP]you go towards the drawbridge.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And Madame de Mauban?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Just opposite, on the left.[SEP]But her door is locked after she has[SEP]entered.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“To keep her in?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Doubtless, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps for another reason?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It is possible.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And the duke, I suppose, has the key?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes.[SEP]And the drawbridge is drawn back at night, and of that, too, the[SEP]duke holds the key, so that it cannot be run across the moat without[SEP]application to him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And where do you sleep?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“In the entrance hall of the chateau, with five servants.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They have pikes, sir, but no firearms.[SEP]The duke will not trust them[SEP]with firearms.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then at last I took the matter boldly in my hands.[SEP]I had failed once at[SEP]“Jacob’s Ladder;” I should fail again there.[SEP]I must make the attack from[SEP]the other side.[EOT]
[SOT]“I have promised you twenty thousand crowns,” said I.[SEP]“You shall have[SEP]fifty thousand if you will do what I ask of you tomorrow night.[SEP]But,[SEP]first, do those servants know who your prisoner is?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, sir.[SEP]They believe him to be some private enemy of the duke’s.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And they would not doubt that I am the King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“How should they?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Look to this, then.[SEP]Tomorrow, at two in the morning exactly, fling open[SEP]the front door of the chateau.[SEP]Don’t fail by an instant.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Shall you be there, sir?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ask no questions.[SEP]Do what I tell you.[SEP]Say the hall is close, or what[SEP]you will.[SEP]That is all I ask of you.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And may I escape by the door, sir, when I have opened it?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, as quick as your legs will carry you.[SEP]One thing more.[SEP]Carry this[SEP]note to madame--oh, it’s in French, you can’t read it--and charge her,[SEP]for the sake of all our lives, not to fail in what it orders.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The man was trembling but I had to trust to what he had of courage and[SEP]to what he had of honesty.[SEP]I dared not wait, for I feared that the King[SEP]would die.[EOT]
[SOT]When the fellow was gone, I called Sapt and Fritz to me, and unfolded[SEP]the plan that I had formed.[SEP]Sapt shook his head over it.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why can’t you wait?[SEP]” he asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“The King may die.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Michael will be forced to act before that.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Then,” said I, “the King may live.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, and if he does?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“For a fortnight?[SEP]” I asked simply.[EOT]
[SOT]And Sapt bit his moustache.[EOT]
[SOT]Suddenly Fritz von Tarlenheim laid his hand on my shoulder.[EOT]
[SOT]“Let us go and make the attempt,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]“I mean you to go--don’t be afraid,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, but do you stay here, and take care of the princess.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]A gleam came into old Sapt’s eye.[EOT]
[SOT]“We should have Michael one way or the other then,” he chuckled;[SEP]“whereas if you go and are killed with the King, what will become of[SEP]those of us who are left?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They will serve Queen Flavia,” said I, “and I would to God I could be[SEP]one of them.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]A pause followed.[SEP]Old Sapt broke it by saying sadly, yet with an unmeant[SEP]drollery that set Fritz and me laughing:[EOT]
[SOT]“Why didn’t old Rudolf the Third marry your--great-grandmother, was it?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Come,” said I, “it is the King we are thinking about.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It is true,” said Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]“Moreover,” I went on, “I have been an impostor for the profit of[SEP]another, but I will not be one for my own; and if the King is not alive[SEP]and on his throne before the day of betrothal comes, I will tell the[SEP]truth, come what may.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You shall go, lad,” said Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]Here is the plan I had made.[SEP]A strong party under Sapt’s command was[SEP]to steal up to the door of the chateau.[SEP]If discovered prematurely, they[SEP]were to kill anyone who found them--with their swords, for I wanted no[SEP]noise of firing.[SEP]If all went well, they would be at the door when Johann[SEP]opened it.[SEP]They were to rush in and secure the servants if their mere[SEP]presence and the use of the King’s name were not enough.[SEP]At the same[SEP]moment--and on this hinged the plan--a woman’s cry was to ring out loud[SEP]and shrill from Antoinette de Mauban’s chamber.[SEP]Again and again she was[SEP]to cry: “Help, help![SEP]Michael, help![SEP]” and then to utter the name of young[SEP]Rupert Hentzau.[SEP]Then, as we hoped, Michael, in fury, would rush out of[SEP]his apartments opposite, and fall alive into the hands of Sapt.[SEP]Still[SEP]the cries would go on; and my men would let down the drawbridge; and it[SEP]would be strange if Rupert, hearing his name thus taken in vain, did not[SEP]descend from where he slept and seek to cross.[SEP]De Gautet might or might[SEP]not come with him: that must be left to chance.[EOT]
[SOT]And when Rupert set his foot on the drawbridge?[SEP]There was my part: for I[SEP]was minded for another swim in the moat; and, lest I should grow weary,[SEP]I had resolved to take with me a small wooden ladder, on which I could[SEP]rest my arms in the water--and my feet when I left it.[SEP]I would rear it[SEP]against the wall just by the bridge; and when the bridge was across, I[SEP]would stealthily creep on to it--and then if Rupert or De Gautet crossed[SEP]in safety, it would be my misfortune, not my fault.[SEP]They dead, two men[SEP]only would remain; and for them we must trust to the confusion we had[SEP]created and to a sudden rush.[SEP]We should have the keys of the door that[SEP]led to the all-important rooms.[SEP]Perhaps they would rush out.[SEP]If they[SEP]stood by their orders, then the King’s life hung on the swiftness with[SEP]which we could force the outer door; and I thanked God that not Rupert[SEP]Hentzau watched, but Detchard.[SEP]For though Detchard was a cool man,[SEP]relentless, and no coward, he had neither the dash nor the recklessness[SEP]of Rupert.[SEP]Moreover, he, if any one of them, really loved Black Michael,[SEP]and it might be that he would leave Bersonin to guard the King, and rush[SEP]across the bridge to take part in the affray on the other side.[EOT]
[SOT]So I planned--desperately.[SEP]And, that our enemy might be the better[SEP]lulled to security, I gave orders that our residence should be[SEP]brilliantly lighted from top to bottom, as though we were engaged in[SEP]revelry; and should so be kept all night, with music playing and people[SEP]moving to and fro.[SEP]Strakencz would be there, and he was to conceal our[SEP]departure, if he could, from Flavia.[SEP]And if we came not again by the[SEP]morning, he was to march, openly and in force to the Castle, and demand[SEP]the person of the King; if Black Michael were not there, as I did not[SEP]think he would be, the Marshal would take Flavia with him, as swiftly as[SEP]he could, to Strelsau, and there proclaim Black Michael’s treachery and[SEP]the probable death of the King, and rally all that there was honest and[SEP]true round the banner of the princess.[SEP]And, to say truth, this was what[SEP]I thought most likely to happen.[SEP]For I had great doubts whether either[SEP]the King or Black Michael or I had more than a day to live.[SEP]Well, if[SEP]Black Michael died, and if I, the play-actor, slew Rupert Hentzau with[SEP]my own hand, and then died myself, it might be that Fate would deal[SEP]as lightly with Ruritania as could be hoped, notwithstanding that she[SEP]demanded the life of the King--and to her dealing thus with me, I was in[SEP]no temper to make objection.[EOT]
[SOT]It was late when we rose from conference, and I betook me to the[SEP]princess’s apartments.[SEP]She was pensive that evening; yet, when I left[SEP]her, she flung her arms about me and grew, for an instant, bashfully[SEP]radiant as she slipped a ring on my finger.[SEP]I was wearing the King’s[SEP]ring; but I had also on my little finger a plain band of gold engraved[SEP]with the motto of our family: “_Nil Quae Feci_.[SEP]” This I took off and put[SEP]on her, and signed to her to let me go.[SEP]And she, understanding, stood[SEP]away and watched me with dimmed eyes.[EOT]
[SOT]“Wear that ring, even though you wear another when you are queen,” I[SEP]said.[EOT]
[SOT]“Whatever else I wear, this I will wear till I die and after,” said she,[SEP]as she kissed the ring.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 17[EOT]
[SOT]Young Rupert’s Midnight Diversions[EOT]
[SOT]The night came fine and clear.[SEP]I had prayed for dirty weather, such as[SEP]had favoured my previous voyage in the moat, but Fortune was this time[SEP]against me.[SEP]Still I reckoned that by keeping close under the wall and in[SEP]the shadow I could escape detection from the windows of the chateau[SEP]that looked out on the scene of my efforts.[SEP]If they searched the moat,[SEP]indeed, my scheme must fail; but I did not think they would.[SEP]They had[SEP]made “Jacob’s Ladder” secure against attack.[SEP]Johann had himself helped[SEP]to fix it closely to the masonry on the under side, so that it could[SEP]not now be moved from below any more than from above.[SEP]An assault with[SEP]explosives or a long battering with picks alone could displace it, and[SEP]the noise involved in either of these operations put them out of the[SEP]question.[SEP]What harm, then, could a man do in the moat?[SEP]I trusted that[SEP]Black Michael, putting this query to himself, would answer confidently,[SEP]“None;” while, even if Johann meant treachery, he did not know my[SEP]scheme, and would doubtless expect to see me, at the head of my friends,[SEP]before the front entrance to the chateau.[SEP]There, I said to Sapt, was the[SEP]real danger.[SEP]“And there,” I added, “you shall be.[SEP]Doesn’t that content[SEP]you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]But it did not.[SEP]Dearly would he have liked to come with me, had I not[SEP]utterly refused to take him.[SEP]One man might escape notice, to double[SEP]the party more than doubled the risk; and when he ventured to hint once[SEP]again that my life was too valuable, I, knowing the secret thought he[SEP]clung to, sternly bade him be silent, assuring him that unless the King[SEP]lived through the night, I would not live through it either.[EOT]
[SOT]At twelve o’clock, Sapt’s command left the chateau of Tarlenheim and[SEP]struck off to the right, riding by unfrequented roads, and avoiding the[SEP]town of Zenda.[SEP]If all went well, they would be in front of the Castle by[SEP]about a quarter to two.[SEP]Leaving their horses half a mile off, they were[SEP]to steal up to the entrance and hold themselves in readiness for the[SEP]opening of the door.[SEP]If the door were not opened by two, they were to[SEP]send Fritz von Tarlenheim round to the other side of the Castle.[SEP]I would[SEP]meet him there if I were alive, and we would consult whether to storm[SEP]the Castle or not.[SEP]If I were not there, they were to return with all[SEP]speed to Tarlenheim, rouse the Marshal, and march in force to Zenda.[SEP]For[SEP]if not there, I should be dead; and I knew that the King would not be[SEP]alive five minutes after I ceased to breathe.[SEP]I must now leave Sapt and[SEP]his friends, and relate how I myself proceeded on this eventful night.[SEP]I went out on the good horse which had carried me, on the night of[SEP]the coronation, back from the hunting-lodge to Strelsau.[SEP]I carried a[SEP]revolver in the saddle and my sword.[SEP]I was covered with a large cloak,[SEP]and under this I wore a warm, tight-fitting woollen jersey, a pair of[SEP]knickerbockers, thick stockings, and light canvas shoes.[SEP]I had rubbed[SEP]myself thoroughly with oil, and I carried a large flask of whisky.[SEP]The[SEP]night was warm, but I might probably be immersed a long while, and it[SEP]was necessary to take every precaution against cold: for cold not only[SEP]saps a man’s courage if he has to die, but impairs his energy if others[SEP]have to die, and, finally, gives him rheumatics, if it be God’s will[SEP]that he lives.[SEP]Also I tied round my body a length of thin but stout[SEP]cord, and I did not forget my ladder.[SEP]I, starting after Sapt, took a[SEP]shorter route, skirting the town to the left, and found myself in the[SEP]outskirts of the forest at about half-past twelve.[SEP]I tied my horse up[SEP]in a thick clump of trees, leaving the revolver in its pocket in the[SEP]saddle--it would be no use to me--and, ladder in hand, made my way to[SEP]the edge of the moat.[SEP]Here I unwound my rope from about my waist, bound[SEP]it securely round the trunk of a tree on the bank, and let myself down.[SEP]The Castle clock struck a quarter to one as I felt the water under me[SEP]and began to swim round the keep, pushing the ladder before me, and[SEP]hugging the Castle wall.[SEP]Thus voyaging, I came to my old friend,[SEP]“Jacob’s Ladder,” and felt the ledge of the masonry under me.[SEP]I crouched[SEP]down in the shadow of the great pipe--I tried to stir it, but it was[SEP]quite immovable--and waited.[SEP]I remember that my predominant feeling[SEP]was neither anxiety for the King nor longing for Flavia, but an intense[SEP]desire to smoke; and this craving, of course, I could not gratify.[EOT]
[SOT]The drawbridge was still in its place.[SEP]I saw its airy, slight framework[SEP]above me, some ten yards to my right, as I crouched with my back against[SEP]the wall of the King’s cell.[SEP]I made out a window two yards my side of it[SEP]and nearly on the same level.[SEP]That, if Johann spoke true, must belong to[SEP]the duke’s apartments; and on the other side, in about the same relative[SEP]position, must be Madame de Mauban’s window.[SEP]Women are careless,[SEP]forgetful creatures.[SEP]I prayed that she might not forget that she was to[SEP]be the victim of a brutal attempt at two o’clock precisely.[SEP]I was rather[SEP]amused at the part I had assigned to my young friend Rupert Hentzau; but[SEP]I owed him a stroke--for, even as I sat, my shoulder ached where he had,[SEP]with an audacity that seemed half to hide his treachery, struck at me,[SEP]in the sight of all my friends, on the terrace at Tarlenheim.[EOT]
[SOT]Suddenly the duke’s window grew bright.[SEP]The shutters were not closed,[SEP]and the interior became partially visible to me as I cautiously raised[SEP]myself till I stood on tiptoe.[SEP]Thus placed, my range of sight embraced a[SEP]yard or more inside the window, while the radius of light did not[SEP]reach me.[SEP]The window was flung open and someone looked out.[SEP]I marked[SEP]Antoinette de Mauban’s graceful figure, and, though her face was in[SEP]shadow, the fine outline of her head was revealed against the light[SEP]behind.[SEP]I longed to cry softly, “Remember![SEP]” but I dared not--and[SEP]happily, for a moment later a man came up and stood by her.[SEP]He tried to[SEP]put his arm round her waist, but with a swift motion she sprang away and[SEP]leant against the shutter, her profile towards me.[SEP]I made out who the[SEP]newcomer was: it was young Rupert.[SEP]A low laugh from him made me sure, as[SEP]he leant forward, stretching out his hand towards her.[EOT]
[SOT]“Gently, gently![SEP]” I murmured.[SEP]“You’re too soon, my boy![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]His head was close to hers.[SEP]I suppose he whispered to her, for I saw her[SEP]point to the moat, and I heard her say, in slow and distinct tones:[EOT]
[SOT]“I had rather throw myself out of this window![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He came close up to the window and looked out.[EOT]
[SOT]“It looks cold,” said he.[SEP]“Come, Antoinette, are you serious?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She made no answer so far as I heard; and he, smiting his hand petulantly[SEP]on the window-sill, went on, in the voice of some spoilt child:[EOT]
[SOT]“Hang Black Michael![SEP]Isn’t the princess enough for him?[SEP]Is he to have[SEP]everything?[SEP]What the devil do you see in Black Michael?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“If I told him what you say--” she began.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, tell him,” said Rupert, carelessly; and, catching her off her[SEP]guard, he sprang forward and kissed her, laughing, and crying, “There’s[SEP]something to tell him![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]If I had kept my revolver with me, I should have been very sorely[SEP]tempted.[SEP]Being spared the temptation, I merely added this new score to[SEP]his account.[EOT]
[SOT]“Though, faith,” said Rupert, “it’s little he cares.[SEP]He’s mad about the[SEP]princess, you know.[SEP]He talks of nothing but cutting the play-actor’s[SEP]throat.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Didn’t he, indeed?[EOT]
[SOT]“And if I do it for him, what do you think he’s promised me?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The unhappy woman raised her hands above her head, in prayer or in[SEP]despair.[EOT]
[SOT]“But I detest waiting,” said Rupert; and I saw that he was about to[SEP]lay his hand on her again, when there was a noise of a door in the room[SEP]opening, and a harsh voice cried:[EOT]
[SOT]“What are you doing here, sir?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Rupert turned his back to the window, bowed low, and said, in his loud,[SEP]merry tones: “Apologizing for your absence, sir.[SEP]Could I leave the lady[SEP]alone?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The newcomer must be Black Michael.[SEP]I saw him directly, as he advanced[SEP]towards the window.[SEP]He caught young Rupert by the arm.[EOT]
[SOT]“The moat would hold more than the King![SEP]” said he, with a significant[SEP]gesture.[EOT]
[SOT]“Does your Highness threaten me?[SEP]” asked Rupert.[EOT]
[SOT]“A threat is more warning than most men get from me.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yet,” observed Rupert, “Rudolf Rassendyll has been much threatened, and[SEP]yet lives![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Am I in fault because my servants bungle?[SEP]” asked Michael scornfully.[EOT]
[SOT]“Your Highness has run no risk of bungling![SEP]” sneered Rupert.[EOT]
[SOT]It was telling the duke that he shirked danger as plain as ever I[SEP]have heard a man told.[SEP]Black Michael had self-control.[SEP]I dare say he[SEP]scowled--it was a great regret to me that I could not see their faces[SEP]better--but his voice was even and calm, as he answered:[EOT]
[SOT]“Enough, enough![SEP]We mustn’t quarrel, Rupert.[SEP]Are Detchard and Bersonin[SEP]at their posts?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They are, sir.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I need you no more.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Nay, I’m not oppressed with fatigue,” said Rupert.[EOT]
[SOT]“Pray, sir, leave us,” said Michael, more impatiently.[SEP]“In ten minutes[SEP]the drawbridge will be drawn back, and I presume you have no wish to[SEP]swim to your bed.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Rupert’s figure disappeared.[SEP]I heard the door open and shut again.[SEP]Michael and Antoinette de Mauban were left together.[SEP]To my chagrin,[SEP]the duke laid his hand on the window and closed it.[SEP]He stood talking[SEP]to Antoinette for a moment or two.[SEP]She shook her head, and he turned[SEP]impatiently away.[SEP]She left the window.[SEP]The door sounded again, and Black[SEP]Michael closed the shutters.[EOT]
[SOT]“De Gautet, De Gautet, man![SEP]” sounded from the drawbridge.[SEP]“Unless you[SEP]want a bath before your bed, come along![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]It was Rupert’s voice, coming from the end of the drawbridge.[SEP]A moment[SEP]later he and De Gautet stepped out on the bridge.[SEP]Rupert’s arm was[SEP]through De Gautet’s, and in the middle of the bridge he detained his[SEP]companion and leant over.[SEP]I dropped behind the shelter of “Jacob’s[SEP]Ladder.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then Master Rupert had a little sport.[SEP]He took from De Gautet a bottle[SEP]which he carried, and put it to his lips.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hardly a drop![SEP]” he cried discontentedly, and flung it in the moat.[EOT]
[SOT]It fell, as I judged from the sound and the circles on the water, within[SEP]a yard of the pipe.[SEP]And Rupert, taking out his revolver, began to shoot[SEP]at it.[SEP]The first two shots missed the bottle, but hit the pipe.[SEP]The[SEP]third shattered the bottle.[SEP]I hoped that the young ruffian would be[SEP]content; but he emptied the other barrels at the pipe, and one, skimming[SEP]over the pipe, whistled through my hair as I crouched on the other side.[EOT]
[SOT]“‘Ware bridge![SEP]” a voice cried, to my relief.[EOT]
[SOT]Rupert and De Gautet cried, “A moment![SEP]” and ran across.[SEP]The bridge was[SEP]drawn back, and all became still.[SEP]The clock struck a quarter-past one.[SEP]I[SEP]rose and stretched myself and yawned.[EOT]
[SOT]I think some ten minutes had passed when I heard a slight noise to my[SEP]right.[SEP]I peered over the pipe, and saw a dark figure standing in the[SEP]gateway that led to the bridge.[SEP]It was a man.[SEP]By the careless, graceful[SEP]poise, I guessed it to be Rupert again.[SEP]He held a sword in his hand, and[SEP]he stood motionless for a minute or two.[SEP]Wild thoughts ran through me.[SEP]On what mischief was the young fiend bent now?[SEP]Then he laughed low[SEP]to himself; then he turned his face to the wall, took a step in my[SEP]direction, and, to my surprise, began to climb down the wall.[SEP]In an[SEP]instant I saw that there must be steps in the wall; it was plain.[SEP]They[SEP]were cut into or affixed to the wall, at intervals of about eighteen[SEP]inches.[SEP]Rupert set his foot on the lower one.[SEP]Then he placed his sword[SEP]between his teeth, turned round, and noiselessly let himself into the[SEP]water.[SEP]Had it been a matter of my life only, I would have swum to[SEP]meet him.[SEP]Dearly would I have loved to fight it out with him then and[SEP]there--with steel, on a fine night, and none to come between us.[SEP]But[SEP]there was the King![SEP]I restrained myself, but I could not bridle my swift[SEP]breathing, and I watched him with the intensest eagerness.[EOT]
[SOT]He swam leisurely and quietly across.[SEP]There were more steps up on[SEP]the other side, and he climbed them.[SEP]When he set foot in the gateway,[SEP]standing on the drawn-back bridge, he felt in his pocket and took[SEP]something out.[SEP]I heard him unlock the door.[SEP]I could hear no noise of its[SEP]closing behind him.[SEP]He vanished from my sight.[EOT]
[SOT]Abandoning my ladder--I saw I did not need it now--I swam to the side of[SEP]the bridge and climbed half way up the steps.[SEP]There I hung with my sword[SEP]in my hand, listening eagerly.[SEP]The duke’s room was shuttered and dark.[SEP]There was a light in the window on the opposite side of the bridge.[SEP]Not a sound broke the silence, till half-past one chimed from the great[SEP]clock in the tower of the chateau.[EOT]
[SOT]There were other plots than mine afoot in the Castle that night.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 18[EOT]
[SOT]The Forcing of the Trap[EOT]
[SOT]The position wherein I stood does not appear very favourable to thought;[SEP]yet for the next moment or two I thought profoundly.[SEP]I had, I told[SEP]myself, scored one point.[SEP]Be Rupert Hentzau’s errand what it might, and[SEP]the villainy he was engaged on what it would, I had scored one point.[SEP]He[SEP]was on the other side of the moat from the King, and it would be by no[SEP]fault of mine if ever he set foot on the same side again.[SEP]I had three[SEP]left to deal with: two on guard and De Gautet in his bed.[SEP]Ah, if I[SEP]had the keys![SEP]I would have risked everything and attacked Detchard and[SEP]Bersonin before their friends could join them.[SEP]But I was powerless.[SEP]I[SEP]must wait till the coming of my friends enticed someone to cross the[SEP]bridge--someone with the keys.[SEP]And I waited, as it seemed, for half an[SEP]hour, really for about five minutes, before the next act in the rapid[SEP]drama began.[EOT]
[SOT]All was still on the other side.[SEP]The duke’s room remained inscrutable[SEP]behind its shutters.[SEP]The light burnt steadily in Madame de Mauban’s[SEP]window.[SEP]Then I heard the faintest, faintest sound: it came from behind[SEP]the door which led to the drawbridge on the other side of the moat.[SEP]It[SEP]but just reached my ear, yet I could not be mistaken as to what it was.[SEP]It was made by a key being turned very carefully and slowly.[SEP]Who was[SEP]turning it?[SEP]And of what room was it the key?[SEP]There leapt before my eyes[SEP]the picture of young Rupert, with the key in one hand, his sword in the[SEP]other, and an evil smile on his face.[SEP]But I did not know what door it[SEP]was, nor on which of his favourite pursuits young Rupert was spending[SEP]the hours of that night.[EOT]
[SOT]I was soon to be enlightened, for the next moment--before my friends[SEP]could be near the chateau door--before Johann the keeper would have[SEP]thought to nerve himself for his task--there was a sudden crash from[SEP]the room with the lighted window.[SEP]It sounded as though someone had flung[SEP]down a lamp; and the window went dark and black.[SEP]At the same instant a[SEP]cry rang out, shrill in the night: “Help, help![SEP]Michael, help![SEP]” and was[SEP]followed by a shriek of utter terror.[EOT]
[SOT]I was tingling in every nerve.[SEP]I stood on the topmost step, clinging to[SEP]the threshold of the gate with my right hand and holding my sword in my[SEP]left.[SEP]Suddenly I perceived that the gateway was broader than the bridge;[SEP]there was a dark corner on the opposite side where a man could stand.[SEP]I[SEP]darted across and stood there.[SEP]Thus placed, I commanded the path, and no[SEP]man could pass between the chateau and the old Castle till he had tried[SEP]conclusions with me.[EOT]
[SOT]There was another shriek.[SEP]Then a door was flung open and clanged against[SEP]the wall, and I heard the handle of a door savagely twisted.[EOT]
[SOT]“Open the door![SEP]In God’s name, what’s the matter?[SEP]” cried a voice--the[SEP]voice of Black Michael himself.[EOT]
[SOT]He was answered by the very words I had written in my letter.[EOT]
[SOT]“Help, Michael--Hentzau![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]A fierce oath rang out from the duke, and with a loud thud he threw[SEP]himself against the door.[SEP]At the same moment I heard a window above my[SEP]head open, and a voice cried: “What’s the matter?[SEP]” and I heard a man’s[SEP]hasty footsteps.[SEP]I grasped my sword.[SEP]If De Gautet came my way, the Six[SEP]would be less by one more.[EOT]
[SOT]Then I heard the clash of crossed swords and a tramp of feet and--I[SEP]cannot tell the thing so quickly as it happened, for all seemed to come[SEP]at once.[SEP]There was an angry cry from madame’s room, the cry of a wounded[SEP]man; the window was flung open; young Rupert stood there sword in hand.[SEP]He turned his back, and I saw his body go forward to the lunge.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah, Johann, there’s one for you![SEP]Come on, Michael![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Johann was there, then--come to the rescue of the duke![SEP]How would he[SEP]open the door for me?[SEP]For I feared that Rupert had slain him.[EOT]
[SOT]“Help![SEP]” cried the duke’s voice, faint and husky.[EOT]
[SOT]I heard a step on the stairs above me; and I heard a stir down to my[SEP]left, in the direction of the King’s cell.[SEP]But, before anything happened[SEP]on my side of the moat, I saw five or six men round young Rupert in[SEP]the embrasure of madame’s window.[SEP]Three or four times he lunged with[SEP]incomparable dash and dexterity.[SEP]For an instant they fell back, leaving[SEP]a ring round him.[SEP]He leapt on the parapet of the window, laughing as he[SEP]leapt, and waving his sword in his hand.[SEP]He was drunk with blood, and he[SEP]laughed again wildly as he flung himself headlong into the moat.[EOT]
[SOT]What became of him then?[SEP]I did not see: for as he leapt, De Gautet’s[SEP]lean face looked out through the door by me, and, without a second’s[SEP]hesitation, I struck at him with all the strength God had given me, and[SEP]he fell dead in the doorway without a word or a groan.[SEP]I dropped on my[SEP]knees by him.[SEP]Where were the keys?[SEP]I found myself muttering: “The keys,[SEP]man, the keys?[SEP]” as though he had been yet alive and could listen; and[SEP]when I could not find them, I--God forgive me![SEP]--I believe I struck a[SEP]dead man’s face.[EOT]
[SOT]At last I had them.[SEP]There were but three.[SEP]Seizing the largest, I felt[SEP]the lock of the door that led to the cell.[SEP]I fitted in the key.[SEP]It was[SEP]right.[SEP]The lock turned.[SEP]I drew the door close behind me and locked it as[SEP]noiselessly as I could, putting the key in my pocket.[EOT]
[SOT]I found myself at the top of a flight of steep stone stairs.[SEP]An oil lamp[SEP]burnt dimly in the bracket.[SEP]I took it down and held it in my hand; and I[SEP]stood and listened.[EOT]
[SOT]“What in the devil can it be?[SEP]” I heard a voice say.[EOT]
[SOT]It came from behind a door that faced me at the bottom of the stairs.[EOT]
[SOT]And another answered:[EOT]
[SOT]“Shall we kill him?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I strained to hear the answer, and could have sobbed with relief when[SEP]Detchard’s voice came grating and cold:[EOT]
[SOT]“Wait a bit.[SEP]There’ll be trouble if we strike too soon.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]There was a moment’s silence.[SEP]Then I heard the bolt of the door[SEP]cautiously drawn back.[SEP]Instantly I put out the light I held, replacing[SEP]the lamp in the bracket.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s dark--the lamp’s out.[SEP]Have you a light?[SEP]” said the other[SEP]voice--Bersonin’s.[EOT]
[SOT]No doubt they had a light, but they should not use it.[SEP]It was come to[SEP]the crisis now, and I rushed down the steps and flung myself against the[SEP]door.[SEP]Bersonin had unbolted it and it gave way before me.[SEP]The Belgian[SEP]stood there sword in hand, and Detchard was sitting on a couch at the[SEP]side of the room.[SEP]In astonishment at seeing me, Bersonin recoiled;[SEP]Detchard jumped to his sword.[SEP]I rushed madly at the Belgian: he gave[SEP]way before me, and I drove him up against the wall.[SEP]He was no swordsman,[SEP]though he fought bravely, and in a moment he lay on the floor before[SEP]me.[SEP]I turned--Detchard was not there.[SEP]Faithful to his orders, he had[SEP]not risked a fight with me, but had rushed straight to the door of the[SEP]King’s room, opened it and slammed it behind him.[SEP]Even now he was at his[SEP]work inside.[EOT]
[SOT]And surely he would have killed the King, and perhaps me also, had it[SEP]not been for one devoted man who gave his life for the King.[SEP]For when I[SEP]forced the door, the sight I saw was this: the King stood in the corner[SEP]of the room: broken by his sickness, he could do nothing; his fettered[SEP]hands moved uselessly up and down, and he was laughing horribly in[SEP]half-mad delirium.[SEP]Detchard and the doctor were together in the middle[SEP]of the room; and the doctor had flung himself on the murderer, pinning[SEP]his hands to his sides for an instant.[SEP]Then Detchard wrenched himself[SEP]free from the feeble grip, and, as I entered, drove his sword through[SEP]the hapless man.[SEP]Then he turned on me, crying:[EOT]
[SOT]“At last![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We were sword to sword.[SEP]By blessed chance, neither he nor Bersonin had[SEP]been wearing their revolvers.[SEP]I found them afterwards, ready loaded,[SEP]on the mantelpiece of the outer room: it was hard by the door, ready to[SEP]their hands, but my sudden rush in had cut off access to them.[SEP]Yes, we[SEP]were man to man: and we began to fight, silently, sternly, and hard.[SEP]Yet I remember little of it, save that the man was my match with the[SEP]sword--nay, and more, for he knew more tricks than I; and that he forced[SEP]me back against the bars that guarded the entrance to “Jacob’s Ladder.[SEP]”[SEP]And I saw a smile on his face, and he wounded me in the left arm.[EOT]
[SOT]No glory do I take for that contest.[SEP]I believe that the man would have[SEP]mastered me and slain me, and then done his butcher’s work, for he was[SEP]the most skilful swordsman I have ever met; but even as he pressed me[SEP]hard, the half-mad, wasted, wan creature in the corner leapt high in[SEP]lunatic mirth, shrieking:[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s cousin Rudolf![SEP]Cousin Rudolf![SEP]I’ll help you, cousin Rudolf![SEP]” and[SEP]catching up a chair in his hands (he could but just lift it from the[SEP]ground and hold it uselessly before him) he came towards us.[SEP]Hope came[SEP]to me.[SEP]“Come on![SEP]” I cried.[SEP]“Come on![SEP]Drive it against his legs.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Detchard replied with a savage thrust.[SEP]He all but had me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Come on![SEP]Come on, man![SEP]” I cried.[SEP]“Come and share the fun![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And the King laughed gleefully, and came on, pushing his chair before[SEP]him.[EOT]
[SOT]With an oath Detchard skipped back, and, before I knew what he was[SEP]doing, had turned his sword against the King.[SEP]He made one fierce cut at[SEP]the King, and the King, with a piteous cry, dropped where he stood.[SEP]The[SEP]stout ruffian turned to face me again.[SEP]But his own hand had prepared[SEP]his destruction: for in turning he trod in the pool of blood that flowed[SEP]from the dead physician.[SEP]He slipped; he fell.[SEP]Like a dart I was upon[SEP]him.[SEP]I caught him by the throat, and before he could recover himself I[SEP]drove my point through his neck, and with a stifled curse he fell across[SEP]the body of his victim.[EOT]
[SOT]Was the King dead?[SEP]It was my first thought.[SEP]I rushed to where he lay.[SEP]Ay, it seemed as if he were dead, for he had a great gash across his[SEP]forehead, and he lay still in a huddled heap on the floor.[SEP]I dropped on[SEP]my knees beside him, and leant my ear down to hear if he breathed.[SEP]But[SEP]before I could there was a loud rattle from the outside.[SEP]I knew the[SEP]sound: the drawbridge was being pushed out.[SEP]A moment later it rang home[SEP]against the wall on my side of the moat.[SEP]I should be caught in a trap[SEP]and the King with me, if he yet lived.[SEP]He must take his chance, to[SEP]live or die.[SEP]I took my sword, and passed into the outer room.[SEP]Who were[SEP]pushing the drawbridge out--my men?[SEP]If so, all was well.[SEP]My eye fell on[SEP]the revolvers, and I seized one; and paused to listen in the doorway of[SEP]the outer room.[SEP]To listen, say I?[SEP]Yes, and to get my breath: and I tore[SEP]my shirt and twisted a strip of it round my bleeding arm; and stood[SEP]listening again.[SEP]I would have given the world to hear Sapt’s voice.[SEP]For[SEP]I was faint, spent, and weary.[SEP]And that wild-cat Rupert Hentzau was yet[SEP]at large in the Castle.[SEP]Yet, because I could better defend the narrow[SEP]door at the top of the stairs than the wider entrance to the room, I[SEP]dragged myself up the steps, and stood behind it listening.[EOT]
[SOT]What was the sound?[SEP]Again a strange one for the place and time.[SEP]An[SEP]easy, scornful, merry laugh--the laugh of young Rupert Hentzau![SEP]I could[SEP]scarcely believe that a sane man would laugh.[SEP]Yet the laugh told me that[SEP]my men had not come; for they must have shot Rupert ere now, if they had[SEP]come.[SEP]And the clock struck half-past two![SEP]My God![SEP]The door had not been[SEP]opened![SEP]They had gone to the bank![SEP]They had not found me![SEP]They had gone[SEP]by now back to Tarlenheim, with the news of the King’s death--and mine.[SEP]Well, it would be true before they got there.[SEP]Was not Rupert laughing in[SEP]triumph?[EOT]
[SOT]For a moment, I sank, unnerved, against the door.[SEP]Then I started up[SEP]alert again, for Rupert cried scornfully:[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, the bridge is there![SEP]Come over it![SEP]And in God’s name, let’s see[SEP]Black Michael.[SEP]Keep back, you curs![SEP]Michael, come and fight for her![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]If it were a three-cornered fight, I might yet bear my part.[SEP]I turned[SEP]the key in the door and looked out.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 19[EOT]
[SOT]Face to Face in the Forest[EOT]
[SOT]For a moment I could see nothing, for the glare of lanterns and torches[SEP]caught me full in the eyes from the other side of the bridge.[SEP]But soon[SEP]the scene grew clear: and it was a strange scene.[SEP]The bridge was in its[SEP]place.[SEP]At the far end of it stood a group of the duke’s servants; two or[SEP]three carried the lights which had dazzled me, three or four held pikes[SEP]in rest.[SEP]They were huddled together; their weapons were protruded before[SEP]them; their faces were pale and agitated.[SEP]To put it plainly, they[SEP]looked in as arrant a fright as I have seen men look, and they gazed[SEP]apprehensively at a man who stood in the middle of the bridge, sword in[SEP]hand.[SEP]Rupert Hentzau was in his trousers and shirt; the white linen[SEP]was stained with blood, but his easy, buoyant pose told me that he was[SEP]himself either not touched at all or merely scratched.[SEP]There he stood,[SEP]holding the bridge against them, and daring them to come on; or, rather,[SEP]bidding them send Black Michael to him; and they, having no firearms,[SEP]cowered before the desperate man and dared not attack him.[SEP]They[SEP]whispered to one another; and in the backmost rank, I saw my friend[SEP]Johann, leaning against the portal of the door and stanching with a[SEP]handkerchief the blood which flowed from a wound in his cheek.[EOT]
[SOT]By marvellous chance, I was master.[SEP]The cravens would oppose me no more[SEP]than they dared attack Rupert.[SEP]I had but to raise my revolver, and I[SEP]sent him to his account with his sins on his head.[SEP]He did not so much as[SEP]know that I was there.[SEP]I did nothing--why, I hardly know to this day.[SEP]I had killed one man stealthily that night, and another by luck rather[SEP]than skill--perhaps it was that.[SEP]Again, villain as the man was, I did[SEP]not relish being one of a crowd against him--perhaps it was that.[SEP]But[SEP]stronger than either of these restrained feelings came a curiosity and[SEP]a fascination which held me spellbound, watching for the outcome of the[SEP]scene.[EOT]
[SOT]“Michael, you dog![SEP]Michael![SEP]If you can stand, come on![SEP]” cried Rupert;[SEP]and he advanced a step, the group shrinking back a little before him.[SEP]“Michael, you bastard![SEP]Come on![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The answer to his taunts came in the wild cry of a woman:[EOT]
[SOT]“He’s dead![SEP]My God, he’s dead![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Dead![SEP]” shouted Rupert.[SEP]“I struck better than I knew![SEP]” and he laughed[SEP]triumphantly.[SEP]Then he went on: “Down with your weapons there![SEP]I’m your[SEP]master now![SEP]Down with them, I say![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I believe they would have obeyed, but as he spoke came new things.[SEP]First, there arose a distant sound, as of shouts and knockings from the[SEP]other side of the chateau.[SEP]My heart leapt.[SEP]It must be my men, come by a[SEP]happy disobedience to seek me.[SEP]The noise continued, but none of the[SEP]rest seemed to heed it.[SEP]Their attention was chained by what now happened[SEP]before their eyes.[SEP]The group of servants parted and a woman staggered on[SEP]to the bridge.[SEP]Antoinette de Mauban was in a loose white robe, her dark[SEP]hair streamed over her shoulders, her face was ghastly pale, and her[SEP]eyes gleamed wildly in the light of the torches.[SEP]In her shaking hand she[SEP]held a revolver, and, as she tottered forward, she fired it at Rupert[SEP]Hentzau.[SEP]The ball missed him, and struck the woodwork over my head.[EOT]
[SOT]“Faith, madame,” laughed Rupert, “had your eyes been no more deadly[SEP]than your shooting, I had not been in this scrape--nor Black Michael in[SEP]hell--tonight![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She took no notice of his words.[SEP]With a wonderful effort, she[SEP]calmed herself till she stood still and rigid.[SEP]Then very slowly and[SEP]deliberately she began to raise her arm again, taking most careful aim.[EOT]
[SOT]He would be mad to risk it.[SEP]He must rush on her, chancing the bullet, or[SEP]retreat towards me.[SEP]I covered him with my weapon.[EOT]
[SOT]He did neither.[SEP]Before she had got her aim, he bowed in his most[SEP]graceful fashion, cried “I can’t kill where I’ve kissed,” and before[SEP]she or I could stop him, laid his hand on the parapet of the bridge, and[SEP]lightly leapt into the moat.[EOT]
[SOT]At that very moment I heard a rush of feet, and a voice I[SEP]knew--Sapt’s--cry: “God![SEP]it’s the duke--dead![SEP]” Then I knew that the King[SEP]needed me no more, and throwing down my revolver, I sprang out on the[SEP]bridge.[SEP]There was a cry of wild wonder, “The King![SEP]” and then I, like[SEP]Rupert of Hentzau, sword in hand, vaulted over the parapet, intent on[SEP]finishing my quarrel with him where I saw his curly head fifteen yards[SEP]off in the water of the moat.[EOT]
[SOT]He swam swiftly and easily.[SEP]I was weary and half crippled with my[SEP]wounded arm.[SEP]I could not gain on him.[SEP]For a time I made no sound, but as[SEP]we rounded the corner of the old keep I cried:[EOT]
[SOT]“Stop, Rupert, stop![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I saw him look over his shoulder, but he swam on.[SEP]He was under the bank[SEP]now, searching, as I guessed, for a spot that he could climb.[SEP]I knew[SEP]there to be none--but there was my rope, which would still be hanging[SEP]where I had left it.[SEP]He would come to where it was before I could.[SEP]Perhaps he would miss it--perhaps he would find it; and if he drew it up[SEP]after him, he would get a good start of me.[SEP]I put forth all my remaining[SEP]strength and pressed on.[SEP]At last I began to gain on him; for he,[SEP]occupied with his search, unconsciously slackened his pace.[EOT]
[SOT]Ah, he had found it![SEP]A low shout of triumph came from him.[SEP]He laid[SEP]hold of it and began to haul himself up.[SEP]I was near enough to hear him[SEP]mutter: “How the devil comes this here?[SEP]” I was at the rope, and he,[SEP]hanging in mid air, saw me, but I could not reach him.[EOT]
[SOT]“Hullo![SEP]who’s here?[SEP]” he cried in startled tones.[EOT]
[SOT]For a moment, I believe, he took me for the King--I dare say I was pale[SEP]enough to lend colour to the thought; but an instant later he cried:[EOT]
[SOT]“Why it’s the play-actor![SEP]How come you here, man?[SEP]”[EOT]
