[SOT]And so saying he gained the bank.[EOT]
[SOT]I laid hold of the rope, but I paused.[SEP]He stood on the bank, sword in[SEP]hand, and he could cut my head open or spit me through the heart as I[SEP]came up.[SEP]I let go the rope.[EOT]
[SOT]“Never mind,” said I; “but as I am here, I think I’ll stay.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He smiled down on me.[EOT]
[SOT]“These women are the deuce--” he began; when suddenly the great bell of[SEP]the Castle started to ring furiously, and a loud shout reached us from[SEP]the moat.[EOT]
[SOT]Rupert smiled again, and waved his hand to me.[EOT]
[SOT]“I should like a turn with you, but it’s a little too hot![SEP]” said he, and[SEP]he disappeared from above me.[EOT]
[SOT]In an instant, without thinking of danger, I laid my hand to the rope.[SEP]I was up.[SEP]I saw him thirty yards off, running like a deer towards the[SEP]shelter of the forest.[SEP]For once Rupert Hentzau had chosen discretion for[SEP]his part.[SEP]I laid my feet to the ground and rushed after him, calling to[SEP]him to stand.[SEP]He would not.[SEP]Unwounded and vigorous, he gained on me at[SEP]every step; but, forgetting everything in the world except him and my[SEP]thirst for his blood, I pressed on, and soon the deep shades of the[SEP]forest of Zenda engulfed us both, pursued and pursuer.[EOT]
[SOT]It was three o’clock now, and day was dawning.[SEP]I was on a long straight[SEP]grass avenue, and a hundred yards ahead ran young Rupert, his curls[SEP]waving in the fresh breeze.[SEP]I was weary and panting; he looked over his[SEP]shoulder and waved his hand again to me.[SEP]He was mocking me, for he saw[SEP]he had the pace of me.[SEP]I was forced to pause for breath.[SEP]A moment later,[SEP]Rupert turned sharply to the right and was lost from my sight.[EOT]
[SOT]I thought all was over, and in deep vexation sank on the ground.[SEP]But I[SEP]was up again directly, for a scream rang through the forest--a woman’s[SEP]scream.[SEP]Putting forth the last of my strength, I ran on to the place[SEP]where he had turned out of my sight, and, turning also, I saw him again.[SEP]But alas![SEP]I could not touch him.[SEP]He was in the act of lifting a girl[SEP]down from her horse; doubtless it was her scream that I heard.[SEP]She[SEP]looked like a small farmer’s or a peasant’s daughter, and she carried[SEP]a basket on her arm.[SEP]Probably she was on her way to the early market at[SEP]Zenda.[SEP]Her horse was a stout, well shaped animal.[SEP]Master Rupert lifted[SEP]her down amid her shrieks--the sight of him frightened her; but he[SEP]treated her gently, laughed, kissed her, and gave her money.[SEP]Then he[SEP]jumped on the horse, sitting sideways like a woman; and then he waited[SEP]for me.[SEP]I, on my part, waited for him.[EOT]
[SOT]Presently he rode towards me, keeping his distance, however.[SEP]He lifted[SEP]up his hand, saying:[EOT]
[SOT]“What did you in the Castle?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I killed three of your friends,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“What![SEP]You got to the cells?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And the King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He was hurt by Detchard before I killed Detchard, but I pray that he[SEP]lives.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“You fool![SEP]” said Rupert, pleasantly.[EOT]
[SOT]“One thing more I did.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And what’s that?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I spared your life.[SEP]I was behind you on the bridge, with a revolver in[SEP]my hand.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No?[SEP]Faith, I was between two fires![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Get off your horse,” I cried, “and fight like a man.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Before a lady![SEP]” said he, pointing to the girl.[SEP]“Fie, your Majesty![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then in my rage, hardly knowing what I did, I rushed at him.[SEP]For a[SEP]moment he seemed to waver.[SEP]Then he reined his horse in and stood waiting[SEP]for me.[SEP]On I went in my folly.[SEP]I seized the bridle and I struck at him.[SEP]He parried and thrust at me.[SEP]I fell back a pace and rushed at him again;[SEP]and this time I reached his face and laid his cheek open, and darted[SEP]back almost before he could strike me.[SEP]He seemed almost dazed at the[SEP]fierceness of my attack; otherwise I think he must have killed me.[SEP]I[SEP]sank on my knee panting, expecting him to ride at me.[SEP]And so he would[SEP]have done, and then and there, I doubt not, one or both of us would have[SEP]died; but at the moment there came a shout from behind us, and, looking[SEP]round, I saw, just at the turn of the avenue, a man on a horse.[SEP]He was[SEP]riding hard, and he carried a revolver in his hand.[SEP]It was Fritz von[SEP]Tarlenheim, my faithful friend.[SEP]Rupert saw him, and knew that the game[SEP]was up.[SEP]He checked his rush at me and flung his leg over the saddle, but[SEP]yet for just a moment he waited.[SEP]Leaning forward, he tossed his hair off[SEP]his forehead and smiled, and said: “_Au revoir_, Rudolf Rassendyll![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then, with his cheek streaming blood, but his lips laughing and his[SEP]body swaying with ease and grace, he bowed to me; and he bowed to the[SEP]farm-girl, who had drawn near in trembling fascination, and he waved his[SEP]hand to Fritz, who was just within range and let fly a shot at him.[SEP]The[SEP]ball came nigh doing its work, for it struck the sword he held, and he[SEP]dropped the sword with an oath, wringing his fingers and clapped his[SEP]heels hard on his horse’s belly, and rode away at a gallop.[EOT]
[SOT]And I watched him go down the long avenue, riding as though he rode for[SEP]his pleasure and singing as he went, for all there was that gash in his[SEP]cheek.[EOT]
[SOT]Once again he turned to wave his hand, and then the gloom of thickets[SEP]swallowed him and he was lost from our sight.[SEP]Thus he vanished--reckless[SEP]and wary, graceful and graceless, handsome, debonair, vile, and[SEP]unconquered.[SEP]And I flung my sword passionately on the ground and cried[SEP]to Fritz to ride after him.[SEP]But Fritz stopped his horse, and leapt down[SEP]and ran to me, and knelt, putting his arm about me.[SEP]And indeed it was[SEP]time, for the wound that Detchard had given me was broken forth afresh,[SEP]and my blood was staining the ground.[EOT]
[SOT]“Then give me the horse![SEP]” I cried, staggering to my feet and throwing[SEP]his arms off me.[SEP]And the strength of my rage carried me so far as where[SEP]the horse stood, and then I fell prone beside it.[SEP]And Fritz knelt by me[SEP]again.[EOT]
[SOT]“Fritz![SEP]” I said.[EOT]
[SOT]“Ay, friend--dear friend![SEP]” he said, tender as a woman.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is the King alive?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He took his handkerchief and wiped my lips, and bent and kissed me on[SEP]the forehead.[EOT]
[SOT]“Thanks to the most gallant gentleman that lives,” said he softly, “the[SEP]King is alive![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]The little farm-girl stood by us, weeping for fright and wide-eyed for[SEP]wonder; for she had seen me at Zenda; and was not I, pallid, dripping,[SEP]foul, and bloody as I was--yet was not I the King?[EOT]
[SOT]And when I heard that the King was alive, I strove to cry “Hurrah![SEP]” But[SEP]I could not speak, and I laid my head back in Fritz’s arms and closed[SEP]my eyes, and I groaned; and then, lest Fritz should do me wrong in his[SEP]thoughts, I opened my eyes and tried to say “Hurrah![SEP]” again.[SEP]But I could[SEP]not.[SEP]And being very tired, and now very cold, I huddled myself close up[SEP]to Fritz, to get the warmth of him, and shut my eyes again and went to[SEP]sleep.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 20[EOT]
[SOT]The Prisoner and the King[EOT]
[SOT]In order to a full understanding of what had occurred in the Castle of[SEP]Zenda, it is necessary to supplement my account of what I myself saw[SEP]and did on that night by relating briefly what I afterwards learnt[SEP]from Fritz and Madame de Mauban.[SEP]The story told by the latter explained[SEP]clearly how it happened that the cry which I had arranged as a stratagem[SEP]and a sham had come, in dreadful reality, before its time, and had thus,[SEP]as it seemed at the moment, ruined our hopes, while in the end it[SEP]had favoured them.[SEP]The unhappy woman, fired, I believe by a genuine[SEP]attachment to the Duke of Strelsau, no less than by the dazzling[SEP]prospects which a dominion over him opened before her eyes, had followed[SEP]him at his request from Paris to Ruritania.[SEP]He was a man of strong[SEP]passions, but of stronger will, and his cool head ruled both.[SEP]He was[SEP]content to take all and give nothing.[SEP]When she arrived, she was not[SEP]long in finding that she had a rival in the Princess Flavia; rendered[SEP]desperate, she stood at nothing which might give, or keep for her, her[SEP]power over the duke.[SEP]As I say, he took and gave not.[SEP]Simultaneously,[SEP]Antoinette found herself entangled in his audacious schemes.[SEP]Unwilling[SEP]to abandon him, bound to him by the chains of shame and hope, yet she[SEP]would not be a decoy, nor, at his bidding, lure me to death.[SEP]Hence the[SEP]letters of warning she had written.[SEP]Whether the lines she sent to Flavia[SEP]were inspired by good or bad feeling, by jealousy or by pity, I do not[SEP]know; but here also she served us well.[SEP]When the duke went to Zenda, she[SEP]accompanied him; and here for the first time she learnt the full measure[SEP]of his cruelty, and was touched with compassion for the unfortunate[SEP]King.[SEP]From this time she was with us; yet, from what she told me, I know[SEP]that she still (as women will) loved Michael, and trusted to gain[SEP]his life, if not his pardon, from the King, as the reward for her[SEP]assistance.[SEP]His triumph she did not desire, for she loathed his crime,[SEP]and loathed yet more fiercely what would be the prize of it--his[SEP]marriage with his cousin, Princess Flavia.[EOT]
[SOT]At Zenda new forces came into play--the lust and daring of young Rupert.[SEP]He was caught by her beauty, perhaps; perhaps it was enough for him that[SEP]she belonged to another man, and that she hated him.[SEP]For many days there[SEP]had been quarrels and ill will between him and the duke, and the scene[SEP]which I had witnessed in the duke’s room was but one of many.[SEP]Rupert’s[SEP]proposals to me, of which she had, of course, been ignorant, in no[SEP]way surprised her when I related them; she had herself warned Michael[SEP]against Rupert, even when she was calling on me to deliver her from both[SEP]of them.[SEP]On this night, then, Rupert had determined to have his will.[SEP]When she had gone to her room, he, having furnished himself with a key[SEP]to it, had made his entrance.[SEP]Her cries had brought the duke, and there[SEP]in the dark room, while she screamed, the men had fought; and Rupert,[SEP]having wounded his master with a mortal blow, had, on the servants[SEP]rushing in, escaped through the window as I have described.[SEP]The duke’s[SEP]blood, spurting out, had stained his opponent’s shirt; but Rupert, not[SEP]knowing that he had dealt Michael his death, was eager to finish the[SEP]encounter.[SEP]How he meant to deal with the other three of the band, I know[SEP]not.[SEP]I dare say he did not think, for the killing of Michael was not[SEP]premeditated.[SEP]Antoinette, left alone with the duke, had tried to stanch[SEP]his wound, and thus was she busied till he died; and then, hearing[SEP]Rupert’s taunts, she had come forth to avenge him.[SEP]Me she had not seen,[SEP]nor did she till I darted out of my ambush, and leapt after Rupert into[SEP]the moat.[EOT]
[SOT]The same moment found my friends on the scene.[SEP]They had reached the[SEP]chateau in due time, and waited ready by the door.[SEP]But Johann, swept[SEP]with the rest to the rescue of the duke, did not open it; nay, he took[SEP]a part against Rupert, putting himself forward more bravely than any[SEP]in his anxiety to avert suspicion; and he had received a wound, in the[SEP]embrasure of the window.[SEP]Till nearly half-past two Sapt waited; then,[SEP]following my orders, he had sent Fritz to search the banks of the moat.[SEP]I was not there.[SEP]Hastening back, Fritz told Sapt; and Sapt was for[SEP]following orders still, and riding at full speed back to Tarlenheim;[SEP]while Fritz would not hear of abandoning me, let me have ordered what I[SEP]would.[SEP]On this they disputed some few minutes; then Sapt, persuaded by[SEP]Fritz, detached a party under Bernenstein to gallop back to Tarlenheim[SEP]and bring up the marshal, while the rest fell to on the great door[SEP]of the chateau.[SEP]For several minutes it resisted them; then, just as[SEP]Antoinette de Mauban fired at Rupert of Hentzau on the bridge, they[SEP]broke in, eight of them in all: and the first door they came to was the[SEP]door of Michael’s room; and Michael lay dead across the threshold, with[SEP]a sword-thrust through his breast.[SEP]Sapt cried out at his death, as I[SEP]had heard, and they rushed on the servants; but these, in fear, dropped[SEP]their weapons, and Antoinette flung herself weeping at Sapt’s feet.[SEP]And[SEP]all she cried was that I had been at the end of the bridge and leapt[SEP]off.[SEP]“What of the prisoner?[SEP]” asked Sapt; but she shook her head.[SEP]Then[SEP]Sapt and Fritz, with the gentlemen behind them, crossed the bridge,[SEP]slowly, warily, and without noise; and Fritz stumbled over the body of[SEP]De Gautet in the way of the door.[SEP]They felt him and found him dead.[EOT]
[SOT]Then they consulted, listening eagerly for any sound from the cells[SEP]below; but there came none, and they were greatly afraid that the King’s[SEP]guards had killed him, and having pushed his body through the great[SEP]pipe, had escaped the same way themselves.[SEP]Yet, because I had been seen[SEP]here, they had still some hope (thus indeed Fritz, in his friendship,[SEP]told me); and going back to Michael’s body, pushing aside Antoinette,[SEP]who prayed by it, they found a key to the door which I had locked, and[SEP]opened the door.[SEP]The staircase was dark, and they would not use a torch[SEP]at first, lest they should be more exposed to fire.[SEP]But soon Fritz[SEP]cried: “The door down there is open![SEP]See, there is light![SEP]” So they went[SEP]on boldly, and found none to oppose them.[SEP]And when they came to the[SEP]outer room and saw the Belgian, Bersonin, lying dead, they thanked God,[SEP]Sapt saying: “Ay, he has been here.[SEP]” Then rushing into the King’s cell,[SEP]they found Detchard lying dead across the dead physician, and the King[SEP]on his back with his chair by him.[SEP]And Fritz cried: “He’s dead![SEP]” and[SEP]Sapt drove all out of the room except Fritz, and knelt down by the King;[SEP]and, having learnt more of wounds and the sign of death than I, he soon[SEP]knew that the King was not dead, nor, if properly attended, would die.[SEP]And they covered his face and carried him to Duke Michael’s room, and[SEP]laid him there; and Antoinette rose from praying by the body of the duke[SEP]and went to bathe the King’s head and dress his wounds, till a doctor[SEP]came.[SEP]And Sapt, seeing I had been there, and having heard Antoinette’s[SEP]story, sent Fritz to search the moat and then the forest.[SEP]He dared send[SEP]no one else.[SEP]And Fritz found my horse, and feared the worst.[SEP]Then, as I[SEP]have told, he found me, guided by the shout with which I had called on[SEP]Rupert to stop and face me.[SEP]And I think a man has never been more glad[SEP]to find his own brother alive than was Fritz to come on me; so that, in[SEP]love and anxiety for me, he thought nothing of a thing so great as would[SEP]have been the death of Rupert Hentzau.[SEP]Yet, had Fritz killed him, I[SEP]should have grudged it.[EOT]
[SOT]The enterprise of the King’s rescue being thus prosperously concluded,[SEP]it lay on Colonel Sapt to secure secrecy as to the King ever having[SEP]been in need of rescue.[SEP]Antoinette de Mauban and Johann the keeper (who,[SEP]indeed, was too much hurt to be wagging his tongue just now) were sworn[SEP]to reveal nothing; and Fritz went forth to find--not the King, but the[SEP]unnamed friend of the King, who had lain in Zenda and flashed for[SEP]a moment before the dazed eyes of Duke Michael’s servants on the[SEP]drawbridge.[SEP]The metamorphosis had happened; and the King, wounded almost[SEP]to death by the attacks of the gaolers who guarded his friend, had[SEP]at last overcome them, and rested now, wounded but alive, in Black[SEP]Michael’s own room in the Castle.[SEP]There he had been carried, his face[SEP]covered with a cloak, from the cell; and thence orders issued, that if[SEP]his friend were found, he should be brought directly and privately to[SEP]the King, and that meanwhile messengers should ride at full speed to[SEP]Tarlenheim, to tell Marshall Strakencz to assure the princess of the[SEP]King’s safety and to come himself with all speed to greet the King.[SEP]The princess was enjoined to remain at Tarlenheim, and there await her[SEP]cousin’s coming or his further injunctions.[SEP]Thus the King would come[SEP]to his own again, having wrought brave deeds, and escaped, almost by a[SEP]miracle, the treacherous assault of his unnatural brother.[EOT]
[SOT]This ingenious arrangement of my long-headed old friend prospered in[SEP]every way, save where it encountered a force that often defeats the most[SEP]cunning schemes.[SEP]I mean nothing else than the pleasure of a woman.[SEP]For,[SEP]let her cousin and sovereign send what command he chose (or Colonel[SEP]Sapt chose for him), and let Marshal Strakencz insist as he would, the[SEP]Princess Flavia was in no way minded to rest at Tarlenheim while her[SEP]lover lay wounded at Zenda; and when the Marshal, with a small suite,[SEP]rode forth from Tarlenheim on the way to Zenda, the princess’s carriage[SEP]followed immediately behind, and in this order they passed through the[SEP]town, where the report was already rife that the King, going the night[SEP]before to remonstrate with his brother, in all friendliness, for that[SEP]he held one of the King’s friends in confinement in the Castle, had been[SEP]most traitorously set upon; that there had been a desperate conflict;[SEP]that the duke was slain with several of his gentlemen; and that the[SEP]King, wounded as he was, had seized and held the Castle of Zenda.[SEP]All of[SEP]which talk made, as may be supposed, a mighty excitement: and the wires[SEP]were set in motion, and the tidings came to Strelsau only just after[SEP]orders had been sent thither to parade the troops and overawe the[SEP]dissatisfied quarters of the town with a display of force.[EOT]
[SOT]Thus the Princess Flavia came to Zenda.[SEP]And as she drove up the hill,[SEP]with the Marshal riding by the wheel and still imploring her to return[SEP]in obedience to the King’s orders, Fritz von Tarlenheim, with the[SEP]prisoner of Zenda, came to the edge of the forest.[SEP]I had revived from[SEP]my swoon, and walked, resting on Fritz’s arm; and looking out from the[SEP]cover of the trees, I saw the princess.[SEP]Suddenly understanding from a[SEP]glance at my companion’s face that we must not meet her, I sank on my[SEP]knees behind a clump of bushes.[SEP]But there was one whom we had forgotten,[SEP]but who followed us, and was not disposed to let slip the chance of[SEP]earning a smile and maybe a crown or two; and, while we lay hidden,[SEP]the little farm-girl came by us and ran to the princess, curtseying and[SEP]crying:[EOT]
[SOT]“Madame, the King is here--in the bushes![SEP]May I guide you to him,[SEP]madame?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Nonsense, child![SEP]” said old Strakencz; “the King lies wounded in the[SEP]Castle.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, sir, he’s wounded, I know; but he’s there--with Count Fritz--and[SEP]not at the Castle,” she persisted.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is he in two places, or are there two Kings?[SEP]” asked Flavia, bewildered.[SEP]“And how should he be there?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He pursued a gentleman, madame, and they fought till Count Fritz came;[SEP]and the other gentleman took my father’s horse from me and rode away;[SEP]but the King is here with Count Fritz.[SEP]Why, madame, is there another man[SEP]in Ruritania like the King?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, my child,” said Flavia softly (I was told it afterwards), and she[SEP]smiled and gave the girl money.[SEP]“I will go and see this gentleman,” and[SEP]she rose to alight from the carriage.[EOT]
[SOT]But at this moment Sapt came riding from the Castle, and, seeing the[SEP]princess, made the best of a bad job, and cried to her that the King was[SEP]well tended and in no danger.[EOT]
[SOT]“In the Castle?[SEP]” she asked.[EOT]
[SOT]“Where else, madame?[SEP]” said he, bowing.[EOT]
[SOT]“But this girl says he is yonder--with Count Fritz.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt turned his eyes on the child with an incredulous smile.[EOT]
[SOT]“Every fine gentleman is a King to such,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why, he’s as like the King as one pea to another, madame![SEP]” cried the[SEP]girl, a little shaken but still obstinate.[EOT]
[SOT]Sapt started round.[SEP]The old Marshal’s face asked unspoken questions.[SEP]Flavia’s glance was no less eloquent.[SEP]Suspicion spread quick.[EOT]
[SOT]“I’ll ride myself and see this man,” said Sapt hastily.[EOT]
[SOT]“Nay, I’ll come myself,” said the princess.[EOT]
[SOT]“Then come alone,” he whispered.[EOT]
[SOT]And she, obedient to the strange hinting in his face, prayed the Marshal[SEP]and the rest to wait; and she and Sapt came on foot towards where we[SEP]lay, Sapt waving to the farm-girl to keep at a distance.[SEP]And when I saw[SEP]them coming, I sat in a sad heap on the ground, and buried my face in my[SEP]hands.[SEP]I could not look at her.[SEP]Fritz knelt by me, laying his hand on my[SEP]shoulder.[EOT]
[SOT]“Speak low, whatever you say,” I heard Sapt whisper as they came up; and[SEP]the next thing I heard was a low cry--half of joy, half of fear--from[SEP]the princess:[EOT]
[SOT]“It is he![SEP]Are you hurt?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]And she fell on the ground by me, and gently pulled my hands away; but I[SEP]kept my eyes to the ground.[EOT]
[SOT]“It is the King![SEP]” she said.[SEP]“Pray, Colonel Sapt, tell me where lay the[SEP]wit of the joke you played on me?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]We answered none of us; we three were silent before her.[SEP]Regardless of[SEP]them, she threw her arms round my neck and kissed me.[SEP]Then Sapt spoke in[SEP]a low hoarse whisper:[EOT]
[SOT]“It is not the King.[SEP]Don’t kiss him; he’s not the King.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She drew back for a moment; then, with an arm still round my neck, she[SEP]asked, in superb indignation:[EOT]
[SOT]“Do I not know my love?[SEP]Rudolf my love![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“It is not the King,” said old Sapt again; and a sudden sob broke from[SEP]tender-hearted Fritz.[EOT]
[SOT]It was the sob that told her no comedy was afoot.[EOT]
[SOT]“He is the King![SEP]” she cried.[SEP]“It is the King’s face--the King’s ring--my[SEP]ring![SEP]It is my love![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Your love, madame,” said old Sapt, “but not the King.[SEP]The King is there[SEP]in the Castle.[SEP]This gentleman--”[EOT]
[SOT]“Look at me, Rudolf![SEP]look at me![SEP]” she cried, taking my face between her[SEP]hands.[SEP]“Why do you let them torment me?[SEP]Tell me what it means![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then I spoke, gazing into her eyes.[EOT]
[SOT]“God forgive me, madame![SEP]” I said.[SEP]“I am not the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I felt her hands clutch my cheeks.[SEP]She gazed at me as never man’s face[SEP]was scanned yet.[SEP]And I, silent again, saw wonder born, and doubt grow,[SEP]and terror spring to life as she looked.[SEP]And very gradually the grasp of[SEP]her hands slackened; she turned to Sapt, to Fritz, and back to me: then[SEP]suddenly she reeled forward and fell in my arms; and with a great cry of[SEP]pain I gathered her to me and kissed her lips.[SEP]Sapt laid his hand on my[SEP]arm.[SEP]I looked up in his face.[SEP]And I laid her softly on the ground, and[SEP]stood up, looking on her, cursing heaven that young Rupert’s sword had[SEP]spared me for this sharper pang.[EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 21[EOT]
[SOT]If love were all![EOT]
[SOT]It was night, and I was in the cell wherein the King had lain in the[SEP]Castle of Zenda.[SEP]The great pipe that Rupert of Hentzau had nicknamed[SEP]“Jacob’s Ladder” was gone, and the lights in the room across the moat[SEP]twinkled in the darkness.[SEP]All was still; the din and clash of strife[SEP]were gone.[SEP]I had spent the day hidden in the forest, from the time when[SEP]Fritz had led me off, leaving Sapt with the princess.[SEP]Under cover of[SEP]dusk, muffled up, I had been brought to the Castle and lodged where I[SEP]now lay.[SEP]Though three men had died there--two of them by my hand--I was[SEP]not troubled by ghosts.[SEP]I had thrown myself on a pallet by the window,[SEP]and was looking out on the black water; Johann, the keeper, still pale[SEP]from his wound, but not much hurt besides, had brought me supper.[SEP]He[SEP]told me that the King was doing well, that he had seen the princess;[SEP]that she and he, Sapt and Fritz, had been long together.[SEP]Marshal[SEP]Strakencz was gone to Strelsau; Black Michael lay in his coffin, and[SEP]Antoinette de Mauban watched by him; had I not heard, from the chapel,[SEP]priests singing mass for him?[EOT]
[SOT]Outside there were strange rumours afloat.[SEP]Some said that the prisoner[SEP]of Zenda was dead; some, that he had vanished yet alive; some, that he[SEP]was a friend who had served the King well in some adventure in England;[SEP]others, that he had discovered the Duke’s plots, and had therefore been[SEP]kidnapped by him.[SEP]One or two shrewd fellows shook their heads and said[SEP]only that they would say nothing, but they had suspicions that more was[SEP]to be known than was known, if Colonel Sapt would tell all he knew.[EOT]
[SOT]Thus Johann chattered till I sent him away and lay there alone,[SEP]thinking, not of the future, but--as a man is wont to do when stirring[SEP]things have happened to him--rehearsing the events of the past weeks,[SEP]and wondering how strangely they had fallen out.[SEP]And above me, in the[SEP]stillness of the night, I heard the standards flapping against their[SEP]poles, for Black Michael’s banner hung there half-mast high, and above[SEP]it the royal flag of Ruritania, floating for one night more over my[SEP]head.[SEP]Habit grows so quick, that only by an effort did I recollect that[SEP]it floated no longer for me.[EOT]
[SOT]Presently Fritz von Tarlenheim came into the room.[SEP]I was standing then[SEP]by the window; the glass was opened, and I was idly fingering the cement[SEP]which clung to the masonry where “Jacob’s Ladder” had been.[SEP]He told me[SEP]briefly that the King wanted me, and together we crossed the drawbridge[SEP]and entered the room that had been Black Michael’s.[EOT]
[SOT]The King was lying there in bed; our doctor from Tarlenheim was in[SEP]attendance on him, and whispered to me that my visit must be brief.[SEP]The[SEP]King held out his hand and shook mine.[SEP]Fritz and the doctor withdrew to[SEP]the window.[EOT]
[SOT]I took the King’s ring from my finger and placed it on his.[EOT]
[SOT]“I have tried not to dishonour it, sire,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“I can’t talk much to you,” he said, in a weak voice.[SEP]“I have had a[SEP]great fight with Sapt and the Marshal--for we have told the Marshal[SEP]everything.[SEP]I wanted to take you to Strelsau and keep you with me, and[SEP]tell everyone of what you had done; and you would have been my best and[SEP]nearest friend, Cousin Rudolf.[SEP]But they tell me I must not, and that the[SEP]secret must be kept--if kept it can be.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They are right, sire.[SEP]Let me go.[SEP]My work here is done.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, it is done, as no man but you could have done it.[SEP]When they see me[SEP]again, I shall have my beard on; I shall--yes, faith, I shall be wasted[SEP]with sickness.[SEP]They will not wonder that the King looks changed in face.[SEP]Cousin, I shall try to let them find him changed in nothing else.[SEP]You[SEP]have shown me how to play the King.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Sire,” said I.[SEP]“I can take no praise from you.[SEP]It is by the narrowest[SEP]grace of God that I was not a worse traitor than your brother.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He turned inquiring eyes on me; but a sick man shrinks from puzzles,[SEP]and he had no strength to question me.[SEP]His glance fell on Flavia’s[SEP]ring, which I wore.[SEP]I thought he would question me about it; but, after[SEP]fingering it idly, he let his head fall on his pillow.[EOT]
[SOT]“I don’t know when I shall see you again,” he said faintly, almost[SEP]listlessly.[EOT]
[SOT]“If I can ever serve you again, sire,” I answered.[EOT]
[SOT]His eyelids closed.[SEP]Fritz came with the doctor.[SEP]I kissed the King’s[SEP]hand, and let Fritz lead me away.[SEP]I have never seen the King since.[EOT]
[SOT]Outside, Fritz turned, not to the right, back towards the drawbridge,[SEP]but to the left, and without speaking led me upstairs, through a[SEP]handsome corridor in the chateau.[EOT]
[SOT]“Where are we going?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]Looking away from me, Fritz answered:[EOT]
[SOT]“She has sent for you.[SEP]When it is over, come back to the bridge.[SEP]I’ll[SEP]wait for you there.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What does she want?[SEP]” said I, breathing quickly.[EOT]
[SOT]He shook his head.[EOT]
[SOT]“Does she know everything?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Yes, everything.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]He opened a door, and gently pushing me in, closed it behind me.[SEP]I found[SEP]myself in a drawing-room, small and richly furnished.[SEP]At first I thought[SEP]that I was alone, for the light that came from a pair of shaded candles[SEP]on the mantelpiece was very dim.[SEP]But presently I discerned a woman’s[SEP]figure standing by the window.[SEP]I knew it was the princess, and I walked[SEP]up to her, fell on one knee, and carried the hand that hung by her[SEP]side to my lips.[SEP]She neither moved nor spoke.[SEP]I rose to my feet, and,[SEP]piercing the gloom with my eager eyes, saw her pale face and the gleam[SEP]of her hair, and before I knew, I spoke softly:[EOT]
[SOT]She trembled a little, and looked round.[SEP]Then she darted to me, taking[SEP]hold of me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Don’t stand, don’t stand![SEP]No, you mustn’t![SEP]You’re hurt![SEP]Sit down--here,[SEP]here![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]She made me sit on a sofa, and put her hand on my forehead.[EOT]
[SOT]“How hot your head is,” she said, sinking on her knees by me.[SEP]Then she[SEP]laid her head against me, and I heard her murmur: “My darling, how hot[SEP]your head is![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Somehow love gives even to a dull man the knowledge of his lover’s[SEP]heart.[SEP]I had come to humble myself and pray pardon for my presumption;[SEP]but what I said now was:[EOT]
[SOT]“I love you with all my heart and soul![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]For what troubled and shamed her?[SEP]Not her love for me, but the fear that[SEP]I had counterfeited the lover as I had acted the King, and taken her[SEP]kisses with a smothered smile.[EOT]
[SOT]“With all my life and heart,” said I, as she clung to me.[SEP]“Always, from[SEP]the first moment I saw you in the Cathedral![SEP]There has been but one[SEP]woman in the world to me--and there will be no other.[SEP]But God forgive me[SEP]the wrong I’ve done you![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“They made you do it![SEP]” she said quickly; and she added, raising her head[SEP]and looking in my eyes: “It might have made no difference if I’d known[SEP]it.[SEP]It was always you, never the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I meant to tell you,” said I.[SEP]“I was going to on the night of the[SEP]ball in Strelsau, when Sapt interrupted me.[SEP]After that, I couldn’t--I[SEP]couldn’t risk losing you before--before--I must![SEP]My darling, for you I[SEP]nearly left the King to die![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I know, I know![SEP]What are we to do now, Rudolf?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I put my arm round her and held her up while I said:[EOT]
[SOT]“I am going away tonight.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Ah, no, no![SEP]” she cried.[SEP]“Not tonight![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I must go tonight, before more people have seen me.[SEP]And how would you[SEP]have me stay, sweetheart, except--?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“If I could come with you![SEP]” she whispered very low.[EOT]
[SOT]“My God![SEP]” said I roughly, “don’t talk about that![SEP]” and I thrust her a[SEP]little back from me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Why not?[SEP]I love you.[SEP]You are as good a gentleman as the King![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Then I was false to all that I should have held by.[SEP]For I caught her in[SEP]my arms and prayed her, in words that I will not write, to come with me,[SEP]daring all Ruritania to take her from me.[SEP]And for a while she listened,[SEP]with wondering, dazzled eyes.[SEP]But as her eyes looked on me, I grew[SEP]ashamed, and my voice died away in broken murmurs and stammerings, and[SEP]at last I was silent.[EOT]
[SOT]She drew herself away from me and stood against the wall, while I sat[SEP]on the edge of the sofa, trembling in every limb, knowing what I had[SEP]done--loathing it, obstinate not to undo it.[SEP]So we rested a long time.[EOT]
[SOT]“I am mad![SEP]” I said sullenly.[EOT]
[SOT]“I love your madness, dear,” she answered.[EOT]
[SOT]Her face was away from me, but I caught the sparkle of a tear on her[SEP]cheek.[SEP]I clutched the sofa with my hand and held myself there.[EOT]
[SOT]“Is love the only thing?[SEP]” she asked, in low, sweet tones that seemed[SEP]to bring a calm even to my wrung heart.[SEP]“If love were the only thing, I[SEP]would follow you--in rags, if need be--to the world’s end; for you hold[SEP]my heart in the hollow of your hand![SEP]But is love the only thing?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I made no answer.[SEP]It gives me shame now to think that I would not help[SEP]her.[EOT]
[SOT]She came near me and laid her hand on my shoulder.[SEP]I put my hand up and[SEP]held hers.[EOT]
[SOT]“I know people write and talk as if it were.[SEP]Perhaps, for some, Fate[SEP]lets it be.[SEP]Ah, if I were one of them![SEP]But if love had been the only[SEP]thing, you would have let the King die in his cell.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I kissed her hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“Honour binds a woman too, Rudolf.[SEP]My honour lies in being true to my[SEP]country and my House.[SEP]I don’t know why God has let me love you; but I[SEP]know that I must stay.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Still I said nothing; and she, pausing a while, then went on:[EOT]
[SOT]“Your ring will always be on my finger, your heart in my heart, the[SEP]touch of your lips on mine.[SEP]But you must go and I must stay.[SEP]Perhaps I[SEP]must do what it kills me to think of doing.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I knew what she meant, and a shiver ran through me.[SEP]But I could not[SEP]utterly fail her.[SEP]I rose and took her hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“Do what you will, or what you must,” I said.[SEP]“I think God shows His[SEP]purposes to such as you.[SEP]My part is lighter; for your ring shall be on[SEP]my finger and your heart in mine, and no touch save of your lips will[SEP]ever be on mine.[SEP]So, may God comfort you, my darling![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]There struck on our ears the sound of singing.[SEP]The priests in the chapel[SEP]were singing masses for the souls of those who lay dead.[SEP]They seemed to[SEP]chant a requiem over our buried joy, to pray forgiveness for our love[SEP]that would not die.[SEP]The soft, sweet, pitiful music rose and fell as we[SEP]stood opposite one another, her hands in mine.[EOT]
[SOT]“My queen and my beauty![SEP]” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“My lover and true knight![SEP]” she said.[SEP]“Perhaps we shall never see one[SEP]another again.[SEP]Kiss me, my dear, and go![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]I kissed her as she bade me; but at the last she clung to me, whispering[SEP]nothing but my name, and that over and over again--and again--and again;[SEP]and then I left her.[EOT]
[SOT]Rapidly I walked down to the bridge.[SEP]Sapt and Fritz were waiting for me.[SEP]Under their directions I changed my dress, and muffling my face, as I[SEP]had done more than once before, I mounted with them at the door of the[SEP]Castle, and we three rode through the night and on to the breaking day,[SEP]and found ourselves at a little roadside station just over the border[SEP]of Ruritania.[SEP]The train was not quite due, and I walked with them in a[SEP]meadow by a little brook while we waited for it.[SEP]They promised to[SEP]send me all news; they overwhelmed me with kindness--even old Sapt was[SEP]touched to gentleness, while Fritz was half unmanned.[SEP]I listened in a[SEP]kind of dream to all they said.[SEP]“Rudolf![SEP]Rudolf![SEP]Rudolf![SEP]” still rang in[SEP]my ears--a burden of sorrow and of love.[SEP]At last they saw that I could[SEP]not heed them, and we walked up and down in silence, till Fritz touched[SEP]me on the arm, and I saw, a mile or more away, the blue smoke of the[SEP]train.[SEP]Then I held out a hand to each of them.[EOT]
[SOT]“We are all but half-men this morning,” said I, smiling.[SEP]“But we have[SEP]been men, eh, Sapt and Fritz, old friends?[SEP]We have run a good course[SEP]between us.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“We have defeated traitors and set the King firm on his throne,” said[SEP]Sapt.[EOT]
[SOT]Then Fritz von Tarlenheim suddenly, before I could discern his purpose[SEP]or stay him, uncovered his head and bent as he used to do, and kissed my[SEP]hand; and as I snatched it away, he said, trying to laugh:[EOT]
[SOT]“Heaven doesn’t always make the right men kings![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Old Sapt twisted his mouth as he wrung my hand.[EOT]
[SOT]“The devil has his share in most things,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]The people at the station looked curiously at the tall man with the[SEP]muffled face, but we took no notice of their glances.[SEP]I stood with my[SEP]two friends and waited till the train came up to us.[SEP]Then we shook hands[SEP]again, saying nothing; and both this time--and, indeed, from old Sapt[SEP]it seemed strange--bared their heads, and so stood still till the train[SEP]bore me away from their sight.[SEP]So that it was thought some great man[SEP]travelled privately for his pleasure from the little station that[SEP]morning; whereas, in truth it was only I, Rudolf Rassendyll, an English[SEP]gentleman, a cadet of a good house, but a man of no wealth nor position,[SEP]nor of much rank.[SEP]They would have been disappointed to know that.[SEP]Yet[SEP]had they known all they would have looked more curiously still.[SEP]For, be[SEP]I what I might now, I had been for three months a King, which, if not[SEP]a thing to be proud of, is at least an experience to have undergone.[SEP]Doubtless I should have thought more of it, had there not echoed through[SEP]the air, from the towers of Zenda that we were leaving far away, into[SEP]my ears and into my heart the cry of a woman’s love--“Rudolf![SEP]Rudolf![SEP]Rudolf![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Hark![SEP]I hear it now![EOT]
[SOT]CHAPTER 22[EOT]
[SOT]Present, Past--and Future?[EOT]
[SOT]The details of my return home can have but little interest.[SEP]I went[SEP]straight to the Tyrol and spent a quiet fortnight--mostly on my back,[SEP]for a severe chill developed itself; and I was also the victim of a[SEP]nervous reaction, which made me weak as a baby.[SEP]As soon as I had reached[SEP]my quarters, I sent an apparently careless postcard to my brother,[SEP]announcing my good health and prospective return.[SEP]That would serve to[SEP]satisfy the inquiries as to my whereabouts, which were probably still[SEP]vexing the Prefect of the Police of Strelsau.[SEP]I let my moustache and[SEP]imperial grow again; and as hair comes quickly on my face, they were[SEP]respectable, though not luxuriant, by the time that I landed myself in[SEP]Paris and called on my friend George Featherly.[SEP]My interview with[SEP]him was chiefly remarkable for the number of unwilling but necessary[SEP]falsehoods that I told; and I rallied him unmercifully when he told me[SEP]that he had made up his mind that I had gone in the track of Madame de[SEP]Mauban to Strelsau.[SEP]The lady, it appeared, was back in Paris, but was[SEP]living in great seclusion--a fact for which gossip found no difficulty[SEP]in accounting.[SEP]Did not all the world know of the treachery and death[SEP]of Duke Michael?[SEP]Nevertheless, George bade Bertram Bertrand be of good[SEP]cheer, “for,” said he flippantly, “a live poet is better than a dead[SEP]duke.[SEP]” Then he turned on me and asked:[EOT]
[SOT]“What have you been doing to your moustache?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“To tell the truth,” I answered, assuming a sly air, “a man now and then[SEP]has reasons for wishing to alter his appearance.[SEP]But it’s coming on very[SEP]well again.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“What?[SEP]Then I wasn’t so far out![SEP]If not the fair Antoinette, there was a[SEP]charmer?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“There is always a charmer,” said I, sententiously.[EOT]
[SOT]But George would not be satisfied till he had wormed out of me (he[SEP]took much pride in his ingenuity) an absolutely imaginary love-affair,[SEP]attended with the proper _soupçon_ of scandal, which had kept me all this[SEP]time in the peaceful regions of the Tyrol.[SEP]In return for this[SEP]narrative, George regaled me with a great deal of what he called “inside[SEP]information” (known only to diplomatists), as to the true course of[SEP]events in Ruritania, the plots and counterplots.[SEP]In his opinion, he told[SEP]me, with a significant nod, there was more to be said for Black Michael[SEP]than the public supposed; and he hinted at a well-founded suspicion that[SEP]the mysterious prisoner of Zenda, concerning whom a good many paragraphs[SEP]had appeared, was not a man at all, but (here I had much ado not to[SEP]smile) a woman disguised as a man; and that strife between the King and[SEP]his brother for this imaginary lady’s favour was at the bottom of their[SEP]quarrel.[EOT]
[SOT]“Perhaps it was Madame de Mauban herself,” I suggested.[EOT]
[SOT]“No![SEP]” said George decisively, “Antoinette de Mauban was jealous of her,[SEP]and betrayed the duke to the King for that reason.[SEP]And, to confirm what[SEP]I say, it’s well known that the Princess Flavia is now extremely cold to[SEP]the King, after having been most affectionate.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]At this point I changed the subject, and escaped from George’s[SEP]“inspired” delusions.[SEP]But if diplomatists never know anything more than[SEP]they had succeeded in finding out in this instance, they appear to me to[SEP]be somewhat expensive luxuries.[EOT]
[SOT]While in Paris I wrote to Antoinette, though I did not venture to call[SEP]upon her.[SEP]I received in return a very affecting letter, in which she[SEP]assured me that the King’s generosity and kindness, no less than her[SEP]regard for me, bound her conscience to absolute secrecy.[SEP]She expressed[SEP]the intention of settling in the country, and withdrawing herself[SEP]entirely from society.[SEP]Whether she carried out her designs, I have never[SEP]heard; but as I have not met her, or heard news of her up to this time,[SEP]it is probable that she did.[SEP]There is no doubt that she was deeply[SEP]attached to the Duke of Strelsau; and her conduct at the time of his[SEP]death proved that no knowledge of the man’s real character was enough to[SEP]root her regard for him out of her heart.[EOT]
[SOT]I had one more battle left to fight--a battle that would, I knew, be[SEP]severe, and was bound to end in my complete defeat.[SEP]Was I not back[SEP]from the Tyrol, without having made any study of its inhabitants,[SEP]institutions, scenery, fauna, flora, or other features?[SEP]Had I not simply[SEP]wasted my time in my usual frivolous, good-for-nothing way?[SEP]That was the[SEP]aspect of the matter which, I was obliged to admit, would present itself[SEP]to my sister-in-law; and against a verdict based on such evidence, I had[SEP]really no defence to offer.[SEP]It may be supposed, then, that I presented[SEP]myself in Park Lane in a shamefaced, sheepish fashion.[SEP]On the whole, my[SEP]reception was not so alarming as I had feared.[SEP]It turned out that I[SEP]had done, not what Rose wished, but--the next best thing--what she[SEP]prophesied.[SEP]She had declared that I should make no notes, record no[SEP]observations, gather no materials.[SEP]My brother, on the other hand,[SEP]had been weak enough to maintain that a serious resolve had at length[SEP]animated me.[EOT]
[SOT]When I returned empty-handed, Rose was so occupied in triumphing over[SEP]Burlesdon that she let me down quite easily, devoting the greater[SEP]part of her reproaches to my failure to advertise my friends of my[SEP]whereabouts.[EOT]
[SOT]“We’ve wasted a lot of time trying to find you,” she said.[EOT]
[SOT]“I know you have,” said I.[SEP]“Half our ambassadors have led weary lives[SEP]on my account.[SEP]George Featherly told me so.[SEP]But why should you have been[SEP]anxious?[SEP]I can take care of myself.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, it wasn’t that,” she cried scornfully, “but I wanted to tell you[SEP]about Sir Jacob Borrodaile.[SEP]You know, he’s got an Embassy--at least,[SEP]he will have in a month--and he wrote to say he hoped you would go with[SEP]him.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Where’s he going to?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“He’s going to succeed Lord Topham at Strelsau,” said she.[SEP]“You couldn’t[SEP]have a nicer place, short of Paris.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Strelsau![SEP]H’m![SEP]” said I, glancing at my brother.[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, _that_ doesn’t matter![SEP]” exclaimed Rose impatiently.[SEP]“Now, you will[SEP]go, won’t you?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I don’t know that I care about it![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, you’re too exasperating![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And I don’t think I can go to Strelsau.[SEP]My dear Rose, would it[SEP]be--suitable?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“Oh, nobody remembers that horrid old story now.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Upon this, I took out of my pocket a portrait of the King of Ruritania.[SEP]It had been taken a month or two before he ascended the throne.[SEP]She[SEP]could not miss my point when I said, putting it into her hands:[EOT]
[SOT]“In case you’ve not seen, or not noticed, a picture of Rudolf V, there[SEP]he is.[SEP]Don’t you think they might recall the story, if I appeared at the[SEP]Court of Ruritania?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]My sister-in-law looked at the portrait, and then at me.[EOT]
[SOT]“Good gracious![SEP]” she said, and flung the photograph down on the table.[EOT]
[SOT]“What do you say, Bob?[SEP]” I asked.[EOT]
[SOT]Burlesdon got up, went to a corner of the room, and searched in a heap[SEP]of newspapers.[SEP]Presently he came back with a copy of the Illustrated[SEP]London News.[SEP]Opening the paper, he displayed a double-page engraving of[SEP]the Coronation of Rudolf V at Strelsau.[SEP]The photograph and the picture[SEP]he laid side by side.[SEP]I sat at the table fronting them; and, as I[SEP]looked, I grew absorbed.[SEP]My eye travelled from my own portrait to Sapt,[SEP]to Strakencz, to the rich robes of the Cardinal, to Black Michael’s[SEP]face, to the stately figure of the princess by his side.[SEP]Long I looked[SEP]and eagerly.[SEP]I was roused by my brother’s hand on my shoulder.[SEP]He was[SEP]gazing down at me with a puzzled expression.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s a remarkable likeness, you see,” said I.[SEP]“I really think I had[SEP]better not go to Ruritania.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]Rose, though half convinced, would not abandon her position.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s just an excuse,” she said pettishly.[SEP]“You don’t want to do[SEP]anything.[SEP]Why, you might become an ambassador![SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I don’t think I want to be an ambassador,” said I.[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s more than you ever will be,” she retorted.[EOT]
[SOT]That is very likely true, but it is not more than I have been.[EOT]
[SOT]The idea of being an ambassador could scarcely dazzle me.[SEP]I had been a[SEP]king![EOT]
[SOT]So pretty Rose left us in dudgeon; and Burlesdon, lighting a cigarette,[SEP]looked at me still with that curious gaze.[EOT]
[SOT]“That picture in the paper--” he said.[EOT]
[SOT]“Well, what of it?[SEP]It shows that the King of Ruritania and your humble[SEP]servant are as like as two peas.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]My brother shook his head.[EOT]
[SOT]“I suppose so,” he said.[SEP]“But I should know you from the man in the[SEP]photograph.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“And not from the picture in the paper?[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“I should know the photograph from the picture: the picture’s very like[SEP]the photograph, but--”[EOT]
[SOT]“It’s more like you![SEP]” said my brother.[EOT]
[SOT]My brother is a good man and true--so that, for all that he is a married[SEP]man and mighty fond of his wife, he should know any secret of mine.[SEP]But[SEP]this secret was not mine, and I could not tell it to him.[EOT]
[SOT]“I don’t think it’s so much like me as the photograph,” said I boldly.[SEP]“But, anyhow, Bob, I won’t go to Strelsau.[SEP]”[EOT]
[SOT]“No, don’t go to Strelsau, Rudolf,” said he.[EOT]
[SOT]And whether he suspects anything, or has a glimmer of the truth, I do[SEP]not know.[SEP]If he has, he keeps it to himself, and he and I never refer to[SEP]it.[SEP]And we let Sir Jacob Borrodaile find another attaché.[EOT]
[SOT]Since all these events whose history I have set down happened I have[SEP]lived a very quiet life at a small house which I have taken in the[SEP]country.[SEP]The ordinary ambitions and aims of men in my position seem to[SEP]me dull and unattractive.[SEP]I have little fancy for the whirl of society,[SEP]and none for the jostle of politics.[SEP]Lady Burlesdon utterly despairs of[SEP]me; my neighbours think me an indolent, dreamy, unsociable fellow.[SEP]Yet[SEP]I am a young man; and sometimes I have a fancy--the superstitious would[SEP]call it a presentiment--that my part in life is not yet altogether[SEP]played; that, somehow and some day, I shall mix again in great affairs,[SEP]I shall again spin policies in a busy brain, match my wits against my[SEP]enemies’, brace my muscles to fight a good fight and strike stout blows.[SEP]Such is the tissue of my thoughts as, with gun or rod in hand, I wander[SEP]through the woods or by the side of the stream.[SEP]Whether the fancy will[SEP]be fulfilled, I cannot tell--still less whether the scene that, led by[SEP]memory, I lay for my new exploits will be the true one--for I love to[SEP]see myself once again in the crowded streets of Strelsau, or beneath the[SEP]frowning keep of the Castle of Zenda.[EOT]
[SOT]Thus led, my broodings leave the future, and turn back on the past.[SEP]Shapes rise before me in long array--the wild first revel with the King,[SEP]the rush with my brave tea-table, the night in the moat, the pursuit in[SEP]the forest: my friends and my foes, the people who learnt to love and[SEP]honour me, the desperate men who tried to kill me.[SEP]And, from amidst[SEP]these last, comes one who alone of all of them yet moves on earth,[SEP]though where I know not, yet plans (as I do not doubt) wickedness, yet[SEP]turns women’s hearts to softness and men’s to fear and hate.[SEP]Where is[SEP]young Rupert of Hentzau--the boy who came so nigh to beating me?[SEP]When[SEP]his name comes into my head, I feel my hand grip and the blood move[SEP]quicker through my veins: and the hint of Fate--the presentiment--seems[SEP]to grow stronger and more definite, and to whisper insistently in my ear[SEP]that I have yet a hand to play with young Rupert; therefore I exercise[SEP]myself in arms, and seek to put off the day when the vigour of youth[SEP]must leave me.[EOT]
[SOT]One break comes every year in my quiet life.[SEP]Then I go to Dresden, and[SEP]there I am met by my dear friend and companion, Fritz von Tarlenheim.[SEP]Last time, his pretty wife Helga came, and a lusty crowing baby with[SEP]her.[SEP]And for a week Fritz and I are together, and I hear all of what[SEP]falls out in Strelsau; and in the evenings, as we walk and smoke[SEP]together, we talk of Sapt, and of the King, and often of young Rupert;[SEP]and, as the hours grow small, at last we speak of Flavia.[SEP]For every year[SEP]Fritz carries with him to Dresden a little box; in it lies a red rose,[SEP]and round the stalk of the rose is a slip of paper with the words[SEP]written: “Rudolf--Flavia--always.[SEP]” And the like I send back by him.[SEP]That[SEP]message, and the wearing of the rings, are all that now bind me and the[SEP]Queen of Ruritania.[SEP]For--nobler, as I hold her, for the act--she has[SEP]followed where her duty to her country and her House led her, and is the[SEP]wife of the King, uniting his subjects to him by the love they bear to[SEP]her, giving peace and quiet days to thousands by her self-sacrifice.[SEP]There are moments when I dare not think of it, but there are others when[SEP]I rise in spirit to where she ever dwells; then I can thank God that I[SEP]love the noblest lady in the world, the most gracious and beautiful, and[SEP]that there was nothing in my love that made her fall short in her high[SEP]duty.[EOT]
[SOT]Shall I see her face again--the pale face and the glorious hair?[SEP]Of that[SEP]I know nothing; Fate has no hint, my heart no presentiment.[SEP]I do not[SEP]know.[SEP]In this world, perhaps--nay, it is likely--never.[SEP]And can it[SEP]be that somewhere, in a manner whereof our flesh-bound minds have no[SEP]apprehension, she and I will be together again, with nothing to come[SEP]between us, nothing to forbid our love?[SEP]That I know not, nor wiser heads[SEP]than mine.[SEP]But if it be never--if I can never hold sweet converse again[SEP]with her, or look upon her face, or know from her her love; why, then,[SEP]this side the grave, I will live as becomes the man whom she loves; and,[SEP]for the other side, I must pray a dreamless sleep.[EOT]
